# Advances in Optics and Photonics

# Fundamentals to emerging concepts and applications of metasurfaces for flat optics: a tutorial

YUBIN GAO $^{1,2}$ ID AND YAOGUANG MA $^{1,2,3,*}$ ID

$^{1}$ State Key Laboratory of Extreme Photonics and Instrumentation, College of Optical Science and Engineering, International Research Center for Advanced Photonics, Zhejiang University, Hangzhou 310058, China

$^{2}$ ZJU-Hangzhou Global Scientific and Technological Innovation Center, Hangzhou 311200, China

$^{3}$ Jiaxing Key Laboratory of Photonic Sensing and Intelligent Imaging, Intelligent Optics and Photonics Research Center, Jiaxing Research Institute, Zhejiang University, Jiaxing 314000, China
\*mayaoguang@zju.edu.cn

Received September 12, 2024; revised July 1, 2025; accepted July 27, 2025; published November 13, 2025

Metasurfaces offer unprecedented freedom in the flexible and efficient manipulation of light across multiple dimensions such as amplitude, phase, and polarization, enabling the realization of low-cost, high-performance, ultra-lightweight, and ultra-thin optical elements with novel functionalities to overcome traditional optical limitations in volume, weight, functionality, and efficiency. In recent years, this potential has attracted growing attention from both academic and industrial communities. After more than a decade of development, on the one hand, the design of a wide range of metasurfaces has gradually become mature, encompassing physical principles, design methodologies, and devices for diverse applications; on the other hand, metasurfaces with novel physical concepts and functionalities have emerged through integration with other disciplines, including nonlinearity, chirality, optical resonance, non-Hermitian physics, Fourier optics, and topological photonics. In this tutorial, we propose a unified and forward-looking theoretical framework that connects traditional metasurfaces with these emerging metasurfaces, aiming to clarify their underlying relationships and development trends. We introduce the mainstream design methodologies, highlighting the most recent advancements. We further examine five representative application areas: metalenses, metasurface holograms, quantum photonic metasurfaces, optical computing metasurfaces, and active metasurfaces, in terms of their principles, evaluation criteria, theoretical analyses, and design methods. By bridging fundamentals and emerging concepts under a unified perspective, this tutorial aims to provide a holistic physical understanding and critical insight into the global metasurface landscape, from fundamentals to emerging concepts and applications. © 2025 Optica Publishing Group. All rights, including for text and data mining (TDM), Artificial Intelligence (AI) training, and similar technologies, are reserved.

# Fundamentals to emerging concepts and applications of metasurfaces for flat optics: a tutorial

YUBIN GAO AND YAOGUANG MA

# 1. INTRODUCTION

From the earliest polished obsidian mirrors to the advanced objectives used in lithography systems, humanity's pursuit of controlling light–matter interactions has continually driven technological progress across civilizations. This enduring endeavor stems from a fundamental truth: our ability to engineer photonic responses at will directly governs the sophistication of optical systems that underpin both scientific discovery and industrial advancement. In the 21st century, metasurfaces have emerged as the apotheosis of this millennia-spanning pursuit, unifying three distinct yet convergent paradigms of light manipulation through subwavelength-structured matter, i.e., diffraction scaling, metamaterial flattening, and antenna engineering.

The dimensionality reduction paradigm: from diffractive optics to subwavelength flatland engineering. Human understanding of diffraction phenomena began with Grimaldi's optical experiments in the 17th century, but it was not until Fresnel's wave theory in the 19th century that diffraction was systematically studied. Diffractive optical elements (DOEs) are meticulously engineered structures that exploit the wave nature of light, relying on diffraction theory to manipulate optical fields [1]. The evolution of DOEs has charted a significant path in the field of wavefront control, beginning with optical holograms and advancing through innovations such as computer-generated holograms and binary optical elements.

Early micro-structured DOEs, typically with pixel sizes larger than the operating wavelength, found widespread application in imaging, display, data transmission, and signal processing. However, their efficiency was limited by inherent multi-order diffraction losses. The 1980s marked a paradigm shift with the advent of binary optics, where micron-scale step structures enabled discrete phase modulation, boosting diffraction efficiency beyond 70%.

With advancements in fabrication and computational technologies, nano-DOEs characterized by subwavelength pixel sizes emerged, enabling more precise control over the phase, amplitude, polarization, and spectrum of light $[2]$ . Researchers soon realized that shrinking structural dimensions to subwavelength scales suppressed higher-order diffraction modes, thereby channeling more than 90% of energy into zero-order transmission. This principle redefined diffractive optics: metasurfaces—comprising arrays of subwavelength scatterers or holes (meta-atoms) on a substrate—have become the most prominent and versatile class of nano-DOEs $[3,4]$ .

In contrast to traditional DOEs, which have been widely utilized in various industries, companies specializing in metasurfaces, such as Metalenz and Najing, are gaining more interest from the industrial sector due to the potential of transforming metasurfaces from scientific research into real-world applications $[5–7]$ . The subwavelength scatterer-based architecture of metasurfaces offers rich resonant behavior and significantly greater design flexibility for light manipulation. This enhanced control

has enabled the development of optical devices with performance surpassing that of conventional DOEs, as well as novel functionalities previously unattainable. Examples include metalenses that simultaneously achieve high efficiency and large numerical aperture (NA) [8,9], metalenses with broad angular bandwidths [10], and polarization-sensitive or multiplexed holographic devices [11,12].

# The metamaterial lineage: from volumetric composites to the planar revolution.

Another foundational origin of metasurfaces lies in metamaterials—macroscopic composites formed from spatially arranged periodic, aperiodic, or even random subwavelength structures such as metal rings, metal rods, etc. [13]. The electromagnetic properties of metamaterials are not solely determined by the constituent materials but also critically depend on their structural configuration [14]. Through careful structural design, metamaterials can exhibit a range of electromagnetic properties not available in natural materials, potentially bringing the control over electromagnetic waves to an unprecedented level. In 1967, Veselago [15] first proposed the concept of left-handed materials (LHMs) and conducted an inspiring study of their electromagnetic characteristics, which heralded the birth of the field of metamaterials. In 1996 and 1999, Pendry and his collaborators designed materials with negative permittivity [16] and materials with negative magnetic permeability [17], respectively. Materials that simultaneously exhibit negative permittivity and negative magnetic permeability, LHMs, were first achieved by Shelby et al. in 2001 [18], thus providing experimental validation for negative refractive index materials. With the development and refinement of theories such as transformation optics [19], homogenization theory for periodic structure metamaterials [20], scatter parameter (S-parameter) methods [21], and effective medium theory [22], the design methodologies for metamaterials have gradually matured, enabling their widespread application in diverse fields including imaging [23], invisibility cloaks [19,24–26], topological photonics [27], and radiative cooling [28,29]. However, the unit structures constituting metamaterials often require subwavelength-scale dimensions, making the fabrication of devices challenging as the operating wavelength decreases. Due to processing limitations, early studies on metamaterials mostly operated near the microwave region [25,26], and extending applications to shorter wavelengths faces significant constraints [30,31].

As a two-dimensional (2D) form of metamaterials, metasurfaces have been proposed both to replace metamaterials in specific applications and to enhance the realization of novel functionalities $[32–35]$ . The dimensional reduction significantly simplifies fabrication processes $[36]$ , while preserving substantial design flexibility and expanding the operational wavelength range. In terms of working principle, compared to metamaterials, metasurfaces rely on phase discontinuities at the interface, occupying a smaller physical space (the thickness of the meta-atoms can even be much less than the operating wavelength $[37]$ ), thereby resulting in lower losses $[38]$ to meet the needs of practical applications.

# The antenna revolution: from Hertzian dipoles to optical nanoantenna arrays.

The integration of antenna principles with metasurfaces builds upon foundational concepts established through the 19th-century development of antenna theory, beginning with Hertz's 1887 dipole experiments, which first demonstrated directional electromagnetic radiation. The traditional approach to wavefront engineering involves using arrays of antennas, such as reflectarray and transmitarray antennas, where subwavelength antenna radiators with phase delay accessories serve as the fundamental elements to achieve independent phase shift control on a 2D plane for the reflected or transmitted wave $[39,40]$ . For instance, by the mid-20th century, microwave phased arrays—such as the AN/FPS-85 radar deployed in 1965—demonstrated beam steering through spatially controlled phase delay units.

Metasurfaces evolved from reflectarray and transmitarray antennas, with many of their fundamental theories borrowed directly from antenna array theories $[41]$ . From the antenna perspective, each meta-atom of the metasurface is viewed as an electrically small antenna that receives power from incident waves and re-emits them to form reflected or transmitted wavefronts. Inspired by antenna arrays, not only were passive metasurfaces for beam steering and focusing proposed $[42]$ , but also active metasurfaces with reconfigurable functions, similar to phased arrays, were developed $[43]$ .

Early demonstrations of antenna-inspired metasurfaces concentrated on microwave, terahertz, and other low-frequency bands. Translating phased-array concepts to optical frequencies encountered two fundamental challenges: (i) fabrication limitations in achieving subwavelength optical antennas with nanoscale precision (antennas have characteristic dimensions on the order of the wavelength of light, requiring fabrication accuracies better than 10 nm $[44]$ ) and (ii) conventional metallic antennas suffering from severe Ohmic losses at visible wavelengths. Recently, with advancements in fabrication processes and the introduction of dielectric metasurfaces, the design and manufacturing of optical metasurfaces have gradually matured. However, realizing a 2D programmable phased array at optical frequencies remains a significant challenge with current processes $[45]$ .

We note that, in recent years, another emerging trend is the use of metasurfaces in antenna designs as substrates, superstrates, or even the radiator aperture to enhance performance and enable novel function manipulation $[41,46]$ . Metasurfaces, initially derived from antenna technology, have now advanced antenna designs, illustrating the fascinating interplay between these two scientific fields.

Compared to traditional optical components, metasurfaces offer an ultra-light, ultra-thin, and easy-to-integrate planar architecture supporting efficient, powerful, and flexible electromagnetic modulation, with the potential for low-cost mass production. With these advantages, metasurfaces are widely regarded as next-generation flat optical devices, offering rich and impactful applications across a broad range of fields such as imaging $[47]$ , holography $[48]$ , biosensing $[49]$ , encryption $[50]$ , metrology $[51]$ , spectrometers $[52]$ , optoelectronic devices $[53]$ , quantum optics $[54]$ , nonlinear optics $[55]$ , structured light $[56]$ , optical vortex $[57]$ , near-eye display $[58]$ , and ultrafast optics $[59]$ , among others. Furthermore, the applications of metasurfaces have covered a wide spectrum, from microwaves $[60,61]$ , terahertz $[62,63]$ , infrared $[64,65]$ , and visible light $[66,67]$ , to even ultraviolet $[68,69]$ .

Over the past decade, the development of metasurfaces has revolutionized our understanding of light–matter interaction and our ability to manipulate light fields at the nanoscale. On the one hand, the physical foundations and design methodologies of metasurfaces have evolved from early explorations $[2,70–72]$ to a mature and well-established framework $[33]$ , with research interest gradually shifting from academia to industry. On the other hand, the integration of metasurfaces with other disciplines—such as quantum optics, nonlinear optics, photonic crystals, Moiré optics, topological photonics, and active optics—has led to the emergence of metasurfaces based on novel physical concepts, unlocking intriguing new applications and injecting fresh vitality into the field. In this context, metasurfaces have transcended their traditional role as specialized optical elements, evolving into a multidisciplinary platform that bridges cutting-edge fundamental research and industrial deployment. Given these advancements, a systematic review and in-depth critical analysis of the global metasurface landscape is both timely and essential—beginning with fundamental principles and extending to emerging physical concepts and frontier applications.

Schematic of structure of the tutorial.

As illustrated in Fig. 1, this tutorial is organized along three key dimensions: physical principles, design methodologies, and representative applications, aiming to distill the fundamental concepts and underlying design philosophies of metasurfaces. First, we present a systematic conceptual framework covering both conventional and emerging metasurface types, along with a discussion of their theoretical limits (Section 2). Second, we review mainstream design methodologies—encompassing both forward and inverse design approaches—and analyze their respective characteristics and application scenarios in detail (Section 3). Third, we provide a comprehensive overview of the most representative and actively researched application domains, including metalenses (Section 4), metasurface holography (Section 5), quantum photonics metasurfaces (Section 6), metasurface-enabled optical computing (Section 7), and active metasurfaces (Section 8). For each category, we outline the operating principles, performance metrics, theoretical foundations, design strategies, and recent progress. Our discussion particularly emphasizes efficiency, field of view (FOV), bandwidth, and various multiplexing techniques, which are critical themes relevant to all metasurface applications. The design strategies reviewed here may also offer valuable insights for the development of other metasurface devices. Finally, we summarize the key challenges that metasurfaces currently face from multiple perspectives, explore potential solutions, and offer a forward-looking discussion on future directions in the field (Section 9).

A collection of open-source design workflows for metasurfaces is provided in this study $[73]$ . Utilizing the API of the three-dimensional (3D) Electromagnetic Simulator (FDTD) from Lumerical Inc. for electromagnetic simulations, the code streamlines the metasurface design and analysis process, achieving an efficient, flexible, and fully automated workflow entirely within the Python environment. The workflows include demonstrations for designing propagation phase metasurfaces, geometric phase metasurfaces, metagratings, metalenses, metasurface holograms, vortex-generating metasurfaces, and quasi-symmetry-protected bound states in the continuum (BIC), among others. Each demonstration is accompanied by a corresponding Python notebook, enabling readers to explore the workflows in detail and adapt them to their own metasurface design projects.

# 2. PRINCIPLES OF TRADITIONAL AND EMERGING METASURFACES

Traditional metasurfaces achieve space-varying amplitude or phase modulation through a single-layer planar arrangement of meta-atoms with varying shapes, dimensions, and rotation angles, enabling highly flexible wavefront manipulation of incident light. As this field has advanced, physical concepts such as nonlocality, BIC, guided mode resonances, exceptional points, and spatiotemporal modulation—originating from disciplines such as quantum mechanics, Fourier optics, photonic crystals, nonlinear optics, and active optics—have been incorporated into metasurfaces. These developments have given rise to a wide range of emerging metasurfaces exhibiting novel physical effects and promising practical applications.

Amid this rapid progression, the theoretical frameworks presented in earlier reviews have become insufficient to encompass recent breakthroughs, leaving gaps in understanding the diverse mechanisms underlying various metasurfaces. To address this gap, this section aims to establish a systematic framework that serves two key objectives:

(1) Reinterpreting traditional metasurface theories by integrating them with recent advancements (Subsection 2.1).  
(2) Providing explicit and detailed discussions of the underlying physics behind various emerging metasurface paradigms (Subsection 2.2).

Additionally, the fundamental limits of metasurfaces are further explored in Subsection 2.3.

# 2.1. Traditional Metasurfaces

Essentially, a metasurface is a 2D array of meta-atoms. Through the elaborate arrangement of diverse meta-atoms, rich and complex applications can be realized. From the perspective of meta-atoms, metasurfaces can be classified into the inhomogeneous metasurface and homogeneous metasurface $[74]$ . The former employs meta-atoms of various shapes, sizes, or rotation angles, each possessing a specific electromagnetic response. The latter consists of a periodic arrangement of identical meta-atoms, and the electromagnetic wave manipulation characteristics of the entire metasurface can be reduced to the examination of a single meta-atom under the periodic boundary condition. Homogeneous metasurfaces can be seen as a special case of inhomogeneous metasurfaces, but they also have their unique applications in areas such as perfect absorbers $[75]$ , polarization control $[76]$ , and enhanced transmittance or reflectance $[77–79]$ . An elaborate review for homogeneous metasurfaces can be found in reference $[74]$ . It is clear that understanding the working principle of homogeneous metasurfaces is fundamental to grasping the concept of inhomogeneous metasurfaces, and the key to understanding homogeneous metasurfaces lies in the electromagnetic properties of meta-atoms.

# 2.1.1. Fundamental Resonances in Meta-Atoms

A general physical picture of resonances in metasurfaces. Meta-atoms are metallic, dielectric, or hybrid structures with subwavelength dimensions. For an isolated metallic meta-atom, localized surface plasmon resonances arise due to collective oscillations of conduction electrons. In contrast, isolated dielectric meta-atoms support resonances that can be broadly categorized into Mie-like modes and Fabry–Pérot-like (FP-like) modes. These resonance characteristics are primarily determined by the shape, size, and material composition of the meta-atoms.

When meta-atoms are arranged periodically, their interactions give rise to collective resonances known as lattice modes. These modes depend on both the intrinsic resonances of individual meta-atoms and the properties of the lattice arrangement, including lattice type and periodicity. Lattice modes are typically classified into two categories: local lattice resonances and nonlocal guided-mode resonances $[80]$ . Local lattice resonances are spatially confined, with weak or negligible coupling between meta-atoms, whereas nonlocal resonances are spatially extended, resulting from strong inter-meta-atom coupling.

Spectral overlap between different isolated or lattice modes enables control over both the amplitude and directionality of scattered fields. For instance, interference between electric dipole (ED) and magnetic dipole (MD) modes of equal strength and in-phase alignment fulfills the Kerker condition, suppressing backward scattering while enhancing forward scattering $[81]$ . Similarly, interference between ED and toroidal dipole (TD) modes can suppress far-field radiation, resulting in a nonradiating mode known as an anapole mode $[82–85]$ .

BICs are nonradiative optical states whose energies lie within the continuum of radiating modes. BICs arise when coupling between the resonant mode and all available radiation channels is eliminated due to symmetry mismatch or continuous system parameter tuning. As a result, radiation loss vanishes and the quality factor (Q-factor) tends to infinity. BICs can occur in both isolated meta-atoms and homogeneous/inhomogeneous metasurfaces. In practice, however, ideal BICs evolve into high-Q-factor resonant modes, commonly referred to as quasi-BICs, due to limitations such as finite metasurface size, material absorption, and fabrication imperfections.

In scattering spectra, resonances—such as localized surface plasmon, Mie, lattice, and quasi-BIC modes—often appear as Fano resonances, characterized by asymmetric spectral line shapes. These arise from the interference between a narrow resonant mode and a spectrally broad background contributed by non-resonant modes $[86,87]$ . The Fano resonance spectrum is described by the Fano formula:

$$
I (\omega) = I _ {0} \frac {1}{1 + q ^ {2}} \frac {(q + \Omega) ^ {2}}{1 + \Omega^ {2}} + I _ {\text {background}}, \tag {1}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $I_0$ is a scaling factor, $q$ is the Fano asymmetry parameter, $\Omega = 2(\omega - \omega_0) / \gamma$ is the dimensionless frequency, $\omega_0$ is the resonance frequency, $\gamma$ is the full width at half maximum (FWHM) of the resonance (or the inverse radiation lifetime [88]), and $I_{\mathrm{background}}$ is the background intensity. Typically, the frequency dependence of $q$ , $I_0$ , and $I_{\mathrm{background}}$ is smooth and thus negligible. By fitting simulated or experimental spectra with Eq. (1), the resonance Q-factor can be extracted as

$$
Q = \frac {\omega_ {0}}{\gamma}. \tag {2}
$$

This subsection focuses on the aforementioned local resonant modes in metasurfaces, while nonlocal modes with significantly higher Q-factors will be further discussed in Subsection 2.2.3.

Electromagnetic response of meta-atoms. The study of subwavelength structures interacting with light can be traced back to the famous paper published by Mie in 1908 [89]. In this paper, Mie derived a rigorous solution based on electromagnetic theory for the interaction between a monochromatic plane wave and a uniform sphere of arbitrary radius and material in a homogeneous medium. Subsequent research has attempted to extend this to non-spherical shapes, but the strict analytical forms of electromagnetic field solutions are monumentally complex, and approximations are often made in

practice [90]. In metasurfaces, meta-atoms commonly take the form of cylinders, rectangular pillars, or irregular pillars with cross-sections shaped like H, V, U, or crosses, etc., and the scattering fields of different meta-atoms coherently interact. Currently, there is no general analytical solution for meta-atoms, only approximate models and formulas for certain cases [91–93]. A classical method for modeling and analyzing homogeneous metasurfaces is the effective medium theory [94,95]. This theory attempts to identify the correspondence between the structure of meta-atoms and the parameters of bulk materials, but it still has many limitations [36].

In fact, studies like Mie theory or effective medium theory are rarely directly applied to the metasurface design, especially for the inhomogeneous metasurface. Generally, researchers employ computational electromagnetics methods, such as the Finite-Difference Time-Domain (FDTD) method $[96]$ , RCWA method $[97]$ , Finite Element Method (FEM) $[98]$ , and Finite Integration Technique (FIT) $[99]$ , to conduct full-wave simulations that determine the electromagnetic response characteristics of meta-atoms. Among these, eigenmode solvers can be used to calculate the frequencies, quality factors, and field distributions of the resonant modes of a given meta-atom. Nonetheless, the aforementioned theoretical research has established a theoretical foundation for the physical picture and mechanism behind the electromagnetic wave manipulation of meta-atoms, playing a significant role in understanding the working principles of metasurfaces and proposing novel metasurface designs.

Local resonances in metallic meta-atoms. In the early stages of metasurface research, thanks to the development of plasmonics, extensive studies on the subwavelength metal structure were conducted, and researchers often chose metals such as gold (Au) [100] and silver (Ag) [101] as the material for meta-atoms.

The interaction between metals and electromagnetic fields primarily depends on the free electrons within the metals, which form a plasma that collectively responds to external fields $[102]$ . As a fundamental excitation of plasma, surface plasmon polaritons (SPPs) are propagating, dispersive electromagnetic waves that travel along the interface between a dielectric and a conductor, with their fields evanescently confined in the direction perpendicular to the interface. These surface waves arise from the coupling between optical-frequency electromagnetic fields and the collective oscillations of free electrons in the conductor's plasma $[103]$ .

In contrast, localized surface plasmon resonances—also known simply as localized plasmon resonances—are non-propagating excitations of the plasmonic system that can be directly excited by incident light $[103]$ . These resonances represent the fundamental modes of subwavelength plasmonic nanostructures, with their resonant frequencies determined by the material's plasma frequency and the geometry of the nanostructures. The typical Q-factor of localized plasmon resonances ranges from 2 to 10 due to the intrinsic high Ohmic loss $[80]$ . Nevertheless, due to their deep subwavelength mode volumes, they can produce substantial local electric field enhancement, with intensities amplified by factors of several hundred.

The metal meta-atom under electromagnetic field excitation forms specific resonant modes, which can be used to modulate electromagnetic characteristics of scattered light such as amplitude, phase, and polarization. For example, consider a metal cylindrical rod with the incident light's polarization direction aligned with the axis of the rod. The free electron gas in the rod undergoes a collective axial displacement under the action of the incident light's electric field, equivalent to generating equal and opposite charges accumulated at both ends of the metal rod, thereby forming an internal electric field and a restoring force opposite to the direction of the overall displacement of the electron gas. By formulating the motion equation, the intrinsic resonance frequency of

the electron gas movement can be calculated [104]. When the frequency of the incident light matches the resonance frequency, intense absorption and phase shift occur. In the full-wave simulation result of an Au rod shown in Fig. 2(a), the resonance effect can be observed around the wavelength of 800 nm. In addition to this electrical resonance, metal meta-atoms can also support magnetic resonant modes through special structural design, such as the typical split ring resonator (SRR) structure [Fig. 2(b)] [107,108] and metal-insulator-metal (MIM) structure [Fig. 2(c)] [109,110].

When metal meta-atoms are arranged in an ordered array, the coupling of localized plasmon resonances via a diffractive order of the lattice can give rise to localized plasmonic surface lattice resonances $[111]$ . These resonances are characterized by significantly enhanced quality factors exceeding 100 and exhibit remarkably narrow spectral linewidths, as small as 1–2 nm $[112]$ .

However, metals exhibit high ohmic losses at optical frequencies, which significantly limit the efficiency of metal-based metasurfaces, particularly in transmission mode $[8,113]$ . Although the introduction of hybrid meta-atom structures, such as the MIM configuration, can marginally enhance device efficiency $[110,114]$ , challenges such as restricted operational frequency ranges and persistently low efficiencies continue to pose obstacles $[115]$ .

Local resonances in dielectric meta-atoms. As an alternative, high-refractive-index dielectric materials, such as titanium dioxide (TiO $_2$ ) [116,117], gallium nitride (GaN) [118,119], and silicon (Si) [120,121], exhibit very low losses in the visible or near-infrared frequency range, along with rich resonant characteristics, promising the realization of high-efficiency metasurface devices. Moreover, materials like silicon and silicon nitride (SiN $_x$ ) are more compatible with modern complementary metal–oxide–semiconductor (CMOS) processes, thus holding greater potential for the mass production of metasurfaces on a large scale. Unlike metals, dielectric materials do not contain free electrons and cannot form conduction currents. However, the incident light excites intense displacement currents in dielectrics, analogous to conduction currents in metals, which serves as the basis for the resonant behavior of dielectric meta-atoms [122].

The resonant modes of a dielectric meta-atom can be broadly categorized into two families: radially oscillating modes and axially oscillating modes. In the following discussion, we refer to radial resonances—originating from the resonant behavior of a meta-atom with effectively infinite height—as Mie-like modes (or simply Mie modes), and axial resonances as FP-like modes (or simply FP modes). As shown in Fig. 2(d), the Mie mode and the FP mode are excited in two cylindrical dielectric meta-atoms with different aspect ratios r/h, where r and h denote the radius and height of the meta-atom, respectively. When dielectric meta-atoms are arranged in ordered arrays, they can support localized collective Mie or FP lattice modes, typically exhibiting weak near-field coupling.

The Mie resonance in dielectric meta-atoms can be understood through the multipole decomposition of electromagnetic fields, which characterizes the scattering intensities and radiation patterns of the dominant excited multipolar modes. For spherical metallic nanoparticles, the ED mode typically dominates the Mie scattering, producing a symmetric radiation pattern. By contrast, in dielectric meta-atoms, the excitation and interference of multiple multipolar modes play a more significant role $[122–124]$ . Taking a dielectric sphere as an example $[106]$ , when the wavelength of the incident light inside the sphere becomes comparable to its diameter, the light couples to circular displacement currents, leading to a resonant MD response. In general, four main resonant modes can be excited in such a sphere: two fundamental Mie resonances—the MD and

Figure 2  
![](images/0dc4fe9ce82321c02dc47ee66daf927d1bf181bedde4c91355d740f1f483b9a3.jpg)

Working principles and phase modulation mechanisms of meta-atoms. (a) Full-wave simulation result of an Au rod with the diameter of 60 nm and the length of 200 nm, which is obtained using the FDTD method. The resonance can be observed near 800 nm, resulting in an abrupt change in transmittance, reflectance, and phase shift. (b) Schematic of two split-ring resonator structures. (c) Schematic of the metal-insulator-metal structure. (d) Left: schematic of an isolated cylindrical meta-atom with radius r and height h. Right: near-field electric field distributions and far-field radiation patterns of two isolated meta-atoms with different aspect ratios. (e) Field distributions of a dielectric sphere under electric dipole (ED) resonance, magnetic dipole (MD) resonance, electric quadrupole (EQ) resonance, and magnetic quadrupole (MQ) resonance. (f) Simulated phase and transmittance distributions for rectangular pillar meta-atoms of different lengths and widths at heights of 500 nm and 800 nm, respectively. The material of meta-atoms is amorphous silicon, the operating wavelength is 940 nm, and the lattice constant is 350 nm. (g) The coordinate system established in the theoretical analysis of the meta-atom. (d) Adapted with permission from Koshelev et al., Sci. Bull. 64, 836–842 (2019) [105]. Copyright © 2018 Science China Press. (e) Adapted with permission from Kuznetsov et al., Science 354, aag2472 (2016) [106]. Copyright © 2016, American Association for the Advancement of Science.

ED modes—and two higher-order Mie resonances—the magnetic quadrupole (MQ) and electric quadrupole (EQ) modes. Notably, the magnetic and electric resonances often exhibit comparable intensities $[81]$ . The field distributions corresponding to these four resonant modes are illustrated in Fig. 2(e).

The Q-factor of meta-atoms tends to increase with the order of the multipole mode. This trend is consistent with a reduction in the angular density of available radiation channels and an increased contribution from large Fourier components in the field distribution $[125]$ . For dielectric meta-atoms with a high refractive index, Mie modes with relatively large Q-factors—reaching values of several dozen—can be achieved.

# 2.1.2. Resonant and Propagation Phase Modulation Mechanisms

The phase modulation mechanism of meta-atoms is crucial for designing inhomogeneous metasurfaces that enable diverse wavefront manipulation. In this subsection, we discuss resonant and propagation phase modulation mechanisms. And the geometric phase modulation mechanism will be introduced in the following Subsection 2.1.3. Beyond these three principal phase modulation mechanisms, detour phase metasurfaces, which are extensively utilized in metasurface holography, will be discussed further in Subsection 2.1.4.

As discussed in Subsection 2.1.1, resonance provides the meta-atom with significant phase accumulation as a function of frequency, serving as a starting point for achieving phase modulation $[126]$ . By varying the shape and size of meta-atoms, it is possible to manipulate their resonant modes to realize a full $2\pi$ phase accumulation for incident light at a specific wavelength and polarization state. This mechanism of phase modulation is referred to as the resonant phase. By tailoring the electric and magnetic polarization characteristics of meta-atoms—for example, by utilizing the interference of spectrally overlapping ED and MD resonances of equal intensity to suppress backward scattering (i.e., the first Kerker condition $[81]$ )—it is possible to achieve full $2\pi$ phase coverage with transmittance close to unity $[122]$ . Such resonant phase metasurfaces are referred to as Huygens metasurfaces, named after the meta-atoms resembling the secondary sources in the Huygens principle, which emit base waves that propagate only forwards $[127–130]$ . Nowadays, there is usually no strict distinction made between the concepts of resonant phase metasurfaces and Huygens metasurfaces $[131,132]$ .

Recently, the concept of the topology phase has emerged, leveraging the exceptional point characteristics of metasurfaces as non-Hermitian systems $[133]$ , as discussed in Subsection 2.2.6. Additionally, the topological origins of various types of resonant phase metasurfaces, including Huygens metasurfaces, have been progressively uncovered through complex frequency analysis $[126,134]$ , which will be introduced in Subsection 2.2.8. In light of these developments, referring to these phase modulation mechanisms collectively as the topology phase may become a more appropriate terminology.

When considering metasurfaces composed of high-index dielectric meta-atoms with significant height, the truncated waveguide principle can be used for their description. In this case, the meta-atoms are regarded as waveguides truncated at the top and bottom. Phase modulation is primarily achieved not through phase shifts induced by the interaction of incident light with the meta-atom near the resonance frequency, but through a process of phase accumulation during guided-wave propagation, thus supporting a broader bandwidth than resonant phase $[135]$ , whereas the resonance effect has a narrow-band property $[136]$ . The phase accumulation $\phi$ can be expressed as

$$
\phi = \frac {2 \pi h}{\lambda_ {0}} n _ {\mathrm{eff}}, \tag {3}
$$

where h is the height of the meta-atom, $\lambda_{0}$ is the wavelength of the incident light in free space, and $n_{eff}$ is the effective refractive index of the meta-atom. This mechanism of phase modulation is known as the propagation phase. The range of the effective refractive index satisfies

$$
n _ {\text {cladding}} \leq n _ {\text {eff}} \leq n _ {\text {meta}}, \tag {4}
$$

whereas $n_{meta}$ and $n_{cladding}$ are the refractive indices of the meta-atom material and the material surrounding the meta-atoms (typically air), respectively. Thus, by adjusting the shape and size of the meta-atoms, the maximum range of phase shift is given by:

$$
\Delta \phi_ {\max} = \frac {2 \pi}{\lambda_ {0}} \left(n _ {\mathrm{meta}} - n _ {\mathrm{cladding}}\right) h. \tag {5}
$$

From Eq. (5), it becomes evident that meta-atoms with greater height can more readily achieve $2\pi$ phase coverage [137]. Moreover, when addressing more complex design tasks, such as achromatic metalenses, employing a higher aspect ratio (aspect ratio is defined as the ratio of the height of the meta-atom to the transverse critical dimension) enables a broader range of group delay (see Subsection 4.4.2). This facilitates meeting the requirements of dispersion engineering [138]. The phase and transmittance distributions for meta-atoms of varying sizes at two different heights are illustrated in Fig. 2(f), from which the impact of the aspect ratio on the design space can be intuitively observed. Using Eq. (5), it is also not difficult to understand that designing metasurfaces for reflection mode is simpler than for transmission mode. This is because, in reflection mode, light traverses the meta-atom multiple times, effectively increasing the h value in Eq. (5) beyond the physical height of the meta-atom.

In practical design, the phase modulation characteristics of resonant and propagation phase modulation mechanisms are quite similar, with the phase shift originating from the adjustment of the shape and size of the meta-atoms. In comparison, resonant phase metasurfaces have a lower aspect ratio than propagation phase metasurfaces, making them easier to fabricate. However, resonant phase metasurfaces are more severely affected by the coupling between meta-atoms [132], which may lead to a degradation in device performance. Ignoring the impact of material dispersion and resonant phase, $n_{\mathrm{eff}}$ in Eq. (3) is independent of $\lambda_0$ , meaning that $\phi$ is proportional to $\frac{1}{\lambda_0}$ . When considering the material dispersion and resonant phase, as depicted in Fig. 3, Eq. (3) becomes

$$
\phi = \frac {2 \pi h}{\lambda_ {0}} n _ {\mathrm{eff}} (\lambda_ {0}). \tag {6}
$$

It is worth noting that although a clear distinction exists between the resonant phase and the propagation phase from a physical perspective, these phases are often coupled when designing meta-atoms. Specifically, the phase accumulation introduced by the propagation phase modulation mechanism may also be influenced by the phase jump near the resonance frequency $[139]$ .

Jones matrices for meta-atoms with different symmetry properties. The amplitude and phase modulation of the meta-atom can be mathematically described using Jones matrices. The coordinate system for the meta-atom is shown in Fig. 2(g). The meta-atoms with diverse symmetric properties result in Jones matrices that manifest varied symmetries $[140]$ . The general form of the Jones matrix can be written as

$$
\mathbf {T} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} A & C \\ D & B \end{array} \right], \tag {7}
$$

where $A, B, C$ , and $D$ are all complex numbers.

Figure 3  
![](images/b97e79a47757346ba404f5e1294a7cd14ea81d1b929b8c2cc202aac37d037f68.jpg)

(c) Phase shift considering material dispersion (d) Phase shift considering material dispersion & resonance  
![](images/82e117537e3bd80659b24c32f3d5bd695de6ce74774bb5ec93deabfe92f08e34.jpg)

Impact of material dispersion and resonant phase on the propagation phase. (a) Propagation phase shift when $n_{eff} = 3$ . The height of meta-atom is 509 nm. (b) Dispersion curve of the effective refractive index $n_{eff}$ . (c) Phase shift when considering the dispersion characteristic of $n_{eff}$ in (b). (d) Phase shift when considering both the dispersion characteristic and the resonant phase. The resonance wavelengths are 450 nm and 600 nm, respectively.

(i) When meta-atoms exhibit mirror symmetry with respect to a plane perpendicular to the z axis, known as out-of-plane symmetry, the off-diagonal elements of the corresponding Jones matrix become identical, i.e.,

$$
\mathbf {T} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} A & C \\ C & B \end{array} \right]. \tag {8}
$$

Generally, typical single-layer meta-atoms possess out-of-plane symmetry, adhering to Eq. (8). While it might appear that the substrate could disrupt this symmetry, its impact is typically minimal [140].

(ii) When meta-atoms exhibit mirror symmetry with respect to the xOz or yOz planes, we have C = D = 0, i.e.,

$$
\mathbf {T} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} A & 0 \\ 0 & B \end{array} \right]. \tag {9}
$$

(iii) When meta-atoms exhibit $C_2$ symmetry with respect to the $z$ axis, the Jones matrix as defined in Eq. (7) inherently fulfills this symmetry requirement.  
(iv) When meta-atoms exhibit rotational symmetry of $C_N$ (where $N$ is an integer greater than or equal to 3) with respect to the $z$ axis, we have $B = A, D = -C$ , i.e.,

$$
\mathbf {T} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} A & C \\ - C & A \end{array} \right]. \tag {10}
$$

(v) When only reciprocal media are considered—that is, when light propagation through the structure obeys time-reversal symmetry—the Jones matrix of a meta-atom under forward illumination (light incident from the bottom substrate to the top superstrate) is

$$
\mathbf {T} ^ {\mathrm{f}} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} A & C \\ D & B \end{array} \right], \tag {11}
$$

then, under backward illumination (light incident from the top superstrate to the bottom substrate), the corresponding Jones matrix becomes [141]

$$
\mathbf {T} ^ {\mathrm{b}} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} A & - D \\ - C & B \end{array} \right]. \tag {12}
$$

We note that the conclusions (i)–(iv) are valid only for normally incident light along the z-axis, as these structural symmetries no longer hold for oblique incidence. Additionally, the symmetry constraints imposed on the meta-atom should ideally match the symmetry of the lattice $[142]$ . However, in most cases, the symmetry of individual meta-atoms can exceed that of the lattice, thereby governing the overall symmetry of the metasurface.

We now mathematically describe the resonant or propagation phase utilizing Jones matrices. We consider meta-atoms that exhibit mirror symmetry with respect to the $xOz$ or $yOz$ planes, which are commonly used in practical designs. Assume the meta-atom introduces resonant or propagation phases $\phi_x$ and $\phi_y$ for linearly polarized light in the $x$ and $y$ directions, respectively, for the operating wavelength. And the amplitude transmission coefficients for the two polarizations are $t_x$ and $t_y$ , respectively (the square of the amplitude transmittance coefficient represents the intensity transmission coefficient, or transmittance [143]). Then, the scattering characteristics of the meta-atom can be described by the Jones matrix:

$$
\mathbf {T} _ {0} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} & 0 \\ 0 & t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}} \end{array} \right]. \tag {13}
$$

When isotropic meta-atom designs such as cylindrical or square pillars are adopted, the symmetry of the resonant or propagation modes ensures that the introduced resonant or propagation phase does not exhibit polarization sensitivity $[144]$ . In this case, $t_{x} = t_{y}$ and $\phi_{x} = \phi_{y}$ in Eq. (14). In contrast, with anisotropic meta-atom designs such as elliptical or rectangular pillars, the phase shift is related to the polarization state of the incident light $[145]$ .

The design workflow for propagation phase metasurfaces is available in our open-source code repository [73].

# 2.1.3. Geometric Phase Modulation Mechanism

For a meta-atom satisfying Eq. (13), when it is rotated by an angle $\theta$ counterclockwise around the z axis (as viewed from the positive direction of the z axis), the Jones matrix expression for the scattering characteristics of the rotated meta-atom can be represented as

$$
\mathbf {T} = \mathbf {R} (\theta) \mathbf {T} _ {0} \mathbf {R} (- \theta), \tag {14}
$$

where $\mathbf{T}_0$ is the Jones matrix before rotation expressed by Eq. (13), and $\mathbf{R}(\theta)$ is the rotation matrix, defined as

$$
\mathbf {R} (\theta) = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} \cos \theta & - \sin \theta \\ \sin \theta & \cos \theta \end{array} \right]. \tag {15}
$$

Obviously, we have

$$
\mathbf {R} (\theta) ^ {- 1} = \mathbf {R} (\theta) ^ {\top} = \mathbf {R} (- \theta), \tag {16}
$$

where the superscript $\top$ denotes matrix transpose, and the superscript $-1$ denotes matrix inverse. Using Eq. (16), we have

$$
\mathbf {T} ^ {\top} = \left(\mathbf {R} (\theta) \mathbf {T} _ {0} \mathbf {R} (- \theta)\right) ^ {\top} = \left(\mathbf {R} (- \theta)\right) ^ {\top} \mathbf {T} _ {0} ^ {\top} \left(\mathbf {R} (\theta)\right) ^ {\top} = \mathbf {R} (\theta) \mathbf {T} _ {0} \mathbf {R} (- \theta) = \mathbf {T}, \tag {17}
$$

which means that the Jones matrix T of the rotated meta-atom is symmetric. Using Eq. (17), we have

$$
\mathbf {T} ^ {\dagger} = \left(\mathbf {T} ^ {\top}\right) ^ {*} = (\mathbf {T}) ^ {*}, \tag {18}
$$

where the superscript $\dagger$ denotes conjugate transpose, also known as Hermitian transpose, and the superscript $*$ denotes the matrix with complex conjugated entries. Supposing $t_{x}=t_{y}=1$ , we obtain

$$
\mathbf {T} ^ {\dagger} = (\mathbf {T}) ^ {*} = \mathbf {R} (\theta) \mathbf {T} _ {0} ^ {*} \mathbf {R} (- \theta) = \mathbf {R} (\theta) \mathbf {T} _ {0} ^ {- 1} \mathbf {R} (- \theta) = \left(\mathbf {R} (\theta) \mathbf {T} _ {0} \mathbf {R} (- \theta)\right) ^ {- 1} = \mathbf {T} ^ {- 1}, \tag {19}
$$

which means that the Jones matrix $\mathbf{T}$ of the rotated meta-atom is unitary when assuming $t_x = t_y = 1$ (i.e., the transmission of the meta-atom is unitary when there is no reflection and absorption [126]). The above two properties in Eqs. (17) and (19) will be used in Subsection 2.1.5.

Substituting Eqs. (13) and (15) into Eq. (14), we have

$$
\mathbf {T} \triangleq \left[ \begin{array}{l l} T _ {x x} & T _ {x y} \\ T _ {y x} & T _ {y y} \end{array} \right] = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} \cos^ {2} \theta + t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}} \sin^ {2} \theta & \frac {1}{2} \left(t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} - t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}}\right) \sin 2 \theta \\ \frac {1}{2} \left(t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} - t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}}\right) \sin 2 \theta & t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} \sin^ {2} \theta + t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}} \cos^ {2} \theta \end{array} \right]. \tag {20}
$$

Considering the incident light is circularly polarized with an amplitude of 1, its Jones vector representation is

$$
\vec {I} = \frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ i \sigma \end{array} \right], \tag {21}
$$

where $\sigma = \pm 1$ corresponds to left-handed circular polarization (LCP) and right-handed circular polarization (RCP), respectively. After interacting with the meta-atom expressed by Eq. (20), the Jones vector of the output light is

$$
\vec {O} = \mathbf {T} \vec {I} = \frac {1}{2 \sqrt {2}} \left(\left(t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} + t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}}\right) \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ i \sigma \end{array} \right] + \left(t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} - t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}}\right) e ^ {i 2 \sigma \theta} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ - i \sigma \end{array} \right]\right), \tag {22}
$$

where the first term represents the output light with the same circular polarization state as the incident light, and the second term represents the output light with the opposite circular polarization state to the incident light.

At this point, the electromagnetic modulation characteristics of the meta-atom are quite complex. However, if all the meta-atoms on the metasurface have the same type of anisotropic structure (the anisotropy condition ensures that $t_x e^{i\phi_x} \neq t_y e^{i\phi_y}$ , i.e., the output light with the opposite circular polarization state exists), the resonant or propagation phase is identical everywhere on the metasurface, and then it is as if there is no effect of resonant or propagation phase under the overview of the entire metasurface. In this case, the only phase shift is provided by the term $e^{i2\sigma\theta}$ in Eq. (22), meaning that a phase shift of $2\sigma\theta$ will be introduced between the opposite circularly polarized output light and the incident light just through the rotation of anisotropic meta-atoms. This is known as the geometric phase, also referred to as the Pancharatnam–Berry (PB)

phase or Berry phase [146,147]. Denoting the geometric phase as $\phi_{\mathrm{geometric}}$ , we have

$$
\phi_ {\text {geometric}} = 2 \sigma \theta . \tag {23}
$$

If the meta-atom is rotated from 0 to $\pi$ , then the phase shift will cover the complete $2\pi$ range.

The previous conclusion is derived under the assumption that the meta-atom satisfies Eq. (13) at $\theta = 0$ . For a more general meta-atom satisfying Eq. (8), we can similarly obtain the Jones vector of the output light for the rotated meta-atom under the incidence of Eq. (21):

$$
\vec {O} = \frac {1}{2 \sqrt {2}} \left((A + B) \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ i \sigma \end{array} \right] + (A - B + 2 i \sigma C) e ^ {i 2 \sigma \theta} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ - i \sigma \end{array} \right]\right). \tag {24}
$$

In this case, the output component with opposite circular polarization to the incident light acquires the same geometric phase factor as in Eq. (23). The newly introduced element C affects only the complex amplitude of this output component. When C = 0, Eq. (24) reduces to Eq. (22).

Indeed, the concept of geometric phase predates the advent of metasurfaces significantly. As early as 1956, Pancharatnam discovered that a cyclic evolution in the polarization state of light is accompanied by an additional phase shift, known as the Pancharatnam phase, which is determined by half of the solid angle covered by the cycle on the Poincaré sphere [148]. In 1984, Berry proposed the general form of geometric phase for the adiabatic transformation of a quantum state [149]. Later, in 1987, he further demonstrated the equivalence between the Pancharatnam phase in optics and the geometric phase in quantum mechanics [150]. The next milestone in this field came in 2001 and 2002 when Hasman's group [70,71,151,152] utilized subwavelength metal gratings to realize space-variant phase modulation based on the principle of geometric phase and experimentally verified its feasibility through the designs of diffractive gratings [151] and vortex beam generators [152], devices that are actually geometric phase metasurfaces [147], although at this time the concept of metasurfaces had not yet been born. In recent years, there has been further development in the theory of geometric phase. In 2021, Xie et al. [153] proposed the concept of generalized geometric phase: when using meta-atoms with high-fold rotational symmetries $C_K$ (where $K \geq 3$ ) in symmetrically incompatible lattices, it is possible to introduce high-order geometric phases that are multiples of the rotation angle.

In Eq. (22), the output light with the same circular polarization state as that of the incident light can be considered as stray light, which can be filtered with a wave plate and a polarizer $[154]$ . The polarization conversion efficiency (PCE) is defined as the ratio of the transmittance of the output light with the opposite circular polarization state to the total transmittance. From Eq. (22), it can be represented by

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{PCE}} = \frac {\left| t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} - t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}} \right| ^ {2}}{\left| t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} + t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}} \right| ^ {2} + \left| t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} - t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}} \right| ^ {2}} = \frac {t _ {x} ^ {2} + t _ {y} ^ {2} - 2 t _ {x} t _ {y} \cos (\phi_ {x} - \phi_ {y})}{2 (t _ {x} ^ {2} + t _ {y} ^ {2})}. \tag {25}
$$

Eq. (25) applies to both $\sigma = +1$ and $\sigma = -1$ , meaning that for the same meta-atom, whether it works in the case of transforming incident LCP light into RCP light or incident RCP light into LCP light, its PCE remains unchanged. According to formula Eq. (25), when the condition

$$
\left| \phi_ {x} - \phi_ {y} \right| = \pi \tag {26}
$$

(also known as the half-wave-plate condition) is met, the PCE reaches its maximum value

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{PCE}, \max} = \frac {1}{2} + \frac {t _ {x} t _ {y}}{t _ {x} ^ {2} + t _ {y} ^ {2}}. \tag {27}
$$

We note that $\eta_{PCE,max}=100\%$ when $t_{x}=t_{y}$ , thus, the level of PCE is not necessarily related to the level of transmittance. When assessing the efficiency of geometric phase metasurfaces, both transmittance and PCE should be evaluated. A more comprehensive metric is the product of transmittance and PCE. In practical designs, to achieve higher device efficiency, the structural parameters of meta-atoms are often iterated to choose one meta-atom that offers the highest PCE and transmittance [155]. When approximations $t_{x}=1$ and $t_{y}=1$ are made, Eq. (25) can be simplified to a very straightforward form:

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{PCE}} = \sin^ {2} \frac {\phi_ {x} - \phi_ {y}}{2}. \tag {28}
$$

From Eq. (23), for incident light with circular polarization state $\sigma = \pm 1$ , the geometric phase introduced by the meta-atom is $\pm 2\theta$ , with the two differing by a negative sign. This means that the geometric phase is generally polarization-sensitive. However, if $\theta$ is restricted to values of either 0 or $\frac{\pi}{2}$ , the geometric phases introduced when $\sigma = \pm 1$ are identical. In this case, the geometric phase is polarization-insensitive, even though the meta-atom is anisotropic. This is because any polarization state of the incident light can always be decomposed into LCP light and RCP light, and both have the same geometric phase and PCE. Besides, there is a constant difference of $\pi$ between the polarization-insensitive geometric phases produced by the meta-atom rotated by 0 and that rotated by $\frac{\pi}{2}$ , which introduces a new degree of design freedom [156]. It should be emphasized that this polarization insensitivity is essentially incomplete when the PCE is below $100\%$ , due to the presence of output light with the same circular polarization state as the incident light.

The characteristics of the resonant or propagation phase with geometric phase are compared here. Geometric phase metasurfaces impose strict requirements on the polarization states of incident and output light, whereas resonant or propagation phase metasurfaces have no such constraints. Resonant or propagation phase metasurfaces rely on specific meta-atom structures, making them sensitive to fabrication errors and thus requiring high precision in fabrication processes. In contrast, geometric phase meta-atoms achieve full phase space coverage just through the rotation of meta-atoms, featuring simpler structures and lower precision requirements in fabrication (generally, errors in the rotation angle are minor, and even if fabrication results in deviations from the designed shape, it does not affect the accuracy of phase shift, though it may reduce the efficiency of the metasurface to some extent) [157]. Moreover, the electromagnetic response characteristics of meta-atoms with a specific shape vary with the wavelength of incident light, resulting in wavelength sensitivity for resonant or propagation phase metasurfaces, also referred to as phase dispersion. In contrast, the geometric phase depends solely on the rotation angle, meaning that the phase shift distribution for a given geometric phase metasurface under different wavelengths varies only by a constant term. This property renders geometric phase metasurfaces wavelength-insensitive, or free from phase dispersion. We note that the efficiency of the geometric metasurface, which depends on the PCE and transmittance, is still wavelength-dependent, but careful meta-atom design can maintain efficiency at a high level within a broad bandwidth [158]. This characteristic determines that geometric phase metasurfaces can be used to create some types of devices with broadband features, such as metasurface holograms that reconstruct the same holographic image under different wavelengths [159,160].

Further discussion can be found in Subsection 5.1.2. However, this feature also prevents geometric phase metasurfaces from relying solely on themselves to implement devices based on dispersion engineering, such as achromatic phase-gradient metasurfaces (e.g., metalenses and metagratings) and wavelength-multiplexed Fraunhofer holograms. In these cases, the target phase shifts of the meta-atom at specific spatial positions must be wavelength-dependent, meaning they must satisfy certain phase dispersion relationships.

When deriving the geometric phase from Eq. (22), it is assumed that the metasurface employs the meta-atoms with the same shape and size. However, it's evident that if the use of meta-atoms with multiple shapes and sizes is allowed, combined with the rotation of these meta-atoms, the electromagnetic modulation freedom will be significantly enhanced, greatly expanding the design space of the metasurface [143]. This approach, also known as the hybrid phase modulation mechanism, will be discussed in detail in Subsection 2.1.5.

The design workflow for geometric phase metasurfaces is available in our open-source code repository [73].

# 2.1.4. Detour Phase Modulation Mechanism

Beyond the three main phase modulation mechanisms mentioned, the detour phase modulation mechanism also finds broad application in the field of metasurface holography $[161,162]$ . Long before the advent of metasurfaces, the principle of detour phase played a crucial role in areas like binary optical holography $[163,164]$ . The key difference between the detour phase and the previously mentioned phase modulation mechanisms is that the latter generally operate under the zeroth-order diffraction of periodic metasurface units, whereas the detour phase modulation mechanism operates under non-zeroth-order diffraction, which is intrinsic to the principle of detour phase.

Given the lattice constant a of a metasurface based on the square lattice, the diffraction angle $\theta_{M}$ for the $M^{th}$ diffraction order is given by

$$
\sin \theta_ {M} = M \cdot \frac {\lambda}{a}, \tag {29}
$$

where $\lambda$ is the wavelength of light in the background medium. Assuming the metasurface units operate at the $M^{th}$ diffraction order and all units adopt the same structure, as shown in the left panel of Fig. 4(a), with a spacing of d = a between two adjacent units, the phase difference $\Delta\phi$ between these units is

$$
\Delta \phi = \frac {2 \pi}{\lambda} d \sin \theta_ {M}. \tag {30}
$$

Introducing a displacement $\Delta d$ to make the spacing between two adjacent units to be $d' = d + \Delta d$ , as illustrated in the right panel of Fig. 4(a), the phase difference $\Delta \phi'$ between adjacent units becomes

$$
\Delta \phi^ {\prime} = \frac {2 \pi}{\lambda} d ^ {\prime} \sin \theta_ {M}. \tag {31}
$$

From Eqs. (30) and (31), the phase shift $\phi_{\mathrm{detour}}$ introduced by the displacement $\Delta d$ for the light of the diffraction order $M$ is

$$
\phi_ {\text {detour}} = \Delta \phi^ {\prime} - \Delta \phi = \frac {2 \pi}{\lambda} \Delta d \sin \theta_ {M}. \tag {32}
$$

This is referred to as the detour phase. We note that the direction of displacement is not arbitrary. Taking Fig. 4(a) as an example, the displacement should be along the x direction, since the diffracted light propagates within the xOz plane. Substituting

Figure 4  
![](images/70b4dcd13d8c358650c856850e1a2e40c5f0442f0e6e2791b79df38f4187b34f.jpg)
