Detour phase metasurfaces. (a) Schematic of the principle of the detour phase metasurface. (b) Scanning electron micrograph (SEM) of the detour phase metasurface based on the structure of multiple ridge waveguides. (c) SEM of the detour phase metasurface based on the streamlined structure. (d) Experimental holographic image generated by the detour phase metasurface hologram under the incident of linearly polarized light at a wavelength of 1350 nm. (b) and (d) Adapted from $[161]$ under a Creative Commons license. (c) Adapted with permission from Wang et al., Small 15, 1900483 (2019) $[165]$ . Copyright © 2019 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim.

Eq. (29) into Eq. (32), and denoting $M$ as $\sigma_{\mathrm{d}}$ , we obtain the expression for the detour phase

$$
\phi_ {\text {detour}} = \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}} 2 \pi \frac {\Delta d}{a}. \tag {33}
$$

Compared to the expression for geometric phase in Eq. (23), it's noted that the diffraction order in detour phase plays a similar role to the symbol $\sigma$ in geometric phase, which is why $M$ is denoted as $\sigma_{\mathrm{d}}$ . Detour phase metasurfaces and geometric phase metasurfaces both use the units with the same structure, but the former introduces a phase shift through displacement, while the latter does so through rotation. Similar to the geometric phase, the detour phase is wavelength-independent, exhibiting phase dispersion-free characteristics. The derivation of Eq. (33) does not assume the polarization state of the incident light; hence, a displacement $\Delta d$ introduces the detour phase expressed by Eq. (33) for light of any polarization state at the diffraction order of $\sigma_{\mathrm{d}}$ . However, the diffraction efficiency at the $M^{\mathrm{th}}$ diffraction order is generally polarization-sensitive, so detour phase metasurfaces often operate under specific polarization states [161,166-168]. Through selecting an appropriate unit structure, detour phase metasurfaces have the potential to achieve excellent incident angle tolerance. In 2018, Deng et al. [167] experimentally demonstrated a detour phase metasurface with an incident angle tolerance close to grazing incidence.

To fulfill the function of phase modulation, it is essential that $\sigma_{\mathrm{d}} \neq 0$ , which necessitates that detour phase metasurface units operate at non-zeroth-order diffraction. From Eq. (33), to achieve a phase coverage of $2\pi$ , $\Delta d$ should satisfy

$$
\Delta d \geq \frac {a}{| \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}} |}. \tag {34}
$$

Furthermore, the unit structure needs to satisfy the dispersion characteristic of the $M^{th}$ diffraction angle as expressed by Eq. (29) to ensure the wavelength-independent feature of the detour phase [161], as seen from the derivation from Eq. (32) to Eq. (33).

Achieving high device efficiency requires specific structural design to ensure that the $M^{\mathrm{th}}$ diffraction order of the unit has high diffraction efficiency. Currently reported detour phase unit structures exhibiting high diffraction efficiency under normal incidence include the structure of multiple parallel ridge waveguides [161] and the streamlined

structure [165], illustrated in Figs. 4(b) and 4(c), respectively. Additionally, tilting the incident light can also enhance the diffraction efficiency of non-zero diffraction orders [167].

Expanding the concept of detour phase metasurfaces from displacements in one direction to displacements in two perpendicular directions, consider a metasurface unit that possesses diffraction orders $\sigma_{d,x}$ along the x direction and $\sigma_{d,y}$ along the y direction. The corresponding diffraction angles $\theta_{x}$ and $\theta_{y}$ can be obtained through Eq. (29). From Eq. (33), when the unit introduces displacements $\Delta d_{x}$ along the x direction and $\Delta d_{y}$ along the y direction simultaneously, the phase shifts $\phi_{detour,x}$ and $\phi_{detour,y}$ introduced to the output light at the diffraction orders of $\sigma_{d,x}$ and $\sigma_{d,y}$ are, respectively,

$$
\phi_ {\text {detour}, x} = \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}, x} 2 \pi \frac {\Delta d _ {x}}{a}, \quad \phi_ {\text {detour}, y} = \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}, y} 2 \pi \frac {\Delta d _ {y}}{a}. \tag {35}
$$

This achieves independent detour phase modulation for the output light at the diffraction angles $\theta_{x}$ and $\theta_{y}$ in the x and y directions, respectively.

In 2016, Capasso's group [161] implemented a detour phase metasurface hologram operating at the $\sigma_{d}=\pm1$ diffraction order, utilizing a unit structure with a unit size of 5.4 $\mu$ m, which was composed of six ridge waveguides made of a–Si, as shown in Fig. 4(c). This metasurface hologram was composed of $256\times256$ units. Operating under the linear polarization state, the hologram achieved a bandwidth from visible to near-infrared and an absolute efficiency (defined in Subsection 5.1.3) of 75% in the wavelength range of 1.0–1.4 $\mu$ m. The holographic image generated by the metasurface hologram at 1350 nm is shown in Fig. 4(d).

Other applications of detour phase metasurfaces in designs for complex amplitude modulation, polarization multiplexing, and wavelength multiplexing will be introduced in Subsections 5.1.2, 5.2.1, and 5.2.2.

# 2.1.5. Hybrid Phase Modulation Mechanism

The hybrid phase modulation mechanism leverages the shape, size, rotational angle, and other design degrees of freedom of the meta-atom, providing maximum flexibility in forward design approaches. Here, we focus on the combination of geometric phase with resonant and propagation phase modulation. However, hybrid phase modulation can also be achieved through other combinations, such as detour phase with geometric phase or topological phase with geometric phase. The hybrid modulation of detour and geometric phases will be discussed in Subsection 5.2.1, while the hybrid modulation of topological and geometric phases has already been introduced in Subsection 2.2.6.

For a fixed wavelength, arbitrary amplitude and phase modulation can be described by the Jones matrix:

$$
\mathbf {J} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} J _ {x x} & J _ {x y} \\ J _ {y x} & J _ {y y} \end{array} \right], \tag {36}
$$

where $J_{xx}, J_{xy}, J_{yx}$ , and $J_{yy}$ are arbitrary complex numbers.

For a single-layer metasurface using a hybrid phase modulation mechanism, the Jones matrix of a meta-atom can be expressed as Eq. (14), which is symmetric [see Eq. (17)] and unitary [see Eq. (19)] when assuming $t_x = t_y = 1$ (the amplitude modulation is ignored here). Now we have three variables in Eq. (14), i.e., $\phi_x, \phi_y$ , and $\theta$ , while there are eight variables in Eq. (36), so it's obviously impossible to realize Eq. (36) just with a single-layer metasurface (here we don't consider the case where each metasurface unit contains several meta-atoms, which will be discussed in Subsections 5.1.2 and 5.2.1).

To make the Jones matrix J realizable with a single-layer metasurface, it is required to be symmetric and unitary, i.e., it should satisfy

$$
\mathbf {J} = \mathbf {J} ^ {\top}, \tag {37}
$$

and

$$
\mathbf {J} \mathbf {J} ^ {\dagger} = \mathbf {I}, \tag {38}
$$

where $\mathbf{I}$ is the identity matrix. Mathematically, any symmetric and unitary matrix $\mathbf{J}$ can always be decomposed into $\mathbf{J} = \mathbf{V}\Lambda \mathbf{V}^{\dagger}$ , where $\mathbf{V}$ is a real unitary matrix composed of the eigenvectors of $\mathbf{J}$ arranged column by column, and $\Lambda$ is a diagonal matrix whose diagonal elements are the corresponding eigenvalues, which are complex numbers with a magnitude of 1. Comparing $\mathbf{J} = \mathbf{V}\Lambda \mathbf{V}^{\dagger}$ with the Jones matrix of the meta-atom under hybrid phase modulation, as shown in Eq. (14), we observe a one-to-one correspondence between $\Lambda$ and $\mathbf{T}_0$ , and between $\mathbf{V}$ and $\mathbf{R}$ , when the transmittance is approximated to 1. This means that any symmetric and unitary Jones matrix can be realized by changing the shape, size, and rotation angle of the meta-atom [143].

Arbitrary polarization and phase transformation. In 2015, Faraon's group [143] theoretically demonstrated that the hybrid phase modulation mechanism could achieve phase modulation from any incident polarization state to any output polarization state. This means satisfying any polarization and phase transformation between the incident light's Jones vector $\vec{E}^{\mathrm{in}} = [E_x^{\mathrm{in}}E_y^{\mathrm{in}}]^{\top}$ and the output light's Jones vector $\vec{E}^{\mathrm{out}} = [E_x^{\mathrm{out}}E_y^{\mathrm{out}}]^{\top}$ , as given by

$$
\vec {E} ^ {\text {out}} = \mathbf {J} \vec {E} ^ {\text {in}}, \tag {39}
$$

where J is limited by Eqs. (37) and (38) to be realizable with a single-layer meta-surface. By combining Eqs. (36)–(39), the expressions for the elements of J can be obtained from the following set of equations:

$$
\left(E _ {x} ^ {\text {out}}\right) ^ {*} J _ {x x} + \left(E _ {y} ^ {\text {out}}\right) ^ {*} J _ {x y} = \left(E _ {x} ^ {\text {in}}\right) ^ {*}, \tag {40}
$$

$$
E _ {x} ^ {\text {in}} J _ {x x} + E _ {y} ^ {\text {in}} J _ {x y} = E _ {x} ^ {\text {out}}, \tag {41}
$$

$$
J _ {y x} = J _ {x y}, \tag {42}
$$

$$
J _ {y y} = - e ^ {i 2 \angle J _ {x y}} \left(J _ {x x}\right) ^ {*}, \tag {43}
$$

where $*$ denotes complex conjugation and $\angle$ denotes the angle of a complex number. This implies that for any polarization and phase transformation between the incident and output light, there always exists a symmetric and unitary Jones matrix solution that can be realized by a single-layer metasurface based on the hybrid phase modulation mechanism.

Polarization multiplexing for arbitrary orthogonal polarization states. In 2017, Mueller et al. [169] utilized the hybrid phase modulation mechanism to achieve independent phase modulation for any orthogonal polarization states, whether linear, circular, or elliptical polarization. Let the Jones vectors for the input orthogonal polarization states be $\vec{E}^{\mathrm{in}, + } = [E_x^{\mathrm{in}, + }E_y^{\mathrm{in}, + }]^\top$ and $\vec{E}^{\mathrm{in}, - } = [E_x^{\mathrm{in}, - }E_y^{\mathrm{in}, - }]^\top$ , and the corresponding Jones vectors for the output orthogonal polarization states be $\vec{E}^{\mathrm{out}, + }$ and $\vec{E}^{\mathrm{out}, - }$ , respectively. To introduce mutually independent phase distributions $\phi^{+}$ and $\phi^{-}$ for the two orthogonal polarization states, the Jones matrix $\mathbf{J}$ satisfies

$$
\mathbf {J} \vec {E} ^ {\text {in,} +} = e ^ {i \phi^ {+}} \vec {E} ^ {\text {out,} +}, \tag {44}
$$

$$
\mathbf {J} \vec {E} ^ {\mathrm{in}, -} = e ^ {i \phi^ {-}} \vec {E} ^ {\mathrm{out}, -}. \tag {45}
$$

Given the constraints of J by Eqs. (37) and (38), for the equations above to have a solution, it is necessary to set the Jones vectors of the output orthogonal polarization

Figure 5  
![](images/6404f3d1c2e2b7e757b6731cdfb71b3bc2e7091e4d06cfc883321f2e36b4cbd8.jpg)

Applications of the hybrid phase modulation mechanism. (a) Holographic images of the polarization-multiplexed metasurface hologram. When illustrating the RCP (LCP) light is used, the holographic image of a dog (cat) is reconstructed. (b) Holographic images of the multi-channel polarization-multiplexed metasurface hologram. When there are two white arrows in the lower right corner, the arrows represent polarization states of the input and output light, respectively. And when there is only one white arrow in the lower right corner, the arrow represents the polarization state of the input light, and the polarization state of the output light is not specified. (c) The broadband characteristic of the multi-channel polarization-multiplexed metasurface (experimental results). (d) The experimental full-color holographic image of the multi-channel polarization-multiplexed metasurface. (a) Reprinted with permission from Mueller et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 118, 113901 (2017) [169]. Copyright © 2017 American Physical Society. (b) Adapted from [170] under a Creative Commons license. (c) and (d) Adapted with permission from Hu et al., Nano Lett. 20, 994–1002 (2020) [135]. Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society.

states to satisfy

$$
\vec {E} ^ {\text {out,} +} = \left(\vec {E} ^ {\text {in,} +}\right) ^ {*}, \quad \vec {E} ^ {\text {out,} -} = \left(\vec {E} ^ {\text {in,} -}\right) ^ {*}. \tag {46}
$$

Substituting Eq. (46) into Eqs. (44) and (45), we obtain

$$
\mathbf {J} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} e ^ {i \phi^ {+}} \left(E _ {x} ^ {\text {in}, +}\right) ^ {*} & e ^ {i \phi^ {-}} \left(E _ {x} ^ {\text {in}, -}\right) ^ {*} \\ e ^ {i \phi^ {+}} \left(E _ {y} ^ {\text {in}, +}\right) ^ {*} & e ^ {i \phi^ {-}} \left(E _ {y} ^ {\text {in}, -}\right) ^ {*} \end{array} \right] \left[ \begin{array}{l l} E _ {x} ^ {\text {in}, +} & E _ {x} ^ {\text {in}, -} \\ E _ {y} ^ {\text {in}, +} & E _ {y} ^ {\text {in}, -} \end{array} \right] ^ {- 1}. \tag {47}
$$

This Jones matrix is symmetric and unitary, so it can be realized by a single-layer metasurface through a hybrid phase modulation mechanism. Using elliptical Si pillars as metasurface atoms, a metasurface hologram operating at a wavelength of 532 nm with polarization multiplexing for RCP and LCP light was designed. The experimental holographic images are shown in Fig. 5(a).

Approaching the limit of polarization multiplexing. For the Jones matrix J expressed as Eq. (36), each element corresponds to the complex amplitude modulation for a specific polarization channel. For example, using Eq. (55), when the incident

light's Jones vector is $[10]^{\top}$ , the transmission coefficient for the polarization state described by the Jones vector $[01]^{\top}$ is represented by the element $J_{yx}$ . Below, we ignore the amplitude modulation, which will be introduced in Subsection 5.1.2.

Based on the resonant or propagation phase modulation mechanism, it's feasible to achieve independent phase modulation for two orthogonal linear polarization states, provided that the meta-atom database can cover all possible phase shift combinations of $\phi_x$ and $\phi_y$ in Eq. (13). Now, we consider how the hybrid phase modulation mechanism can be utilized to achieve a higher number of polarization multiplexing. Further discussion can be found in Subsection 5.2.1.

Ideally, we aim to introduce independent phase modulation for each polarization channel within the Jones matrix while ensuring they have the same amplitude transmission coefficient. This can be formalized as

$$
\mathbf {J} = \frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left[ \begin{array}{l l} e ^ {i \phi_ {x x}} & e ^ {i \phi_ {x y}} \\ e ^ {i \phi_ {y x}} & e ^ {i \phi_ {y y}} \end{array} \right]. \tag {48}
$$

To make Eq. (48) realizable with a single-layer metasurface, J needs to satisfy Eqs. (37) and (38). Substituting Eq. (48) into Eq. (37), we obtain

$$
\phi_ {x y} = \phi_ {y x}. \tag {49}
$$

Substituting Eqs. (48) and (49) into Eq. (38), we obtain

$$
\sin \left(\phi_ {x x} - \phi_ {x y}\right) - \sin \left(\phi_ {y y} - \phi_ {x y}\right) = 0, \tag {50}
$$

$$
\cos \left(\phi_ {x x} - \phi_ {x y}\right) + \cos \left(\phi_ {y y} - \phi_ {x y}\right) = 0. \tag {51}
$$

Taking the sum of squares of Eqs. (50) and (51) and adding them together, we obtain

$$
\phi_ {x x} + \phi_ {y y} - 2 \phi_ {x y} = \pi + 2 N \pi , \tag {52}
$$

where N is an arbitrary integer. Thus, when Eq. (48) satisfies Eqs. (49) and (52) simultaneously, the Jones matrix J is symmetric and unitary and can be realized with a single-layer metasurface using a hybrid phase modulation mechanism.

Based on this approach, in 2018, Zhao et al. [170] realized a complex polarization-multiplexed metasurface hologram with an independent channel number of three. The Jones matrix of the meta-atom can be expressed as Eq. (48) limited by Eqs. (49) and (52). When the polarization state of the input light is linearly polarized along the x-direction, linearly polarized along the y-direction, LCP, or RCP, respectively, the Jones vectors of the output light are given as

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathbf {J} \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] = \frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left[ \begin{array}{c} e ^ {i \phi_ {x x}} \\ e ^ {i \phi_ {x y}} \end{array} \right], \\ \mathbf {J} \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 1 \end{array} \right] = \frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left[ \begin{array}{c} e ^ {i \phi_ {x y}} \\ e ^ {i \phi_ {y y}} \end{array} \right], \\ \mathbf {J} \frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ i \end{array} \right] = \frac {e ^ {i \phi_ {x x}} + e ^ {i \phi_ {y y}}}{4} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ i \end{array} \right] + \frac {e ^ {i \phi_ {x x}} + 2 e ^ {i \left(\phi_ {x y} + \pi / 2\right)} + e ^ {i \left(\phi_ {y y} - \pi\right)}}{4} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ - i \end{array} \right], \tag {53} \\ \mathbf {J} \frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ - i \end{array} \right] = \frac {e ^ {i \phi_ {x x}} + 2 e ^ {i (\phi_ {x y} - \pi / 2)} + e ^ {i (\phi_ {y y} - \pi)}}{4} \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ i \end{array} \right] + \frac {e ^ {i \phi_ {x x}} + e ^ {i \phi_ {y y}}}{4} \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ - i \end{array} \right]. \\ \end{array}
$$

Under the constraints of Eqs. (49) and (52), it can be seen that there are only two independent phase modulations available in Eq. (48), for example, $\phi_{xx}$ and $\phi_{xy}$ . However, using the modified computer-generated holography (CGH) algorithm, it is possible

to make the third phase modulation $\phi_{yy}=2\phi_{xy}-\phi_{xx}+\pi$ (N in Eq. (52) is taken as 0) generate an independent holographic image different from holographic images generated by $\phi_{xx}$ and $\phi_{xy}$ . Therefore, this setup allows the reconstruction of three independent holographic images (1, 2, 3) and all their possible combinations $(1+2, 2+3, 1+3, 1+2+3)$ , creating up to 12 channels corresponding to different input and output polarization states. Based on this principle, a metasurface hologram composed of rectangular Si pillar meta-atoms was demonstrated, whose simulation and experimental holographic images are shown in Fig. 5(b). Experimentally, the device bandwidth spans from 600 nm to 800 nm.

It's noteworthy that the resonant or propagation phase metasurface is wavelength sensitive, and device performance will degrade if the operating wavelength deviates from the designated wavelength, so such a broadband property seems unlikely. In 2020, Hu et al. [135] explored the broadband characteristics of such metasurface devices, enabling the design of full-color metasurface holograms. For a metasurface operating at 532 nm, three different holographic images were encoded into $\phi_{xx}$ , $\phi_{xy}$ , and $\phi_{yy}$ . The polarization multiplexing effect is demonstrated in the second row of Fig. 5(c) for 532 nm. Surprisingly, at wavelengths of 635 nm and 450 nm, the generated holographic images, shown in the first and third rows of Fig. 5(c), respectively, were largely consistent with the 532 nm case except for size changes due to the chromatic aberration (the chromatic aberration is explained in Subsection 5.1.1). This broadband characteristic stems from the fact that for most meta-atoms in the metasurface, the difference in phase shift between two wavelengths is nearly constant. By assigning specific polarization channels to different wavelengths (one selection method is indicated by golden frames in Fig. 5(c)), it's possible to independently generate holographic images for red, green, and blue wavelengths, thereby achieving full-color holographic display, as illustrated in Fig. 5(d).

We note that the aforementioned limitation in polarization multiplexing can be overcome by using multilayer metasurfaces $[171,172]$ or by introducing engineered noise $[173]$ , which will be detailed in Subsection 5.2.1.

Beyond the applications mentioned above, the hybrid phase modulation mechanism also finds extensive applications in broadband achromatic metalenses, complex amplitude modulation metasurfaces, and more. The relevant design methods will be introduced in Sections 4 and 5.

# 2.1.6. Wavefront Manipulation by Planar Arrangement of Meta-Atoms

For inhomogeneous metasurfaces, the electromagnetic response characteristics can be regarded as a function in 2D space. Extending the coordinate system shown in Fig. 2(g) from a single meta-atom to the entire metasurface, at a certain position $(x,y)$ on the metasurface, the meta-atom introduces resonant or propagation phase shifts $\phi_{x}(x,y)$ and $\phi_{y}(x,y)$ for incident light linearly polarized along the x and y directions, respectively. The corresponding amplitude transmission coefficients are $t_{x}(x,y)$ and $t_{y}(x,y)$ , respectively, and the rotation angle of meta-atom is $\theta(x,y)$ . Using Eqs. (13) and (14), the Jones matrix describing the scattering characteristics of the metasurface can be expressed as

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathbf {T} (x, y) \triangleq \left[ \begin{array}{c c} T _ {x x} (x, y) & T _ {x y} (x, y) \\ T _ {y x} (x, y) & T _ {y y} (x, y) \end{array} \right] \\ = \mathbf {R} (\theta (x, y)) \left[ \begin{array}{c c} t _ {x} (x, y) e ^ {i \phi_ {x} (x, y)} & 0 \\ 0 & t _ {y} (x, y) e ^ {i \phi_ {y} (x, y)} \end{array} \right] \mathbf {R} (- \theta (x, y)), \tag {54} \\ \end{array}
$$

where $T_{xy}(x,y) = T_{yx}(x,y)$ .

Figure 6  
![](images/78bdeb326c6db2c5aab832cb79092b096f47db4b4e861fa4949f26e1c5b8aeae.jpg)

Related theories of the meta-atom arrangement. (a) Schematic of the simplification process for analyzing the electromagnetic wave manipulation of the metasurface. (b) Schematic of wavefront transformation by a metagrating. (c) Schematic used for the derivation of the generalized law of refraction. (d) Schematic of the generalized laws of refraction and reflection in the three-dimensional case. (e) FDTD simulation results of the scattered electric field for each V-shaped meta-atom in the array. (c) and (e) Reprinted with permission from Yu et al., Science 334, 333–337 (2011) [174]. Copyright © 2011 American Association for the Advancement of Science.

Considering the incident light with arbitrary polarization state represented by Jones vector $\vec{I}$ and the output light with arbitrary polarization state represented by Jones vector $\vec{O}$ , the complex amplitude distribution $t(x,y)e^{i\phi(x,y)}$ of the output light can be expressed as

$$
t (x, y) e ^ {i \phi (x, y)} = \vec {O} ^ {\dagger} \mathbf {T} (x, y) \vec {I}. \tag {55}
$$

Now we focus on the phase modulation for a specific polarization channel (metasurfaces for complex amplitude modulation will be discussed in Subsection 5.1.2, and polarization-multiplexed metasurfaces will be introduced in Subsections 2.1.5 and 5.2.1), and assume the amplitude transmission coefficients are constant at 1, which can be ensured by selecting meta-atoms with high transmittance. In this case, as illustrated in Fig. 6(a), it is sufficient to examine an interface with a spatially varying phase shift that is abstracted from the metasurface, and all the scattering effects occurring within the metasurface are no longer of concern.

In the following, we will examine the wavefront manipulation of metasurfaces from different perspectives, taking the metagrating as an example.

Wavefront transformation. For a metagrating designed to deflect normally incident light by an angle $\zeta$ , as illustrated in Fig. 6(b), the required phase compensation between the output and input wavefronts leads to the following target phase distribution:

$$
\phi (x) = k x \sin \zeta , \tag {56}
$$

where k is the wavenumber in the output medium.

Generalized laws of refraction and reflection. The generalized laws of refraction and reflection can be used to describe the impact of spatially varying phase shift introduced at an interface on the behavior of light between two media.

We consider a 2D case firstly, as shown in Fig. 6(c), where the phase shift introduced by the interface is a function of position, denoted as $\phi(x)$ , with the red and blue lines representing two paths infinitely close to the actual path of light. Then, by Fermat's principle, the phase difference accumulated along the two paths should be zero [175], leading to the derivation of the following generalized law of refraction [174]:

$$
n _ {\mathrm{t}} \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{t}} - n _ {\mathrm{i}} \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{i}} = \frac {\lambda_ {0}}{2 \pi} \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x)}{\mathrm{d} x}, \tag {57}
$$

where $n_{i}$ and $n_{t}$ are the refractive indices of the input and output media, respectively, $\theta_{i}$ and $\theta_{t}$ are the angles of incidence and refraction, respectively, and $\lambda_{0}$ is the wavelength of light in free space. Similarly, the generalized law of reflection is

$$
\sin \theta_ {\mathrm{r}} - \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{i}} = \frac {\lambda_ {0}}{2 \pi} \frac {1}{n _ {\mathrm{i}}} \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x)}{\mathrm{d} x}, \tag {58}
$$

where $\theta_{r}$ is the angle of reflection. These laws indicate that the refracted and reflected beams can be bent in any direction within their respective half-incident planes by designing the phase shift distribution at the interface to provide a certain phase gradient and appropriately selecting the refractive indices of the surrounding media. When $\theta_{i}=0$ , Eqs. (57) and (58) are equivalent to Eq. (56).

In 2011, Yu et al. defined Eqs. (57) and (58) as the generalized refraction and reflection laws, respectively, and experimentally verified them using metasurfaces [174]. In the experiment, the amplitude of the scattering field from all meta-atoms should remain consistent, and the spacing between adjacent meta-atoms in the array should be much smaller than the wavelength, combining to produce refracted or reflected waves with plane wavefronts [176], as shown in Fig. 6(e). It is worth noting that the derivation of these laws was also mentioned by Li and Cen in their book published in 2007 [177].

Momentum conservation. The generalized laws of refraction and reflection can be interpreted through the principle of momentum conservation.

We now continue the discussion in the 2D case. Defining the wavenumber of light in free space as $k_{0}=2\pi/\lambda_{0}$ , and the wavenumbers in the two media as $k_{t}=n_{t}k_{0}$ and $k_{i}=n_{i}k_{0}$ , respectively, and further incorporating these variables into Eqs. (57) and (58), we obtain

$$
k _ {\mathrm{t}} \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{t}} - k _ {\mathrm{i}} \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{i}} = \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x)}{\mathrm{d} x}, \tag {59}
$$

and

$$
k _ {\mathrm{i}} \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{r}} - k _ {\mathrm{i}} \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{i}} = \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x)}{\mathrm{d} x}. \tag {60}
$$

The left side of Eqs. (59) or (60) represents the change of the in-plane wavevector/momentum of light, while the right side corresponds to the phase gradient introduced by the interface. This implies that the essence of the aforementioned laws is the law of momentum conservation.

As shown in Fig. 6(d), when considering a 3D case, where the phase shift distribution introduced by the interface is denoted as $\phi(x,y)$ , Eqs. (59) and (60) can be further expanded into the following forms:

$$
\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{t}, \mathrm{T}} - \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{i}, \mathrm{T}} = \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x , y)}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}}, \tag {61}
$$

and

$$
\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{r}, \mathrm{T}} - \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{i}, \mathrm{T}} = \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x , y)}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}}, \tag {62}
$$

where $\vec{k}_{i,T}=k_{i,x}\vec{e}_{x}+k_{i,y}\vec{e}_{y}$ , $\vec{k}_{t,T}=k_{t,x}\vec{e}_{x}+k_{t,y}\vec{e}_{y}$ , and $\vec{k}_{r,T}=k_{r,x}\vec{e}_{x}+k_{r,y}\vec{e}_{y}$ are the transverse wavevectors of the incident, refracted, and reflected light, respectively, $\vec{r}=x\vec{e}_{x}+y\vec{e}_{y}$ ( $\vec{e}_{x}$ and $\vec{e}_{y}$ are unit vectors along the x and y directions, respectively), and

$$
\frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x , y)}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} = \frac {\partial \phi (x , y)}{\partial x} \vec {e} _ {x} + \frac {\partial \phi (x , y)}{\partial y} \vec {e} _ {y} \tag {63}
$$

is the phase gradient. Given that the phase gradient is on the 2D interface, the transition from incident to refracted or reflected light allows for changes in the transverse component of the wavevector followed by Eq. (61), while the components in the z direction can be solved by

$$
\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{t}, \mathrm{z}} = \sqrt {\left(k _ {\mathrm{t}} ^ {2} - \left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{t} , \mathrm{T}} \right| ^ {2}\right)}, \quad \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{r}, \mathrm{z}} = - \sqrt {\left(k _ {\mathrm{r}} ^ {2} - \left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{r} , \mathrm{T}} \right| ^ {2}\right)}. \tag {64}
$$

Assuming the x axis lies within the incident plane, the component of the phase gradient perpendicular to the incident plane $\frac{\partial\phi(x,y)}{\partial y}$ leads to refraction and reflection outside the incident plane, thereby achieving bending of the refracted and reflected beams in any direction within their respective half-spaces [178].

According to Eq. (64), when the magnitude of the transverse wavevector $\vec{k}_{t,T}$ in Eq. (61) is larger than the wavenumber in the output medium $k_{t}$ , i.e.,

$$
\left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{t}, \mathrm{T}} \right| = \left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{i}, \mathrm{T}} + \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x , y)}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} \right| > k _ {\mathrm{t}}, \tag {65}
$$

the wavevector component in the propagation direction, $k_{z}$ , becomes imaginary, leading to the formation of evanescent waves [109]. For the perpendicularly incident light ( $\vec{k}_{i,T}=0$ ), we can obtain a more straightforward form for Eq. (65):

$$
\left| \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x , y)}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} \right| > k _ {\mathrm{t}}. \tag {66}
$$

Metagrating equation. In fact, beyond the phase modulation imparted by individual meta-atoms, incident light interacting with a metagrating also experiences diffraction arising from the periodic repetition of the meta-atom arrangement. This diffraction effect was neglected in the preceding discussion; as a result, the previously derived equations are insufficient to fully characterize the optical behavior of a metagrating.

The metagrating equations for devices operating in transmission or reflection mode in the 2D case can be expressed as

$$
\frac {2 \pi}{\lambda_ {0}} \left(n _ {\mathrm{t}} \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{t}} - n _ {\mathrm{i}} \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{i}}\right) = \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x)}{\mathrm{d} x} + M \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda} \tag {67}
$$

and

$$
\frac {2 \pi}{\lambda_ {0}} n _ {\mathrm{i}} \left(\sin \theta_ {\mathrm{r}} - \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{i}}\right) = \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x)}{\mathrm{d} x} + M \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda}, \tag {68}
$$

respectively, where M is the diffraction order, and $\Lambda$ is the structural period of the meta-grating along the x-direction. It is important to note that $\Lambda$ is distinct from the lattice constant of the meta-atom array. By introducing the diffraction effect into Eqs. (61) and (62), we obtain the metagrating equations in the 3D case:

$$
\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{t}, \mathrm{T}} - \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{i}, \mathrm{T}} = \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x , y)}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} + M _ {x} \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda_ {x}} \vec {e} _ {x} + M _ {y} \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda_ {y}} \vec {e} _ {y} \tag {69}
$$

and

$$
\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{r}, \mathrm{T}} - \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{i}, \mathrm{T}} = \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi (x , y)}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} + M _ {x} \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda_ {x}} \vec {e} _ {x} + M _ {y} \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda_ {y}} \vec {e} _ {y}, \tag {70}
$$

where $\Lambda_{x}$ and $\Lambda_{y}$ denote the structural periods of the metagrating along the x- and y-directions, respectively, and $M_{x}$ and $M_{y}$ represent the corresponding diffraction orders.

Specifically, for a metagrating based on a square lattice, when the phase shift between adjacent meta-atoms is $\Delta\phi$ , the phase gradient is given by $\Delta\phi/a$ , and the structural period $\Lambda$ satisfies

$$
\Lambda = \frac {\mathrm{LCM} \left(\Delta \phi / \pi , 2 \pi / \pi\right)}{\Delta \phi / \pi} a, \tag {71}
$$

where LCM denotes the least common multiple of the two arguments. In this case, the phase gradient of the metagrating corresponds to one of the discrete diffraction orders.

The metagrating equation reveals two key consequences of diffraction in the light manipulation process:

(1) To achieve efficient light deflection into the desired direction, the phase modulation of meta-atoms needs to direct light into a spatial spectrum range around the target diffraction order and ensure that no other orders fall within this range. The diffraction effect then enforces precise confinement of the deflected light into the target angle.  
(2) Stray light of the metagrating arises from non-target diffraction orders permitted by the metagrating equation.

The design workflow for metagratings is available in our open-source code repository [73].

# 2.2. Emerging Metasurfaces

# 2.2.1. From Single-Layer to Multilayer Metasurfaces

The discussions above focus on single-layer metasurfaces. Analogous to traditional optical design, cascading multiple layers of metasurfaces to work in coordination could enable the achievement of design tasks challenging for a single-layer metasurface, such as aberration correction in metalenses $[179]$ and generation of high-purity vortex beams $[180]$ . It could also facilitate the realization of novel functionalities, including secret sharing $[181]$ and optical neural networks $[182]$ . In addition, active Moiré metasurfaces based on multilayer metasurfaces will be introduced in Subsection 8.10.

(I) When the spacing between layers of metasurfaces is sufficiently large, allowing the metasurfaces to decouple, the overall electromagnetic response of these multiple layers can be computed using ray tracing $[179]$ or the Rayleigh–Sommerfeld diffraction formula in Eq. (157) $[172,183]$ . In this way, we can optimize the interlayer spacings and the target phase distribution for each metasurface layer $[184,185]$ . The design of each metasurface layer is carried out independently based on the target phase distribution requirement, and its theory and methods are entirely consistent with those of a single-layer metasurface. In 2016, Arbabi et al. $[179]$ used this method to realize a bilayer metalens with a layer spacing of 1 mm and an operating wavelength of 850 nm, achieving an F-number of 0.9, an FOV greater than $60^{\circ}$ , and an absolute focusing efficiency of 70% (with a diameter of effective focusing area of 15 mm), with its imaging result shown in Fig. 7(a).

Figure 7  
![](images/f6654ff11ef787a9c5e6f184b254473da150623cb8e22dccfd5ac478bdd302c7.jpg)

Theories and applications of the cascaded metasurfaces. (a) Image taken with the bilayer metalens. Scale bar, 100 $\mu$ m. The insets show an enlarged view of the image at the locations indicated by rectangular boxes, which are outlined in the same color and correspond to viewing angles of 0°, 15°, and 30°. Scale bar, 10 $\mu$ m. (b) Schematic of the approximation for analyzing the electromagnetic wave manipulation of the bilayer metasurface. (c) Impact of the thickness of the spacer layer on the resonance characteristics of the bilayer metasurface. FDTD simulation results of the optical transmittance through the Au and Ag layers spaced with silica. (d) Electric field amplitude distribution of a pair of cascaded meta-atoms at wavelengths of 1180 nm and 1680 nm. (e) Holographic images of the multi-wavelength metasurface hologram at wavelengths of 1180 nm and 1680 nm. Left: simulation results. Right: experimental results. (a) Reprinted from [179] under a Creative Commons license. (c) Reprinted from [186] under a Creative Commons license. (d) Adapted with permission from Zhou et al., Nano Lett. 18, 7529–7537 (2018) [187]. Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society. (e) Adapted from [188] under a Creative Commons license.

(II) Metasurfaces with closely stacked multiple layers with interlayer coupling neglected. However, the limitations brought by interlayer distances of several hundred micrometers or even larger are quite evident. First, it negates the advantage of the ultrathin thickness of the metasurface. Second, it may lead to limitations in design freedom and performance, failing to fully utilize the advantages of multilayer metasurfaces [187]. Now we consider the case where multiple layers of metasurfaces are closely arranged. As illustrated in Fig. 7(b), a bilayer metasurface is taken as an example. Neglecting the interlayer coupling, the Jones matrix $\mathbf{T}_{\mathrm{bilayer}}(x,y)$ of the bilayer metasurface can be expressed as

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathbf {T} _ {\text {bilayer}} (x, y) = \mathbf {T} (x, y) \odot \mathbf {T} ^ {\prime} (x, y) \\ = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} T _ {x x} (x, y) T _ {x x} ^ {\prime} (x, y) & T _ {x y} (x, y) T _ {x y} ^ {\prime} (x, y) \\ T _ {y x} (x, y) T _ {y x} ^ {\prime} (x, y) & T _ {y y} (x, y) T _ {y y} ^ {\prime} (x, y) \end{array} \right], \tag {72} \\ \end{array}
$$

where $\mathbf{T}(x,y)$ and $\mathbf{T}'(x,y)$ are the Jones matrices of the two metasurface layers, respectively, and $\odot$ represents the Hadamard product. Eq. (72) means that the overall amplitude transmission coefficient is the product of the amplitude transmission coefficients of each metasurface layer, and the overall phase shift is the sum of the phase shifts of each metasurface layer. To satisfy the relationship in Eq. (72), the interlayer spacing must not be too large, as the generalized law of refraction indicates that the phase gradient will cause some degree of wavefront divergence, which becomes more severe with increasing interlayer distance. Additionally, the interlayer spacing cannot be too small to avoid significant coupling between meta-atoms across layers. For example, as shown in Fig. 7(c), the impact of the thickness of a silica spacer layer on

the resonance characteristics of a bilayer metasurface operating in the visible is calculated. Below a thickness of 90 nm, the interlayer coupling causes significant changes in the resonance properties of each layer $[186]$ . The reasonable range of interlayer distances can be determined through simulation. For instance, in Fig. 7(d), the electric field amplitude distributions of a pair of cascaded meta-atoms upon incidence of light at wavelengths of 1180 nm and 1680 nm were calculated to ensure no electric field coupling occurs between layers at the set interlayer distance $[187]$ . This method has been applied to achieve complex designs such as the three-layer metalens that eliminates red–green–blue chromatic aberration in the visible $[186]$ , the wavelength-multiplexed metasurface hologram [with its holographic images shown in Fig. 7(e)] $[188]$ , and the multi-wavelength achromatic bilayer metalens with the NA of 0.42 and absolute focusing efficiencies of 38% and 52% at wavelengths of 1180 nm and 1680 nm, respectively $[187]$ .

In 2025, Capasso's group [189] experimentally demonstrated bilayer metasurfaces composed of two free-standing $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ meta-atoms stacked directly on top of one another and surrounded by air. In this configuration, the interlayer spacing is zero to avoid optical losses and unwanted reflections caused by the interlayer material or the gap between the stacked metasurfaces. This design relies on selective development, achieved by carefully choosing two sets of electron-beam resist and developers for patterning the bottom and top layers, minimizing interference with the previously fabricated layer. To ensure negligible interlayer coupling, neither of the two meta-atoms is at resonance, and the top meta-atom has smaller dimensions than the bottom one.

(III) Metasurfaces with closely stacked multiple layers accounting for interlayer coupling. In prior designs, coupling between layers of cascaded metasurfaces is a factor that need to be avoided. However, from another perspective, it also presents a new degree of design freedom which traditional forward design methods struggle to exploit $[190]$ . In 2020, Mansouree et al. $[191]$ proposed an inverse design method for multilayer metasurfaces based on the adjoint method, utilizing interlayer coupling to expand the design space. Experimentally, they achieved a dual-wavelength, dual-focus polarization-insensitive metalens with absolute focusing efficiency (with an effective focusing area diameter of 3.6 mm) exceeding 30% at both wavelengths. This inverse design method will be further introduced in Subsection 3.3.

Despite the applications mentioned earlier, the key advantage of multilayer metasurfaces over single-layer metasurfaces lies in their ability to break the out-of-plane symmetry $[192]$ . As shown in Eq. (8), the Jones matrix of a single-layer metasurface is inherently symmetric. In contrast, multilayer metasurfaces support a full-parameter Jones matrix, enabling the design of chiral metasurfaces $[193]$ and enhancing polarization multiplexing with a larger number of channels $[171,172]$ . These aspects are discussed in detail in Subsections 5.2.1 and 5.2.4. Moreover, multilayer metasurfaces that break the out-of-plane mirror symmetry can be used to realize intrinsic chirality, which will be introduced in Subsection 2.2.5.

# 2.2.2. From Local to Nonlocal Metasurfaces

Until now, our discussion has focused exclusively on local metasurfaces. We will now broaden our exploration to include more general types of metasurfaces.

The general form of the metasurface electromagnetic response at the specific wavelength can be described as

$$
\vec {E} ^ {\text {out}} (x, y) = \iint_ {\Sigma} \mathbf {H} \left(x, y, x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}\right) \mathbf {T} \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}\right) \vec {E} ^ {\text {in}} \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}\right) \mathrm{d} x ^ {\prime} \mathrm{d} y ^ {\prime}, \tag {73}
$$

where $\Sigma$ is the spatial region of the metasurface, $\vec{E}^{in}\left(x',y'\right)$ and $\vec{E}^{out}\left(x,y\right)$ are input and output fields, respectively, $\mathbf{T}\left(x,y\right)$ describes the spatial complex amplitude modulation of the metasurface, and $\mathbf{H}\left(x,y,x',y'\right)$ is the transformation function of the metasurface in real space.

For a local metasurface, the response at a certain point depends only on the input field at the same point, which can be described by

$$
\mathbf {H} \left(x, y, x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}\right) = \delta \left(x - x ^ {\prime}, y - y ^ {\prime}\right) \mathbf {I}, \tag {74}
$$

where $\delta(x - x', y - y')$ is the 2D Dirac Delta function, and $\mathbf{I}$ is the identity matrix. Substituting Eq. (74) into Eq. (73), we obtain

$$
\vec {E} ^ {o u t} (x, y) = \mathbf {T} (x, y) \vec {E} ^ {i n} (x, y). \tag {75}
$$

The Fourier transformation of Eq. (74) gives

$$
\tilde {\mathbf {H}} \left(\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}}\right) = \mathbf {I}, \tag {76}
$$

where $\vec{k}_{T}=k_{x}\vec{e}_{x}+k_{y}\vec{e}_{y}$ is the transverse wavevector/momentum, on the plane of the metasurface [194]. Equation (76) means that for local metasurfaces, the electromagnetic response of the metasurface atoms should be independent of in-plane momentum $\vec{k}_{T}$ , i.e., it does not change with the angle of incidence of the light.

Different from local metasurfaces, the response of the nonlocal metasurface at a center point depends on the input field across an extended region of space $[195]$ . For general nonlocal metasurfaces, namely nondiffractive nonlocal metasurfaces $[196]$ , the transformation function satisfies translation symmetry $[196]$ , i.e.,

$$
\mathbf {H} \left(x, y, x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}\right) = \mathbf {H} \left(x - x ^ {\prime}, y - y ^ {\prime}\right), \tag {77}
$$

and complex amplitude modulation is not provided, i.e.,

$$
\mathbf {T} \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}\right) = \mathbf {I}, \tag {78}
$$

Substitute Eqs. (77) and (78) into Eq. (73), and the Fourier transformation of the result can be written as follows using the convolution theorem:

$$
\tilde {\vec {E}} ^ {o u t} \left(\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}}\right) = \tilde {\mathbf {H}} \left(\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}}\right) \tilde {\vec {E}} ^ {i n} \left(\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}}\right). \tag {79}
$$

By comparing Eq. (79) with Eq. (75), it can be seen that local metasurfaces modulate the light field in real space, while nonlocal metasurfaces modulate the light field in momentum space. Generally, nondiffractive nonlocal metasurfaces are homogenous metasurfaces, and their design focuses on configuring the metasurface units to introduce a specific incident angle-dependent electromagnetic response, also known as spatial dispersion [195], described by $\tilde{\mathbf{H}}\left(\vec{k}_{\mathrm{T}}\right)$ .

Recently, nonlocal metasurfaces have achieved numerous significant applications in optical analog computing and image processing $[197–200]$ , free space squeezing $[201,202]$ , light localization $[203]$ , and so on. The advances in optical analog computing and image processing will be detailed in Subsection 7.1.1, and the progress of free space squeezing will be introduced in Subsection 2.3.2. Systematic introductions for nonlocal metasurfaces can be found in Ref. $[195]$ .

Clarifying the use of the term “nonlocal metasurfaces.” We believe it is essential to clearly distinguish between different interpretations of the term “nonlocal metasurfaces” to avoid conceptual confusion.

First, it is important to emphasize that the aforementioned nonlocal metasurfaces and metasurfaces that consider nonlocal coupling between adjacent meta-atoms in the design process are not equivalent. In the former context, “nonlocal” refers to the metasurface’s functional behavior in momentum space, where the output light field at a specific point in real space depends on the input light field over an extended region. In contrast, in the latter case, “nonlocal” pertains to the coupling between neighboring meta-atoms. It is worth noting that some researchers also describe the latter category as nonlocal metasurfaces $[204–206]$ .

Additionally, metasurfaces that support nonlocal resonant modes are frequently referred to as nonlocal metasurfaces $[207–211]$ . This represents yet another distinct concept. While such metasurfaces can implement nonlocal modulation functions $\tilde{\mathbf{H}}\left(\vec{k}_{\mathrm{T}}\right)$ due to their strong, narrowband spatial frequency responses—as introduced in Subsection 7.1.1—this functionality is not exclusive to metasurfaces with nonlocal resonant modes. For example, similar transformation functions can also be realized using metasurfaces based on local lattice modes $[212]$ , indicating that the two concepts are not equivalent. To avoid conceptual ambiguity, we use the term “high-Q-factor metasurfaces” in this tutorial to specifically refer to metasurfaces with nonlocal resonant modes.

# 2.2.3. From Low-Q-Factor to High-Q-Factor Metasurfaces

Traditional metasurfaces based on local resonances typically exhibit low Q-factors, generally below several tens. In contrast, high-Q-factor metasurfaces rely on nonlocal resonances with significantly higher Q-factors, which can be realized through mechanisms such as guided mode resonances and quasi-BICs. These high-Q-factor metasurfaces serve as an effective platform for enhanced light–matter interactions, lasing, sensing, boosted nonlinear effect, and narrowband wavefront shaping $[84,125,196,213]$ .

Photonic crystal slabs, consisting of arrays of nanostructures—such as rods or holes—arranged in an ordered lattice, exhibit 2D transverse periodicity with a finite thickness $[214]$ . Research on high-Q-factor resonances, including guided mode resonances and BICs, was initially carried out in the context of photonic crystal slabs $[215–218]$ , encompassing both theoretical analyses based on photonic crystal theory and experimental validation. Photonic crystal slabs share strong structural similarities with metasurfaces, particularly homogeneous metasurfaces. However, the former is primarily based on spatially extended nonlocal modes, while the latter emphasizes localized modes and spatially varying light-field modulation, leading to significantly different physical interpretations and applications for the two $[213]$ . Nonetheless, recent developments have introduced high-Q-factor nonlocal resonant modes into the metasurface domain, bridging the gap between the two platforms. Due to this historical development, both the terms “photonic crystal slab” $[219]$ and “metasurface” $[220]$ are now used to describe these devices with similar structures and functions. In this Subsection, we focus specifically on recent advances in high-Q-factor metasurfaces.

Guided mode resonances in metasurfaces. Guided mode resonance is a type of nonlocal lattice resonance that emerges from the coupling between the individual resonances of meta-atoms and transversely propagating waveguide modes within a waveguiding layer. In metallic metasurfaces, this waveguiding layer is typically formed by introducing a separate dielectric layer between the metallic meta-atoms and the substrate. In such configurations, the near fields are localized both at the surface of the metallic meta-atoms and within the dielectric layer $[80]$ . In contrast, for dielectric metasurfaces, although an additional dielectric layer may also be introduced to serve as the waveguiding layer $[221]$ , the waveguiding functionality can be provided directly by the periodic array of dielectric meta-atoms themselves $[220,222]$ .

First, we consider a waveguiding layer with refractive index $n_{g}$ , surrounded by a medium with refractive index $n_{s}$ , where $n_{g} > n_{s}$ . As illustrated in Fig. 8(a), the light line of the surrounding medium is defined by the dispersion relation

$$
\omega = \frac {c _ {0}}{n _ {\mathrm{s}}} \left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} \right|, \tag {80}
$$

where $\vec{k}_{T}$ is the transverse wavevector, $\omega$ is the angular frequency, and $c_{0}$ is the speed of light in vacuum. Since

$$
\left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} \right| ^ {2} + k _ {z} ^ {2} = (n _ {\mathrm{s}} k _ {0}) ^ {2} = \left(\frac {n _ {\mathrm{s}} \omega}{c _ {0}}\right) ^ {2}, \tag {81}
$$

where $k_{0}$ is the wavenumber in vacuum, the region above the light line, i.e.,

$$
\omega > \frac {c _ {0}}{n _ {\mathrm{s}}} \left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} \right|, \tag {82}
$$

is referred to as the light cone or radiation continuum, where modes are radiative—that is, the waveguiding layer can couple energy into the surrounding medium. In contrast, modes below the light line, i.e.,

$$
\omega <   \frac {c _ {0}}{n _ {\mathrm{s}}} \left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} \right|, \tag {83}
$$

are referred to as guided modes, which are non-radiative and remain confined within the waveguiding layer, as $k_{z}$ becomes imaginary and the fields decay exponentially away from the slab.

To couple incident light into guided modes within the waveguiding layer, the transverse wavevector imparted by the metasurface atop the layer must satisfy the following conditions:

$$
\left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} \right| > n _ {\mathrm{s}} \frac {\omega}{c _ {0}}, \tag {84}
$$

$$
\left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} \right| <   n _ {\mathrm{g}} \frac {\omega}{c _ {0}}, \tag {85}
$$

ensuring that the resulting mode lies within the waveguiding slab—above the light line of the surrounding medium but below that of the waveguiding layer. According to the metagrating equations [Eqs. (69) and (70)], such a transverse wavevector shift can be introduced through the phase gradient and diffraction effects generated by the metagrating. Most guided mode resonance metasurfaces rely primarily on diffraction to achieve this shift. Once the guided modes are excited, they propagate within the waveguiding layer while simultaneously interacting with the periodic modulation of the metagrating. This interaction enables coupling between the guided and radiative modes, thereby giving rise to guided mode resonances. It is worth noting that a “guided mode resonance” is, in fact, a radiative mode—rather than a guided mode, as the name might suggest.

Now consider a homogeneous metagrating based on a square lattice positioned atop a waveguiding layer, which is surrounded by a substrate with refractive index $n_{sub}$ and a superstrate with refractive index $n_{sup}$ . In this configuration, the phase gradient is zero. Focusing on the x-direction only—i.e., setting $k_{y} \equiv 0$ and $M_{y} \equiv 0$ —the metagrating equation [Eq. (69) or Eq. (70)] simplifies to

$$
k _ {\mathrm{T}} = k _ {\mathrm{i,T}} + M _ {x} \frac {2 \pi}{a}, \tag {86}
$$

Figure 8  
![](images/4af7561e65a656c4daf706c20cc7e9333702cfad25feeb50c26d83da51cc390b.jpg)

Guided mode resonances in metasurfaces. (a) Schematic illustration of radiative modes and guided modes. (b) Formation of guided mode resonances and a symmetry-protected BIC above the light cone, resulting from band folding of two guided modes from neighboring Brillouin zones. (c) Top left: schematic of the metasurface-waveguide system. A 330 nm-thick resist layer with air holes is patterned on a silicon-on-insulator wafer comprising a 220 nm-thick Si layer and a 2 $\mu$ m-thick $SiO_{2}$ layer. Top right: simulated field distributions of the guided mode resonance $TE_{311}$ . Bottom left: measured reflectance spectra of four fabricated samples with a fixed lattice constant of 545 nm and varying hole radii from 72 nm to 105 nm. Bottom right: measured (red bars) and simulated (dashed line) Q-factors of the guided mode resonance as a function of hole radius R. (b) Adapted with permission from Koshelev et al., Optics Photonics News 31, 38–45 (2020) [223]. Copyright © 2020 Optica Publishing Group. (c) Adapted from [221] under a Creative Commons license.

where $k_{T}$ represents either $k_{t,T}$ or $k_{r,T}$ , depending on whether the metagrating operates in transmission or reflection mode. Substituting Eq. (86) into Eqs. (84) and (85), we obtain the following conditions for coupling into guided modes:

$$
\left| k _ {\mathrm{i}, \mathrm{T}} + M _ {x} \frac {2 \pi}{a} \right| > n _ {\text {sub}} \frac {\omega}{c _ {0}}, \quad \left| k _ {\mathrm{i}, \mathrm{T}} + M _ {x} \frac {2 \pi}{a} \right| > n _ {\sup} \frac {\omega}{c _ {0}}, \tag {87}
$$

$$
\left| k _ {\mathrm{i}, \mathrm{T}} + M _ {x} \frac {2 \pi}{a} \right| <   n _ {\mathrm{g}} \frac {\omega}{c _ {0}}. \tag {88}
$$

Furthermore, the periodicity of the metagrating results in a band structure that is periodic in momentum space with a period of $2\pi/a$ [214]. The region $-\pi/a < k_{T} \leq \pi/a$ , which contains non-redundant values of $k_{T}$ , is known as the Brillouin zone. As illustrated in Fig. 8(b), this periodicity allows guided mode bands that lie below the light line to be folded back into the light cone ( $M_{x} = \pm1$ ), thereby enabling the formation of guided mode resonances. Notably, near the $\Gamma$ point ( $k_{T} = 0$ ), a pair of degenerate guided mode resonances exhibit anti-crossing behavior. Strong coupling between them leads to both constructive and destructive interference: the former results in a guided mode resonance with doubled radiation loss, while the latter gives rise to a symmetry-protected BIC with an ideally infinite Q-factor. We see that Eqs. (87) and (88) define the critical conditions for the realization of guided mode resonances.

In 2020, Han et al. [220] experimentally demonstrated this phenomenon using a terahertz homogeneous metasurface composed of cuboid Si meta-atoms arranged in a square lattice. In their design, the 2D array of Si meta-atoms functioned both as a grating and as an effective waveguiding layer.

In 2023, Huang et al. [221] experimentally realized the similar design by integrating a multilayer waveguide system with a homogeneous metasurface formed by resist holes in a square lattice, as shown in the top left panel of Fig. 8(c). This structure supported the guided mode resonance $TE_{311}$ , illustrated in the top right panel of Fig. 8(c). As seen in the bottom left panel of Fig. 8(c), decreasing the hole radius of the metasurface led to a narrowing of the resonance linewidth and a slight redshift in the resonance wavelength. The corresponding Q-factors, extracted via Fano fitting, are shown in the bottom right panel, with the highest value reaching $2.39 \times 10^{5}$ at a hole radius of 72 nm. This ultrahigh Q-factor is attributed to the simplified fabrication process, which avoids dry etching by using an ultrathin photoresist layer patterned via electron beam lithography. However, for metasurfaces with smaller hole radii, the resonance peaks become increasingly difficult to distinguish from experimental noise and background.

The aforementioned metasurfaces convert incident free-space light into guided modes within a waveguide layer—and subsequently couple these modes to radiative channels—to realize guided mode resonances. In contrast, the reverse process is also possible: incident guided waves can be converted into free-space radiation with arbitrarily tailored wavefronts using metasurfaces based on resonant or propagation phase $[224,225]$ , detour phase $[226,227]$ , or geometric phase $[228]$ , which has garnered significant attention in recent years.

Quasi-BIC in metasurfaces. The concept of BIC, originally proposed in quantum mechanics nearly a century ago, has been adopted in photonics to describe modes whose energy remains completely localized with entirely suppressed radiative losses—even though the mode lies within the radiation continuum of the surrounding environment $[223,229–233]$ .

BICs in photonic crystal slabs are typically classified into three types [233], each associated with a distinct Friedrich–Wintgen (FW) origin (from this perspective, BICs arise from the destructive interference of radiation from their constituent modes) [234]:

(1) The FW BIC occurs near the avoided crossing point of the bands of two distinct guided mode resonances. As shown in Fig. 9(a), its formation is clearly explained by the original FW mechanism.  
(2) The symmetry-protected BIC is located at the $\Gamma$ point in momentum space [as shown in Fig. 8(b)] and is guaranteed by the structural symmetry of the system. For example, consider a structure that possesses inversion symmetry with respect to the in-plane coordinates, $\vec{r}_{\mathrm{T}} \rightarrow -\vec{r}_{\mathrm{T}}$ , where $\vec{r}_{\mathrm{T}} = x\vec{e}_{x} + y\vec{e}_{y}$ . Outside the structure, the radiative plane waves are invariant under inversion and correspond to even modes that satisfy

$$
\vec {H} (\vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}) = \vec {H} (- \vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}), \quad \vec {E} (\vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}) = - \vec {E} (- \vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}). \tag {89}
$$

If the modes supported by the structure are odd under inversion, satisfying

$$
\vec {H} (\vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}) = - \vec {H} (- \vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}), \quad \vec {E} (\vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}) = \vec {E} (- \vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}), \tag {90}
$$

Figure 9  
![](images/12313af3f67b9c8ae7181bb06d123e45359fdfe55398f71992ee6d27bdd08921.jpg)

BICs in metasurfaces. (a) Schematic of the FW origins of three types of BICs. GR: guided mode resonance. FP, FP resonance; GM, guided mode (b) First column: scattering spectra of nanorods with varying aspect ratios $r / h$ , where $r$ and $h$ are the radius and height of the nanorod, respectively. The permittivity of the nanorods is 80. The size parameter $\chi = \omega r / c_0$ represents the normalized frequency. Second column: resonant frequencies of the Mie-like mode $\mathrm{TE}_{020}$ and the FP-like mode $\mathrm{TE}_{012}$ in the avoided crossing regime. Cyan and green curves correspond to the lower- and higher-frequency modes, respectively (consistent throughout). Third column: Fano asymmetry parameters $q$ for both modes. Insets: scattering spectra of nanorods with $r / h = 0.703$ ( $q \to \infty$ ), $r / h = 0.700$ ( $q \to -1$ ), $r / h = 0.699$ ( $q \to 0$ ), and $r / h = 0.698$ ( $q \to 1$ ). Fourth column: Q-factors of both modes. (c) Top: simulated transmittance spectrum of a single nanorod with a permittivity of 80 as a function of $\chi$ and $r / h$ . Bottom: simulated transmittance spectrum of a nanorod array arranged in a square lattice using the same nanorod as in the top panel. The red circle indicates the positions of the quasi-FW BICs. (d) Simulated eigenmode and transmittance spectra as functions of wavelength and rotation angle $\theta$ . Error bars indicate the inverse radiation lifetime of each mode. Inset: schematic of the asymmetric metasurface unit. (e) Simulated Q-factors as a function of the asymmetry parameter $\alpha$ (log–log scale) for various symmetry-broken metasurface unit designs. (f) Schematic of a metallic metasurface supporting a (continued)

(continued) quasi-BIC. (g) Simulated Q-factors as a function of the normalized detuning wavelength $\Delta$ (log–log scale) for various metallic metasurface unit designs supporting bright and dark localized surface plasmon resonances. Here, $\alpha$ denotes the scaling factor for the meta-atom height, where $\alpha > 1$ indicates an increase and $\alpha < 1$ a decrease. The parameter $\Delta = (\lambda - a)/a$ represents the normalized detuning, with $\lambda$ being the resonance wavelength and a the lattice constant. (a) Adapted with permission from Hu et al., Optica 9, 1353–1361 (2022) [234]. Copyright © 2022 Optica Publishing Group. (b) Adapted with permission from Rybin et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 119, 243901 (2017) [235]. Copyright © 2017 American Physical Society. (d) and (e) Adapted with permission from Koshelev et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 121, 193903 (2018) [88]. Copyright © 2018 American Physical Society. (f) Adapted with permission from Liang et al., Nano Lett. 20, 6351–6356 (2020) [236]. Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society. (g) Adapted with permission from Liang et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 133, 053801 (2024) [237]. Copyright © 2024 American Physical Society.

then they are symmetry-incompatible with the external plane waves. As a result, such modes cannot couple to free space and thus form symmetry-protected BICs. As shown in Fig. 9(a), the FW origin of symmetry-protected BIC can be attributed to the coupling between two degenerate guided mode resonances.

(3) The accidental BIC located at the off- $\Gamma$ point appears to originate from a single resonant mode. However, as shown in Fig. 9(a), it actually results from the coupling between a guided mode resonance and an FP resonance. The large deviation from the avoided crossing point compared to the coupling strength makes it appear as if it arises from a single mode.

Pure BICs with infinite Q-factors exist only as a mathematical ideal. In practice, factors such as material absorption, finite sample size, the presence of a substrate (which introduces additional radiation channels due to out-of-plane asymmetry), and fabrication imperfections (e.g., surface roughness, structural disorder, and non-uniformity) cause BICs to transform into quasi-BICs with finite but still extremely high Q-factors. These quasi-BICs offer a practical route to achieving ultra-high-Q resonant modes.

In the field of metasurfaces, both quasi-FW BICs and quasi-symmetry-protected BICs have been demonstrated. Here, we extend the concept of FW BICs to refer broadly to BICs that arise from destructive interference between radiation from two lattice modes—either local or nonlocal (not limited to guided mode resonances)—and are realized through fine-tuning of system parameters. Currently, quasi-BIC metasurfaces have already achieved many novel applications, such as molecular fingerprint detection $[238,239]$ , thermal regulation $[240]$ , narrowband optical devices $[207,209]$ , wavefront selection $[241]$ , and special structural color $[242]$ .

In the following, we discuss these two types of quasi-BICs in metasurfaces in greater detail.

(I) Quasi-FW BICs. In 2017, Rybin et al. [235] investigated the formation of quasi-FW BICs in isolated subwavelength dielectric resonators with high refractive index. In such nanorods, when two eigenmodes with different polarizations—associated with a Mie mode and an FP mode—strongly interact near the avoided crossing regime, they interfere predominantly outside the resonator, as they are nearly orthogonal within it. This external interference results in the formation of a quasi-BIC. As shown in the first column of Fig. 9(b), the scattering spectra of nanorods with varying aspect ratios $r / h$ reveal the evolution of two resonant modes: a Mie-like mode $\mathrm{TE}_{020}$ and an FP-like mode $\mathrm{TE}_{012}$ . The second column shows that their resonant frequencies

exhibit an avoided crossing behavior. In the third and fourth columns, it is evident that for the higher-frequency mode, the Fano asymmetry parameter $q$ approaches $\infty$ and the Q-factor reaches a maximum value of 64,760 when $r / h \to 0.703$ , confirming the formation of a quasi-BIC. Further simulations showed that for realistic Si resonators, Q-factors on the order of $\sim 200$ can be achieved. In 2019, Bogdanov et al. [243] experimentally confirmed the existence of quasi-FW BICs in a single resonator at microwave frequencies. In 2020, Koshelev et al. [244] utilized this mechanism to enhance nonlinear optical effects, which will be discussed in detail in Subsection 2.2.4.

Notably, quasi-FW BICs in isolated resonators can be readily extended to periodic meta-atom arrays. As shown in Fig. 9(c), the transmittance spectra of a single nanorod and a nanorod array exhibit similar resonance features—transforming from isolated resonant modes into localized lattice modes—indicating that the quasi-FW BIC formed in a single resonator can be preserved in the array configuration.

(II) Quasi-symmetry-protected BICs. Homogeneous metasurfaces composed of metallic or dielectric meta-atoms with broken in-plane symmetry can be used to realize quasi-symmetry-protected BICs $[88,238,245–249]$ . In this way, Q-factors on the order of several hundred have been experimentally achieved.

Taking the asymmetric metasurface shown in the inset of Fig. 9(d) as an example, in-plane symmetry is broken by introducing a rotation angle $\theta$ between the two meta-atoms. The ideal (lossless and infinitely extended) metasurface with $\theta = 0$ supports a symmetry-protected BIC at normal incidence. As shown in Fig. 9(d), breaking the inversion symmetry of the unit cell transforms this BIC into a quasi-BIC with a finite Q-factor.

The dependence of the Q-factor on the asymmetry parameter $\alpha$ for metasurfaces with various broken-symmetry unit designs is shown in Fig. 9(e). Here, $\alpha$ quantifies the degree of in-plane asymmetry in the metasurface unit. For example, in the metasurface unit shown in the inset of Fig. 9(d), $\alpha = \sin \theta$ . It can be observed that the Q-factor of the quasi-BIC scales inversely with $\alpha^{2}$ for small $\alpha$ values [88].

For metallic metasurfaces, realizing quasi-BICs is more challenging due to the strong dissipative losses inherent to plasmonic materials. In 2020, Liang et al. [236] experimentally demonstrated a quasi-symmetry-protected BIC in a metallic metasurface consisting of vertically oriented double-pillar meta-atoms coated with a thin layer of Au, as shown in Fig. 9(f). A Q-factor of approximately 17 was achieved in the fabricated sample. In 2024, Liang et al. [237] proposed that higher Q-factors in metallic metasurfaces can be achieved by engineering the resonant modes to transition from local to nonlocal regimes through reduction of the meta-atom height, as illustrated in Fig. 9(g).

The design workflow for quasi-symmetry-protected BIC metasurfaces is available in our open-source code repository [73].

High-Q-factor wavefront manipulation. Wavefront manipulation is a key application of inhomogeneous metasurfaces, typically realized through the spatial arrangement of low-Q-factor meta-atoms with varying geometries and orientations. However, in the case of high-Q-factor homogeneous metasurfaces that support nonlocal resonant modes, the optical response becomes highly sensitive to variations among adjacent meta-atoms. This sensitivity makes it challenging to implement high-Q-factor wavefront manipulation. Such metasurfaces are referred to as diffractive nonlocal metasurfaces by Overvig and Alù [196]. In this scenario, $\tilde{\mathbf{H}}\left(\vec{k}_{\mathrm{T}}\right)\neq\mathbf{I}$ [which violates Eq. (76)], and $\mathbf{T}\left(x^{\prime},y^{\prime}\right)\neq\mathbf{I}$ [which violates Eq. (78)], meaning that neither Eq. (75) nor Eq. (79) can fully describe the system. Therefore, the generalized formulation given in Eq. (73) should be adopted to completely characterize these metasurfaces.

(I) High-Q-factor metagratings based on guided mode resonances. In 2020, Lawrence et al. [222] demonstrated a high-Q-factor metagrating based on guided mode resonances. As shown in the first column of Fig. 10(a), the metagrating consists of 600 nm-tall Si bars patterned atop a sapphire substrate, incorporating periodic notches with a period $\Lambda_{p}$ and a depth d. The Si bars simultaneously function as scatterers that modulate the incident free-space light and as the effective waveguiding layer. The periodic notches provide the necessary spatial momentum shift to couple guided modes into radiative diffraction orders, thereby forming guided mode resonances. As shown in the second column of Fig. 10(a), for a sample with $\Lambda_{p} = 570$ nm and d = 100 nm, the measured diffraction spectra exhibit sharp resonance peaks, corresponding to a Q-factor of 1500. As illustrated in the third and fourth columns, decreasing the notch depth from 150 nm to 50 nm further enhances the Q-factor to 2500. Based on this method, a beam splitter directing light into the $\pm1$ st diffraction orders with a Q-factor of 1088 was also experimentally demonstrated.

In 2021, Song et al. [208] adopted a similar design to realize a high-Q-factor meta-grating capable of decoupling optical functionalities across wavelengths. Their device enables broadband transmission in the visible range, while simultaneously redirecting near-infrared light at 870 nm into the +1st diffraction order, as shown in Fig. 10(b). This functionality has potential applications in eye-tracking systems for wearable displays.

The same principle can be extended to metagratings with various structural configurations [250,251].

(II) High-Q-factor metagratings based on quasi-BICs and geometric phase. In 2020, Overvig et al. [207,252] proposed a design strategy that integrates the geometric phase with quasi-symmetry-protected BICs to realize high-Q-factor wavefront manipulation. As illustrated in the inset of Fig. 10(c), the metasurface unit consists of a pair of orthogonally placed cylinders with a structural perturbation $\delta$ , and the meta-atoms are rotated by an angle $\alpha$ . The perturbation $\delta$ breaks the in-plane symmetry of the unit cell, resulting in the emergence of a quasi-BIC. As shown in Fig. 10(c), the Q-factor can be tuned by varying $\delta$ , while rotating the meta-atoms introduces a geometric phase approximately equal to $4\alpha$ . Arbitrary wavefront shaping can thus be achieved by spatially varying the rotation angles of the meta-atoms. However, introducing meta-atoms with different rotation angles degrades the nonlocal resonant mode, thereby reducing the Q-factor of the metasurface.

This design concept was experimentally demonstrated in 2022 by Malek et al. [209]. As shown in the left panel of Fig. 10(d), a wavelength-selective metalens was realized using a metasurface unit similar to the one depicted in the inset of Fig. 10(c). In this device, the wavelength selectivity is governed by the quasi-BIC, while the geometric phase enables wavefront shaping. As shown in the right panel of Fig. 10(d), the metasurface unit exhibits a geometric phase that approximates $4\alpha$ . The middle panel presents the measured transmission and focusing performance of the fabricated metalens, which features an NA of 0.2 and a diameter of $800~{\mu\mathrm{m}}$ . The metalens operates at a resonance wavelength centered at $1590~\mathrm{nm}$ with a Q-factor of approximately 86.

This design approach can be extended to other high-Q-factor metasurfaces with alternative structural configurations $[211,253]$ .

# 2.2.4. From Linear to Nonlinear Metasurfaces

By integrating the fields of metasurfaces and nonlinear optics, nonlinear metasurfaces incorporate nonlinear materials into their structural design, enabling significant applications in enhancing nonlinear effects, controlling polarization in nonlinear processes,

Figure 10

Third column: +1st-order diffraction spectra of metagratings with notch depth d decreasing from 150 nm to 50 nm. Fourth column: extracted Q-factors from the measured spectra shown in the third column. (c) Left: simulated Q-factor as a function of the normalized perturbation $\delta/a$ for metasurface units with different rotation angles $\alpha$ . Inset: schematic of the metasurface unit characterized by the rotation angle $\alpha$ and perturbation $\delta$ . Right: simulated geometric phase as a function of $\alpha$ for metasurface units with varying perturbations $\delta$ . (d) Left: schematics of the wavelength-selective metasurface and its unit cell, characterized by a rotation angle $\alpha$ and a perturbation $\delta$ . Copyright © 2020 Springer Nature. (b) Adapted with permission from Song et al., Nat. Nanotechnol., 16, 1224–1230 (2021) [208]. Copyright © 2021 Springer Nature. (c) Adapted with permission from Overvig et al., Phys. Rev. Lett., 125, 017402 (2020) [207]. Copyright © 2020 American Physical Society. (d) Adapted from [209] under a Creative Commons license.

and manipulating the wavefronts of nonlinear light [80,254-258]. Here, we present an overview of the physical mechanisms and design principles of nonlinear metasurfaces. Applications of nonlinear metasurfaces in quantum photonics and active metasurfaces are discussed in Subsections 6.1.2 and 8.5, respectively.

Enhancement of nonlinear effects based on resonances. Traditional nonlinear optics relies on bulk nonlinear crystals such as lithium niobate and potassium dihydrogen phosphate (KDP), where nonlinear processes must satisfy energy conservation and momentum conservation (phase matching) conditions. For metasurfaces, the momentum conservation condition can be relaxed to some extent (for a detailed analysis of the spontaneous parametric down-conversion process, see Subsection 6.1.2). However, achieving efficient nonlinear effects at the nanoscale is highly challenging, as the nonlinear optical interaction length is usually subwavelength. To improve the conversion efficiency of nonlinear processes, resonances with high Q-factors can be introduced to confine the optical field to subwavelength scales, enhancing the local optical power density (since the intensity of parametric processes scales with the fourth or sixth power of the fundamental field strength $[256]$ ) and extending the effective interaction time between photons and the nonlinear material.

In 2014, Shcherbakov et al. [259] investigated the impact of Mie resonances in Si metasurfaces on third-harmonic generation (THG). As shown in Fig. 11(a), a two-orders-of-magnitude enhancement of the harmonic intensity relative to unstructured bulk Si was experimentally achieved. Notably, the two peaks in the THG spectrum coincide with the spectral positions of the ED and MD resonances of the metasurface, demonstrating the influence of resonant modes on nonlinear conversion efficiency.

To further improve the conversion efficiency, the following strategies can be employed.

(I) Designing the metasurface to resonate at both the pump and signal light frequencies. In 2015, Celebrano et al. [260] designed a Au nanoantenna array with two surface plasmon resonances, whose resonant wavelengths corresponded to the pump and signal light wavelengths, respectively, as shown in Fig. 11(b). Experimentally, a conversion efficiency $P_{\mathrm{signal}}/P_{\mathrm{pump}}^{2}$ of $\sim5\times10^{-10}~W^{-1}$ for second harmonic generation (SHG) was achieved, resulting in a second harmonic photon yield of more than $3\times10^{6}$ photons per second.

(II) Utilizing materials with high nonlinear coefficients. In 2014, Lee et al. [261] employed quantum-engineering electronic intersubband transitions in n-doped multi-quantum-well (MQW) semiconductor heterostructures to achieve a large nonlinear optical response. They combined the MQW layered substrates with plasmonic metasurfaces, as shown in Fig. 11(c), with resonances overlapping at both the pump and second harmonic wavelengths. Experimentally, they realized a 400-nm-thick metasurface with a nonlinear susceptibility greater than $5 \times 10^{4}$ picometers per volt and a conversion efficiency ( $P_{signal}/P_{pump}$ ) of $\sim2 \times 10^{-6}$ using a pump intensity of $15  kW \cdot cm^{-2}$ for SHG at a pump wavelength of $\sim8  \mu m$ .

(III) Introducing nonlocal modes with higher Q-factors. In 2020, Koshelev et al. [244] experimentally demonstrated that the quasi-BIC can effectively enhance nonlinear effects, increasing SHG efficiency with a directly measured conversion efficiency ( $P_{signal}/P_{pump}^{2}$ ) of $\sim1.3\times10^{-6}W^{-1}$ , as shown in Figs. 11(d) and 11(e). The isolated AlGaAs nanoantenna structure was carefully designed to exhibit a high-order Mie mode at the second harmonic wavelength with a Q-factor of 65 and a quasi-FW BIC with a measured Q-factor of $188\pm5$ at the pump wavelength. Additionally, a cylindrical vector beam with azimuthal polarization was used as the illumination light to match the spatial profile of the pump with the distribution of the excited mode, maximizing SHG efficiency.

Rotational symmetry selection rules for metasurfaces with circularly polarized incidence. Under the incidence of circularly polarized pump light, the nonlinear process of harmonic generation in nonlinear bulk crystals and metasurfaces follows the

Figure 11  
![](images/b8a61ccf409ff9291ebfef89b149f707f40c4621a5c572f81a7c99562b8b74c6.jpg)
