(a) Adapted with permission from Shcherbakov et al., Nano Lett. 14, 6488–6492 (2014) [259]. Copyright © 2014 American Chemical Society. (b) Adapted with permission from Celebrano et al., Nat. Nanotechnol. 10, 412–417 (2015) [260]. Copyright © 2015 Springer Nature. (c) Reprinted with permission from Lee et al., Nature 511, 65–69 (2014) [261]. Copyright © 2014 Springer Nature. (d) and (e) Adapted with permission from Koshelev et al., Science 367, 288–292 (2020) [244]. Copyright © 2020 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (f) Adapted with permission from Li et al., Nat. Mater. 14, 607–612 (2015) [262]. Copyright © 2015 Springer Nature. (g) Adapted with permission from Segal et al., Nat. Photonics 9, 180–184 (2015) [263]. Copyright © 2015 Springer Nature. (h) Adapted from [264] under a Creative Commons license.

selection rules [265,266], which are rooted in the conservation of angular momentum. Paraxial optical beams can carry three types of angular momentum: (i) spin angular momentum, (ii) intrinsic orbital angular momentum (often simply referred to as orbital angular momentum), and (iii) external orbital angular momentum for beams propagating at a distance from the coordinate origin [267]. The process we consider here involves only spin angular momentum. For the $M$ -th order harmonic generation process, the total angular momentum of the input photons is given by

$$
S _ {\mathrm{i}} = M \sigma_ {\mathrm{i}} \hbar , \tag {91}
$$

where $\sigma_{i}=\pm1$ corresponds to the circular polarization state of the incident light (LCP/RCP). The total angular momentum of the output photon is

$$
S _ {\mathrm{o}} = \sigma_ {\mathrm{o}} \hbar , \tag {92}
$$

where $\sigma_{o}=\pm1$ corresponds to the cases when the output photon is LCP and RCP, respectively. Assuming the metasurface has K-fold rotational symmetry (where K is an integer greater than or equal to 1), by the law of conservation of angular momentum, we have

$$
S _ {\mathrm{o}} - S _ {\mathrm{i}} = N K \hbar , \tag {93}
$$

where $N$ is an arbitrary integer. Substituting Eqs. (91) and (92) into Eq. (93), we obtain

$$
M \sigma_ {\mathrm{i}} - \sigma_ {\mathrm{o}} = N K. \tag {94}
$$

Introducing $\tilde{\sigma}=\sigma_{0}/\sigma_{i}$ ( $\tilde{\sigma}=\pm1$ indicates that the circular polarization state of the output photon is the same or opposite to that of the incident photons, respectively), Eq. (94) can be rewritten as

$$
M - \tilde {\sigma} = N K, \tag {95}
$$

which imposes constraints on the polarization characteristics of nonlinear bulk crystals and metasurfaces in harmonic generation. For example, for a metasurface with threefold rotational symmetry $(K = 3)$ , when generating second harmonics $(M = 2)$ , we have

$$
2 - \tilde {\sigma} = 3 N. \tag {96}
$$

We find that for $\tilde{\sigma} = +1$ , this equation cannot hold for any integer value of $N$ , meaning it is not possible to generate a second harmonic with the same circular polarization

<table><tr><td>Harmonic orderM</td><td>Sign of $\tilde{\sigma}$ </td><td> $\mathbf{K=1}$ </td><td> $\mathbf{K=2}$ </td><td> $\mathbf{K=3}$ </td><td> $\mathbf{K=4}$ </td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">1</td><td>+</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><td>-</td><td> $-2\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td> $-2\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td><td>×</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">2</td><td>+</td><td> $+\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td><td>×</td><td>×</td></tr><tr><td>-</td><td> $-3\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td><td> $-3\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">3</td><td>+</td><td> $+2\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td> $+2\theta\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}$ </td><td>×</td><td>×</td></tr><tr><td>-</td><td> $-4\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td> $-4\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td><td> $-4\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">4</td><td>+</td><td> $+3\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td><td> $+3\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td></tr><tr><td>-</td><td> $-5\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td><td>×</td><td>×</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">5</td><td>+</td><td> $+4\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td> $+4\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td><td> $+4\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td></tr><tr><td>-</td><td> $-6\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td> $-6\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td> $-6\sigma_{\mathrm{i}}\theta$ </td><td>×</td></tr></table>

$^{a}\tilde{\sigma}=\pm1$ indicates whether the circular polarization state of the output light is the same as $(+1)$ or opposite to $(-1)$ that of the input light. $\sigma_{i}=\pm1$ represents the circular polarization state of the incident light, where +1 corresponds to LCP and -1 to RCP. The symbol $\times$ denotes cases prohibited by the selection rules. K represents the order of rotational symmetry of the metasurface.

state as the incident light. Instead, there is no such restriction for $\tilde{\sigma} = -1$ . Similarly, when generating third harmonics, we have

$$
3 - \tilde {\sigma} = 3 N. \tag {97}
$$

For $\tilde{\sigma}=\pm1$ , this equation cannot hold for any integer value of N, meaning that the generation of third harmonics is prohibited. Based on this, we can derive all the prohibited cases shown in Table 1.

In 2014, Konishi et al. [268] and Chen et al. [269] independently demonstrated the above selection rules experimentally in nonlinear metasurfaces with high-fold rotational symmetries.

In fact, Eq. (95) can also be applied to linear optics, representing the constraints on the polarization characteristics of K-fold rotationally symmetric metasurfaces due to angular momentum conservation. In this case, M = 1, and Eq. (95) becomes

$$
1 - \tilde {\sigma} = N K. \tag {98}
$$

For the case of $\tilde{\sigma}=+1$ , this equation holds for any value of K. Interestingly, for the case of $\tilde{\sigma}=-1$ , this equation only holds for K=1 or K=2, and does not hold for $K\geq3$ , meaning that circular polarization conversion is impossible for metasurfaces with rotational symmetries of order 3 or higher.

Finally, we further discuss the rotational symmetry of metasurfaces. In fact, the rotational symmetry of a metasurface comes from two aspects: the rotational symmetry of the meta-atoms and the rotational symmetry of the lattice. A square lattice has $C_{4}$ rotational symmetry, while a hexagonal lattice has $C_{6}$ rotational symmetry. When the rotational symmetries of the meta-atoms and the lattice are incompatible (e.g., a $C_{3}$ meta-atom array on a square lattice), in most cases, the rotational symmetry of the metasurface mainly depends on the symmetry of the meta-atoms. However, with careful structural design, the influence of the lattice cannot be ignored in certain cases [153].

Wavefront manipulation for nonlinear waves. Compared to linear metasurfaces, the phase modulation mechanism of nonlinear metasurfaces differs in some ways.

(I) Nonlinear geometric phase modulation mechanism. The geometric phase in the nonlinear regime can be understood through a simple coordinate transformation process [262]. For the generation of M-order harmonics, the geometric phase is given by

$$
\phi_ {\text {nonlinear geometric}} = (M - \tilde {\sigma}) \sigma_ {\mathrm{i}} \theta , \tag {99}
$$

where $\theta$ is the rotation angle of the meta-atom. Based on Eq. (99), we can obtain the geometric phase modulation for scenarios in Table 1. Taking the $C_{3}$ metasurface used for SHG as an example, when the incident circular polarization state of the pump light is $\sigma_{i}$ , the circular polarization state of the output fundamental harmonic remains unchanged and is not modulated in phase by the rotation of the meta-atom. Instead, the output second harmonic signal light has a circular polarization state of $\sigma_{o} = -\sigma_{i}$ , and it is subject to a geometric phase modulation of $-3\sigma_{i}\theta$ .

We note that linear geometric phase requires the metasurface to have rotational symmetries of order lower than 3 to enable polarization conversion, whereas no such limitation exists for nonlinear geometric phase.

In 2015, Li et al. [262] experimentally demonstrated Au metagratings with $C_{2}$ and $C_{4}$ symmetries based on nonlinear geometric phase, where their THG diffraction patterns shown in Fig. 11(f) matched the phase modulation effects predicted by Table 1. In the same year, Tymchenko et al. [270] experimentally verified the nonlinear geometric phase in SHG metasurfaces.

(II) Mimicking patterned poling of nonlinear crystals, in 2015, Segal et al. [263] achieved a binary phase shift modulation (0 or $\pi$ ) for SHG using Au SRR metasurfaces. As shown in the top panel of Fig. 11(g), when a fundamental harmonic beam is polarized along the base of the SRR, the resulting second harmonic currents are predominantly cross-polarized relative to the incident beam and exhibit asymmetry with respect to the SRR base. As a result, performing a mirror inversion of the SRR across its base has little impact on the fundamental harmonic beam but induces a $\pi$ phase shift in the generated second harmonic wave. Based on this principle, a nonlinear binary-phase Fresnel zone plate with a focal length of 1 mm was experimentally demonstrated, as shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 11(g).

(III) Resonance and propagation phase modulation mechanisms. Using nonlinear full-wave simulations (such as nonlinear FDTD $[264,271]$ and FEM $[272]$ ), the forward design method for linear metasurfaces based on resonant and propagation phases can be extended to the design of nonlinear metasurfaces. For example, in 2016, Almeida et al. $[264]$ constructed a database consisting of meta-atoms with rectangular holes of different sizes. Building on this, they experimentally demonstrated a nonlinear meta-grating, as shown in Fig. 11(h), and a nonlinear metalens with focal lengths of several microns for a four-wave mixing beam at a wavelength of 633 nm.

# 2.2.5. From Achiral to Chiral Metasurfaces

Chirality, a fundamental property of nature, refers to the geometric characteristic of an object that lacks mirror symmetry and cannot be superimposed onto its mirror image by any combination of rotations and translations. Although light itself has no intrinsic geometry and is therefore not chiral in the conventional sense, its chirality can be characterized through specific parameters. Specifically, for LCP or RCP light, chirality arises from the helical rotation of the electric and magnetic field vectors as the wave propagates $[273]$ . Chiral materials respond differently to LCP and RCP light, giving

rise to a range of chiroptical phenomena. These include differences in transmission, reflection, or absorption between the two polarizations—commonly referred to as circular dichroism (CD)—as well as the rotation of the polarization plane of linearly polarized light, known as optical rotation (OR) or optical activity (OA).

Natural chiral materials typically exhibit weak optical chirality. In contrast, chiral metasurfaces, composed of meta-atoms that lack mirror symmetry, have been extensively studied for their ability to achieve strong chiral responses $[80,274–279]$ . Various types of chiral metasurfaces have been developed, based on different underlying physical mechanisms.

Taking CD in transmission as an example, consider a metasurface characterized by the transmission matrix

$$
\left[ \begin{array}{c c} \tau_ {+ +} & \tau_ {+ -} \\ \tau_ {- +} & \tau_ {- -} \end{array} \right],
$$

where $\tau_{ij}$ denotes the transmittance into polarization state i under illumination of polarization j, and the subscripts + and - represent LCP and RCP, respectively. The CD is defined as

$$
\mathrm{CD} = \frac {\tau_ {+} - \tau_ {-}}{\tau_ {+} + \tau_ {-}} = \frac {\left(\tau_ {+ +} + \tau_ {- +}\right) - \left(\tau_ {- -} + \tau_ {+ -}\right)}{\left(\tau_ {+ +} + \tau_ {- +}\right) + \left(\tau_ {- -} + \tau_ {+ -}\right)}, \tag {100}
$$

where $\tau_{\pm}$ are the total transmittance for incident LCP and RCP light, respectively. The true chirality requires that the cross-polarized transmission components $\tau_{+-}$ and $\tau_{-+}$ are negligible, indicating that LCP and RCP are the eigen-polarization states of the metasurface. In such cases, Eq. (100) simplifies to

$$
\mathrm{CD} = \frac {\tau_ {+ +} - \tau_ {- -}}{\tau_ {+ +} + \tau_ {- -}}. \tag {101}
$$

Interestingly, Eq. (100) also reveals that a large CD can be achieved through polarization conversion, specifically when there is a significant difference between the cross-polarized components $\tau_{+-}$ and $\tau_{-+}$ . This phenomenon is referred to as false chirality.

Realizing true and false chirality using metasurfaces: a Jones matrix perspective. To identify the symmetry conditions required for realizing true and false chirality in metasurfaces, we analyze their Jones matrices.

For a general metasurface, as expressed in Eq. (7), the Jones matrix takes the form

$$
\mathbf {T} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} A & C \\ D & B \end{array} \right] = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} a _ {1} + i a _ {2} & c _ {1} + i c _ {2} \\ d _ {1} + i d _ {2} & b _ {1} + i b _ {2} \end{array} \right], \tag {102}
$$

where $a_{1}, a_{2}, b_{1}, b_{2}, c_{1}, c_{2}, d_{1}$ , and $d_{2}$ are real-valued coefficients.

Since our analysis focuses on circularly polarized light, we express T in the circular polarization basis:

$$
\mathbf {T} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} T _ {+ +} & T _ {+ -} \\ T _ {- +} & T _ {- -} \end{array} \right] = \frac {1}{2} \left[ \begin{array}{l l} A + B + i (C - D) & A - B - i (C + D) \\ A - B + i (C + D) & A + B - i (C - D) \end{array} \right]. \tag {103}
$$

(I) True chirality arises when the cross-polarized components vanish, i.e.,

$$
A - B - i (C + D) = 0, \quad A - B + i (C + D) = 0. \tag {104}
$$

Solving these equations yields the following conditions:

$$
A - B = 0, \quad C + D = 0. \tag {105}
$$

According to Eq. (100), the CD is determined by the difference between $\tau_{+}$ and $\tau_{-}$ . Using Eqs. (103) and (105), we obtain

$$
\tau_ {+ +} - \tau_ {- -} = \left| T _ {+ +} \right| ^ {2} - \left| T _ {- -} \right| ^ {2} = 2 \left(a _ {2} c _ {1} - a _ {1} c _ {2}\right). \tag {106}
$$

This result shows that achieving nonzero CD requires

$$
A \neq 0, \quad C \neq 0. \tag {107}
$$

Equations (105) and (107) together define the fundamental criteria for realizing true chirality in metasurfaces. Based on these, we can further identify the required symmetry conditions:

(1) From Eq. (10), Eq. (105) implies that the metasurface must possess $C_N$ rotational symmetry about the $z$ -axis, where $N \geq 3$ .  
(2) From Eqs. (8) and (9), Eq. (107) further requires that the metasurface must break mirror symmetry along all spatial directions.

(II) False chirality. When Eq. (105) no longer holds, the effect of false chirality may appear. Now we consider a 2D metasurface, which usually preserves out-of-plane mirror symmetry. Our discussion below demonstrates that although such a metasurface violates the symmetry requirements for true chirality, it can still exhibit false chirality when satisfying specific symmetry conditions.

From Eq. (8), a 2D metasurface that preserves out-of-plane mirror symmetry satisfies C = D. The Jones matrix T, expressed in the circular polarization basis, can be rewritten as:

$$
\mathbf {T} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} T _ {+ +} & T _ {+ -} \\ T _ {- +} & T _ {- -} \end{array} \right] = \frac {1}{2} \left[ \begin{array}{c c} A + B & A - B - 2 i C \\ A - B + 2 i C & A + B \end{array} \right]. \tag {108}
$$

Using Eq. (108), we obtain

$$
\tau_ {+} - \tau_ {-} = \left(\left| T _ {+ +} \right| ^ {2} + \left| T _ {- +} \right| ^ {2}\right) - \left(\left| T _ {- -} \right| ^ {2} + \left| T _ {+ -} \right| ^ {2}\right) = 2 \left(c _ {1} \left(a _ {2} - b _ {2}\right) - c _ {2} \left(a _ {1} - b _ {1}\right)\right). \tag {109}
$$

For the circular dichroism to be nonzero, the Jones matrix T must satisfy

$$
c _ {1} \left(a _ {2} - b _ {2}\right) \neq c _ {2} \left(a _ {1} - b _ {1}\right), \tag {110}
$$

which implies the following conditions:

$$
A \neq B, \tag {111}
$$

and

$$
C \neq 0. \tag {112}
$$

Equations (111) and (112) thus represent the fundamental criteria for realizing false chirality in a 2D metasurface. Based on these, the corresponding symmetry conditions are as follows:

(1) From Eq. (10), Eq. (111) requires that the metasurface exhibit only $C_1$ or $C_2$ rotational symmetry.  
(2) From Eq. (9), Eq. (112) requires that the metasurface break in-plane mirror symmetry, i.e., it must not exhibit mirror symmetry with respect to the $xOz$ or $yOz$ planes.

It is important to note that the observed chirality in practical metasurfaces may be a hybrid of both true and false chirality contributions.

In the following, we categorize chiral metasurfaces into two distinct classes: (i) 3D chiral metasurfaces, which break both in-plane and out-of-plane mirror symmetries; and (ii) 2D chiral metasurfaces, which typically preserve out-of-plane mirror symmetry under normal incidence. Compared with previous classification approaches $[274, 276–279]$ , this division provides a clearer and more physically intuitive framework for understanding the origin and mechanisms of chiroptical responses in metasurfaces.

3D chiral metasurfaces breaking both in-plane and out-of-plane mirror symmetries. To achieve true chirality, metasurfaces must break mirror symmetries along all spatial directions. This necessitates the use of 3D metasurfaces, which are typically realized through specialized fabrication techniques or multilayer architectures that explicitly break out-of-plane mirror symmetry.

(I) Low-Q-factor chiral 3D metasurfaces. In recent years, numerous metal and dielectric 3D chiral metasurfaces have been experimentally demonstrated. Representative examples include the bilayer Au nanodisk array shown in Fig. 12(a) [280]; the Ag nanodisk array with four distinct nanodisk heights, fabricated via facile hole-mask colloidal lithography, shown in Fig. 12(b) [281]; and the hexagonally arranged array of Au:Cu (80:20) nanohelices produced using nanoglancing angle deposition, shown in Fig. 12(c) [282].

A record-high CD of 0.7 and an OR of $2.67 \times 10^{5}$ deg/mm were achieved by Tanaka et al. in 2020 using a bilayer dielectric metasurface [193]. As depicted in the left panel of Fig. 12(d), the metasurface unit cell exhibits $C_{4}$ rotational symmetry, which suppresses polarization conversion between LCP and RCP light. To break the out-of-plane mirror symmetry, the two dielectric layers are rotated relative to each other about the z-axis by a fixed angle of $60^{\circ}$ . As shown in the right panel of Fig. 12(d), negligible polarization conversion is observed in transmission, and a maximum CD of 0.7 is achieved. This strong chiral response is attributed to an MD resonance located at a wavelength of 1.49 $\mu$ m, which couples selectively to RCP light. As a result, strong absorption occurs under RCP illumination, while the LCP component remains largely unaffected.

(II) Chiral quasi-BIC. In addition to chirality, another critical parameter for enhancing chiral light–matter interactions is the Q-factor of the associated optical resonance. High-Q chiral metasurfaces can be realized by exploiting chiral quasi-BICs.

To maximize optical chirality while achieving a quasi-symmetry-protected BIC, both in-plane and out-of-plane mirror symmetries must be broken. This not only enables true chirality but also transforms a BIC into a quasi-BIC that couples selectively to one circular polarization state while remaining decoupled from the other—thus forming a chiral quasi-BIC. In this manner, a chiral quasi-BIC metasurface operating in transmission mode enables selective transmission of one circular polarization, while the opposite handedness is resonantly reflected. This results in near-unity circular dichroism in transmission within a narrow spectral bandwidth $[283]$ .

Recently, a variety of 3D chiral quasi-BIC metasurface designs have been proposed and experimentally demonstrated $[283,285–290]$ , marking significant progress toward the realization of high-Q-factor chiral photonic platforms.

(i) Breaking the out-of-plane symmetry through meta-atoms with different heights.

In 2020, Gorkunov et al. [283] proposed a chiral quasi-symmetry-protected BIC metasurface, as illustrated in Fig. 12(e). The metasurface unit cell consists of four dimers

Figure 12  
![](images/23d6259e7a5fecf4139f03bf4767944e73a94ce84b7257f2843201045a236c34.jpg)

3D chiral metasurfaces breaking mirror symmetries in all directions. (a) Tilted view of SEM of chiral Au molecules. (b) SEM of chiral Ag molecules. The varying shades of gray correspond to nanodisks with heights of 40 nm (brightest), 30 nm, 20 nm, and 10 nm (darkest). (c) Side-on SEM of the chiral metasurface composed of nanohelix meta-atoms. (d) Left: schematic of the bilayer chiral metasurface unit with $C_{4}$ rotational symmetry. Right: 0th-order transmittance spectra of the bilayer chiral metasurface for circularly polarized illumination. (e) Schematic of a metasurface unit hosting chiral quasi-symmetry-protected BIC. Inset: schematic of the meta-atom dimer vertically offset by d and rotated by $\theta$ . (f) Measured spectra of LCP/RCP co- and cross-polarized transmittance of a chiral quasi-BIC metasurface. Inset: Schematic of the metasurface unit cell. (g) 2D and 3D schematics of a metasurface unit hosting chiral quasi-symmetry-protected BIC. $H^{0}$ , $V_{1}$ , and $V_{2}$ represent the unperturbed metasurface unit and the perturbed bottom and top layer metasurface units, respectively. (h) Top-view and side-view SEMs of a chiral quasi-symmetry-protected BIC metasurface. (i) Top: schematic of the slant-perturbation metasurface to realize chiral quasi-symmetry-protected BICs. The black lines represent linear polarizations, and elliptical polarizations are depicted by ellipses, with red and blue colors corresponding to right- and left-handed states, respectively. Bottom: measured spectra of LCP/RCP co- and cross-polarized reflectance of the 200 $\mu$ m metasurface sample. (continued) (b) Adapted with permission from Ogier et al., ACS Photonics 1, 1074–1081 (2014) [281]. Copyright © 2014 American Chemical Society. (c) Reprinted from [282] under a Creative Commons license. (d) Adapted with permission from Tanaka et al., ACS Nano 14, 15926–15935 (2020) [193]. Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society. (e) Adapted with permission from Gorkunov et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 125, 093903 (2020) [283]. Copyright © 2020 American Physical Society. (f) Adapted from [284] under a Creative Commons license. (g) Adapted with permission from Overvig et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 126, 073001 (2021) [285]. Copyright © 2021 American Physical Society. (h) Adapted with permission from Zhang et al., Science 377, 1215–1218 (2022) [286]. Copyright © 2022 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (i) Adapted with permission from Chen et al., Nature 613, 474–478 (2023) [287]. Copyright © 2023 Nature Springer.

of elliptical meta-atoms, which are vertically offset by a distance d and rotated by an angle $\theta$ . The overall structure exhibits $C_{4}$ rotational symmetry, allowing it to support LCP and RCP as eigen-polarization states. All mirror symmetries are broken through the combination of d and $\theta$ , and these two parameters are carefully optimized to ensure that the resulting quasi-BIC couples exclusively to one circular polarization state. The experimental realization of this design in the microwave regime was subsequently demonstrated in 2021 [288].

In the optical regime, chiral quasi-BIC metasurfaces composed of meta-atoms with varying heights can be fabricated using grayscale lithography $[289]$ or a two-step fabrication process $[284]$ .

In 2023, Kühner et al. [284] experimentally demonstrated a similar chiral quasi-symmetry-protected BIC metasurface, with a transmittance difference $(\tau_{+}-\tau_{-})$ reaching 0.7 and a Q-factor exceeding 100. As shown in the inset of Fig. 12(f), compared with the structure in Fig. 12(e), this metasurface discards the $C_{4}$ symmetry to simplify the structure, which reduces the true chirality and induces a small false chiral response at the resonant wavelength, as depicted in Fig. 12(f).

(ii) Breaking the out-of-plane symmetry through bilayer structures. Evolved from the quasi-BIC metasurface depicted in Figs. 10(c) and 10(d), in 2021, Overvig et al. [285] proposed a bilayer metasurface supporting a chiral quasi-symmetry-protected BIC, as shown in Fig. 12(g). In this design, the bottom and top layers are engineered to couple to linearly polarized light oriented at angles of approximately $2\alpha$ and $2(\alpha + \Delta\alpha)$ , respectively. By tuning the parameters $\alpha$ and $\Delta\alpha$ , the eigen-polarizations of the resulting quasi-BIC can be continuously controlled, enabling the realization of arbitrary elliptical dichroism.

(iii) Breaking the out-of-plane symmetry through slant meta-atoms. In 2022, Zhang et al. [286] experimentally demonstrated chiral emission and lasing using a tilted $TiO_{2}$ metasurface operating at optical frequencies, as shown in Fig. 12(h). This structure closely resembles those shown in Figs. 10(c) and 10(d), but it features a tilting angle introduced via slanted reactive ion etching, which effectively breaks the out-of-plane mirror symmetry.

In 2023, Chen et al. [287] achieved a chiral quasi-BIC with a CD of 0.93 and a Q-factor exceeding 2663 in the visible spectrum. As shown in the top panel of Fig. 12(i), the metasurface consists of a square hole array incorporating both an in-plane deformation angle $\alpha_{0}$ and an out-of-plane slant angle $\phi_{0}$ . The evolution of the eigen-polarization states in momentum space as a function of the geometric perturbation parameters $\alpha_{0}$ and $\phi_{0}$ is illustrated in the middle panel of Fig. 12(i). When a nonzero in-plane de-

formation angle $\alpha_{0}$ is introduced, the original integer-charged V point at the $\Gamma$ point splits into a pair of half-charged C points, denoted $C_{+}$ and $C_{-}$ , symmetrically positioned on either side of the $\Gamma$ point. Upon introducing a nonzero out-of-plane slant angle $\phi_{0}$ , one of the C points can be shifted back to the $\Gamma$ point, thereby forming a chiral quasi-symmetry-protected BIC. As shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 12(i), the cross-polarization reflectance remains zero, indicating that the strong chiral response originates from the true chirality of the metasurface.

2D chiral metasurfaces. 2D planar metasurfaces that preserve out-of-plane mirror symmetry typically do not exhibit true structural chirality. However, they can still produce a strong CD response through false chirality arising from anisotropy-induced polarization conversion. In addition, out-of-plane symmetry can be broken either by oblique incidence or by deliberately designed 2D metasurfaces based on special meta-atom structures or guided mode resonances. These scenarios suggest that true chirality is also achievable in 2D metasurfaces under specific conditions.

(I) False chiral 2D metasurfaces based on anisotropy-induced polarization conversion. Recently, a variety of anisotropic metasurfaces that satisfy the symmetry conditions given by Eqs. (111) and (112) have been proposed to realize false chirality $[291–295]$ . For example, in 2022, Shi et al. $[293]$ experimentally demonstrated a quasi-symmetry-protected BIC-induced false chiral metasurface, achieving a Q-factor of 121 and a CD of 0.93 at optical frequencies. As shown in the left panel of Fig. 13(a), the BIC is converted into a quasi-BIC by introducing an asymmetric structural perturbation $\delta$ . As shown in the right panel of Fig. 13(a), the quasi-BIC induces strong polarization conversion from RCP to LCP light in transmission, resulting in a significant increase in $\tau_{-}$ and, consequently, an enhanced CD.

It is worth noting that, in addition to structural perturbation $\delta$ , quasi-BICs can also be realized in an unperturbed metasurface under oblique incidence. In such cases, the observed large CD arises primarily from polarization conversion and therefore still falls under the category of false chirality.

(II) True chirality of 2D metasurfaces breaking the out-of-plane symmetry. Some special meta-atom structures that preserve out-of-plane mirror symmetry can implicitly overcome the symmetry constraints imposed by the Jones matrix form of Eq. (8), such as the gammadion meta-atoms [296]. In 2018, Zhu et al. [296] experimentally demonstrated a true chiral metasurface exhibiting a CD of approximately $90\%$ at a wavelength of $540~\mathrm{nm}$ , as shown in Fig. 13(b). The gammadion meta-atom, depicted in the inset of Fig. 13(b), possesses $C_4$ rotational symmetry, ensuring the absence of polarization conversion. Notably, it follows the Jones matrix structure described in Eq. (10) with $C \neq 0$ . The true chiral response originates from electromagnetic modes excited purely within the meta-atom itself and can be further enhanced by incorporating a waveguiding layer. Due to the intrinsic chirality of the gammadion meta-atom, the effective refractive indices for LCP and RCP incidence differ, allowing only the desired circular polarization to be efficiently coupled into the guided mode resonance, thereby enhancing the chiral response of the structure.

For guided-mode resonance metasurfaces, out-of-plane symmetry can also be broken by the refractive index contrast between the substrate and the superstrate. In 2025, Li et al. [297] experimentally demonstrated a guided mode resonance metagrating with a Q-factor of 183 and a CD of 0.62. As shown in Fig. 13(c), the strong CD primarily arises from the difference in transmittance between the LCP → LCP and RCP → RCP components, rather than from false chirality induced by polarization conversion.

Figure 13  
![](images/2990a6579f9ba46f536895d820ab3b68213a60b2b528f2d5f8fc11b19460bc17.jpg)

2D chiral metasurfaces. (a) Left: schematic of the chiral meta-atom supporting quasi-symmetry-protected BICs. Middle: measured spectra of the transmission Jones matrix elements for five chiral metasurface samples with varying $\delta$ . Right: corresponding transmission CD spectra for the same five samples. Here, $CD = \tau_{-} - \tau_{+}$ . (b) Experimental 0th-order transmittance spectra of the chiral metasurface under RCP (red) and LCP (blue) illuminations. Inset: schematic of the metasurface unit. Here, $\Lambda = 500$ nm, $h_{s} = 340$ nm, and $h_{wg} = 300$ nm. (c) Measured spectra of the transmission Jones matrix elements for different values of $\delta_{d}$ . Inset: schematic and SEMs of the metasurface units. Here, $\delta_{d} = P_{x}/2 - d$ , $P_{x} = 950$ nm, $P_{y} = 635$ nm, $t_{1} = 215$ nm, and $t_{2} = 275$ nm. (d) Top: schematic of a chiral MIM metasurface composed of Au meta-atoms with a diameter of 350 nm and a height of 25 nm. Here, $\theta_{i}$ denotes the incident angle, and $\phi_{i}$ is the in-plane rotation angle in the xOy plane. The periods along the x- and y-directions are 760 nm and 420 nm, respectively. Bottom: measured reflectance spectra of the metasurface under both LCP and RCP incidences with $\theta_{i} = 45^{\circ}$ and $\phi_{i} = 70^{\circ}$ . (a) Adapted from [293] under a Creative Commons license. (b) Adapted from [296] under a Creative Commons license. (c) Adapted with permission from Li et al., Nano Lett. 25, 2519–2527 (2025) [297]. Copyright © 2025 American Chemical Society. (d) Adapted with permission from Mao et al., ACS Photonics 7, 375–383 (2020) [298]. Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society.

(III) True chirality of 2D metasurfaces under oblique incidence. The out-of-plane symmetry can be broken by obliquely incident light, providing a tunable approach for realizing true chiral optical responses using 2D metasurfaces.

In 2020, Mao et al. [298] experimentally demonstrated a chiral metasurface mirror depicted in the top panel of Fig. 13(d), which achieved a maximum CD in reflectance of 0.43 in the near-infrared region. As depicted in the bottom panel of Fig. 13(d), the pronounced chiral response arises under oblique incidence at an appropriate angle, and the observed chirality primarily originates from the difference in reflectance between the LCP → LCP and RCP → RCP components, indicating that the response is due to true chirality. Furthermore, the experiment also confirmed continuously tunable CD by varying the azimuthal angle $\phi_{i}$ at a fixed incidence angle $\theta_{i}$ .

# 2.2.6. Exceptional Points in Non-Hermitian Metasurfaces

In a broad sense, non-Hermitian systems refer to all open systems that exchange energy, matter, or information with their environment, where physical quantities can be described by non-Hermitian operators $[299–301]$ . Traditional quantum mechanics requires the Hamiltonian to be Hermitian to ensure real eigenvalues. However, although non-Hermitian systems typically possess complex eigenvalues and non-orthogonal eigenstates, a non-Hermitian Hamiltonian that satisfies parity-time (PT) symmetry can, under certain conditions, cause two or more eigenvalues to coalesce into real values, with their corresponding eigenstates also degenerate. This phenomenon is known as exceptional points.

Because the wave equations governing classical optical waves resemble the form of the Schrödinger equation, optical systems have become a crucial platform for studying non-Hermitian physics $[302]$ . Non-Hermitian optics not only provides a classical analogy for the behavior of open quantum systems but also offers new insights into the role of gain and loss in optical systems. This perspective has led to the discovery of a variety of novel and intriguing physical effects $[303–305]$ .

As a novel optical element for the flexible and efficient manipulation of optical fields, metasurfaces—inherently non-Hermitian systems—have become an ideal platform for studying non-Hermitian optics $[258]$ . The optical properties of metasurfaces can be characterized using response matrices such as the Jones matrix and the scattering matrix. For a deliberately designed metasurface, both the eigenvalues and eigenvectors of a specific response matrix (e.g., the Jones matrix) coalesce, corresponding to a particular type of exceptional point. In the following, we provide a detailed discussion of four typical types of exceptional points in metasurfaces and their corresponding electromagnetic response characteristics. Additionally, the exceptional point where two zeros coincide in complex frequency analysis will be introduced in Subsection 2.2.8.

Exceptional points of the Jones matrix and topological phase. For a single-layer meta-atom with a Jones matrix J given by Eq. (8), let its eigenvalues and eigenvectors (i.e., eigen-polarization states) be denoted as $w_{1,2}$ and $\vec{p}_{1,2}$ , respectively, satisfying

$$
\mathbf {J} \vec {p} _ {1, 2} = w _ {1, 2} \vec {p} _ {1, 2}. \tag {113}
$$

Using linear algebra, the eigenvalues can be expressed as

$$
w _ {1, 2} = \frac {1}{2} (A + B \pm \sqrt {(A - B) ^ {2} + 4 C ^ {2}}). \tag {114}
$$

The degeneracy condition for the formation of an exceptional point is $w_{1} = w_{2}$ , which leads to

$$
(A - B) ^ {2} + 4 C ^ {2} = 0, \tag {115}
$$

i.e.,

$$
A - B + 2 i C = 0, \tag {116}
$$

or

$$
A - B - 2 i C = 0. \tag {117}
$$

For the first degeneracy condition, the eigen-polarization states satisfy

$$
\vec {p} _ {1} = \vec {p} _ {2} = \frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ i \end{array} \right], \tag {118}
$$

indicating that the degenerate eigen-polarization state is LCP. For the second degeneracy condition, the eigen-polarization states satisfy

$$
\vec {p} _ {1} = \vec {p} _ {2} = \frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ - i \end{array} \right], \tag {119}
$$

indicating that the degenerate eigen-polarization state is RCP. By defining $\sigma = \pm1$ to represent the cases where the degenerate eigen-polarization states are LCP and RCP, respectively, the degeneracy conditions above can be unified as

$$
A - B + 2 i \sigma C = 0. \tag {120}
$$

For a meta-atom whose eigen-polarization states degenerate to LCP (RCP), the output light remains LCP (RCP) under LCP (RCP) incidence, ensuring that polarization conversion is forbidden. By applying mirror symmetry to the meta-atom geometry, the handedness of the exceptional point eigenmode can be flipped between LCP and RCP [306].

The degeneracy condition can also be directly derived from Eq. (24). From Eq. (24), setting the coefficient of the output component with the opposite circular polarization to the incident light to zero (in which case the circular polarization of the incidence is the degenerated eigen-polarization state), we once again obtain the unified degeneracy condition in Eq. (120).

In 2014, Lawrence et al. [307] experimentally demonstrated a degenerated circularly polarized state in a terahertz metasurface. In 2020, Park et al. [308] observed the same phenomenon using a different terahertz metasurface design. In 2021, Song et al. [133] proposed achieving such polarization exceptional points in the visible using the MIM metasurface unit shown in Fig. 14(a). By performing full-wave simulations to compute the Jones matrix of the meta-atom, the eigenvalues and eigenvectors were further obtained. As shown in Figs. 14(b) and 14(c), by sweeping the geometric parameters $L_{1}$ and $L_{3}$ of the meta-atom, the exceptional point where both the eigenvalues and eigenvectors coalesce can be identified. As shown in Fig. 14(d), for a meta-atom at an exceptional point, the two eigen-polarization states degenerate into RCP at a specific wavelength, indicating that this exceptional point is an RCP exceptional point. Figures 14(e) and 14(f) illustrate that for an RCP-degenerate meta-atom, polarization conversion from RCP to LCP is prohibited, i.e., $r_{+-} = 0$ , where the subscript $+ -$ represents the conversion from RCP ( $\sigma = -1$ ) to LCP ( $\sigma = +1$ ). Based on this, when an RCP-degenerate meta-atom is rotated (in practice, the rotated meta-atom only approximately satisfies the exceptional point condition due to structural modifications, so $r_{+-}$ is not strictly zero), the introduced geometric phase only operates for RCP output under LCP incidence but not for LCP output under RCP incidence. This property can be applied to vectorial metasurface holography [306], color holographic displays without the issue of twin images projected by conventional geometric phase metasurfaces [309], etc.

Figure 14  
![](images/f43c242b0ba3bbe0e96bc10b6b41f62548332930dac9ed62dac32b59add65d73.jpg)

Exceptional points of the Jones matrix. (a) Perspective and top view of the MIM meta-atom. The dimensions are $L_{2}=140$ nm, $P=300$ nm, w=50 nm, g=70 nm, h=30 nm, $h_{1}=40$ nm, and $h_{2}=150$ nm. (b) and (c) Simulated amplitude and phase of two eigenvalues as functions of $L_{1}$ and $L_{3}$ at a wavelength of 600 nm. The exceptional point is indicated with the black dot. (d) Position of the eigen-polarization states on the Poincaré sphere as a function of the wavelength, which degenerate to RCP at 600 nm. (e) and (f) Simulated polarization conversion coefficients $r_{+-}$ and $r_{-+}$ in the parameter space $(L_{1}, L_{3})$ . (g) Topological analysis for the phase accumulation by encircling a polarization exceptional point. Left: meta-atom parameter space $(L_{1}, L_{3})$ . Right: complex plane of the polarization conversion coefficient $r_{+-}$ . Adapted with permission from Song et al., Science 373, 1133–1137 (2021) [133]. Copyright © 2021 American Association for the Advancement of Science.

From the $r_{+-}$ distribution shown in Fig. 14(e), we observe that for any closed path encircling the exceptional point where $r_{+-}=0$ in the parameter space $(L_{1},L_{3})$ , the reflected light exhibits a $2\pi$ phase accumulation, which remains independent of the specific closed path taken. This phenomenon is topologically protected, and thus, this phase modulation mechanism is referred to as the topological phase.

As illustrated in the top panel of Fig. 14(g), when a closed path in the parameter space crosses the exceptional point, its corresponding trajectory in the complex plane of $r_{+-}$ must pass through the origin. In the bottom panel of Fig. 14(g), for a blue closed path encircling the exceptional point in parameter space, its corresponding trajectory in the complex plane of $r_{+-}$ can either enclose (solid red line) or not enclose (red dashed line) the origin. In the former case, $r_{+-}$ accumulates a phase of $2\pi$ along the closed path, while in the latter case, the phase accumulation is less than $2\pi$ . To ensure the occurrence of the former case, a suitable parameter space must be chosen, such as $(L_{1}, L_{3})$ rather than $(L_{1}, L_{2})$ in this case. This $2\pi$ phase accumulation is robust because it is topologically protected. Specifically, as shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 14(g), assuming the blue closed path encircling the exceptional point exhibits a $2\pi$ phase accumulation, this accumulation must persist even if the path is continuously deformed into the green path—unless the green path in the parameter space no longer encircles the exceptional point or simultaneously encloses additional exceptional points.

The topological phase enables complete phase modulation by selecting meta-atoms of different sizes around the exceptional point, a property similar to that of resonant phase. In fact, resonant phase also has a topological origin, making it fundamentally a type of topological phase, which we will further discuss in Subsection 2.2.8. Both topological

Figure 15  
![](images/b5577c01cd33f3461dab66c2f12f54488be24718fab723a5a8b6304378f90f4a.jpg)

Exceptional points of the scattering matrix. (a) Schematic of the metagrating exhibiting asymmetric reflections at the exceptional point. (b) Measured far-field scattered light from all angles with incident angles of $30^{\circ}$ and $-30^{\circ}$ at a wavelength of 532 nm. Inset: schematic of the bilayer metagrating. (a) Adapted with permission from Wang et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 123, 214302 (2019) [310]. Copyright © 2019 American Physical Society. (b) Adapted from [311] a Creative Commons license.

and geometric phases operate under circularly polarized incidence and output. However, as shown in Figs. 14(e) and 14(f), the topological phase only modulates the phase in one of the two conversion scenarios, either LCP → RCP or RCP → LCP, depending on whether the exceptional point is an LCP or RCP exceptional point. In contrast, the geometric phase operates in both conversion scenarios. Furthermore, the topological phase can be combined with the geometric phase to realize the hybrid phase modulation mechanism, which can be applied to polarization-multiplexed metasurface holography [133] and other applications.

Exceptional points of the scattering matrix. In order to introduce the exceptional point of the scattering matrix, a two-port metagrating must first be constructed. From Eq. (68), when the phase gradient of the metagrating satisfies

$$
\frac {\mathrm{d} \phi}{\mathrm{d} x} = \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda}, \tag {121}
$$

and $n_{\mathrm{i}} = 1$ , the metagrating equation simplifies to

$$
\frac {2 \pi}{\lambda_ {0}} \left(\sin \theta_ {\mathrm{r}} - \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{i}}\right) = (M + 1) \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda}. \tag {122}
$$

To form a two-port system, only two diffraction orders can propagate while others are evanescent. As shown in Fig. 15(a), without loss of generality, the angle of incidence is set as $\theta_{\mathrm{i}} = \pm \pi /4$ for the left- or right-side incidence, and $\Lambda = \lambda_0 / \sqrt{2}$ . In this way, Eq. (122) can be simplified to

$$
\sin \theta_ {\mathrm{r}} - \sin \theta_ {\mathrm{i}} = (M + 1) \sqrt {2}. \tag {123}
$$

Using Eq. (123), for the left-side incidence $(\theta_{\mathrm{i}} = +\pi/4)$ , only the diffraction orders of M = -1 and M = -2 produce propagating waves, corresponding to right-side output $(\theta_{\mathrm{r}} = +\pi/4)$ with reflection coefficient $r_{RL}$ and left-side output $(\theta_{\mathrm{r}} = -\pi/4)$ with $r_{LL}$ , respectively. For the right-side incidence $(\theta_{\mathrm{i}} = -\pi/4)$ , only the diffraction orders of M = 0 and M = -1 give rise to propagating waves, corresponding to right-side output $(\theta_{\mathrm{r}} = +\pi/4)$ with $r_{RR}$ and left-side output $(\theta_{\mathrm{r}} = -\pi/4)$ with $r_{RL}$ , respectively.

Such a two-port metagrating can be described by a $2 \times 2$ scattering matrix,

$$
\mathbf {S} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} r _ {\mathrm{RL}} & r _ {\mathrm{RR}} \\ r _ {\mathrm{LL}} & r _ {\mathrm{LR}} \end{array} \right], \tag {124}
$$

which satisfies

$$
\left[ \begin{array}{l} A _ {\mathrm{R}} ^ {\mathrm{o}} \\ A _ {\mathrm{L}} ^ {\mathrm{o}} \end{array} \right] = \mathbf {S} \left[ \begin{array}{l} A _ {\mathrm{L}} ^ {\mathrm{i}} \\ A _ {\mathrm{R}} ^ {\mathrm{i}} \end{array} \right], \tag {125}
$$

where $\left[A_{L}^{i}\quad A_{R}^{i}\right]^{\top}$ represents the complex amplitudes of the input of the left and right ports, and $\left[A_{R}^{o}\quad A_{L}^{o}\right]^{\top}$ represents the complex amplitudes of the output of the right and left ports. Due to the reciprocity, we have

$$
r _ {\mathrm{RL}} = r _ {\mathrm{LR}}. \tag {126}
$$

The eigenvalues of Eq. (124) under the condition of Eq. (126) are given by

$$
w _ {1, 2} = r _ {\mathrm{RL}} \pm \sqrt {r _ {\mathrm{RR}} r _ {\mathrm{LL}}}. \tag {127}
$$

Therefore, the condition for the exceptional point is

$$
r _ {\mathrm{RR}} = 0, \tag {128}
$$

or

$$
r _ {\mathrm{LL}} = 0. \tag {129}
$$

At the exceptional point, extremely asymmetric reflection can be achieved. Specifically, in the first case of Eq. (128), the right-side diffracted light of the metagrating under right-side incidence is completely suppressed, while the left-side diffracted light under left-side incidence is observable in the far field. In the second case of Eq. (129), the left-side diffracted light under left-side incidence is completely suppressed, while the right-side diffracted light under right-side incidence is observable.

In 2019, Wang et al. [310] experimentally demonstrated the scattering exceptional point using an acoustic metasurface. In 2020, Dong et al. [312] achieved a similar demonstration with a metasurface composed of a judiciously tailored tri-meta-atom supercell operating at a frequency of 11.8 GHz. In 2023, He et al. [311] optimized and fabricated a bilayer metagrating in the visible (operating at $\theta = \pm \pi / 6$ ), exhibiting highly efficient asymmetric reflection at the exceptional point $r_{RR} = 0$ , as shown in Fig. 15(b).

Resonance exceptional points. A resonance exceptional point can form when two (or more) resonant modes of the metasurface become degenerate, meaning their resonance frequencies and loss rates simultaneously coincide.

In 2020, Park et al. [313] experimentally demonstrated the existence of resonance exceptional points using a bilayer metasurface, as shown in Fig. 16(a). The metasurface consists of two layers of identical Au meta-atom arrays, where the bottom-layer meta-atoms are embedded in a dielectric slab with a refractive index of 1.5. As shown in Fig. 16(b), $P_{x}$ is fixed at 400 nm, while $P_{y}$ and $d_{x}$ are adjusted to reach an exceptional point where both the resonance frequencies and loss rates become simultaneously degenerate. Around the exceptional point, the metasurface exhibits resonance splitting proportional to the square root of structural perturbations, which serves as the basis for its sensing capability. Experimentally, the application of the metasurface at the exceptional point as a sensor for anti-immunoglobulin G, the most abundant immunoglobulin isotype in human serum, was demonstrated.

Figure 16  
![](images/f6765c2983fd5f433ff2a8537862c0fbd961f9ccbf29de6e56108aea398a9688.jpg)

Resonance exceptional points. (a) Left: top-view SEM of the fabricated bilayer metasurface with $d_{x}=100$ nm. Right: magnified top (xy plane) and side (xz plane) views, showing the top and bottom Au meta-atoms. Here, $P_{x}$ and $P_{y}$ denote the period of the meta-atom array along the x- and y-directions, respectively, while $d_{x}$ represents the lateral shift between the top and bottom layers. (b) Simulated real and imaginary parts of the two eigenmodes (red and blue) of the bilayer metasurface in (a) as functions of $d_{x}$ and $P_{y}$ . (c) Simulated real and imaginary parts of the two eigenmodes (red and blue) of a modified metasurface with an air spacer, varying $d_{x}$ and $P_{y}$ . Adapted with permission from Park et al., Nat. Phys. 16, 462–468 (2020) [313]. Copyright © 2020 Springer Nature.

In the former configuration, breaking the mirror symmetry along the z-direction via the dielectric spacer is essential for the formation of an exceptional point. In contrast, replacing the spacer material of the metasurface in Fig. 16(a) with air restores mirror symmetry along the z-direction, causing the two resonant modes to be purely symmetric and anti-symmetric. As illustrated in Fig. 16(c), their loss rates then differ significantly and remain distinct, preventing the emergence of an exceptional point.

Figure 17  
![](images/3a34835d1778acf8ced9160e06ca39e9cb642aefee34e8d694279431acda7b97.jpg)  
SPP exceptional points. (a) Schematic of the metagrating for unidirectional excitation of SPPs. (b) Real space and Fourier space images of SPPs for metagratings with different values of s, characterized by leakage radiation microscopy. Scale bar, 10 $\mu$ m. Adapted from Xu et al., Sci. Adv. 9, eadf3510 (2023) [314], under a Creative Commons license.

Only when the mirror symmetry along the z-direction is broken, the resonant modes deviate from purely symmetric or anti-symmetric states, enabling interference via radiation and allowing exceptional points to emerge.

SPP exceptional points. In 2023, Xu et al. [314] demonstrated the unidirectional excitation of SPPs using non-Hermitian metagratings. As shown in Fig. 17(a), the metagrating consists of nine unit cells, and the excitation of SPPs can be modulated by varying the separation distance s. By carefully designing the geometric parameters of the unit cell, the SPP eigenstates of the system can coalesce at an exceptional point, leading to the unidirectional SPP mode propagating in the +x or -x direction. Experimentally, as shown in Fig. 17(b), continuous tuning of SPP excitation was achieved by adjusting the geometric parameter s of the metagrating. This controllable directionality and robust performance are attributed to the phase transition near the exceptional point.

# 2.2.7. Beyond Spatial Modulation: Spatiotemporal Metasurfaces

Traditional metasurfaces introduce spatially varying amplitude and phase modulation to achieve arbitrary wavefront manipulation, which is applied in beam steering, imaging, holography, and other applications. However, one may naturally wonder if it is possible to introduce control in other dimensions. Spatiotemporal metasurfaces extend the spatial modulation by additionally incorporating temporal modulation of the optical field $[315–318]$ . This not only means (I) that traditional static metasurfaces are upgraded to have actively tunable functionalities (such as variable-focus metalenses), but also (II) that it opens new directions for the study of light–matter interactions, bringing forth a series of previously inaccessible exotic physical effects and optical devices with novel functionalities $[319–321]$ .

In Section 8, we will introduce the physical realization of spatiotemporal metasurfaces—various types of active metasurfaces based on different principles—and their research progress in the aforementioned direction (I), a direction that has been progressively refined in current studies. Here, we will focus on two representative physical effects of spatiotemporal metasurfaces in the nascent direction (II), presenting their theoretical foundations and numerical/experimental validations.

Frequency conversion using spatiotemporal metasurfaces. The frequency conversion effect of time-varying materials has been recognized for quite some time. For example, in 2014, Xiao et al. [322] demonstrated that an electromagnetic wave encountering a temporal boundary—where the refractive index of a homogeneous material is instantaneously changed from $n_{1}$ to $n_{2}$ —undergoes a shift in its carrier frequency. In the early stages of metasurface research, researchers had already noticed frequency conversion effects resulting from temporal modulation of metasurfaces [323], and various designs of spatiotemporal metasurfaces for frequency conversion were subsequently proposed [324–326].

(I) Metasurfaces with temporal phase modulation. The electric field vector of a time-harmonic monochromatic plane wave can be expressed as

$$
\vec {E} = \vec {E} _ {0} e ^ {i \phi (\vec {r}, t)} = \vec {E} _ {0} e ^ {i \left(\vec {k} \cdot \vec {r} - \omega t\right)}, \tag {130}
$$

where $\vec{E}_{0}$ is a constant amplitude vector, $\vec{k}$ is the wavevector, $\omega$ is the angular frequency, and $\vec{r}=x\vec{e}_{x}+y\vec{e}_{y}+z\vec{e}_{z}$ . The spatial and temporal derivatives of $\phi(\vec{r},t)$ yield the wavevector and angular frequency, respectively:

$$
\vec {k} = \vec {\nabla} \phi , \tag {131}
$$

$$
\omega = - \frac {\partial \phi}{\partial t}, \tag {132}
$$

where $\vec{\nabla}_{\mathrm{T}} = \frac{\partial}{\partial x}\vec{e}_x + \frac{\partial}{\partial y}\vec{e}_y + \frac{\partial}{\partial z}\vec{e}_z.$

Consider a wave with an initial phase factor

$$
\phi_ {\mathrm{i}} \left(\vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}, t\right) = \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{i}} \cdot \vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}} - \omega_ {\mathrm{i}} t, \tag {133}
$$

where $\vec{r}_{T}=x\vec{e}_{x}+y\vec{e}_{y}$ , incident on a spatiotemporal metasurface located at z=0. This metasurface imparts a space-time-dependent phase shift $\phi_{\mathrm{m}}\left(\vec{r}_{\mathrm{T}},t\right)$ to the output (transmitted or reflected) wave. The resulting phase distribution of the output wave is given by

$$
\phi_ {\mathrm{o}} \left(\vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}, t\right) = \phi_ {\mathrm{i}} \left(\vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}, t\right) + \phi_ {\mathrm{m}} \left(\vec {r} _ {\mathrm{T}}, t\right). \tag {134}
$$

By applying Eqs. (131) and (132), the transverse wavevector and angular frequency of the output wave can be expressed as

$$
\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{o}, \mathrm{T}} = \vec {\nabla} _ {\mathrm{T}} \phi_ {\mathrm{o}} = \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{i}, \mathrm{T}} + \vec {\nabla} _ {\mathrm{T}} \phi_ {\mathrm{m}}, \tag {135}
$$

$$
\omega_ {\mathrm{o}} = - \frac {\partial \phi_ {\mathrm{o}}}{\partial t} = \omega_ {\mathrm{i}} - \frac {\partial \phi_ {\mathrm{m}}}{\partial t}, \tag {136}
$$

where $\vec{\nabla}_{T}=\frac{\partial}{\partial x}\vec{e}_{x}+\frac{\partial}{\partial y}\vec{e}_{y}$ . Equations (135) and (136) indicate that a spatial phase gradient introduces an abrupt change in the transverse photonic momentum given by

$$
\Delta \vec {p} _ {\mathrm{T}} = \hbar \Delta \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} = \hbar \vec {\nabla} _ {\mathrm{T}} \phi_ {\mathrm{m}}, \tag {137}
$$

while a temporal phase gradient results in a photonic energy change of

$$
\Delta E = \hbar \Delta \omega = - \hbar \frac {\partial \phi_ {\mathrm{m}}}{\partial t}. \tag {138}
$$

It is worth noting that Eq. (135) has been discussed in Subsection 2.1.6, and Eq. (136) highlights the frequency conversion effect induced by the time-varying phase shift of the spatiotemporal metasurface [323].

Illustrations of Eqs. (135) and (136) for a one-dimensional (1D) metasurface operating in reflection mode, with a phase shift distribution $\phi(x,t)$ , are shown in the left two panels of Fig. 18(a). In this case, $k_{y} \equiv 0$ , and the wavevector components satisfy the dispersion relation

$$
k _ {x} ^ {2} + k _ {z} ^ {2} = \left(\frac {\omega}{c}\right) ^ {2}, \tag {139}
$$

where c is the speed of light. As illustrated in the bottom left panel of Fig. 18(a), when an angular frequency shift $\Delta\omega$ is introduced, $k_{x}$ remains unchanged due to the conservation of transverse momentum, while $k_{z}$ increases as a result of the frequency change, in accordance with Eq. (139).

In 2016, Liu et al. [324] proposed an active terahertz metasurface based on graphene microribbon arrays to achieve temporal phase modulation. The working mechanism of the graphene metasurface is discussed in Subsection 8.1. As shown in Fig. 18(c), numerical simulations demonstrate that applying a 1 GHz alternating current signal induces time-varying phase shifts on the metasurface, resulting in a frequency shift of the reflected terahertz wave ranging from $5 \times 10^{-2}$ GHz to 5 GHz, depending on the operating wavelength.

In 2024, Atwater's group [326] demonstrated an electrically tunable active MIM meta-surface incorporating indium tin oxide (ITO), composed of a 1D array of Au nanoantennas. These nanoantennas were electrically connected via two independent electrodes, each contacting alternating groups of 12 antennas, as illustrated in the top panels of Fig. 18(d). The operating principle of this active metasurface is discussed in Subsection 8.2. All nanoantennas were modulated using the same waveform at a frequency $\omega_{\mathrm{m}}$ , with a temporal delay of half a period between the two antenna groups. As a result, incident light at angular frequency $\omega_{\mathrm{i}}$ was reflected and diffracted into a set of harmonic frequencies given by $\omega_{\mathrm{i}} \pm N\omega_{\mathrm{m}}$ , where $N$ is an integer. Experimentally, the metasurface was illuminated with normally incident light at a wavelength of $1530~\mathrm{nm}$ and modulated at megahertz frequencies using an arbitrary waveform generator. When driven with a $1\mathrm{MHz}$ sine wave, as shown by the blue curves in the bottom panels of Fig. 18(d), a strong signal appeared at $1\mathrm{MHz}$ , as expected. However, an undesired harmonic component at $2\mathrm{MHz}$ was also observed. To suppress this unwanted frequency, an optimization procedure was performed to maximize the power at $1\mathrm{MHz}$ while minimizing power at other harmonics. As shown by the orange curves in the bottom panels of Fig. 18(d), the optimized waveform achieved effective suppression of higher-order harmonics.

(II) Linear frequency conversion through sudden merging of meta-atoms. In 2018, Lee et al. [325] proposed a time-varying metasurface composed of an array of two concentric SRRs, as shown in the top panel of Fig. 18(e). When the conductivity of the region between the two SRRs is abruptly increased, the original resonant mode of the meta-atom is annihilated, and a new resonant mode—corresponding to a merged single SRR—is formed. When the timing of this abrupt conductivity change, denoted $t_1$ , closely matches the arrival time $t_2$ of the incident light pulse (specifically, when $t_1 - t_2 \to 0^+$ ), the metasurface exhibits the frequency conversion effect, since its operation involves dynamic mode conversion. In the experimental demonstration, a narrowband terahertz input pulse with a pump fluence of $10~\mu \mathrm{J~cm}^{-2}$ was coupled to the metasurface with a time delay of $t_1 - t_2 = 9.8~\mathrm{ps}$ . The power spectral densities of the output signal showed significant frequency conversion without notable spectral broadening

Figure 18  
![](images/5e6d251e2947ec32eff0e504b55a0a06ed21a8313e370bf1855a3cde36b4ecbb.jpg)

Bottom right: time-reversal test of a time-gradient metasurface. The dashed line shows the reversed path; the solid line shows the original trace. (b) Schematic of an optical isolator with identical input/output frequencies, constructed using two time-varying metasurfaces and a high-Q frequency filter. (c) Simulated frequency shift of reflected terahertz waves under periodic temporal modulation at various operation wavelengths.
