(d) Reprinted with permission from Sisler et al., Nat. Nanotechnol. 19, 1491–1498 (2024) [326]. Copyright © 2024 Springer Nature. (e) Reprinted with permission from Lee et al., Nat. Photonics 12, 765–773 (2018) [325]. Copyright © 2018 Springer Nature. (f) Reprinted with permission from Shi and Fan, Appl. Phys. Lett. 108, 021110 (2016) [327]. Copyright © 2016 American Institute of Physics.

across various input power levels. The reference spectrum was obtained by transmitting the input pulse through an unpatterned GaAs substrate with identical dielectric layers. Furthermore, the converted power at 0.92 THz exhibited a linear relationship with the input power at 0.6 THz, confirming the linearity of the frequency conversion process.

Nonreciprocity of spatiotemporal metasurfaces. Breaking optical reciprocity enables the development of photonic devices, such as optical isolators. Currently, most optical isolators rely on the magneto-optic Faraday effect, which requires strong magnetic fields and long interaction lengths. In contrast, spatiotemporal metasurfaces can achieve nonreciprocal responses without relying on magneto-optic effects, offering a promising route toward compact and integrable optical isolators.

(I) Metasurfaces supporting temporal phase modulation. Let us return to the space-time gradient metasurfaces illustrated in Fig. 18(a) and examine their time-reversal responses. As shown in the top panels of Fig. 18(a), a space-gradient metasurface exhibits reciprocal behavior, since the transverse momentum changes in the forward and reverse directions cancel each other out. In contrast, as depicted in the bottom panels of Fig. 18(a), a time-gradient metasurface is inherently nonreciprocal because the frequency shifts in both propagation directions are additive and do not cancel. Exploiting this nonreciprocal behavior, Shaltout et al. [323] proposed an optical isolator that maintains the same input and output frequencies by employing two time-varying metasurfaces in conjunction with a frequency filter based on a high-Q resonance.

In the same year, Hadad et al. demonstrated the use of a spatiotemporal metasurface to realize a nonreciprocal classical analog of electromagnetically induced transparency, creating a narrow transmission window for one-way propagation through an otherwise opaque surface.

(II) Metasurfaces consisting of photonic transition meta-atoms. In 2016, Shi and Fan [327] proposed an optical isolator based on photonic transitions in meta-atoms. Consider a meta-atom that supports eigenstates $|1\rangle$ and $|2\rangle$ at angular frequencies $\omega_{1}$ and $\omega_{2}$ , respectively, where $\omega_{1}<\omega_{2}$ . By applying a time-dependent dielectric function modulation that couples these two states, i.e.,

$$
\epsilon (\vec {r}, t) = \epsilon_ {\mathrm{DC}} (\vec {r}) + \delta (\vec {r}) \cos \left(\left(\omega_ {2} - \omega_ {1}\right) t + \phi\right), \tag {140}
$$

where $\phi$ is the modulation phase and $\delta (\vec{r})$ defines the spatial modulation profile. During the upward transition from $|1\rangle$ to $|2\rangle$ , the output light acquires a phase shift of $\phi$ , while in the downward transition from $|2\rangle$ to $|1\rangle$ , the same modulation imparts a phase shift of $-\phi$ . Based on this principle, an active metasurface composed of independently modulated meta-atoms can be realized. An optical isolator design was subsequently proposed. As illustrated in Fig. 18(f), output light at frequency $\omega_{1}$ is

deflected from the original normal direction. This occurs because the meta-atoms impose spatial phase gradients with opposite signs for the original and time-reversed incidence, corresponding to upward and downward transitions, respectively.

# 2.2.8. Beyond Real Frequency: Complex-Frequency Metasurfaces

The aforementioned emerging metasurfaces achieve novel functionalities such as high-Q factors, topological phases, and frequency conversion by introducing physical mechanisms such as nonlocal modes, singularities, and temporal phase modulation through carefully designed metasurface structures. In contrast, the focus is now shifting to the excitation light of metasurfaces, aiming to (I) change it from a classical light to a quantum light or (II) change it from a real-frequency light to a complex-frequency light $[328]$ , which may reveal new physical effects and applications. For (I), a detailed discussion will be provided in Section 6. Here, we focus on the exploration of (II).

The electric field vector of a time-harmonic complex-frequency electromagnetic wave can be expressed as

$$
\vec {E} (\vec {r}, t) = \vec {E} _ {1} (\vec {r}) E _ {2} (t) = \vec {E} _ {1} (\vec {r}) e ^ {- i \tilde {\omega} _ {0} t}, \tag {141}
$$

where

$$
\tilde {\omega} _ {0} = \omega_ {0} + i \tau \tag {142}
$$

represents the complex frequency, with $\omega_{0}$ being the real frequency and $\tau$ being the temporal growth or attenuation factor. Substituting Eq. (142) into Eq. (141), we obtain

$$
\vec {E} (\vec {r}, t) = e ^ {\tau t} \vec {E} _ {1} (\vec {r}) e ^ {- i \omega_ {0} t}, \tag {143}
$$

which indicates that the complex-frequency wave exhibits either temporal growth ( $\tau > 0$ ) or attenuation ( $\tau < 0$ ). This behavior implies that the imaginary part of the complex frequency can effectively represent either virtual gain or virtual loss.

Several theoretical proposals have recently been put forward to explore exotic physical effects in metasurfaces through the excitation of complex-frequency light. However, from a practical standpoint, synthesizing complex-frequency waves in optical systems remains a significant challenge, and, as a result, no experimental demonstrations of complex-frequency metasurfaces have been reported to date. Nonetheless, a recent demonstration of synthesizing complex-frequency responses offers promise for advancing experimental work in this area $[329]$ .

In the following, we first present the theoretical and simulation studies of novel physical effects in metasurfaces under complex-frequency excitation, followed by an introduction to the experimental techniques for synthesizing complex-frequency responses.

Novel physical effects under complex-frequency excitation. (I) Complex frequency analysis and the topological origin of resonant phase. Assuming that the response function $\Gamma(\tilde{\omega})$ —such as the transmission coefficient T, reflection coefficient R, or scattering coefficient S—only possesses simple zeros and poles, it can be expanded using the Weierstrass factorization theorem [91,93,330], yielding

$$
\Gamma (\tilde {\omega}) = A e ^ {i B \tilde {\omega}} \prod_ {N = 1} ^ {\infty} \frac {\tilde {\omega} - \tilde {\omega} _ {N} ^ {+}}{\tilde {\omega} - \tilde {\omega} _ {N} ^ {-}}, \tag {144}
$$

where A and B are constants that depend on the properties of the metasurface, and $\tilde{\omega}_{N}^{\pm}$ are the complex frequencies corresponding to the zeros and poles, respectively. While the poles are associated with resonances or quasi-normal modes of photonic structures, the zeros offer important insights into physical phenomena, such as BIC [93].

By evaluating the winding number along a counterclockwise contour C encircling the singularities in the complex frequency plane, the topological charge can be defined as

$$
q = \frac {1}{2 \pi} \oint_ {C} \frac {\mathrm{d} \angle \Gamma (\tilde {\omega})}{\mathrm{d} \tilde {\omega}} \mathrm{d} \tilde {\omega}. \tag {145}
$$

The topological charges of the zeros and poles are +1 and -1, respectively, meaning that the phase undergoes a $2\pi$ variation around the zeros, while the poles cause a phase variation of $-2\pi$ . Zeros and poles occur in pairs, connected by a branch cut, where the phase undergoes a discontinuous jump of $\pm\pi$ when taking the phase value within the range $(- \pi, \pi]$ .

In the following, we analyze the topological origins of the resonant phases in various types of metasurfaces using complex frequency analysis based on zeros and poles.

(i) Resonant phase of metasurfaces satisfying PT symmetry. It is commonly assumed that an isolated resonance in a metasurface induces a phase shift of $\pi$ . However, strictly speaking, this assumption holds only for metasurfaces that satisfy PT symmetry. In this context, the parity symmetry corresponds to mirror symmetry along the z-direction (the normal direction of the metasurface), i.e., $(x,y,z)\to(x,y,-z)$ , while the time symmetry corresponds to time-reversal symmetry, i.e., $t\to-t$ .

For a metasurface that preserves time-reversal symmetry, the zeros of the response function under forward and backward illumination [as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 19(c)] must be complex conjugates of each other:

$$
\tilde {\omega} _ {\text {forward}} ^ {+} = \left(\tilde {\omega} _ {\text {backward}} ^ {+}\right) ^ {*}, \tag {146}
$$

where $\tilde{\omega}_{forward}^{+}$ and $\tilde{\omega}_{backward}^{+}$ are the complex frequencies of the zeros under forward and backward illumination, respectively. For a metasurface satisfying parity symmetry, reciprocity dictates that the zeros under forward and backward illumination must be identical:

$$
\tilde {\omega} _ {\text {forward}} ^ {+} = \tilde {\omega} _ {\text {backward}} ^ {+}. \tag {147}
$$

Combining Eqs. (146) and (147), we conclude that for a metasurface satisfying PT symmetry, the zeros lie on the real axis.

Moreover, in passive systems, causality constraints require that the poles must have a negative imaginary component.

As shown in Fig. 19(a), the reflection coefficient of a metasurface satisfying PT symmetry exhibits a pair of a zeros and a poles, with the zero located on the real axis and the pole positioned below the real axis, as expected. In this case, the phase shift variation along the real frequency axis is approximately $\pi$ .

(ii) Resonant phase of metasurfaces breaking PT symmetry. To achieve a resonant phase accumulation of $2\pi$ as a function of real frequency, a necessary condition is the presence of a zero–pole pair separated by the real axis. Since, for a passive metasurface, poles are inherently constrained to lie below the real axis, breaking PT symmetry is required to shift the zero above the real axis. This can be realized either by introducing gain or loss to the metasurface to break time-reversal symmetry or by breaking mirror symmetry along the z-axis. The latter can be achieved by using superstrates and substrates with different refractive indices [126] or by employing conically shaped meta-atoms [134].

Figure 19  
![](images/eff13ce8319266c9066ab592d30c8191d280b9690d631e0e5901130c9c891406.jpg)

Complex-frequency metasurfaces. (a) Complex frequency analysis for the meta-atom satisfying PT symmetry. Top left: schematic of the meta-atom, where $h$ denotes the height of the meta-atom, and $\epsilon_{\mathrm{sup}}$ and $\epsilon_{\mathrm{sub}}$ denote the permittivity of the superstrate and substrate of the metasurface, respectively. Bottom left: amplitude reflection coefficient and reflection phase shift of the meta-atom as functions of real frequency. Right: reflection phase of the meta-atom as a function of complex frequency. $\omega_{\mathrm{RZ}}$ and $\omega_{\mathrm{RP}}$ represent the complex frequencies of the zero and pole of the reflection coefficient, respectively. (b) Complex frequency analysis for the meta-atom breaking the parity symmetry. The meanings of the panels are the same as in (a). (c) Complex frequency analysis for the meta-atoms with different heights $h$ . First column: schematic of the meta-atom satisfying PT symmetry. The green and orange arrows indicate the directions of forward and backward illumination. The last three columns: transmission phase of the meta-atoms with different $h$ as a function of complex frequency. $\omega_{\mathrm{TZ}i}$ and $\omega_{\mathrm{TP}i}$ are the complex frequencies of the $i$ -th pair of zeros and poles of the transmission coefficient. (d) Amplitude transmission coefficient and transmission phase shift of the meta-atom in (c) as functions of real frequency and $h$ . (e) Imaginary parts of the zeros as a function of the meta-atom height. $\mathrm{EP}_1$ and $\mathrm{EP}_2$ are the exceptional points where two zeros degenerate. (f) Forward scattering efficiency of a dielectric sphere in the complex frequency plane. The zero is marked by the blue circle. (a)-(e) Adapted from [126] under a Creative Commons license. (f) Reprinted with permission from Kim et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 129, 203601 (2022) [331]. Copyright © 2022 American Physical Society.

As shown in Fig. 19(b), compared to the metasurface in Fig. 19(a), the refractive indices of the superstrate and substrate have been modified to 2.12 and 1, respectively, thereby breaking parity symmetry. Consequently, the zero shifts to the upper half of the complex plane. This configuration enables a phase shift accumulation of approximately $2\pi$ along the real frequency axis.

(iii) Resonant phase of Huygens metasurfaces based on the exceptional point of zeros. As mentioned in Subsection 2.1.2, Huygens metasurfaces support a $2\pi$ phase shift accumulation without the need to break PT symmetry. How is this possible? In fact, in Huygens metasurfaces, the constraint that requires zeros to be real is spontaneously broken due to the interaction between multiple zero–pole pairs [126].

For the metasurface shown in Fig. 19(c), two zero–pole pairs are present, and the two zeros are complex conjugates of each other. This unique configuration allows the zeros to move away from the real axis while still satisfying the PT symmetry constraints given in Eqs. (146) and (147). By selecting an appropriate meta-atom height such that a zero and its corresponding pole are separated across the real axis, a Huygens metasurface can be realized, as shown in Figs. 19(c) and 19(d).

The exceptional point of zeros plays a crucial role in this process. The trajectories of the two zeros are shown in Figs. 19(c) and 19(e). When the meta-atom height h > 183.58 nm, the zeros remain on the real axis, preventing a $2\pi$ phase accumulation. At h = 183.58 nm, the two zeros coalesce, forming an exceptional point. As h decreases beyond this threshold, the zeros separate and move away from the real axis along different directions. Since one zero shifts above the real axis and the corresponding pole remains below, a $2\pi$ phase shift variation along the real frequency is achieved. When $h \leq 160$ nm, a symmetric process occurs.

(II) Engineering light scattering of nanostructures beyond conventional limits. In 2022, Kim et al. [331] manipulated the scattering of nanostructures through excitations at complex frequencies, demonstrating extreme scattering responses, such as zero forward scattering and the cancellation of scattered fields in both forward and backward directions. For instance, the forward scattering efficiency of a tailored dielectric sphere with radius $a$ is shown in Fig. 19(f), where it exhibits a pair of zero (darkest spot) and pole (brightest spot). Since the zero is located below the real axis, fully suppressing forward scattering is impossible when exciting the sphere with a real-frequency wave, unless gain is added to the particle material or the refractive index of the material is increased. In contrast, the zero of forward scattering can be achieved by exciting the sphere with a wave at the complex frequency $\omega a / c = 0.824 - i0.063$ , thus breaking the conventional limit for minimum forward scattering efficiency.

Complex-frequency response synthesized by combining measurements made at multiple real frequencies. The mathematical expression of an ideal complex-frequency wave is given in Eq. (141). However, this formulation is physically unattainable, as it implies energy divergence when $t \rightarrow +\infty$ ( $t \rightarrow -\infty$ ) for $\tau > 0$ ( $\tau < 0$ ). Therefore, a temporal truncation is necessary to ensure physical feasibility. Specifically, for $\tau < 0$ , the truncated complex-frequency wave can be expressed as

$$
\vec {E} _ {\mathrm{t}} (\vec {r}, t) = \vec {E} _ {1} (\vec {r}) E _ {2 \mathrm{t}} (t) = \vec {E} _ {1} (\vec {r}) e ^ {- i \tilde {\omega} _ {0} t} \Theta (t), \tag {148}
$$

where

$$
\Theta (t) = \left\{ \begin{array}{l l} 0, & t <   0, \\ 1, & t \geq 0. \end{array} \right. \tag {149}
$$

Applying Fourier transformation, Eq. (148) can be rewritten as an integral representation in terms of real-frequency components:

$$
\vec {E} _ {\mathrm{t}} (\vec {r}, t) = \frac {1}{2 \pi} \int_ {- \infty} ^ {+ \infty} \frac {1}{i (\tilde {\omega} _ {0} - \omega)} \vec {E} _ {1} (\vec {r}) e ^ {- i \omega t} \mathrm{d} \omega . \tag {150}
$$

Thus, any complex-frequency response $\Gamma\left(\tilde{\omega}\right)$ of the system—including the permittivity and the transfer function—can be evaluated through the integral of the

corresponding real-frequency response $F(\omega)$ :

$$
F \left(\tilde {\omega} _ {0}\right) \approx \frac {1}{2 \pi} \int_ {- \infty} ^ {+ \infty} F \left(\omega\right) \frac {1}{i \left(\tilde {\omega} _ {0} - \omega\right)} e ^ {- i \omega t + i \tilde {\omega} _ {0} t} \mathrm{d} \omega , \tag {151}
$$

for a sufficiently long duration t. In practice, the complex-frequency response can be further discretized as

$$
F \left(\tilde {\omega} _ {0}\right) \approx \frac {1}{2 \pi} \sum_ {i} F \left(\omega_ {i}\right) \frac {1}{i \left(\tilde {\omega} _ {0} - \omega_ {i}\right)} e ^ {- i \omega_ {i} t + i \tilde {\omega} _ {0} t} \Delta \omega , \tag {152}
$$

where $\Delta\omega$ represents a discrete frequency interval.

This method of synthesizing complex-frequency responses was proposed by Zhang's group and their collaborators in 2023 [329]. It has since been applied to various fields, including overcoming losses in superlenses [329], ultrahigh-sensitivity molecular sensing [332], and compensating losses in phonon polariton propagation [333].

# 2.3. Fundamental Limits of Metasurfaces

Understanding the fundamental limits of metasurfaces can provide valuable physical guidance for advanced designs. To determine the working limits of metasurfaces, the main approach is to model metasurfaces theoretically and derive performance limitation formulas through mathematical analysis $[334]$ . Additionally, topology optimization, which offers the highest degree of design freedom, can also be used to approximate and estimate the limits of metasurface performance $[335]$ . Here we focus on theoretical analysis, and topology optimization will be discussed in detail in Subsection 3.3.

The performance limitations of metalenses, such as NA and achromatic bandwidth, along with the multiplexing channel limitations of metasurface holography, which are device-specific, will be discussed separately in Sections 4 and 5, respectively.

# 2.3.1. Thickness Limit of Metasurfaces

The advent of metasurfaces has significantly compressed the thickness of optical components. A question that arises from this is whether there is a limit to how thin these components can be. In 2023, Miller [336] discussed why optical systems require a certain thickness and the limit of this thickness based on wave optics analysis. Considering an optical system with nonlocality, where the output at a certain position depends on inputs from many locations, the system must communicate sideways. When two different output positions depend on overlapping ranges of input positions, we have overlapping nonlocality (ONL), referring to the required number of sideways communication channels in the system. Due to the diffractive nature of light, each channel's communication has a limit thickness of $\frac{\lambda_0}{2n_{\mathrm{max}}}$ (each limit thickness can only be used for communication in a single channel and cannot be shared by two or more channels), where $\lambda_0$ is the wavelength in free space, and $n_{\mathrm{max}}$ is the maximum refractive index within the area where the system interacts with the incident light. The more channels there are, the greater the total thickness requirement of the system. Thus, it's the ONL that imposes a minimal limit on the thickness of optical systems. Specifically, by calculating the ONL value based on the optical system's function, a quantitative limit on the system's thickness can be determined. This method can be applied to calculate the limit thickness of a metasurface performing a specific function.

In 2022, Li and Hsu [337] theoretically examined the fundamental trade-off between the FOV and thickness of metalens systems. Due to the Fourier transform duality between space and momentum, any multi-channel system with angle-dependent response inherently requires nonlocality and a certain system thickness. The device functionality

dictates a specific angle-dependent electromagnetic response, from which its transfer matrix can be derived, allowing for the calculation of the degree of nonlocality and thereby determining the device's minimum thickness. For metalenses, the thickness of the metasurface $h$ is limited by

$$
h \gtrsim \left(\frac {1}{3} \mathrm{NA}\right) D _ {\mathrm{out}} \sin \left(\frac {\pi}{2} \sin \frac {\mathrm{FOV}}{2}\right), \tag {153}
$$

where NA is the numerical aperture, FOV is the FOV angle, and $D_{\mathrm{out}}$ is the lens diameter (more strictly, the diameter of the output aperture). Eq. (153) aligns with previous design outcomes for metalenses, and the thicknesses of two multilayer metasurface designs obtained using topology optimization [338,339] approach the limit value given by the formula, indicating that the formula's bounds are tight. This method can also be applied to establish thickness limits for other types of nonlocal metasurface devices.

# 2.3.2. Miniaturization Limit of Optical Systems

One of the primary motivations for developing metasurfaces is to achieve miniaturized optical systems that combine high performance and low loss $[340,341]$ . Consider the example of the metalens. In 2021, Ma et al. $[342]$ introduced a novel metric called volumetric imaging efficiency (VIE) to assess the compactness and capability of imaging systems relative to the diffraction limits. Their comparative analysis of VIE between lenses with curved image surfaces and metalenses demonstrated that metalenses can lead to significant improvements in VIE, exceeding the performance limits of traditional lenses.

It should be noted that the ultrathin thickness of metasurfaces does not necessarily mean that the entire optical system is compact in length, as there is invariably a significant amount of free space within optical systems. In a general sense, free space is also considered a special optical component of the system and often occupies a large part of the system's volume. Miniaturization of optical systems requires not only the miniaturization of optical elements but also a significant compression of free space. For the former, the limit has been discussed in the context of the minimum thickness of metasurfaces in Subsection 2.3.1, while for the latter, the main technological approaches include the following.

High-NA devices. The use of devices such as metalenses with high NAs can make the entire system more compact. Compared to traditional refractive and diffractive lenses, metasurfaces have an absolute advantage in achieving high-NA devices with high efficiency, and relevant experimental implementations are already available $[8,9,116,343]$ .

Free space squeezing. As schematically illustrated in Fig. 20(a), viewing free space as an optical component and replacing it with metasurfaces can address this issue more fundamentally. The action of a metasurface on incident light can be characterized by Eq. (79) with the transformation function expressed as

$$
\tilde {H} \left(\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}}\right) = e ^ {- i d \sqrt {k _ {0} ^ {2} - \left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} \right| ^ {2}}}, \tag {154}
$$

where d is the distance that the light travels in the z direction, and $k_{0}$ is the wavenumber in free space. Here, we do not consider the vector nature of light; hence, the transformation function in Eq. (154) is presented in scalar form. Since this transformation function is spatial-momentum-related, nonlocal metasurfaces must be adopted.

In 2020, Guo et al. [201] theoretically proposed that nonlocal devices could be used to compress free space and utilized photonic crystal slabs to realize the device design

Figure 20  
![](images/447da3bf520f25463763cb6021c5bcaf41d2d4caca6f786f8deb718a3184d712.jpg)

The fundamental limits of metasurfaces. (a) Schematic of free space compression using nonlocal devices. (b) Schematic of the nonlocal metasurface composed of multilayer planar structure. Dielectric plates with high relative permittivity $\epsilon_{1}$ (orange) are separated by materials with relative permittivity close to 1 (gray), where $\lambda_{r}$ is the free-space wavelength of the incident light at the resonance frequency. (c) Schematic of pancake optics for optical path folding. BS, beam splitter; RS, reflective polarizer. The red arrow represents the RCP light, while the blue arrow represents the LCP light, and (d) the same. (d) Schematic for optical path folding utilizing the metasurface. (e) Top: images taken with the metalens with optical path folding. Bottom: images taken with the normal metalens. (a) Reprinted with permission from Guo, et al., Optica 7, 1133–1138 (2020) [201]. Copyright © 2022 Optica Publishing Group. (b) Adapted with permission from Chen and Monticone, ACS Photonics 8, 1439–1447 (2021) [202]. Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society. (c)–(e) Adapted with permission from Chen et al., Optica 9, 1314–1322 (2022) [344]. Copyright © 2022 Optica Publishing Group.

(it's worth mentioning that methods to achieve nonlocality are not limited to metasurfaces, and even natural materials exhibit nonlocality, albeit weakly. For a detailed explanation, see reference [195]). This device can be designed to accommodate different NAs and can be placed at any location in the optical path to reduce free space as needed. Numerical simulations showed that the compression ratio (the ratio of the lengths of free space before and after compression) could be as high as 144, but the device's working angular range is very limited [202]. In 2021, Reshef et al. [345] achieved a spatial compression of $3.4\mathrm{mm}$ using a uniaxial crystal slab made of calcite in a background medium of linseed oil, experimentally proving the concept of free space squeezing to be physically feasible. The device had a considerable working angular range, but the compression ratio was small, only 1.12, which is far from meeting the requirements for practical applications.

In 2021, targeting nonlocal structures based on a single resonant mode, Chen and Monticone [202] established a quantitative trade-off relationship between the length

of compressed free space l and the working angular range:

$$
\frac {l}{\lambda_ {0}} \left(\frac {\left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} \right| _ {\max}}{k _ {0}}\right) \leq 1, \tag {155}
$$

where $\left|\vec{k}_{T}\right|_{max}$ is the maximum magnitude of the transverse wavevector. Equation (155) explains the limited performance of previously proposed free space compression devices. As shown in Fig. 20(b), by stacking the aforementioned resonant structures in n layers, this constraint can be further relaxed:

$$
\frac {l}{\lambda_ {0}} \left(\frac {\left| \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}} \right| _ {\max}}{k _ {0}}\right) \leq n. \tag {156}
$$

Thus, it becomes possible to compress any length of free space region within a wide angular range through multilayer structures.

Folded optics. Despite the theoretical and simulation groundwork, efficiently compressing free space experimentally using nonlocal metasurfaces remains an open question. In contrast, incorporating folded optical paths into optical systems to reduce the volume and path length of components is more feasible $[346,347]$ . In 2022, Chen et al. $[344]$ drew inspiration from pancake optics to significantly shorten the volume of the entire metalens imaging system. Figure 20(c) illustrates the concept of a traditional folded optical path achieved through pancake optics without metasurfaces, which consists of a beam splitter and a reflective polarizer. It can be seen that while effectively reducing the system's volume, a significant downside is the 75% loss of light. As shown in Fig. 20(d), the folded optical path system based on metasurfaces consists of a single bifunctional metasurface and a perforated mirror. The metasurface transmits RCP light in the case of forward incidence and reflects it with backward incidence. Ultimately, light can exit the cavity through the perforated mirror, theoretically avoiding light loss. Building upon this functional foundation, by superimposing the focusing phase modulation on the aforementioned metasurface, a metalens with a folded optical path function is achieved. Experimentally, the metalens with an operating wavelength of 980 nm, a diameter of 1 mm, and an effective focal length of 2.4 mm was realized, achieving an FWHM of focal point of 2.8 $\mu$ m, a Strehl ratio of 0.68, and a compression ratio of $\frac{1}{3}$ . The imaging results are shown in Fig. 20(e).

# 3. DESIGN METHODS FOR METASURFACES

Metasurface design is restricted by natural materials, simulation software, fabrication processes, etc. Grasping these constraints is essential to understanding why specific design approaches are developed and adopted in practice. The design methods for metasurfaces can be categorized into traditional forward design as well as emerging inverse design.

As a preliminary basis for simple design tasks, it's crucial to organize the basic workflow of the forward design method, with particular attention to material selection and fabrication methods (Subsection 3.1). However, relying on human intuition, forward design often adopts meta-atoms with regular geometric shapes and neglects the coupling between meta-atoms, resulting in a limited design space [348]. A detailed discussion on the limitations of the forward design method is provided in Subsection 3.2.2. Against this backdrop, inverse design methods have emerged. Technically,

metasurface inverse design methods can be divided into two categories, based respectively on optimization theory (Subsection 3.3) and machine learning (Subsection 3.4). In recent years, inverse design has played an increasingly important role in breaking through device performance limitations and realizing novel designs for complex functionalities.

# 3.1. Basic Design Process of Metasurfaces

Metasurfaces operating in either reflection or transmission mode have similar design approaches, which typically follow the process outlined below.

(I) Firstly, based on the requirements of the device to be designed, the basic configuration of the metasurface is determined, including the operating wavelength, lattice constant, whether to choose a single-layer metasurface or multilayer metasurfaces, and the materials of the meta-atom and substrate.

For a periodic photonic structure with a lattice constant of $a$ , the diffraction angle $\theta_{M}$ for the $M^{\mathrm{th}}$ diffraction order is given by Eq. (29). When determining the lattice constant, Eq. (29) indicates that it should be subwavelength to prevent the occurrence of additional diffraction orders, allowing only the zeroth order [223]. Additionally, further constraints need to be considered based on the specific performance requirements of the device. For instance, achieving high-NA metalenses or wide-FOV metasurface holograms requires a sufficiently small lattice constant. A detailed discussion can be found in Subsections 4.2 and 5.1.3.

The principle for selecting substrate materials is to ensure minimal absorption at the operating wavelength, combining with the processing requirements. Common substrate materials include sapphire $\left(\mathrm{Al}_{2}\mathrm{O}_{3}\right)$ , Si, and silica $\left(\mathrm{SiO}_{2}\right)$ . The principle for selecting meta-atom materials is to choose materials that have as high a refractive index and as low an absorption coefficient as possible at the operating wavelength and that are compatible with the adopted fabrication process $[349]$ . High refractive index characteristics expand the design space [see Eq. (4)], enabling the construction of a meta-atom database that ensures complete coverage of phase and group delay, among other parameters, without necessitating an excessively high aspect ratio. And low absorption characteristics can prevent loss of efficiency. It should be noted that the actual choice of meta-atom materials results from a balance of various factors, such as the efficiency, fabrication cost, etc.

As shown in Fig. 21, typical meta-atom materials that operate in the band ranging from ultraviolet to mid-infrared are listed. In the visible spectrum, $TiO_{2}$ and GaN have nearly zero absorption coefficients and refractive indices around 2.7 and 2.4, respectively, making them ideal meta-atom materials, but their fabrication is relatively difficult and often adopted by research institutions during prototyping. In spite of the slight absorption in the blue spectrum, $SiN_{x}$ also has good transmittance in the visible, and its fabrication process is largely compatible with Si, making it the material of choice for the industrial manufacture of metasurface devices.

Amorphous silicon (a–Si), crystalline silicon (c–Si), and polycrystalline silicon (poly–Si) exhibit varying degrees of absorption loss but have higher refractive indices, and their processing is well established. Therefore, when designing metasurfaces for the near-infrared and mid-infrared bands, many designs tend to overlook the loss and choose these materials for the meta-atoms $[356–361]$ . We note that, while traditionally regarded as undesirable and avoided in previous cases, loss can play an indispensable role in non-Hermitian metasurfaces (see Subsection 2.2.6) and in reducing the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms $[362]$ , among other applications.

Typical meta-atom materials operating at different wavelengths, where E means efficiency, T means transmittance, DE means diffraction efficiency, RFE means relative focusing efficiency, and AFE means absolute focusing efficiency. (a) Adapted from [350] under a Creative Commons license. (b) Adapted with permission from Ossiander et al., Science 380, 59–63 (2023) [68]. Copyright © 2023 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (c) Adapted with permission from Wang et al., Nat. Nanotechnol. 13, 227–232 (2018) [351]. Copyright © 2018 Springer Nature. (i) Adapted with permission from Zhang et al., Appl. Phys. Lett. 113, 111104 (2018) [355]. Copyright © 2018 American Institute of Physics. (j) Adapted from [37] under a Creative Commons license.

Tantalum pentoxide ( $Ta_{2}O_{5}$ ) and niobium pentoxide ( $Nb_{2}O_{5}$ ) are two promising meta-atom materials for visible-spectrum metasurfaces. $Ta_{2}O_{5}$ has a refractive index around 2.1 and negligible absorption across a broad spectrum. It can be easily deposited over large areas with high quality using physical vapor deposition and patterned into sub-wavelength nanostructures with aspect ratios as high as $\sim12$ via fluorine-gas-based reactive ion etching [363]. $Nb_{2}O_{5}$ exhibits a refractive index around 2.3 and has a broad transparency window covering the ultraviolet, visible, and infrared regions, along with excellent mechanical strength and chemical stability [364,365].

(II) Secondly, the target electromagnetic response of the metasurface is determined by the device's function. For most devices, such as metalenses and metagratings, the target complex amplitude distribution can be explicitly defined. In cases where the target response cannot be directly expressed—such as in metasurface holograms—specific computational algorithms, including CGH methods, or phase optimization tools in optical design software [366], can be employed to determine the desired response. It's noted that, in some cases, the electromagnetic responses of multiple metasurfaces performing successive functions can be directly superimposed, accomplished by a single metasurface. For example, as shown in Fig. 22(a), the metasurface needs to perform two functions: firstly, to deflect obliquely incident light to exit horizontally, with the introduced phase distribution denoted as $\phi_{1}$ ; secondly, to focus the horizontally ex-

Figure 22  
![](images/31453ff79f4bcb572051b5a11ed257e495360343112ca101a117541e2f4aa5aa.jpg)

The basic design process of metasurface. (a) Schematic of the superposition of metasurface functions. (b) Left: photograph of 45 fabricated metalenses fabricated using photolithography on a 4-in. diameter $SiO_{2}$ wafer. Right: tilted SEM view of meta-atoms of the metalens. (c) Schematic of the one-step fabrication process based on nanoimprint lithography. (d) SEM of a 3D-printed three-layer metasurface. (e) SEM of a bilayer metasurface from the cross-sectional view. (a) Adapted with permission from Shaltout et al., Science 365, 374–377 (2019) [367]. Copyright © 2019 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (b) Adapted with permission from Park et al., Nano Lett. 19, 8673–8682 (2019) [368]. Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society. (c) Adapted with permission from Kim et al., Laser Photonics Rev. 16, 2200098 (2022) [369]. Copyright © 2022 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (d) Reprinted from [3] under a Creative Commons license. (e) Reprinted with permission from Mansouree et al., Optica 7, 77–84 (2020) [191]. Copyright © 2020 Optica Publishing Group.

iting light, with the introduced phase distribution denoted as $\phi_{2}$ . In reality, a single metasurface introducing the phase distribution $\phi = \phi_{1} + \phi_{2}$ can achieve both functions simultaneously [367].

(III) Subsequently, choose an appropriate design method to realize the target electromagnetic response. Limited by the memory requirements and solution time of full-wave simulations, typically only the near-field response of the metasurface is calculated. When interested in the far-field (for example, to calculate the field intensity distribution at the focal plane of a metalens), scalar diffraction theory is often used to calculate the far-field from the near-field. Given its applicability range and computational accuracy, the Rayleigh–Sommerfeld diffraction theory is commonly employed to calculate the far-field distribution of the metasurface. The first kind of

Rayleigh–Sommerfeld diffraction integral formula is as follows [90]:

$$
E (\vec {r}) = \iint_ {\Sigma} E \left(\vec {r} ^ {\prime}\right) \left(\frac {1}{2 \pi \bar {r} + \frac {1}{i \lambda}}\right) \cos (\vec {n}, \vec {\bar {r}}) \frac {e ^ {i k \bar {r}}}{\bar {r}} \mathrm{d} s, \tag {157}
$$

where $\Sigma$ represents the diffracted field region, $\lambda$ and $k$ are the wavelength and wavenumber of light in the background medium, respectively, $\vec{r}$ is the spatial position vector of the observation point, $\vec{r}'$ is the spatial position vector of a point on the diffraction surface, $\vec{n}$ is the unit normal vector of the area element ds of this point on the diffraction surface, $\vec{\vec{r}} = \vec{r} - \vec{r}'$ , and $\vec{r} = |\vec{\vec{r}}|$ . When the distance between the observation point and the diffraction surface is sufficiently far, Eq. (157) can be approximated as

$$
E (\vec {r}) = \iint_ {\Sigma} E (\vec {r} ^ {\prime}) \frac {1}{i \lambda} \cos (\vec {n}, \vec {\bar {r}}) \frac {e ^ {i k \bar {r}}}{\bar {r}} \mathrm{d} s, \tag {158}
$$

(IV) After the design and simulation verification of the metasurface are completed, the layout file is generated for further manufacturing. The following factors should be considered comprehensively to choose the appropriate fabrication process: the shape complexity, minimum size, and precision requirement of the metasurface structure; the materials of the meta-atoms and the substrate; the aspect ratio; the size of the metasurface (the size determines the processing time, and for larger sizes, such as centimeter-level, the processing time of some fabrication techniques may be unacceptable); whether mass production is required; etc.

High-quality fabrication is crucial for ensuring the consistency of metasurface performance between simulations and actual measurements. For instance, in 2024, Johansen et al. [370] employed EBL to fabricate a series of metalenses operating at 940 nm with NA ranging from 0.08 to 0.93 and discovered that nanoscale precision and control within the manufacturing pipeline could bridge the efficiency gap between simulations and experimental outcomes, particularly for metalenses with lower NAs.

Given the significance of the fabrication process, it is wise to incorporate manufacturability considerations into the design phase to develop metasurface structures that are resilient to fabrication-induced imperfections:

(1) Adopting meta-atom geometries with inherent fabrication robustness. For instance, in 2021, Kühne et al. [371] compared three types of quasi-symmetry-protected BIC metasurface unit cells: tilted ellipses, asymmetric double rods, and split rings. The Q-factor of these high-Q-factor metasurfaces is highly sensitive to geometrical deviations. Among the configurations, all-dielectric tilted ellipses exhibited the highest Q-factors across a wide range of geometric variations, highlighting their superior robustness.  
(2) Robust metasurface design via topology optimization. Topology optimization can enhance robustness by explicitly incorporating fabrication-induced perturbations—such as geometric erosion and dilation—into the iterative optimization algorithm. This approach has been shown to yield metasurfaces that maintain performance under fabrication variability $[372,373]$ .  
(3) Machine learning-assisted robust metasurface design. Machine learning offers a powerful tool to account for fabrication tolerances in metasurface design [374]. As discussed in Subsection 3.4, machine learning models are categorized as forward or inverse models. Forward models can be integrated with optimization algorithms to efficiently explore the design space and identify robustconfigurations

[375,376]. Additionally, they can be used to construct large meta-atom databases significantly faster than traditional full-wave simulations, facilitating the selection of fabrication-tolerant geometries [377]. Inverse models, on the other hand, can incorporate robustness criteria directly into their training loss functions to generate metasurface designs that are resilient to fabrication imperfections [378].

Below, we briefly introduce the mainstream metasurface fabrication methods.

(i) Photolithography utilizes a photon beam to transfer the pattern from a mask to the photoresist, offering short production times and low costs once the mask is prepared. It is promising for industrial-scale mass production of large-area metasurfaces. The achievable feature size depends on the sophistication of the lithography equipment. It's essential to note that resists are generally not accepted as meta-atom materials due to their low refractive index (unless through specific designs $[379]$ ), so transferring the pattern from the resist to the meta-atom layer is usually required by additional processing steps, such as direct etching $[158]$ , lift-off $[174,380]$ , or hard mask etching $[381,382]$ . In 2019, as shown in Fig. 22(b), Capasso's group $[368]$ successfully used deep ultraviolet (DUV) lithography to fabricate 45 all-glass metalenses on a fused-silica wafer with a diameter of 4 inches. These metalenses, composed of $SiO_{2}$ pillars with a height of 1.5 $\mu$ m, operate at a wavelength of 633 nm, each with a diameter of 1 mm and an NA of 0.1. In 2023, Ni's group $[383]$ used DUV lithography to fabricate an a–Si metalens with a diameter of 80 mm, achieving a peak absolute focusing efficiency of 80.84% at the wavelength of 1450 nm (with an effective focusing area diameter appropriately 3–5 times the FWHM of the focal spot). In 2024, Capasso's group $[384]$ employed DUV lithography to fabricate an all-glass metalens in the visible spectrum, comprising 18.7 billion nanostructures. This metalens features a diameter of 100 mm and a fast f-number (f/1.5, NA = 0.32).  
(ii) Nanoimprint lithography (NIL) is a pattern transfer method through mechanical deformation, which is emerging as an alternate manufacturing method for large-scale production due to its low cost and high throughput. NIL transfers the metasurface structure pattern of a prefabricated mold onto the resist, such as the curable liquid polymer, which is then solidified under certain pressure using heat or UV light. Because the refractive index of typical resists is too low for use as meta-atoms, additional processing is usually required to transfer the patterns from the resist to the meta-atom layer $[385]$ . It is worth noting that slight etching of the resist pattern can remove any residue, making it suitable for lift-off processes. However, the patterned resist itself can serve as the final metasurface when specialized resists are used—such as $TiO_{2}$ -nanoparticle-embedded resin [as shown in Fig. 22(c)] $[117,369]$ —or when the printed resin nanostructures are coated with a thin layer of high-index material $[386]$ . This approach eliminates the need for additional pattern transfer steps. Although the achievable aspect ratio with NIL is generally low due to the impact of shear stress exerted on the nanostructures by the mold $[387]$ , it can be enhanced with process optimizations. For instance, in 2023, Choi et al. $[387]$ demonstrated a centimeter-scale metalens design with an aspect ratio of 5.8, fabricated by NIL using a water-soluble mold made from polyvinyl alcohol.  
(iii) Electron beam lithography (EBL) and focused ion beam (FIB), both maskless lithography techniques that rely on point-by-point scanning, allow a feature size on the order of 10 nm but are time-consuming, low-throughput, and costly, making them primarily suitable for lab-scale prototype verification. EBL can only etch structures on low-refractive-index resists, so subsequent processes must be followed to transfer patterns from resist to meta-atom layer. FIB can directly etch hard metals $[388]$ and dielectric materials $[389]$ with support for freeform surface patterning $[389]$ , at the cost of a higher expense and a lower processing efficiency $[390]$ .

(iv) Direct laser writing (DLW) uses the scanning of a focused laser beam to change the properties of materials, thereby achieving specific structures. It is known for its low cost, flexibility, and capability for large-area fabrication. Among various DLW technologies, 3D laser printing technology based on two-photon polymerization $[391]$ is the most widely used in the fabrication of both single-layer metasurfaces $[392]$ and cascaded metasurfaces $[3]$ . For example, a three-layer metasurface fabricated with this technology is shown in Fig. 22(d). Compared to traditional single-photon polymerization technology, it allows the feature size to be reduced from 1 $\mu$ m to about 200 nm. Its main advantage lies in allowing a high aspect ratio and even supporting the processing of structures that break out-of-plane symmetry $[393]$ .

(v) Additionally, new fabrication methods are continuously emerging, such as self-assembly lithography [394,395], laser ablation [396], etc., which are not covered here. For a detailed introduction to metasurface manufacturing, see references [397-399].

(vi) For cascaded metasurfaces, except for DLW methods, there are generally two fabrication strategies: one is to align multiple metasurface layers during fabrication $[186,191]$ ; the other is to fabricate each layer of the metasurface separately and then align and bond them together $[187,188]$ . For the former approach, when the interlayer distance is small, as shown in Fig. 22(e), a common practice is that, after fabricating the bottom metasurface layer, an interlayer material is introduced through methods such as spin-coating $[191]$ and film deposition $[186]$ , and its surface is polished before continuing with the fabrication of the upper metasurface layer. For larger interlayer distances, the substrate thickness can be controlled to match the interlayer distance, and each layer of the metasurface can be directly fabricated on the respective sides of the substrate $[184,185]$ . A common practice to align between layers is to fabricate specific alignment marks additionally outside the metasurface structure $[185,191]$ . The limit of interlayer alignment error is approximately in the order of tens of nanometers $[191]$ .

# 3.2. Forward Design Method

# 3.2.1. Locally Periodic Approximation and Forward Design Method

When solving for the electromagnetic response of a meta-atom in Subsection 2.1, the periodic boundary condition is assumed. This assumption implies that all surrounding meta-atoms have identical structures. As a result, the influence of adjacent meta-atoms—which may differ in an actual inhomogeneous metasurface—is neglected in the meta-atom's electromagnetic response characteristics. This approximation is known as the locally periodic approximation (LPA).

In the traditional forward design strategy, the metasurface is divided into units or unit cells arranged periodically, which are typically in the shape of a square or a hexagon with a fixed lattice constant to cover the entire surface. For square or hexagonal units, the lattice constant is often defined as the side length, also referred to as the unit cell size or unit size. The simulation boundary condition for the meta-atoms should be matched to the lattice of the metasurface. A meta-atom, selected from a pre-constructed database based on iterative adjustments of structural parameters, is placed at the center of each unit according to the desired electromagnetic modulation.

When the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms is neglected under the LPA, the overall electromagnetic response of the metasurface to incident light is equivalent to the concatenation of the local electromagnetic responses of all the meta-atoms. It is important to note that when combining the near-field responses of the meta-atoms to derive the overall near-field response of the metasurface for further analysis, it is preferable

not to approximate the near-field distribution of a single meta-atom as a uniform field determined solely by the amplitude transmission coefficient t and phase shift $\phi$ , as often done in theoretical analyses. Instead, one should use the near-field distribution within the meta-atom region calculated under the periodic boundary condition for a more accurate approximation [370,400].

It is important to note that the core feature of the forward design method is the construction of the meta-atom database. In contrast, for the inverse design methods introduced in Subsections 3.3 and 3.4, constructing a meta-atom database is not necessary. Instead, the meta-atom design for the desired electromagnetic modulation is obtained directly through optimization algorithms or neural networks. While LPA serves as the foundation for the forward design method, inverse design methods can also leverage LPA to facilitate large-area metasurface design $[401]$ .

In the discussion above, each metasurface unit contains only a single meta-atom at its center. It is worth noting that unit cells consisting of multiple meta-atoms can significantly enhance the design flexibility, enabling more complex metasurface configurations. This approach will be explored further in Section 5.

# 3.2.2. Limitations of the Forward Design Method

Although the forward design method is the most commonly used approach for various types of metasurface devices, it may introduce certain limitations on design freedom and device performance $[402]$ .

(I) Discretization and sampling of continuous wavefront modulation. The target electromagnetic response of various types of devices, such as the lens, varies continuously in space. Under the LPA, the size of the metasurface unit determines the spatial sampling rate of the discretized electromagnetic response introduced by the metasurface. When the sampling of the metasurface is insufficient relative to the spatial variation of the target electromagnetic response, it can lead to significant performance degradation $[403]$ .  
In 2020, Chung et al. [403] theoretically calculated the efficiency limit of forward-designed metalenses under the LPA. By employing modal decomposition analysis, they calculated the ratio of the field intensity at the focal point of a forward-designed metalens, which is characterized by a specific operating wavelength, lattice constant, and NA, to the field intensity at the focal point of an ideal lens. This ratio is defined as the upper limit of focusing efficiency. Reasons for the limit include, but are not limited to, the effect of insufficient spatial sampling for the target phase profile mentioned above. Mathematically, this limit arises because, for the forward-designed metalens, the ideal outgoing light field distribution on each unit is non-periodic and cannot be perfectly represented by its periodic diffraction orders. The upper limits of focusing efficiency for metalenses with different NAs are shown in Fig. 23, where the maximum focusing efficiency drastically decreases as the NA increases. Utilizing inverse design methods that optimize multiple units or even the entire metasurface simultaneously could potentially break through this performance limitation.  
(II) Neglecting the effect of coupling between adjacent meta-atoms. From the perspective of mode analysis, the limitation of the LPA lies in neglecting the impact of modes that span the space between adjacent meta-atoms, also known as the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms, which must be considered in many cases.  
(i) When the difference between target phase shifts of two adjacent units is large, the corresponding difference in shape, size, or rotation angles of adjacent

Figure 23  
![](images/9c9c7afaf3c241caff942eeb62849d5a50fc2ba762adff71668fd34bdf17886e.jpg)

| --- | --- | --- | --- |
