The focusing efficiencies of the forward-designed metalenses with different NAs. The diameter of the metalens is 100 times the wavelength. $N_{d}$ is the number of diffraction orders per unit. Reprinted with permission from Chung and Miller, Opt. Express 28, 6945–6965 (2020) [403]. Copyright © 2020 Optica Publishing Group.

meta-atoms is always significant. In this case, the premise of the LPA no longer holds, leading to significant discrepancies between the actual introduced phase shifts and the target values. Large phase gradients are often unavoidable for applications such as high-NA lenses, wide-FOV holograms, etc. (refer to Sections 4 and 5); thus, the forward design approach will inherently impose limitations on device performance.

(ii) For high-refractive-index dielectric meta-atoms, examination of the near-field coupling shows that enhancing the effective magnetic response by changing the distance between meta-atoms can be used to control electromagnetic response characteristics [404].  
(iii) In general, for metasurfaces operating under off-axis illumination, the larger the angle of incidence, the poorer the locality of the meta-atom's interaction with light, and the stronger the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms. This angle-dependent electromagnetic response characteristic, typically undesirable in traditional forward design strategies (see the design of wide-FOV metalenses in Subsection 4.3 as an example), is extensively utilized in nonlocal metasurfaces [197]. For more details on nonlocal metasurfaces, refer to Subsection 2.2.2.  
(iv) For high-Q-factor metasurfaces, the introduction of a nonlocal resonant mode to achieve a large Q-factor results in strong coupling between adjacent meta-atoms. Consequently, the Q-factor becomes highly sensitive to variations in individual meta-atoms within the meta-atom array. A detailed discussion on high-Q-factor metasurfaces is provided in Subsection 2.2.3.

In recent years, the coupling between meta-atoms has gradually gained attention, and novel metasurface devices based on this have been developed $[209,238,405]$ . In addition, advanced design methods that incorporate the coupling into consideration through optimization-based inverse design methods $[204,343]$ or deep learning $[406]$ have emerged, giving rise to a variety of metasurface designs with novel functionality or high performance. Related works will be introduced in Sections 3.3 and 3.4.

# 3.3. Inverse Design Methods Based on Optimization Theory

# 3.3.1. Overview of Inverse Design in Metasurface Optimization

In the context of optimization, the design for a metasurface can be treated as a mathematical optimization problem that can be formally stated as [407]

$$
\begin{array}{l} \max _ {\epsilon (\vec {r}), \mu \mathrm{m} (\vec {r})} \quad F \left(\vec {E} (\vec {r}), \vec {H} (\vec {r})\right) \\ \text {subject to} \quad \vec {\nabla} \cdot \epsilon (\vec {r}) \vec {E} (\vec {r}) = \rho (\vec {r}), \\ \vec {\nabla} \cdot \mu \mathrm{m} (\vec {r}) \vec {H} (\vec {r}) = 0, \tag {159} \\ \vec {\nabla} \times \vec {E} (\vec {r}) = - i \omega \mu \mathrm{m} (\vec {r}) \vec {H} (\vec {r}), \\ \vec {\nabla} \times \vec {H} (\vec {r}) = \vec {J} (\vec {r}) + i \omega \vec {E} (\vec {r}), \\ \end{array}
$$

...

where $\omega$ is the incident light's angular frequency, $\vec{r} = x\vec{e}_x + y\vec{e}_y + z\vec{e}_z$ , $\rho(\vec{r})$ and $\vec{J}(\vec{r})$ are the spatial distributions of charge density and current density, respectively, and the optimization variables $\epsilon(\vec{r})$ and $\mu \mathrm{m}(\vec{r})$ are the spatial distributions of permittivity and permeability across the metasurface, respectively, the optimization objective $F$ is defined by the device's functional requirements and generally can be expressed as a function of the electromagnetic response of the metasurface, i.e., the electric field strength $\vec{E}(\vec{r})$ and magnetic field strength $H(\vec{r})$ . The relation between the permittivity and permeability distributions and the electromagnetic response follows the constraints set by Maxwell's equations. Given the limitations of natural materials and manufacturing processes, $\epsilon(\vec{r})$ and $\mu \mathrm{m}(\vec{r})$ are further subjected to other constraints.

In practical designs, the material for the metasurface can often be determined first. When disregarding multilayer metasurfaces to ignore changes in material properties in the z-direction, the spatial distributions of permittivity and permeability can be directly determined by a 2D distribution function $\mathcal{B}(x,y)$ of the meta-atom material, where a function value of 1 indicates the presence of material at the location $(x,y)$ , and a value of 0 indicates its absence. In practice, this function $\mathcal{B}(x,y)$ is subject to various degrees of simplification, leading to different types of metasurface designs with specific expressions for the metasurface structural parameter vector $\vec{p}=[p_{1} p_{2} \cdots p_{i} \cdots p_{N}]$ and different methods for solving the optimization problem.

(i) As illustrated in Figs. 24(a) and 24(b), when employing finite elements with the feature size around the $10^{-2}\lambda$ scale to discretize the material distribution and directly designing the material's topology structure, this approach, namely topology optimization, allows for the highest degree of design freedom [410].  
(ii) As shown in Figs. 24(c) and 24(d), when discretizing the metasurface into a pixel array with the pixel feature size around the $10^{-1}\lambda$ scale, where pixels are the smallest structural units of the metasurface with only filled and unfilled states (here, “filled” can be interpreted in two ways, corresponding to two types of pixelated metasurfaces: one where the metasurface material completely fills the pixel space [411,412], and the other where a meta-atom with fixed shape and size is placed at the pixel position [409]), this type of metasurface is known as the pixelated metasurface.  
(iii) As depicted in Figs. 24(e) and 24(f), mimicking forward design by introducing the concept of meta-atoms for structural division of the metasurface, but instead of creating a database by traversing structural parameters, this method uses optimization techniques for the design of the shape and size of meta-atoms. More importantly, the design region is no longer confined to individual meta-atoms but is allowed to extend to an array of meta-atoms or even the entire metasurface,

Figure 24  
![](images/6044b6209d0a88d18777942a0b14271454468e5f9e44349499f3dd449acbd51d.jpg)

Schematics of inverse design methods based on the optimization theory (a), (c), (e) and the SEMs of the corresponding metasurface designs (b), (d), (f). (b) Adapted with permission from Sell et al., Nano Lett. 17, 3752–3757 (2017) [408]. Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society. (d) Adapted with permission from Jin et al., ACS Nano 13, 821–829 (2019) [409]. Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society. (f) Adapted with permission from Mansouree et al., ACS Photonics 8, 455–463 (2021) [343]. Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society.

which takes into account the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms, thus effectively expanding the design space $[413]$ . For simplicity, we refer to this kind of optimization directly as shape optimization later.

# 3.3.2. Techniques for Solving Metasurface Optimization Problems

To solve the optimization problem of metasurface design, there are mainly two types of methods: gradient descent methods and heuristic algorithms. The former are local optimization algorithms, while the latter include both global optimization algorithms, such as genetic algorithms, and local optimization algorithms, such as the simulated annealing algorithm. A common reproach for gradient descent methods is that they can only obtain local optimum solutions. However, it's important to note that global optimization algorithms like genetic algorithms also do not guarantee a global optimum solution. In fact, given the complex non-convex nature of the optimization problem in metasurface inverse design, no optimization algorithm can guarantee a global optimum solution. Instead, these algorithms can only iteratively approximate the optimum solution. Furthermore, employing mature gradient descent algorithms like the Adam (adaptive moment estimation) algorithm $[414]$ , or applying neural network technology $[415]$ (which will be further introduced in Subsection 3.4.2), can help gradient optimization algorithms escape from local optima and saddle points, thereby improving optimization results and accelerating convergence.

(I) Gradient descent methods require calculating the gradient of the objective function F with respect to the metasurface structural parameter $p_{i}$ , represented as $\frac{\partial F}{\partial p_{i}}$ . After acquiring the gradient information, structural parameters are updated based on the gradient descent algorithm to progressively approach the optimal configuration of the metasurface. Four primary methods for gradient calculation are outlined below.  
(i) The finite difference method is the simplest approach, whose gradient formula is

$$
\frac {\partial F}{\partial p _ {i}} = \frac {F \left(p _ {1} , p _ {2} , \dots , p _ {i} + \Delta p , \dots , p _ {N}\right) - F \left(p _ {1} , p _ {2} , \dots , p _ {i} , \dots , p _ {N}\right)}{\Delta p}, \tag {160}
$$

where $\Delta p$ is a small perturbation of structural parameter $p_{i}$ . It can be seen that the gradient of each structural parameter is calculated by simulating the metasurface additionally after applying a small increment $\Delta p$ to that parameter, thus requiring $N + 1$ simulations to solve all gradients. This method is time-consuming due to the computational burden of electromagnetic simulations, making it suitable only for small-area metasurface optimizations. In practice, it is mainly used for fine-tuning the forward design result to enhance device performance [416,417].

(ii) The adjoint method is the most widely used approach for solving gradients. It can be seen from Eq. (159) that metasurface optimization is a type of optimization problem constrained by partial differential equations, for which the adjoint method is particularly well-suited [418]. By conducting one additional special adjoint simulation on top of the normal forward simulation for the metasurface with current structural parameters, gradients of the objective function with respect to all structural parameters can be obtained. This method has a very high gradient solving speed, and the maximum area of the metasurface that can be optimized is only limited by the capability of electromagnetic simulation software. Up to now, the adjoint method has been widely used in the topology optimization [419] and the shape optimization for metasurfaces with rectangular meta-atoms [343]. More specifically, within these two methodologies, the optimization variable for the latter is the size parameters describing the shape of the meta-atoms, while the optimization variable for the former is the dielectric constant distribution $\epsilon(x,y)\in\left[\epsilon_{\mathrm{cladding}},\epsilon_{\mathrm{meta}}\right]$ of the metasurface, where $\epsilon_{\mathrm{meta}}$ and $\epsilon_{\mathrm{cladding}}$ are dielectric constants for the meta-atom material and the material encapsulating the meta-atoms, respectively. Additional algorithmic processing [420] should be applied to gradually converge this distribution to reasonable values of $\epsilon_{\mathrm{meta}}$ or $\epsilon_{\mathrm{cladding}}$ .

As shown in Fig. 25(a), by introducing the level set function $\Phi(x,y)$ , where the boundary of $\Phi(x,y) \geq 0$ defines the cross-sectional shape contour of the meta-atom, adjoint method can be further extended to support meta-atoms with freeform cross-sectional shapes [421]. To our knowledge, there are currently no instances of the adjoint method being applied to pixelated metasurfaces, but there is no theoretical barrier to its application.

(iii) Automatic differentiation is a powerful and popular technique for solving partial derivatives. Gradients can be derived using electromagnetic solvers integrated with automatic differentiation frameworks. With this approach, a single forward simulation allows for the calculation of gradients of the objective function with respect to all structural parameters via backward propagation. Developing a comprehensive electromagnetic simulation tool based on automatic differentiation for metasurface design is indeed challenging. Nonetheless, preliminary efforts have been made in this direction $[424,425]$ . For example, in 2021, Colburn and Majumdar $[425]$ developed an RCWA solver using TensorFlow, a widely used automatic differentiation framework. This advancement facilitates flexible shape and topology optimization for various multilayer and multifunctional metasurface devices.

Figure 25  
![](images/1fc7d6040fba6907fbc16eefaf8cf4edb24f413a26ecebf9f981c010e3fc0eaa.jpg)

Inverse design methods based on the optimization theory and their applications. (a) Schematic of the level set function defining the shape of the meta-atom. (b) Amplitude distributions of the magnetic field strength of eight propagation modes of the topology-optimized meta-atom. The effective refractive index corresponding to the mode is marked in the upper left corner of each picture, and the outlines of the meta-atom are drawn in green. (c) Plot of the deflection efficiency of the topology-optimized metagrating as a function of the number of modes (the modes are added in the order of decreasing the effective refractive index). (d) Schematic of the section-by-section topology optimization algorithm. (e) Schematic of the algorithm for segmented hierarchical design of pixelated metasurfaces. (f) Experimental holographic images of two full-color holograms based on the pixelated metasurface. (g) Comparison of focusing efficiencies of metalenses with different NAs designed through forward and inverse design methods. (a) Adapted with permission from Mansouree and Arbabi, International Applied Computational Electromagnetics Society Symposium (2019) [421]. Copyright © 2019 International Applied Computational Electromagnetics Society Symposium. (b) and (c) Reprinted with permission from Sell et al., Nano Lett. 17, 3752–3757 (2017) [408]. Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society. (d) Reprinted from [422] under a Creative Commons license. (e) and (f) Adapted with permission from Jin et al., ACS Nano 13, 821–829 (2019) [409]. Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society. (g) Reprinted from [204] under a Creative Commons license. (h) and (i) Reprinted with permission from Zhou et al., Laser Photonics Rev. 18, 2300988 (2024) [423]. Copyright © 2024 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim.

(iv) Surrogate models have been proposed recently to replace traditional electromagnetic solvers for forward simulation, based on interpolation methods $[426]$ or neural networks $[427]$ . These models offer rapid solutions and differentiability, thus not only accelerating the solution process but also facilitating the easy acquisition of gradient information via direct derivation or automatic differentiation. This approach is also referred to as the adjoint method in some studies for metasurface shape optimization $[426,428,429]$ . Actually, the surrogate model is obtained through interpolation, fitting,

or machine learning methods based on a database of meta-atoms, which generally cannot take into account the coupling between meta-atoms. This results in significant differences in design degrees of freedom between shape optimizations based on the surrogate model and adjoint method. Nonetheless, the shape optimization based on surrogate models can outperform forward design methods in certain scenarios. For example, its advantages in rapid solution and differentiability enable end-to-end design for metasurface holograms, making multi-wavelength designs feasible even in limited design spaces [429]. Surrogate models will be further discussed in Subsection 3.4.1.

(II) Heuristic algorithms, unlike gradient descent methods, do not rely on gradient information and are essentially search methods based on certain intelligent strategies. The heuristic algorithms commonly used in metasurface design include particle swarm optimization $[430]$ , genetic algorithms and their variants such as the differential evolution algorithm $[431]$ , among others. Compared to gradient descent methods that leverage gradient information to converge after relatively few iterations, heuristic algorithms, while avoiding the need for gradient calculation, tend to converge more slowly and often require a great number of electromagnetic simulations. This issue becomes more severe with an increase in optimization variables. Therefore, heuristic algorithms are generally used for small-area pixelated metasurfaces $[409,432]$ , and for the shape optimization of small-area metasurfaces with metasurface sizes typically around several tens of micrometers $[204]$ . Using surrogate models instead of traditional electromagnetic solvers for simulation can help the algorithm converge in less time due to their faster solution speed $[412]$ .

# 3.3.3. Progress in Inverse Design Methods Based on Optimization Theory

Below, we briefly review representative works in various types of inverse-designed metasurfaces.

(I) In the design of topology-optimized metasurfaces, the adjoint method is generally employed to solve for gradients, with detailed optimization theories available in references $[420,433,434]$ . In this field, significant contributions have been made by Fan's group $[372,408,415,419,422,435]$ , Johnson's group $[339,401,436]$ , and others over time. In 2017, Sell et al. $[408]$ demonstrated a metagrating supporting freeform meta-atom designs through topology optimization. The metagrating, made of poly–Si meta-atoms, operated at a wavelength of 1050 nm, exhibiting the diffraction angle up to $75^{\circ}$ with absolute deflection efficiencies (defined as the ratio of the power of the deflected beam at the diffraction angle to the power of the incident light through the substrate) of 75% and 74% for perpendicularly incident light polarized along the x and y axes, respectively. The meta-atom supports eight propagating Bloch modes, with the mode field distribution for perpendicularly incident light polarized along the x axis shown in Fig. 25(b). As depicted in Fig. 25(c), a single mode cannot effectively deflect the incident light to the specified direction, but the interference of all modes can achieve this, explaining the origin of high performance of topology-optimized devices. Topology optimization can also be applied to the design of multifunctional metagratings for wavelength multiplexing, which deflect perpendicularly incident light of M different wavelengths into M distinct angles. These designs achieve average absolute deflection efficiencies at the level of $\frac{1}{\sqrt{M}}$ , far surpassing the typical benchmark of $\frac{1}{M}$ set by current advanced designs $[435]$ . These designs are limited to homogeneous metasurfaces, mainly because topology optimization requires a large number of computational resources, with computational load increasing significantly with the size of the metasurface to be optimized, thus limiting its application to small-area designs. In 2019, to facilitate the design of large-area topology-optimized metasurfaces, Phan et al. $[422]$ introduced a computationally efficient design algorithm, as

shown in Fig. 25(d). The target phase distribution is spatially divided into linear regions on the scale of wavelengths, with the metasurface structure of each region designed through topology optimization before being stitched together. Using c–Si as the meta-atom material, 1D metalenses of 200 $\mu$ m width with NAs of 0.2, 0.5, and 0.8 were designed, achieving a relative focusing efficiency of over 89% and absolute focusing efficiency of over 67% for the TM-polarized light at the wavelength of 640 nm.

(II) Pixelated metasurfaces are usually designed using heuristic algorithms, facing limitations related to device size: as the design space increases with device size, the number of iterations required for optimization convergence and the computational cost of full-wave simulations increase significantly. To address this issue, in 2019, Jin et al. [409] proposed conducting the optimization process in phases from low to high resolution, where low resolution refers to using a larger pixel size and high resolution the opposite. The algorithm's schematic is shown in Fig. 25(e). Once the metasurface design at low resolution converges, it is used as the initial structure for the optimization at the high-resolution phase, thereby accelerating the convergence of the metasurface optimization at high resolution. By utilizing the strategy of spatial multiplexing, designs of pixelated metasurfaces for multiple wavelengths are interlaced (for more details and applications of the spatial multiplexing strategy, see Subsection 5.2), achieving the full-color metasurface hologram. The experimental holographic images of two such metasurface holograms are shown in Fig. 25(f). In recent years, machine learning has started to be applied in the design of pixelated metasurfaces. In 2021, Zhu et al. [437] implemented an inverse model based on transfer learning networks, which can directly generate the structure of pixelated meta-atoms from the target phase. In 2021 and 2024, Zhang et al. [412] and Chen et al. [438] relatively applied a surrogate model to accelerate the optimization of pixelated metasurfaces using genetic algorithms. These efforts help to explore the design space more efficiently and effectively shorten the design cycle of pixelated metasurfaces.

(III) For shape-optimized metasurfaces, both heuristic algorithms and gradient descent methods based on the adjoint method can be used for optimization, with the latter offering faster convergence and supporting larger device sizes. In 2020, Cai et al. [204] utilized the traditional forward design method and the evolutionary optimization method based on genetic algorithms to design a set of metalenses with different NAs. The metalenses are composed of cylindrical $TiO_{2}$ meta-atoms and operate at a wavelength of 532 nm. As shown in Fig. 25(g), the simulation and experimental comparison results regarding focusing efficiency (the focusing efficiency is defined as the focal spot power divided by the glass background power of the same size as the metalens), highlighted the superiority of inverse design, although the device diameter is limited to tens of micrometers. In 2021, Mansouree et al. [343] applied the gradient descent method based on adjoint method to the design of metasurfaces using rectangular a–Si pillar meta-atoms with a height of 430 nm. The experiment demonstrated a metalens operating at a wavelength of 840 nm, with a diameter of 50 $\mu$ m and an NA as high as 0.94. The absolute focusing efficiency (with an effective focusing area diameter of 7 $\mu$ m) was 49%, which was higher than the 43% achieved by the forward-designed metalens under identical conditions. In 2024, Bao et al. [439] further extended the applicability of this algorithm from non-rotatable rectangular pillar meta-atoms to rotatable polygonal pillar meta-atoms, thus capable of optimizing metasurfaces based on the hybrid phase modulation mechanism. Based on this method, they experimentally demonstrated a dual-focus metalens multiplexing orthogonal circular polarization states, with an NA of 0.65 and a focal length of 20 $\mu$ m. Operating at a wavelength of 940 nm, the metalens exhibited a average absolute focusing efficiency (with an effective focusing area diameter six times the FWHM of the focal spot) for LCP and RCP light of 58.81%. Another application of this method

is the multilayer metasurface design [191] introduced in Subsection 2.2.1. Compared to topology optimization, despite this method's lower design degrees of freedom, the shape of the meta-atoms is controllable and easier to fabricate, and the computational costs are slightly lower, thus supporting larger-area metasurface designs.

Counterintuitively, topology optimization can also facilitate the design of metasurfaces based on the shape optimization. This provides a new avenue for gradient optimization of metasurfaces made from meta-atoms with shapes that do not lend themselves to straightforward gradient calculation formulas of the adjoint method. This algorithm was proposed by Fan's group in 2024 [423], and was used for optimizing metasurfaces consisting of elliptical cylindrical meta-atoms for validation. The algorithm schematic is shown in Fig. 25(h). Characterizing the size and position of all meta-atoms by introducing a set of latent variables $p_1, p_2, \ldots, p_i, \ldots, p_N$ , the dielectric constant distribution $\epsilon(x, y)$ of the metasurface can be expressed as a function of these latent variables, denoted as $\tilde{\epsilon}(p_1, p_2, \ldots, p_i, \ldots, p_N)$ . By the chain rule of differentiation, the gradient of the optimization objective function $F$ with respect to the latent variable $p_i$ can be expressed as

$$
\frac {\partial F}{\partial p _ {i}} = \frac {\partial F}{\partial \tilde {\epsilon}} \frac {\partial \tilde {\epsilon}}{\partial p _ {i}}, \tag {161}
$$

where the first term on the right side represents the gradient of the objective function with respect to the dielectric constant distribution, which can be obtained through topology optimization, and the second term on the right side can be solved through backpropagation using automatic differentiation, even if the expression of $\tilde{\epsilon}\left(p_{1},p_{2},\ldots,p_{N}\right)$ may be very complex. The optimization trajectory, as depicted in Fig. 25(i), shows that an initial solution of random dielectric cylinders with varying gray levels quickly converges to an optimized solution of elliptical cylindrical meta-atoms.

# 3.4. Design Methods Based on Machine Learning

Machine learning in metasurface design primarily falls into two categories: first, substituting traditional electromagnetic simulation software to solve Maxwell's equations for specific cases (for instance, calculating the visible transmittance spectrum of cylindrical meta-atoms), and second, generating metasurface structural designs directly from design objectives $[440–442]$ . The former deals with the forward problem of relating specific structures to their optical responses, while the latter tackles the inverse problem of deducing unknown structures from desired optical responses. For convenience, these two types of machine learning models are respectively termed as forward models and inverse models, with the forward model also referred to as the surrogate model mentioned in Subsection 3.3. Given a certain tolerance for error, multiple structures might correspond to the same optical response, hence, these two types of problems usually exhibit many-to-one and one-to-many mapping relationships between inputs and outputs, respectively.

The general workflow for both forward models and inverse models includes several key steps. First, based on the characteristics of the problem, determine the model inputs and outputs, and choose an appropriate model (for deep learning, consider which neural network architecture to employ). Second, use electromagnetic simulation software to prepare the training dataset (the size and composition of the training set may need to be further adjusted if the underfitting or overfitting issue is observed after training the model). Finally, train, validate, and fine-tune the model. According to the amount and type of supervision during training, machine learning can be categorized into

four main types: supervised learning, unsupervised learning, semi-supervised learning, and reinforcement learning $[443]$ . From an algorithmic strategy perspective, machine learning algorithms mainly consist of discriminative algorithms and generative algorithms, with their corresponding machine learning models known as discriminative models and generative models, respectively. Roughly speaking, for many-to-one forward problems, discriminative algorithms are suitable for learning the mapping relationship between metasurface structures and their optical responses; whereas for one-to-many inverse problems, generative algorithms are appropriate for learning the pattern characteristics behind metasurface structures to generate new structural designs $[402]$ .

# 3.4.1. Forward Models and Their Applications in Metasurface Design

Compared to full-wave electromagnetic simulation methods, surrogate models based on machine learning offer the significant advantages of rapid solution and differentiability. However, achieving accurate predictions is not trivial, and this presents the most critical challenge. The difficulty stems from the complex mapping relationship between the model inputs and outputs in practical applications, such as when the metasurface structure allows for complex variations or when a wide spectral range is considered.

In 2019, Nadell et al. [444] implemented a surrogate model for solving the transmittance spectrum of the metasurface supercell, which is depicted on the left side of Fig. 26(a). Here, a supercell, also known as a molecule, refers to the metasurface unit consisting of multiple adjacent meta-atoms, as opposed to the unit containing only a single meta-atom. Although each meta-atom only has two variables, i.e., radius and height, and the entire supercell has only eight variables, considering each variable can take 13 discrete values within a certain range, the supercell can have a vast number of possible geometric shapes ( $\sim8\times10^{8}$ ). This exemplifies the potential for the curse of dimensionality that can arise with an increase in the number of variables in machine learning [443]. By the way, this instance also illustrates the difficulty of the forward design method in building the supercell database for the unbearable computational cost. Moreover, due to resonant effects and the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms, the transmittance spectrum can exhibit sudden changes, as indicated by the black arrow in the rightmost panel of Fig. 26(a). Therefore, for the frequency range from 0.8 THz to 1.5 THz, it was necessary to set 2003 discrete sampling points to avoid overlooking abrupt changes due to a low sampling rate. The cost of this is a severe mismatch between the dimensions of the model input and output. Addressing this issue inevitably requires complex neural network architectures and relatively large training sets. The neural network architecture used is shown in Fig. 26(a), and the dataset covered $\sim21,000$ different geometries of supercell and their simulated spectra. Ultimately, the model achieved an average mean square error (MSE) of $1.16\times10^{-3}$ on the evaluation set.

In 2019, An et al. [445] trained a surrogate model to solve for the amplitude and phase spectra of cylindrical meta-atoms with varying radii, heights, dielectric constants, and lattice constants within the frequency range from 30 THz to 60 THz. As shown in the top panel of Fig. 26(b), the amplitude and phase spectra of these meta-atoms exhibit multiple abrupt changes due to resonant effects, especially the discontinuity of $\pi$ of the phase, posing significant challenges to accurate prediction. To address this issue, the model's output was changed from the amplitude and phase spectra to the spectra of the real and imaginary parts of the complex amplitude, as shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 26(b). This adjustment made spectra smoother and non-singular, allowing for a lower sampling rate for spectral sampling (requiring only 31 sampling points) and avoiding the problem of input and output dimension mismatch. With a simple network
