Forward models and their applications in the inverse design of metasurfaces. (a) Schematic of the neural network architecture for predicting the transmittance spectrum of the supercell. (b) Top: the amplitude and phase distribution of a meta-atom. Bottom: the real and imaginary part distribution of the equivalent complex amplitude. (c) The neural network architecture of the surrogate model considering the coupling between meta-atoms. (d) Representation of the complex amplitudes in the polar graph of three types of meta-atom responses for a set of data randomly selected from the test set. (e) Schematic of the inverse design algorithm with the surrogate model based on the heuristic algorithm. (f) Schematic of the inverse design algorithm with the surrogate model based on the inverse model. (a) Adapted with permission from An et al., Opt. Express 27, 27523–27535 (2019) [444]. Copyright © 2019 Optica Publishing Group. (b) and (f) Adapted with permission from An et al., ACS Photonics 6, 3196–3207 (2019) [445]. Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society. (c)–(e) Adapted with permission from An et al., Adv. Opt. Mater. 10, 2102113 (2022) [406]. Copyright © 2021 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim.

architecture comprising only several fully connected layers and a dataset with the size of 50,000, the model achieved a relative prediction error of 0.5% for both amplitude and phase on the test set. Further testing indicated that this strategy is also applicable to meta-atoms with complex shapes (e.g., H-shaped meta-atoms).

Machine learning provides an opportunity for designing metasurfaces considering the influence of the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms, which is difficult for traditional forward design methods as discussed in Subsection 3.2.2. In 2022, An et al. [406] conducted simulation verification for this in a 1D scenario. The neural network, as shown in Fig. 26(c), considered the influence of the surrounding 8 meta-atoms on the target meta-atom. The meta-atoms were rectangular pillars with a refractive index of 3.67, a height of 270 nm, an operating wavelength of 1.55 $\mu$ m, a lattice constant of 800 nm, but different cross-sectional size parameters. The dataset size was 200,000. The trained model achieved an average prediction standard deviation of 0.005 and 3.15° for amplitude and phase, respectively, on the test set. For the scenario shown in Fig. 26(d), the periodic response from traditional forward simulation significantly differed from the local response with coupling considered in the simulation, while the surrogate model prediction closely matched the latter. Similarly, in 2021, Zhelyeznyakov et al. [427] demonstrated a 2D scenario considering the coupling between meta-atoms.

The training cost of surrogate models may increase rapidly with the complexity of the task, which can be mitigated to some extent through active learning techniques $[446]$ and data augmentation techniques $[447]$ .

Apart from the direct applications in forward problems, surrogate models can also be used for the inverse design of metasurfaces. Mathematically, a surrogate model can be seen as a vector-valued function $G(\vec{p})$ mapping the structure of the metasurface (represented by a vector of structural parameters $\vec{p}$ ) to its electromagnetic response (represented by a vector of response parameters $\vec{s}$ ). To obtain the optimal structure design $\vec{p}^{*}$ that achieves the target response $\vec{s}^{*}$ , one needs to solve the optimization problem

$$
\vec {p} ^ {\star} = \underset {\vec {p}} {\arg \min} D (G (\vec {p}), \vec {s} ^ {\star}), \tag {162}
$$

where D is a distance operator, which could be chosen as, for example, the Euclidean distance. To solve this optimization problem, there are primarily four algorithmic strategies.

(i) For all possible values of $\vec{p}$ , use the surrogate model to compute the corresponding output, build a lookup table, and then find the optimal solution through exhaustive search [444]. This method, due to its high time and space complexity, is suitable only for small-scale problems. Furthermore, since the structural parameters are often continuous, the lookup table inevitably leads to some loss of precision.  
(ii) For larger problem scales, as shown in Fig. 26(e), heuristic algorithms can be used to iteratively approximate the optimal solution, such as employing the double-sequence simulated annealing method [406].  
(iii) Utilizing the differentiability of the surrogate model, the gradient of the objective function $D$ with respect to the vector of structural parameters can be expressed as $\frac{\mathrm{d}D}{\mathrm{d}\vec{p}} = \frac{\mathrm{d}D}{\mathrm{d}G}\frac{\mathrm{d}G}{\mathrm{d}\vec{p}}$ , where the first term on the right side can be obtained directly from the expression of $D$ , and the second term can be obtained from the surrogate model through backpropagation. On this basis, the optimization problem can be solved using gradient descent methods [448,449].  
(iv) Combining the inverse model with the surrogate model, as shown in Fig. 26(f), the surrogate model can be utilized to train the inverse model to learn how to generate a set of metasurfaces that meet design requirements [445].

The approaches above showcase the versatility and potential of surrogate models in tackling the challenging problem of metasurface design.

# 3.4.2. Inverse Models and Their Applications in Metasurface Design

Compared to forward problems, inverse problems are more challenging due to the one-to-many mapping relationship between the model input and output. Generative models address this challenge by computing the distribution of all possible structures that can achieve similar target responses $[450]$ . Generative models that have been applied to the inverse design of metasurfaces include the Generative Adversarial Network (GAN) $[451–454]$ , Variational Auto-Encoder (VAE) $[455]$ , Compositional Pattern Producing Network (CPPN) $[456]$ , and others. These generative models offer powerful tools for exploring the vast design space of metasurfaces, enabling the discovery of novel structures that fulfill the target electromagnetic response.

Since Liu et al. [451] introduced the GAN into the inverse model of metasurface design in 2018, GANs have been widely applied in the inverse design of metasurfaces [452–454]. In the work by Liu et al., to directly generate the corresponding meta-atom structure (represented as a $64 \times 64$ binarized image) from the transmittance spectrum (represented by four vectors $\vec{\tau}_{xx}$ , $\vec{\tau}_{yy}$ , $\vec{\tau}_{xy} = \vec{\tau}_{yx}$ , each vector taking 32 frequency points at equal intervals from 170 THz to 600 THz), the inverse model consists of three relatively independent networks [all are convolutional neural networks (CNNs), just with some differences in configuration]: the simulator, the generator, and the discriminator, as depicted in Fig. 27(a). The simulator is a pre-trained surrogate model that remains unchanged during the training of the inverse model, which is used for ensuring the spectral accuracy of the generated meta-atom. The generator and the discriminator together form the GAN. During training, the discriminator's goal is to determine whether a meta-atom structure is generated by the generator or comes from the training data, while the generator aims to produce meta-atom structures that the discriminator cannot distinguish. In this way, the generator eventually learns the structural features of meta-atoms in the training data. As shown in Fig. 27(b), for a randomly generated transmittance spectrum, the inverse model can generate meta-atoms that exhibit consistent spectral characteristics. The most serious problem with this method is that, while this network architecture ensures stable convergence of the neural network, it also limits the model's freedom to discover novel structures, causing the structure of the meta-atoms generated from a target spectrum to be very similar to that of the meta-atoms associated with a spectrum that is close to the target spectrum in the training data. Further work was completed by An et al. in 2021 [454]. By combining the structure of the Wasserstein Generative Adversarial Network (WGAN) with the approach of the Conditional Generative Adversarial Network (CGAN), a new neural network architecture was proposed, as shown in Fig. 27(c). Given a target complex amplitude, the model generates a set of meta-atom structures represented as $64 \times 64$ binarized images. As shown in Fig. 27(d), the test result of the inverse model for a specific target complex amplitude indicates that the model has the advantages of being time-efficient, accurate in prediction, and strong in generalization capability.

In the forward design of metasurfaces, it's a common practice to cluster meta-atoms with similar geometric shapes into several groups. Fine-tuning the size parameters of each group can then yield similar optical responses. In 2019, Ma et al. [455] implemented this by incorporating a VAE into the neural network architecture, as shown in Fig. 27(e). Latent variables are introduced as a probabilistic representation of the meta-atom structure, encoding both the structure and its corresponding optical response into a high-dimensional latent space. New meta-atom designs are obtained by sampling and decoding in the latent variable space, thus allowing the generation of various geometric shapes of meta-atoms that meet the target optical response. Furthermore, the model supports a semi-supervised learning strategy, enabling performance improvement through unlabeled data. As a validation, the inverse model was trained to generate a set of meta-atoms (represented as $64 \times 64$ binarized images) from the reflectance spectrum (represented as four vectors $\vec{r}_{xx}, \vec{r}_{yy}, \vec{r}_{xy} = \vec{r}_{yx}$ , each vector taking 31 frequency points at equal intervals from 40 THz to 100 THz). After reducing the 20-dimensional latent space to a 2D space, the distribution of the latent variables is shown in Fig. 27(f), where meta-atoms cluster according to their shapes, indicating that the model has automatically learned how to distinguish between different shape types. The model's test results for a target reflectance spectrum, as shown in Fig. 27(h), reveal that the generated meta-atoms have different shape types and their reflectance spectra match well with the target, demonstrating the effectiveness of the model in generating meta-atoms with the desired optical responses.

Figure 27  
![](images/34eb9ab91a4f14b09a2f957bb5a8659fe6ad65b10c18902749e62016b3313b58.jpg)

Inverse models and their applications in the inverse design of metasurfaces. (a) Schematic of the neural network architecture of the inverse model based on GAN. (b) Left: the target transmittance spectrum. Right: the transmittance spectrum of the meta-atom (inset) generated by the model. (c) The neural network architecture of the inverse model based on WGAN and CGAN. (d) Left: the complex amplitude distribution (blue dots) of 100 meta-atoms generated by the inverse model for the target complex amplitude response (the red dot). Right: structures of the generated meta-atoms. The complex amplitude in the region surrounded by red lines has the absolute amplitude difference less than 0.1 and the absolute phase difference less than $10^{\circ}$ relative to the target complex amplitude. (e) Neural network architecture of the inverse model based on VAE. (f) Latent variable distribution in the latent space reduced from 20-dimensional space to 2D space. Red dots, green dots, and blue dots represent cross, split ring, and H-shaped meta-atoms, respectively. (g) Experimental holographic images of the 8-channel wavelength and polarization-multiplexed metasurface hologram. (h) Top: the target reflectance spectrum. Middle and bottom: the reflectance spectra of two meta-atoms (inset) generated by the inverse model based on VAE for the target reflectance spectrum. (i) Schematic of conditional Global Topology Optimization Network (GLONet) optimization algorithm. (j) The deflection (continued)

(continued) efficiencies of metagratings with different operating wavelengths and deflection angles, designed by topology optimization based on the adjoint method (top) and conditional GLONet optimization (bottom), respectively. (k) Embedding machine learning models in the gradient-based optimization loop for the multi-functional metalens design. (l) Embedding machine learning models in the Gerchberg–Saxton (GS) algorithm for the multi-functional metasurface hologram design. (a) and (b) Adapted with permission from Liu et al., Nano Lett. 18, 6570–6576 (2018) [451]. Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society. (c) and (d) Adapted with permission from An et al., Adv. Opt. Mater. 9, 2001433 (2021) [454]. Copyright © 2021 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (e), (f), and (h) Adapted with permission from Ma et al., Adv. Mater. 31, 1901111 (2019) [455]. Copyright © 2019 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (g), (k), and (l) Adapted with permission from Ma et al., Adv. Mater. 34, 2110022 (2022) [449]. Copyright © 2022 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (i) and (j) Adapted with permission from Jiang and Fan, Nano Lett. 19, 5366–5372 (2019) [415]. Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society.

Beyond merely utilizing inverse models for metasurface design $[457]$ , these models, along with potential forward models, can be integrated with optimization methods outlined in Subsection 3.3. This integration helps overcome the limitations of existing design approaches, thereby enabling the design of metasurfaces with enhanced performance and more complex functionalities.

In 2019, Jiang et al. [415,458] combined generative models with topology optimization to propose the unconditional and conditional Global Topology Optimization Networks (GLONets). The conditional GLONet is an upgrade of the unconditional GLONet, for the former can generate efficient 1D metagratings for specified wavelengths and deflection angles, while the latter is trained only for the fixed wavelength and deflection angle. The architecture of the conditional GLONet is shown in Fig. 27(i). The generative model takes as input a specified wavelength, deflection angle, and random noise vector, and outputs a 256-dimensional vector $\vec{p}$ consisting of structural parameters of a 1D metagrating, where each element in $\vec{p}$ represents the refractive index of a finite element of the metagrating, with values between that of air and the meta-atom material. The core of the algorithm is the use of topology optimization based on the adjoint method for model training. The goal of the generative model is to produce metagratings with as high a deflection efficiency $E$ as possible for any wavelength and deflection angle. In each iteration of model training, for any weight coefficient $w$ of the generative model, the gradient $\frac{\partial E}{\partial w}$ needs to be calculated for updating the model weights. By the chain rule of differentiation, we have

$$
\frac {\partial E}{\partial w} = \frac {\partial E}{\partial \vec {p}} \frac {\partial \vec {p}}{\partial w}, \tag {163}
$$

where the first term on the right side can be obtained by the adjoint method and the second term by neural network backpropagation. The wavelength range from 600 nm to 1300 nm and the deflection angles from 40° to 80° are covered by the generative model. By designing a set of metagratings for different wavelengths and deflection angles through both topology optimization and conditional GLONet and selecting the highest-efficiency device, the device efficiency comparison shown in Fig. 27(j) indicates that conditional GLONet, with its global nature, achieves higher device performance compared to topology optimization.

In 2022, Ma et al. [449] combined both forward and inverse models with optimization algorithms, achieving multifunctional metasurface designs with the number of

multiplexing channels beyond the reach of the traditional forward design method. The challenge with traditional methods is the need for $2\pi$ phase coverage for each multiplexed channel, which becomes impractical for a large number of channels even if the design space is theoretically capable, due to the prohibitive time required to build a complete database through enumeration. In this work, the forward model was built using a mix of the convolutional neural network and recurrent neural network with residual connections, enabling accurate prediction from the supercell structure to the reflectance spectrum, and the inverse model was essentially the same as the VAE-based model $[455]$ introduced earlier. Each supercell is composed of three rectangular pillar meta-atoms, as depicted in Fig. 27(k). Based on the models, two kinds of design algorithms were proposed.

(i) Combining the models with a gradient optimization algorithm, as shown in Fig. 27(k), for a given design goal, an initial metasurface design is generated using the inverse model, followed by further optimization based on gradient descent using the forward model. With this algorithm, an experimental multifocal metalens was implemented, featuring eight multiplexing channels comprising two orthogonally polarized states and four frequencies (270 THz, 350 THz, 375 THz, and 400 THz).  
(ii) Utilizing the models' rapid solution, as shown in Fig. 27(l), an end-to-end meta-surface hologram design method was proposed by combining the models with the CGH algorithm. With this algorithm, a Fresnel hologram multiplexing for the aforementioned eight channels was designed, and experimentally reconstructed holographic images are shown in Fig. 27(g).

# 4. METASURFACES FOR IMAGING: METALENSES

The metalens, a type of metasurface designed for focusing and imaging, has garnered increased attention from the research community due to its substantial benefits over traditional lenses, such as lighter weight, thinner profiles, multifunctionality, and easier integration. Such attributes have catalyzed significant advancements in achieving high-efficiency and high-quality imaging $[459–462]$ . Nowadays, metalenses have been widely applied in various fields, such as endoscopy $[463]$ , microscopic imaging $[340]$ , virtual reality $[464]$ , and polarization cameras $[465,466]$ , showcasing their potential to revolutionize numerous optical applications.

The design workflow for metalenses, including fundamental design and analysis techniques, is available in our open-source code repository $[73]$ .

# 4.1. Ideal Phase Distributions for Imaging

Let $\lambda_0$ be the wavelength of incident light in free space, $n_{\mathrm{back}}$ the refractive index of the background environment medium, $n_{\mathrm{meta}}$ the refractive index of the meta-atom material, $n_{\mathrm{cladding}}$ the refractive index of the material encapsulating the meta-atom structures [these refractive index-related symbols are distinguished in Fig. 6(a)], $c_0$ the speed of light in free space, $\lambda = \frac{\lambda_0}{n_{\mathrm{back}}}$ the wavelength of light in the environmental medium, $k = \frac{2\pi}{\lambda}$ the wavenumber of light in the environmental medium, and $\omega = kc = k\frac{c_0}{n_{\mathrm{back}}}$ the angular frequency. A coordinate system is established, as shown in Fig. 28(a). The angle between the projection of the light's direction vector on the $xOz$ ( $yOz$ ) plane and the $z$ -axis is defined as the $x$ -direction ( $y$ -direction) incident angle $\alpha_x$ ( $\alpha_y$ ). Since the angles in media with different refractive indices vary, we note that $\alpha_x$ and $\alpha_y$ are defined in the background environment medium. When the $x$ -component ( $y$ -component)

Figure 28  
![](images/948df478468ebf08296d396fc0bf84e1a1bb7a98a5e8ee59c1b633b91351525e.jpg)

The principle and ideal phase distributions of metalens. (a) Schematic of the focusing principle of metalens under the perpendicularly incident light. (b) Schematic of the focusing principle of metalens under the obliquely incident light. (c) The ideal phase profile when $x_{\mathrm{f}}(\alpha_{x}) = f \tan \alpha_{x}$ and $y_{\mathrm{f}}(\alpha_{y}) = f \tan \alpha_{y}$ . Here, $x \in [-f, f]$ , y = 0, $\alpha_{x} \in \left[-\frac{\pi}{2}, \frac{\pi}{2}\right]$ , $\alpha_{y} = 0$ , and $\lambda = 0.03f$ (the same as below). The ideal phase $\phi_{ideal}$ vs $\sin \alpha_{x}$ when x = -0.5f is drawn at the bottom (the same as below). (d) The ideal phase profile when $x_{\mathrm{f}}(\alpha_{x}) = f \alpha_{x}$ and $y_{\mathrm{f}}(\alpha_{y}) = f \alpha_{y}$ . (e) The ideal phase profile when $x_{\mathrm{f}}(\alpha_{x}) = f \sin \alpha_{x}$ and $y_{\mathrm{f}}(\alpha_{y}) = f \sin \alpha_{y}$ .

of the incident light's direction vector is in the same direction as the positive $x$ -axis (y-axis), $\alpha_{x}(\alpha_{y})$ is taken as positive; otherwise, it is negative. For example, the $x$ -direction incident angle $\alpha_{x}$ in Fig. 28(b) is positive.

An ideal lens can achieve aberration-free, ideal focusing on the focal plane for light of any angular frequency $\omega$ and incident angle $\left(\alpha_{x},\alpha_{y}\right)$ . Figures 28(a) and 28(b) show the scenarios of ideal focusing under normal and oblique incidences, respectively. The phase distribution of an ideal lens is defined as the ideal phase distribution $\phi_{\mathrm{ideal}}$ , which is a function of the coordinate $(x,y)$ on the lens, the angular frequency, and the incident angle. Light of different angular frequencies should focus at the same point on the focal plane to eliminate chromatic aberration. For light with different incident angles, the focusing position is a function of the incident angle, denoted as $\left(x_{\mathrm{f}}\left(\alpha_{x}\right),y_{\mathrm{f}}\left(\alpha_{y}\right)\right)$ . By Fermat's principle, the formula for the ideal phase distribution can be derived [467]

$$
\begin{array}{l} \phi_ {\text {ideal}} \left(x, y, \omega , \alpha_ {x}, \alpha_ {y}\right) \\ = - \frac {\omega}{c} \left(x \sin \alpha_ {x} + y \sin \alpha_ {y} + \sqrt {f ^ {2} + (x - x _ {\mathrm{f}}) ^ {2} + (y - y _ {\mathrm{f}}) ^ {2}} - \sqrt {f ^ {2} + x _ {\mathrm{f}} ^ {2} + y _ {\mathrm{f}} ^ {2}}\right) \\ + C \left(\omega , \alpha_ {x}, \alpha_ {y}\right), \tag {164} \\ \end{array}
$$

where $f$ is the lens's focal length, $C\left(\omega, \alpha_x, \alpha_y\right)$ is a position-independent constant term, indicating that adding a constant phase shift to the ideal phase distribution for a certain angular frequency and incident angle does not affect the focusing effect but can introduce a new design degree of freedom for achieving complex designs such as wide-FOV lenses [468] and broadband achromatic lenses [351]. For brevity, in Eq. (164), $x_{\mathrm{f}}\left(\alpha_x\right)$ and $y_{\mathrm{f}}\left(\alpha_y\right)$ are abbreviated as $x_{\mathrm{f}}$ and $y_{\mathrm{f}}$ , respectively.

For undistorted ideal imaging, $x_{\mathrm{f}}(\alpha_{x})$ and $y_{\mathrm{f}}(\alpha_{y})$ satisfy

$$
x _ {\mathrm{f}} \left(\alpha_ {x}\right) = f \tan \alpha_ {x}, \quad y _ {\mathrm{f}} \left(\alpha_ {y}\right) = f \tan \alpha_ {y}. \tag {165}
$$

In this case, the focusing effects of a metalens that meets the ideal phase distribution at different incident angles are shown in Figs. 29(a)–29(d). Unexpectedly, when $\alpha_{x}=60^{\circ}$ , the focal spot exhibits a slight broadening in the x direction, indicating a decrease in the effective NA. This phenomenon can be attributed to the constraint imposed by the spatial bandwidth product of the lens. When taking

$$
x _ {\mathrm{f}} \left(\alpha_ {x}\right) = f \alpha_ {x}, \quad y _ {\mathrm{f}} \left(\alpha_ {y}\right) = f \alpha_ {y}, \tag {166}
$$

or

$$
x _ {\mathrm{f}} \left(\alpha_ {x}\right) = f \sin \alpha_ {x}, \quad y _ {\mathrm{f}} \left(\alpha_ {y}\right) = f \sin \alpha_ {y}. \tag {167}
$$

Although distortions are intentionally introduced, comparing Figs. 28(c)–28(e) reveals that the dependence of the ideal phase distribution on the incident angle is reduced, i.e., the phase changes more flatly with the incident angle, making it easier to approximate with a phase distribution that does not depend on the incident angle. Moreover, while distortions may affect angular resolution in certain directions near the edge of FOV, they do not significantly degrade the intrinsic imaging resolution and can often be corrected through post-processing, albeit with additional computational cost. Therefore, this approach greatly aids in the design of metalenses with wide FOV [467].

For a monochromatic plane wave incident perpendicularly, as shown in Fig. 28(a), an ideal lens modifies the wavefront of the incident light from a plane to a spherical surface centered at the focal point. Substituting Eq. (165) into Eq. (164), and taking $\alpha_{x} = \alpha_{y} = 0$ for perpendicularly incident light, the ideal phase distribution becomes

$$
\phi_ {\text {hyperbolic}} (r, \omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - f\right) + C (\omega), \tag {168}
$$

where $r = \sqrt{x^{2} + y^{2}}$ is the distance from a point $(x, y)$ on the metalens to the center. This is the classic hyperbolic phase distribution. A metalens that conforms to this hyperbolic phase distribution is referred to as a hyperbolic metalens. Eq. (168) can further be simplified to

$$
\phi_ {\text {hyperbolic}} (r, \omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} + \frac {\omega}{c} f + C (\omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} + C ^ {\prime} (\omega). \tag {169}
$$

The hyperbolic phase distribution has the advantage of being free of the spherical aberration [469] and is suited for applications with small FOV. It's important to emphasize that, strictly speaking, the hyperbolic phase distribution is free of spherical aberration only for parallel incident light, i.e., light from an object at infinity. The wavefront of light from a point object at a finite distance is spherical, and when the point object is close to the lens, it cannot be approximated as a plane wave, resulting in significant

Figure 29  
![](images/a7c60ebe745e16a87d488e2d49f5894adc6b96b5c78dc0842dfbe417b6886e80.jpg)

<details>
<summary>heatmap</summary>

| Phase profile type | \(\alpha x = 0{}^{\circ}\) | \(\alpha x = 30{}^{\circ}\) | \(\alpha x = 60{}^{\circ}\) |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Ideal | Complex wave pattern with high intensity at center | Distinct wave pattern with high intensity at center | Distinct wave pattern with high intensity at center |
| Hyperbolic | Complex wave pattern with high intensity at center | Distinct wave pattern with high intensity at center | Distinct wave pattern with high intensity at center |
| Spherical | Complex wave pattern with high intensity at center | Distinct wave pattern with high intensity at center | Distinct wave pattern with high intensity at center |
| Quadratic | Complex wave pattern with high intensity at center | Distinct wave pattern with high intensity at center | Distinct wave pattern with high intensity at center |
</details>

Simulated focusing effects of metalenses with the ideal phase distribution (a)–(d), hyperbolic phase distribution (e)–(h), spherical phase distribution (i)–(l), and quadratic phase distribution (m)–(p). The first column shows the type of phase profiles. The second column shows the phase profiles of metalenses. Note that only the phase profile when $\alpha_{x}=0$ is given, considering the ideal phase distribution is dependent on the incident angle. The last three columns illustrate the focusing effect by showing the electric field intensity profiles on the xOz longitudinal section and the focal plane for metalenses when $\alpha_{x}$ equals $0^{\circ}$ , $30^{\circ}$ , and $60^{\circ}$ , respectively. The white dashed lines mark the ideal focus position, namely, $x_{\mathrm{f}}\left(\alpha_{x}\right)=f\tan\alpha_{x}, y_{\mathrm{f}}\left(\alpha_{y}\right)=f\tan\alpha_{y}, z_{f}=f$ for the first three phase profiles, and $x_{\mathrm{f}}\left(\alpha_{x}\right)=f\sin\alpha_{x}, y_{\mathrm{f}}\left(\alpha_{y}\right)=f\sin\alpha_{y}, z_{f}=f$ for the last phase profile. The green dashed lines mark the upper and lower bounds in the x direction of the metalens. Simulation parameters: the radius of the metalens is $12\ \mum$ , the focal length is $5\ \mum$ , the wavelength of the incident light in the environmental media is 633 nm, $\alpha_{y}$ is fixed at $0^{\circ}$ , the metalens is put at z=0, and $\rho=20\ \mum$ for the spherical phase profile.

spherical aberration [470]. For a point object at a fixed object distance, a phase distribution capable of correcting chromatic and spherical aberrations simultaneously can be derived based on the generalized law of refraction in Eq. (57), with more details available in references [470,471].

Given the material and structural limitations, meta-atoms exhibit limited dispersion characteristics, which may not simultaneously meet the phase distribution requirements across different wavelengths of an ideal or hyperbolic lens for multiple wavelengths or broadband operation. When the spatial dispersion of meta-atoms (see Subsection 2.2.2) is not considered, the phase shift introduced by a meta-atom is consistent for light incident at different angles. However, this contradicts the fact that the ideal phase distribution in Eq. (164) is angle-dependent. That is to say, unless angle-dependent electromagnetic responses are considered in the design, the target phase distribution adopted in the wide-FOV metalens design must inevitably be a compromise among the ideal phase distributions at various incident angles. Differences between the target phase distribution and the ideal phase distributions will further result in limitations regarding NA, efficiency, and other aspects $[472,473]$ .

# 4.2. Design Metrics and Analysis of Metalenses

This Subsection introduces the main design metrics in metalens design, as well as the relationships and constraints among these indicators. Achieving excellence across multiple performance indicators simultaneously is particularly challenging. In practice, it often requires a balance, focusing on the most crucial indicators based on specific application scenarios. When evaluating the performance of metalenses, it is recommended to consult the comment $[474]$ to maintain uniformity in the evaluation criteria for metalenses.

Except for specially designed Janus metasurfaces $[475–477]$ (discussed in Subsection 5.2.4), metasurfaces typically exhibit the same electromagnetic response and corresponding device function for both forward and backward incidences. Therefore, the performance metrics of a metalens are generally not differentiated between the object and image sides as with traditional optical systems.

Considering monochromatic light incident at a certain angle, due to differences between the target phase distribution used in the design and the ideal phase distribution, discrepancies between the actual electromagnetic response and the design target, as well as the effect of discretizing the continuous phase distribution determined by the lattice constant, the actual wavefront always differs from the ideal spherical wavefront, resulting in varying focusing effects and thereby affecting imaging resolution. This can be measured by indicators such as the point spread function (PSF), Strehl ratio, and modulation transfer function (MTF) [478]. The PSF is defined as the image formed by a point object through the optical system, the Strehl ratio is defined as the ratio of the PSF peak of the actual lens to that of an ideal lens, and the MTF is defined as the magnitude of the Fourier transform of the PSF. The PSF of an ideal lens is the Airy pattern, whose function can be expressed as

$$
I _ {\text {Airy}} (\xi) = I _ {0} \left(\frac {2 J _ {1} (\xi)}{\xi}\right) ^ {2}, \tag {170}
$$

where $I_{0}$ is the maximum light intensity at the center of the Airy pattern, and $J_{1}(\xi)$ is the first-order Bessel function of the first kind. The experimentally measured PSF $I_{\mathrm{PSF}}(x)$ ( $x \in [-x_{\mathrm{m}}, x_{\mathrm{m}}]$ , meaning that the measured PSF is always truncated to a limited physical size of $2x_{m}$ ) requires normalization, which can be done using the property that the power of the actual PSF and the ideal PSF (the Airy pattern) are consistent, namely [479]

$$
\int_ {- \infty} ^ {+ \infty} I _ {\mathrm{Airy}} (\xi) \mathrm{d} \xi = \int_ {- x _ {\mathrm{m}}} ^ {x _ {\mathrm{m}}} I _ {\mathrm{PSF}} (x) \mathrm{d} x. \tag {171}
$$

Substituting Eq. (170) into Eq. (171), $I_0$ can be solved. It's noted that the experimentally measured $I_{\mathrm{PSF}}(x)$ is always discrete, so the integral on the far right of Eq. (171) is taken as a summation in actual calculations. Thus, the expression for the normalized PSF $\bar{I}_{\mathrm{PSF}}(x)$ is

$$
\bar {I} _ {\mathrm{PSF}} (x) = \frac {1}{I _ {0}} I _ {\mathrm{PSF}} (x). \tag {172}
$$

Clearly, the Strehl ratio is simply $\bar{I}_{\mathrm{PSF}}(0)$ . When the Strehl ratio exceeds 0.8, it is considered that the difference between the actual PSF and the Airy pattern can be neglected [90], meaning the optical system's aberrations are within acceptable limits and do not affect the resolution at the diffraction limit. In 2023, Menon's group [479] pointed out that in the experimental measurement of these indicators, if the actual PSF is prematurely truncated due to the limited FOV of the magnifying objective, the Strehl ratio and MTF could be overestimated. Irregularities in actual PSF measurements can lead

to a loss of credibility in characterizing the performance of metalenses, which is detrimental to the verification of theoretical analyses and the long-term development of the field.

Taking metasurfaces operating in transmission mode as an example, now we introduce the relevant indicators describing the efficiency of metalenses. The indicators for metasurfaces operating in reflection mode can be obtained similarly.

The transmittance $\tau$ is defined as the ratio of the transmitted light power $P_{out}$ through the metasurface to the incident light power $P_{in}$ , i.e.,

$$
\tau = \frac {P _ {\text {out}}}{P _ {\text {in}}}. \tag {173}
$$

A higher transmittance of the metasurface implies less energy loss due to reflection and absorption.

Effective focusing is defined as the concentration of incident light into a circular area of a certain diameter (typically an iris in experiments) in the center of the focal plane. Unfortunately, the choice of diameter for the effective focusing circular area is not consistent across studies, leading to significant difficulties in comparing the focusing efficiency of different metalenses, and some studies overestimate the focusing efficiency of metalenses $[479]$ . Specifying the diameter of the effective focusing area can alleviate this problem to some extent. This is the approach maintained in this tutorial, unless no related data are found.

The absolute focusing efficiency ( $\eta_{AFE}$ ) is defined as

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{AFE}} = \frac {P _ {\text {focusing}}}{P _ {\text {in}}}, \tag {174}
$$

where $P_{focusing}$ is the focused light power within the effective focusing area. The relative focusing efficiency ( $\eta_{RFE}$ ) is defined as

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{RFE}} = \frac {P _ {\text {focusing}}}{P _ {\text {out}}} \tag {175}
$$

The relative focusing efficiency (more strictly, $\frac{\eta_{RFE}}{1-\eta_{RFE}}$ ) measures the energy ratio of the focused transmitted light to the unfocused transmitted light, the latter of which can cause background noise and secondary foci, resulting in haze and ghost images, respectively [480]. The relationship among these three indicators is

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{AFE}} = \tau \eta_ {\mathrm{RFE}}, \tag {176}
$$

which means that the absolute focusing efficiency accounts for both the efficiency losses due to reflection and absorption by the metasurface (quantified by transmittance) and the focusing effect of the transmitted light, as modulated by the metalens (quantified by relative focusing efficiency) [481].

Another similar set of indicators is the absolute diffraction efficiency $\eta_{ADE}$ and relative diffraction efficiency $\eta_{ADE}$ , which are defined as

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{ADE}} = \frac {P _ {\text {diffraction}}}{P _ {\text {in}}}, \tag {177}
$$

and

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{RDE}} = \frac {P _ {\text {diffraction}}}{P _ {\text {out}}}, \tag {178}
$$

respectively, where $P_{diffraction}$ is the light power reaching the target diffraction order. Here, the target diffraction order refers to the entire area from the center of the PSF to where it completely flattens [482]. Similarly, we have

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{ADE}} = \tau \eta_ {\mathrm{RDE}}. \tag {179}
$$

The relative diffraction efficiency (more strictly, $\frac{\eta_{RDE}}{1-\eta_{RDE}}$ ) quantifies the relative magnitude of the light power in the target diffraction order to the stray background light (known in photography as veiling glare), which is just the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) of the imaging.

Without specifying absolute or relative, focusing efficiency and diffraction efficiency generally refer to absolute focusing efficiency and absolute diffraction efficiency, respectively.

Note that the above evaluation of efficiency does not take into account the fact that some metasurfaces operate under the specific polarization states for incident and output light, for which it is necessary to introduce additional indicators. For instance, metasurfaces based on geometric phase require the introduction of the PCE, with relevant definitions and discussions found in Subsection 2.1.3.

In the monochromatic case, the two most important metrics describing imaging performance are resolution and SNR. Levy's group recommends using the MTF to characterize resolution and using transmittance and absolute diffraction efficiency to represent SNR [474]. There are two main reasons why focusing on efficiency is not considered a suitable metric. First, as mentioned above, the effective focusing area diameter used to evaluate focusing efficiency varies across different studies, leading to inconsistency. Second, and more fundamentally, focusing efficiency inherently intertwines both resolution and SNR, making it less suitable for clear and independent analysis [474].

The NA is a dimensionless parameter that describes the angular range over which an optical system can collect or emit light, which is defined as

$$
\mathrm{NA} = n _ {\text {back}} \sin \theta , \tag {180}
$$

where $\theta$ is half the angle of the maximum light cone entering or leaving the lens. As shown in Fig. 28(a), when examining the image side, the NA relates to the focal length f and the radius R of the metalens as

$$
\mathrm{NA} = n _ {\mathrm{back}} \sin \left(\arctan \left(\frac {R}{f}\right)\right). \tag {181}
$$

The NA is also a crucial indicator of imaging resolution. Under the diffraction limit, the FWHM of the metalens's focal spot satisfies

$$
\mathrm{FWHM} \approx 0. 5 1 \frac {\lambda_ {0}}{\mathrm{NA}}, \tag {182}
$$

indicating that the imaging resolution is inversely proportional to the NA, i.e., with a fixed wavelength, metalenses with a larger NA can theoretically support higher resolutions. However, it must be noted that metalenses often struggle to reach the theoretical limit of imaging resolution in practice due to various aberrations $[471]$ . It's important to emphasize that merely comparing the experimentally measured FWHM with Eq. (182) is not sufficient to assert that the imaging has reached the diffraction limit, as aberrations tend to redistribute light energy from the central lobe of the focal spot to the side lobes rather than changing the width of the central lobe $[474]$ . As mentioned earlier, comparing the actual MTF of a metalens with the ideal MTF under the diffraction limit is a better approach.

Due to the certain physical dimensions of the unit in a metasurface, the actual phase distribution is always a discretized approximation of the continuous target phase distribution. When sampling a phase range of $2\pi$ using N units, the phase gradient, denoted as $\frac{d\phi}{d\vec{r}} = \frac{\partial\phi}{\partial x}\vec{e}_{x} + \frac{\partial\phi}{\partial y}\vec{e}_{y}$ (where $\vec{r} = x\vec{e}_{x} + y\vec{e}_{y}$ ), is limited by (the discussion here considers the square units)

$$
\left| \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} \right| \leq \frac {2 \pi}{N a}, \tag {183}
$$

where $a$ is the lattice constant. The smaller $N$ is, the larger the error between the actual wavefront generated by the discretized phase distribution and the ideal wavefront, which can result in lower focusing efficiency and more severe imaging aberrations. According to the Nyquist sampling criterion, the sampling period should be less than half the signal period to fully resolve the high-frequency components. Therefore, at least two meta-atoms are required to sample a spatial phase change of $2\pi$ [483], i.e.,

$$
N \geq 2. \tag {184}
$$

Substituting Eq. (184) into Eq. (183), we obtain

$$
\left| \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} \right| \leq \frac {\pi}{a}, \tag {185}
$$

which means that the maximum absolute value of the phase gradient supported by the metasurface is $\frac{\pi}{a}$ , limited by the Nyquist sampling criterion, instead of $\frac{2\pi}{a}$ [when taking N = 1 in Eq. (183)].

For the hyperbolic phase distribution, the phase gradient $\frac{d\phi}{dr}$ (since the phase distribution is solely a function of r, the phase gradient can be expressed in scalar form) is

$$
\frac {\mathrm{d} \phi}{\mathrm{d} r} = \frac {\partial \phi_ {\text {hyperbolic}} (r , \omega)}{\partial r} = - \frac {\omega}{c} \frac {r}{\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}}}. \tag {186}
$$

Clearly, the phase gradient above must satisfy Eq. (183), that is, it is limited by the lattice constant and the Nyquist sampling criterion. By substituting Eq. (186) into Eq. (183), we can solve for the maximum theoretically feasible radius of the metalens as

$$
r _ {\max} = \frac {f}{\sqrt {(N a / \lambda) ^ {2} - 1}}. \tag {187}
$$

The actual radius $R$ of the metalens must satisfy $R \leq r_{\mathrm{max}}$ . When $R > r_{\mathrm{max}}$ , the contribution of regions with radii exceeding $r_{\mathrm{max}}$ on the metalens is questionable. Using Eq. (181), when $R = r_{\mathrm{max}}$ , the maximum theoretically feasible NA of the metalens can be derived as

$$
\mathrm{NA} _ {\max} = n _ {\text {back}} \sin \left(\arctan \left(\frac {1}{\sqrt {(N a / \lambda) ^ {2} - 1}}\right)\right). \tag {188}
$$

Equations (187) and (188) reveal the limitations imposed by the lattice constant on the radius and NA of the metalens. When the lattice constant satisfies

$$
a = \frac {\lambda}{N}, \tag {189}
$$

we have $r_{max} \rightarrow \infty$ and NA $\rightarrow n_{back}$ , meaning that, from a theoretical perspective, a metalens operating under perpendicular incident monochromatic light would not have size limitations, and the achievable maximum NA would reach the upper limit of a lens.

Generally speaking, due to constraints on design degrees of freedom and fabrication processes, the lattice constant is chosen to be around half the wavelength, leading to a maximum size limitation for metasurfaces as given by Eq. (187), although inverse design methods may slightly relax this limitation. This presents a challenge in designing large-area metalenses. Additionally, considering a metalens with the radius of R, a larger NA means a shorter focal length [according to Eq. (181)]; thus the maximum radius $r_{max}$ that the metasurface can support becomes smaller [according to Eq. (187)]. This indicates there is a fundamental trade-off between the NA and the maximum achievable size of the metalens.

To achieve a lens with a specific NA, using Eq. (181), the maximum magnitude of the transverse wavevector required is $k \sin \theta = \frac{2\pi}{\lambda_0} \mathrm{NA}$ . Thus, for the phase gradient provided by the metasurface to meet the lens's requirements regarding the transverse wavevector, we have

$$
\left| \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} \right| \geq \frac {2 \pi}{\lambda_ {0}} \mathrm{NA}. \tag {190}
$$

Substituting Eq. (183) into Eq. (190), i.e.,

$$
\frac {2 \pi}{\lambda_ {0}} \mathrm{NA} \leq \left| \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} \right| \leq \frac {2 \pi}{N a}, \tag {191}
$$

we obtain

$$
a \leq \frac {\lambda_ {0}}{N \cdot \mathrm{NA}}. \tag {192}
$$

At the Nyquist sampling limit, i.e., $N = 2$ , we have

$$
a \leq \frac {\lambda_ {0}}{2 \cdot \mathrm{NA}}. \tag {193}
$$

When the lattice constant satisfies Eq. (193), it ensures that the phase gradient provided by the metasurface is sufficient to deflect the light incident perpendicularly at any position on the metalens to the focus. However, Eq. (193) does not guarantee the exclusion of higher-order diffraction orders $[484,485]$ . It can be seen from Eq. (192) that a sufficiently small lattice constant must be chosen to obtain a high-NA metalens, and the shorter the operating wavelength, the smaller the required lattice constant. However, the lattice constant is constrained by several factors. First, in terms of the meta-atom design, smaller units result in more limited design spaces, making it difficult to achieve a $2\pi$ phase shift coverage while maintaining high efficiency $[486,487]$ . Furthermore, smaller units mean shorter distances between meta-atoms, potentially exacerbating the coupling among them. Additionally, the fabrication process imposes limits on reducing lattice constants excessively.

# 4.3. Design of Metalenses with Wide Field of View

The key factor limiting the FOV is the presence of various off-axis aberrations, particularly coma, which leads to asymmetric imaging and severely degrades image quality. As shown in Figs. 29(e)–29(h), despite the hyperbolic phase distribution offering the advantages of being free from the spherical aberration and distortion, it introduces significant coma $[467,478]$ . Therefore, it is necessary to introduce new phase distributions to accommodate imaging applications with a wide FOV. When the spatial dispersion is not considered, the new target phase distribution must be independent of the angle of incidence. In this case, the phase distribution of a single planar metasurface cannot perfectly correct both the spherical aberration and coma simultaneously $[467,478]$ .

Any phase distribution will be a compromise between the spherical aberration, coma, numerical aperture, and efficiency $[472]$ . Therefore, the key lies in finding the optimal compromise for specific application scenarios.

# 4.3.1. Wide-FOV Metalenses Based on the Optimized Phase Distribution

A common approach in designing wide-FOV metalenses is to obtain the target phase distribution through optimization based on methods like ray tracing $[179,185]$ . A typical form of the target phase distribution, proposed by Capasso's group $[185]$ , involves modifying the hyperbolic phase distribution in Eq. (168) by introducing several polynomial terms to be optimized:

$$
\phi_ {\text {optim}} (r, \omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - f\right) + \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {n} c _ {i} \left(\frac {r}{R}\right) ^ {2 i}, \tag {194}
$$

where $c_{i}$ is the coefficient of the polynomial term to be optimized, and $n$ is the number of polynomial terms. The hyperbolic phase distribution term in Eq. (194) can be omitted [480,488]. The core reason why the optimized phase distribution can eliminate coma lies in introducing an effective aperture that moves laterally with the incident angle [472]. The effective aperture is not a physical aperture but a region within the metalens that actually contributes to focusing the light. Its mechanism is that, although it's difficult to introduce angle-dependent phase control, the effective aperture can delineate an angle-dependent active region on the metalens, causing incident light at different angles to interact with different areas of the metalens. This essentially constitutes a spatial multiplexing strategy. From the perspective of the effective aperture, the analysis by Liang et al. [472] further shows that the above-optimized phase distribution exhibits consistent characteristics with the phase distribution of the traditional spherical lens (hereinafter referred to as the spherical phase distribution), which is mathematically expressed as

$$
\phi_ {\text {spherical}} (r, \omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \frac {\rho}{f} \left(\rho - \sqrt {\rho^ {2} - r ^ {2}}\right), \tag {195}
$$

where $\rho$ is the radius of curvature of the spherical lens. The focusing effects of a metalens following the spherical phase distribution at different incident angles are shown in Figs. 29(j)–29(l). Compared to the hyperbolic phase distribution, the spherical phase distribution exhibits significantly less coma, although it also presents distortions, which are difficult to avoid in wide-FOV imaging. As a trade-off, the effective aperture limits the NA of the imaging system, and regions outside the effective aperture may produce stray light, leading to a reduction in the imaging SNR. Therefore, it is best to restrict it with an additional aperture in front [472], as shown in Fig. 30(a). If the optimized phase distribution in Eq. (194) introduces significant spherical aberration, an additional metalens can be added to correct it. This corrective metalens can be overlapped with the aperture to make the system more compact, as shown in Fig. 30(b). In addition, the doublet metasurface architecture can also be employed to eliminate distortion in wide-FOV imaging, as illustrated in Fig. 30(c) [489].

Based on this approach, in 2016, Arbabi et al. [179] achieved a dual-metalens imaging system with an FOV of $60^{\circ}$ , as introduced in Subsection 2.2.1. In 2017, Groever et al. [185] utilized the system design shown in Fig. 30(b) to realize a dual-metalens system operating at a wavelength of 532 nm, with a focal length of 342.5 $\mu$ m, an NA of 0.44, an absolute focusing efficiency of about 50%, and an FOV of $50^{\circ}$ . Two $TiO_{2}$ metasurfaces based on the geometric phase were fabricated on both sides of a silica substrate with a thickness of 0.5 mm. The MTF curves at different FOVs, as shown in Fig. 30(d), demonstrate near-diffraction-limit resolution at various incident angles. In 2020, Shalaginov et al. [488] employed the system structure shown in Fig. 30(a)

Design methods for wide-FOV metalenses. (a) Schematic of a wide-FOV imaging system composed of a single metalens and a front aperture. (b) Schematic of a wide-FOV imaging system composed of two metalenses, with one metalens overlapping with the aperture. (c) Schematic of a wide-FOV imaging system composed of two metalenses and a front aperture. (d) The MTF curve along the x direction at different incident angles of an imaging system composed of two metalenses. (e) Imaging results at different incident angles obtained with an imaging system composed of a metalens and an aperture. (f) The measured MTF curve at different incident angles of an imaging system composed of a metalens and an aperture. The horizontal axis is in units of line pairs per millimeter. Dashed curves: tangential MTF. Solid curves: sagittal MTF. Inset: schematic of the imaging system. The metalens substrate thickness is 0.45 mm, the quartz substrate thickness is 4.99 mm, and the aperture diameter is 1 mm. (g) Imaging result obtained with a near-infrared wide-FOV metalens system. Inset: imaging setup. (h) Schematic of correcting the spherical aberration of a quadratic metalens using an extra metasurface. (i) The imaging result of a USAF 1964 chart obtained with a quadratic metalens. (j) Imaging result of a quadratic metalens camera. Inset: imaging setup. (k) Imaging of a ruler taken with a quadratic metalens. (l) Schematic of utilizing a wavevector filter to alleviate the spherical aberration of the quadratic metalens. (m) Comparison between the imaging results obtained with a quadratic metalens, whether to use the wavevector filter or not.
