KGaA, Weinheim.

to design a fisheye metalens system operating at a wavelength of 5.2 $\mu$ m, with a focal length of 2 mm, a diameter of 5.2 mm, and an effective NA of 0.24. The meta-atoms were made of PbTe with a lattice constant of 2.5 $\mu$ m, and the substrate was made of $CaF_{2}$ with a thickness of 2 mm. The imaging results at different incident angles, as shown in Fig. 30(e), exhibited a large FOV close to 180°.

As illustrated in Fig. 30(a) or 30(b), the larger the FOV required by such systems, the larger the size of the metalens needed. Hence, the size of the metalens restricts the maximum achievable FOV. Using a medium with a higher refractive index as the spacer between the aperture and the metalens can induce greater bending of light, thereby mitigating the size requirements of the metalens due to the FOV to some extent $[488]$ .

In 2021, Luo's group [158] used the system shown in the inset of Fig. 30(f) to develop a metalens system operating at a wavelength of $940~\mathrm{nm}$ , with a focal length of $4.48~\mathrm{mm}$ , a diameter of $12~\mathrm{mm}$ , and a large FOV of $178^{\circ}$ . By utilizing an optimized, streamlined Si metasurface to make phase control spatially continuous, the system exhibited an absolute diffraction efficiency above $80\%$ and a relative diffraction efficiency close to $100\%$ at all incident angles. The MTF curves at different FOVs, as shown in Fig. 30(f), indicate the near-diffraction-limit resolution of the metalens system at various incident angles. Under the illumination of an ordinary near-infrared LED light source, the imaging result with an ultra-wide FOV is shown in Fig. 30(g).

The thickness of the aforementioned non-single-element imaging systems is comparable to or even exceeds the focal length, which negates the advantage of the ultrathin thinness of metasurface devices. This could be understood with a straightforward explanation. For simplicity, we consider the case in Fig. 30(b) when $\alpha_{y}=0$ . To deflect the incident light at an angle of $\alpha_{x}$ to the position of $x_{\mathrm{f}}(\alpha_{x})$ on the focal plane, the thickness d of the medium between the aperture metalens and the focusing metalens satisfies [492]

$$
d \left(\alpha_ {x}\right) \approx \frac {x _ {\mathrm{f}} \left(\alpha_ {x}\right)}{\tan \left(\sin^ {- 1} \frac {\sin \alpha_ {x}}{n _ {\mathrm{gap}}}\right)}, \tag {196}
$$

where $n_{gap}$ is the refractive index of the medium. This preliminarily explains why the thickness of the medium layer is comparable to the focal length.

It should be noted that there indeed exists a fundamental trade-off between the FOV and the thickness of the imaging system, namely Eq. (153) introduced in Subsection 2.3.1. However, the thickness of the aforementioned metalens systems, as well as the thickness values derived from Eq. (196), have not yet reached this theoretical limit. It's possible to realize thinner wide-FOV imaging systems through introducing new design methods.

# 4.3.2. Wide-FOV Metalenses Based on the Quadratic Phase Distribution

The quadratic phase distribution was first proposed by Luo's group in 2017 [487], with its expression as follows:

$$
\phi_ {\text {quadratic}} (r, \omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \frac {1}{2 f} r ^ {2} + C (\omega). \tag {197}
$$

A metalens that conforms to this phase distribution is referred to as a quadratic metalens. When light is incident perpendicularly, the phase distribution of the light emerging from the metalens is

$$
\phi_ {\text {out}} (x, y, \omega) = \phi_ {\text {quadratic}} (r, \omega). \tag {198}
$$

Now, we consider light incident at angles $\alpha_{x}$ and $\alpha_{y}$ in the x and y directions, respectively. Due to the oblique incidence, the light arriving at the metalens already carries an initial phase distribution of

$$
\phi_ {\text {init}} (x, y, \omega) = k x \sin \alpha_ {x} + k y \sin \alpha_ {y}. \tag {199}
$$

Here we assume the initial phase is 0 when x = 0 and y = 0. Thus, the phase distribution of the light emerging from the metalens is the sum of this initial phase distribution and

Eq. (197), i.e.,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \phi_ {\text {out}} (x, y, \omega) = \phi_ {\text {init}} (x, y, \omega) + \phi_ {\text {quadratic}} (r, \omega) \\ = - \frac {\omega}{c} \frac {1}{2 f} \left(\left(x - f \sin \alpha_ {x}\right) ^ {2} + \left(y - f \sin \alpha_ {y}\right) ^ {2}\right) + C ^ {\prime} (\omega), \tag {200} \\ \end{array}
$$

where $C'(\omega) = C(\omega) + \frac{\omega f}{2c}\left(\sin^2\alpha_x + \sin^2\alpha_y\right)$ is a position-independent phase term. From Eq. (200), it is apparent that for light incident at the angle of $(\alpha_x, \alpha_y)$ , compared to perpendicularly incident light, the only difference is the movement of the focal point on the focal plane from position (0,0) to position $(f\sin \alpha_x, f\sin \alpha_y)$ , with no other differences in focusing effects or any other aspects. In this case, although the distortion described by Eq. (167) is introduced, other off-axis aberrations such as coma are effectively eliminated. The correction of coma by a quadratic metalens can be intuitively seen in Figs. 29(m)-29(p).

From a physical perspective, we could further elucidate on the aforementioned characteristic of the quadratic phase distribution. From Eq. (200), the phase gradient of the emerging light's phase distribution is

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi_ {\text {out}}}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} = \frac {\partial \phi_ {\text {out}} (x , y , \omega)}{\partial x} \vec {e} _ {x} + \frac {\partial \phi_ {\text {out}} (x , y , \omega)}{\partial y} \vec {e} _ {y} \tag {201} \\ = - \frac {\omega}{c f} \left(\left(x - f \sin \alpha_ {x}\right) \vec {e} _ {x} + \left(y - f \sin \alpha_ {y}\right) \vec {e} _ {y}\right). \\ \end{array}
$$

Using Eq. (65), for areas where $\left|\frac{d\phi}{d\vec{r}}\right|>k$ , the output light becomes an evanescent wave. Thus, the positions of the propagating waves satisfy

$$
\left| \frac {\mathrm{d} \phi_ {\mathrm{out}}}{\mathrm{d} \vec {r}} \right| <   k. \tag {202}
$$

Substituting Eq. (201) into Eq. (202), we obtain

$$
(x - f \sin \alpha_ {x}) ^ {2} + (y - f \sin \alpha_ {y}) ^ {2} <   f ^ {2}, \tag {203}
$$

which implies that for light incident at the angle of $(\alpha_{x}, \alpha_{y})$ , only a circular region centered at $(f \sin \alpha_{x}, f \sin \alpha_{y})$ with a radius $r_{v} = f$ is permitted to pass through. As shown in Figs. 29(n)–29(p), such a non-physical aperture that moves with the incident angle is referred to as a virtual aperture. Light incident outside the virtual aperture undergoes total internal reflection and contributes neither to focusing nor to stray light, suggesting that theoretically, a quadratic metalens does not need an additional aperture to filter out stray light. For wide-FOV imaging, the virtual aperture plays a similar role to the effective aperture mentioned earlier. Therefore, the limitation for NA brought by the effective aperture also applies to quadratic metalenses. Specifically, substituting the virtual aperture radius $r_{v}$ into Eq. (181) (taking $R = r_{v} = f$ ), we obtain the upper limit of the NA

$$
\mathrm{NA} _ {\max, \text {quadratic}} = \frac {n _ {\text {back}}}{\sqrt {2}}, \tag {204}
$$

meaning that even if the NA derived directly from Eq. (181) exceeds this limit, the actual NA can only reach up to $NA_{max,quadratic}$ [483].

Ideally, a quadratic metalens has an FOV of $180^{\circ}$ [483]. However, in practice, we may not be able to achieve a lattice constant small enough to satisfy the requirements of the quadratic phase distribution, where phase gradients in the x and y directions linearly increase with x and y, as described by Eq. (201). This consequently limits the maximum achievable FOV. Substituting Eq. (201) into (183), the maximum feasible radius

of the metalens is

$$
r _ {\max} = \frac {2 \pi c f}{N a \omega} = \frac {\lambda f}{N a}. \tag {205}
$$

With Eq. (203), when ensuring an NA of $NA_{max,quadratic}$ across all angles of incidence, the maximum achievable FOV is

$$
\mathrm{FOV} _ {\max, \text {quadratic}} = 2 \alpha_ {\max, \text {quadratic}} = 2 \arcsin \left(\frac {r _ {\max} - f}{f}\right) = 2 \arcsin \left(\frac {\lambda}{N a} - 1\right). \tag {206}
$$

The final step in the above equation relies on Eq. (205). For incident light with an FOV slightly higher than $FOV_{max,quadratic}$ , its NA will be lower than $NA_{max,quadratic}$ .

Based on Eq. (206), we further discuss three scenarios based on different values of $r_{max}$ [the value of $r_{max}$ is determined by Eq. (205)].

(i) When $r_{max} = 2f$ , we have $FOV_{max,quadratic} = 180^{\circ}$ , meaning the effective radius of the quadratic metalens need only reach 2f to achieve imaging with an NA of $NA_{max,quadratic}$ for all incident angles. When taking R > 2f, areas of r > 2f on the metalens contribute nothing to focusing.  
(ii) When $r_{\mathrm{max}} < f$ , no reasonable solution exists for $\mathrm{FOV}_{\mathrm{max,quadratic}}$ , indicating the NA cannot reach $\mathrm{NA}_{\mathrm{max,quadratic}}$ at any incident angle. For a metalens with radius $R \leq r_{\mathrm{max}}$ , when light is incident perpendicularly, it achieves the maximum NA of $n_{\mathrm{back}} \sin \left(\arctan \frac{R}{f}\right)$ . As the angle of incidence increases, the NA decreases, leading to a reduction in imaging resolution, and the area of the metalens that contributes to focusing decreases, resulting in a diminish in image illumination.  
(iii) When $f \leq r_{\mathrm{max}} \leq 2f$ , Eq. (206) provides the maximum incident angle that satisfies $\mathrm{NA} = \mathrm{NA}_{\mathrm{max, quadratic}}$ . As the incident angle increases beyond this limit, both the imaging resolution and illumination decrease due to the reasons discussed in (ii).

The analysis above suggests a fundamental trade-off between the FOV and NA for the quadratic metalens. This trade-off arises naturally as a consequence of the limited spatial bandwidth product of the optical system $[493]$ .

In 2018, Liu et al. [468] experimentally demonstrated a 1D Fourier metalens based on the quadratic phase distribution, with a lattice constant of 450 nm and a metasurface dimension of 190 $\mu$ m × 190 $\mu$ m, achieving an FOV from 0° to 60° and a working bandwidth spanning from 1100 nm to 1700 nm. However, it lacked chromatic aberration correction, resulting in a focal length that decreases with increasing wavelength. In 2020, Martins et al. [491] demonstrated a quadratic metalens operating at a wavelength of 532 nm, with a lattice constant of only 190 nm, a diameter of 2 mm and a focal length of 750 $\mu$ m. Meta-atoms were made from c–Si cylinders with a height of 230 nm. The absolute focusing efficiency of the metalens under perpendicular light incidence (with an effective focusing area diameter of 15 $\mu$ m) was 3.5%. The imaging result for an object at the edge incident angle of 70° is shown in Fig. 30(i). In the same year, Levy's group [482] developed a quadratic metalens operating at a wavelength of 850 nm, with a diameter of 2 mm and a focal length of 3.36 mm, showing good imaging quality within an FOV of 30°, suitable for outdoor photography and surveillance. Meta-atoms were made from a–Si cylinders with a height of 135 nm, arranged in a hexagonal lattice with a lattice constant of 500 nm. After packaging the imaging system with a camera, as illustrated in the inset of Fig. 30(j), the outdoor scene imaging result is presented in Fig. 30(j). The observed decrease in image illumination from center to edge aligns with the discussion in (ii) above. In 2021, Lassalle et al. [483] fabricated a quadratic metalens operating at a wavelength of 740 nm, with a lattice

constant of 300 nm, a diameter of 500 $\mu$ m, and a focal length of 203 $\mu$ m. The average absolute focusing efficiency across various incident angles was about 6%, and the imaging result for an object at a distance of 3 cm is shown in Fig. 30(k). The metal-ens exhibited a large FOV of 120°, but the imaging resolution in regions with larger incident angles noticeably decreased, consistent with the discussion (iii) above. In the same year, Luo's group [158] achieved a quadratic metalens operating in the long-wave infrared range at a wavelength close to 10 $\mu$ m, with a focal length of 6.5 mm and a diameter of 25.4 mm, exhibiting an FOV of over 140°. In 2024, Sharma et al. [494] realized a quadratic metalens operating at a wavelength of 740 nm, with a diameter of 5 mm, a focal length of 2.03 mm, and an FOV of nearly 180°. In the same year, Rho's group [495] extended the application of quadratic phase distribution to acoustics, realizing a metalens for sound capture with a wide field-of-hearing of approximately 140°.

Compared to the ideal phase distribution, a single-element quadratic metalens introduces significant spherical aberration, leading to a reduction in resolution as well as noticeable stray light issues that reduce the imaging SNR and decrease the focusing efficiency $[496]$ , as illustrated by the comparison between Figs. 29(b)–29(d) and 29(n)–29(p). It's also worth mentioning that, on the other hand, the presence of spherical aberration and defocus aberration endows the quadratic metalens with the characteristic of a large depth of field (DOF) $[483]$ . To correct for spherical aberration, as shown in Fig. 30(h), Martins et al. proposed placing a metasurface in front of the quadratic metalens to apply a corrective phase, thereby making the wavefront of the emerging light closer to the ideal spherical wave. Moreover, in 2023, Luo's group $[492]$ introduced another solution using a wavevector filter (WF). The comparison between Eq. (200) and the ideal phase distribution in Eq. (164) [or considering the case of perpendicular incidence, comparing Eq. (198) and Eq. (168)] reveals that regions closer to the edge of the virtual aperture introduce more severe spherical aberration. Therefore, as illustrated in Fig. 30(l), by introducing a wavevector filter immediately after the quadratic metalens that allows only a central portion of the virtual aperture to pass through, it is possible to mitigate the impact of spherical aberration on imaging at the cost of reducing the NA and focusing efficiency, thereby effectively enhancing the imaging SNR. The imaging results with and without a wavevector filter placed behind the quadratic metalens are compared in Fig. 30(m). Compared to the approach of introducing a metasurface to correct for spherical aberration, although the NA is somewhat reduced, the thickness-to-focal-length ratio of the entire imaging system can be compressed to about $\frac{1}{55}$ of its original value.

# 4.3.3. Wide-FOV Metalenses Based on Other Design Methods

Forward design methods. Beyond the aforementioned methods, there are several other approaches to achieve wide-FOV imaging. In 2013, Capasso's group $[478]$ proposed a non-planar metasurface on a spherical substrate, which theoretically can correct both spherical and coma aberrations but poses significant manufacturing challenges. In 2020 and 2022, Li's group $[340,497]$ proposed a computational imaging method that stitches together images from a metalens array operating at different incident angles to obtain a wide-field imaging and experimentally demonstrated wide-angle imaging with a horizontal FOV of $120^{\circ}$ , using an integrated camera composed of 17 metalenses $[497]$ . In 2023, Hu's group $[498]$ proposed an analytical design approach for the metalens system shown in Fig. 30(a). This approach was further validated experimentally in 2024 with an all-silicon metalens operating at a wavelength of $10.6 \mu m$ , featuring a diameter exceeding 4 cm and an FOV of $140^{\circ}$ $[499]$ . Further elaboration on these methods is omitted in this context.

Inverse design methods. In the design of the aforementioned wide-FOV metalens systems, the target phase distribution adopted is angle-independent. Therefore, it is necessary to have the electromagnetic response of the meta-atoms independent of the incident angle, which is usually unavoidable for large incident angles. Additionally, it's crucial to minimize the influence of the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms. However, the problem is that the coupling becomes increasingly significant as the angle of incidence increases. This leads to a decrease in lens efficiency at larger incident angles $[496]$ and introduces limitations to the achievable maximum FOV $[500]$ . To achieve ideal imaging with wide-FOV metalenses, it is essential to revert to the ideal phase distribution expressed by Eq. (164), necessitating considerations of the spatial dispersion and meta-atom coupling in the design process.

As a preliminary exploration, in 2018, targeting the angle-dependent phase distribution, Lin et al. [338] utilized topology optimization to design a cylindrical metalens with an NA of 0.35, which corrected aberrations at the incident angles of $0^{\circ}$ , $7.5^{\circ}$ , $15^{\circ}$ , and $20^{\circ}$ . However, the five-layer cascaded metasurface structure is challenging to fabricate with existing manufacturing processes.

The efficiency of the wide-FOV metalens systems discussed previously is relatively low owing to the inherent trade-off between FOV and efficiency. For optical systems designed for a wide FOV, whether employing physical apertures, effective apertures, or virtual apertures, due to the constraint of optical étendue conservation (manifested as the Abbe sine condition in imaging design problems), the area of the entrance pupil is inevitably much smaller than the area of the image plane and, consequently, much smaller than the physical cross-sectional area of the optical system. Therefore, for any given angle of incidence, the imaging system only collects a small portion of the light reaching the first surface of the optical system. This explains why designs for wide-FOV metalens systems generally exhibit lower efficiency metrics $[496]$ .

# 4.4. Achromatic Metalens Design

# 4.4.1. Theory of Achromatic Metalens Design

Due to the lack of a comprehensive theoretical framework, the initial designs of achromatic metalenses often featured very limited achromatic bandwidths $[501,502]$ . Since the pivotal achievements by Capasso's group $[139]$ and Tsai's group $[351]$ in 2018, the design theory of achromatic metalenses has been progressively developed and refined, as will be discussed in the following.

The ideal phase distribution of a metalens is frequency-dependent, exhibiting specific dispersion characteristics. Let the target phase distribution be denoted as $\phi(x,y,\omega)$ . For simplicity, we disregard the target phase distribution's dependency on the incident angle.

Assuming the achromatic imaging targets a frequency range centered around $\omega_{c}$ with a bandwidth of $\Delta\omega$ , $\phi(x,y,\omega)$ can be expanded into a Taylor series around $\omega_{c}$ [139]

$$
\begin{array}{l} \phi (x, y, \omega) = \phi (x, y, \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}) + \frac {\partial \phi (x , y , \omega)}{\partial \omega} \Bigg | _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}} (\omega - \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}) \\ + \left. \frac {\partial^ {2} \phi (x , y , \omega)}{\partial \omega^ {2}} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}} \frac {(\omega - \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}) ^ {2}}{2} + \mathcal {O} (\omega^ {3}). \\ \end{array}
$$

From Eq. (207), to achieve achromatic imaging, the meta-atoms at position $(x,y)$ need to not only provide a phase shift $\phi(x,y,\omega_{\mathrm{c}})$ at frequency $\omega_{c}$ but also satisfy higher-order derivative terms (usually considering up to the second order), namely $\left.\frac{\partial\phi(x,y,\omega)}{\partial\omega}\right|_{\omega=\omega_{\mathrm{c}}}$ and $\left.\frac{\partial^{2}\phi(x,y,\omega)}{\partial\omega^{2}}\right|_{\omega=\omega_{\mathrm{c}}}$ , which are referred to as group delay and group

delay dispersion, respectively. The group delay and group delay dispersion are typically used to describe the dispersion characteristics of the target phase distribution. When meta-atoms fail to meet the required phase dispersion, chromatic aberrations occur even if the target phase distribution at frequency $\omega_{c}$ is achieved. This results in the actual focal length of the metalens being frequency-dependent. For example, by applying the generalized refraction law in Eq. (57), we can see that for a metalens utilizing the geometric phase, chromatic aberration causes higher frequencies to have longer focal lengths.

For small FOV achromatic metalens design, it is common to adopt hyperbolic phase distribution, i.e., $\phi(x,y,\omega)=\phi_{\mathrm{hyperbolic}}(r,\omega)$ . Substituting the hyperbolic phase distribution expressed by Eq. (168) into Eq. (207), the conditions needed to achieve an achromatic metalens are as follows:

(i) Phase distribution at frequency $\omega_{c}$ satisfies

$$
\phi (r, \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}) = - \frac {\omega_ {\mathrm{c}}}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - f\right) + C (\omega_ {\mathrm{c}}). \tag {208}
$$

(ii) Group delay distribution at frequency $\omega_{c}$ satisfies

$$
\left. \frac {\partial \phi (r , \omega)}{\partial \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}} = - \frac {1}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - f\right) + \left. \frac {\mathrm{d} C (\omega)}{\mathrm{d} \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}}. \tag {209}
$$

(iii) Group delay dispersion distribution at frequency $\omega_{c}$ satisfies

$$
\left. \frac {\partial^ {2} \phi (r , \omega)}{\partial \omega^ {2}} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}} = \left. \frac {\mathrm{d} ^ {2} C (\omega)}{\mathrm{d} \omega^ {2}} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}}. \tag {210}
$$

Generally, the group delay dispersion expressed by Eq. (210) is typically assumed to be 0 according to the actual phase dispersion characteristic of meta-atoms (more strictly, other higher-order derivative terms in Eq. (207) are also 0), implying that $\phi(r, \omega)$ is linearly related to $\omega$ . For the case where the group delay dispersion is not 0, refer to the discussion on the asymptotic matched achromatic metalens [354] in Subsection 4.4.3. From this, it can be inferred that $\frac{\mathrm{d}C(\omega)}{\mathrm{d}\omega}$ is a constant independent of $\omega$ . Generally, $C(\omega)$ is taken in the form of

$$
C (\omega) = C _ {1} \omega + C _ {2}, \tag {211}
$$

where $C_{1}$ and $C_{2}$ are constants. Note that $C(\omega)$ introduces additional design degrees of freedom into the realization of the target phase distribution [see Eq. (208)] and group delay distribution [see Eq. (209)]. Physically, its effect is equivalent to placing a generic dispersive phase shifter in front of the metalens, whose dispersion characteristics might distort incident light pulses but do not affect focusing since no spatially varying modulation is introduced [136].

As illustrated in Fig. 31, condition (i), through the introduction of phase compensation, ensures that light at any position on the metalens at frequency $\omega_{c}$ reaches the focus simultaneously (simultaneous arrival of the output light at the focus means that the output light forms an ideal spherical wavefront centered at the focus). Conditions (ii) and (iii), by introducing group delay compensation while controlling group delay dispersion to be 0, ensure that at any position on the metalens, light at any frequency $\omega$ within the bandwidth $\Delta\omega$ arrives at the focus simultaneously with the light at the center frequency $\omega_{c}$ . Note that the group delay has the dimension of time, typically on the order of femtoseconds [139] for meta-atoms. The so-called group delay compensation can be understood as a form of temporal compensation, where through imposing the

Figure 31  
![](images/e17ee4023ee216e2946f8e61cd590d27bc9c91aa226c2b953d4c2fd97bb602a8.jpg)

<details>
<summary>flowchart</summary>

This diagram illustrates a propagation phase metasurface model for a waveguide system, showing the relationship between wave numbers, propagation parameters, and their corresponding phase derivatives.
</details>

Schematic of the theory of the achromatic metalens design. For a propagation phase metasurface, the upper limit of the span of group delay is $\Delta GD_{max} = \frac{h}{c} \frac{n_{meta} - n_{cladding}}{n_{back}}$ [Eq. (218)], where h represents the height of meta-atoms, and $n_{meta}$ , $n_{cladding}$ , and $n_{back}$ are the refractive indices of the meta-atom material, the cladding material, and the background environment medium, respectively. The central frequency is denoted by $\omega_{c}$ , the bandwidth by $\Delta \omega$ , and the delay time by $\Delta T$ , as expressed by Eq. (225). The dimensionless quantity $\kappa$ limits the product of $\Delta T$ and $\Delta \omega$ , known as the time-bandwidth product [Eq. (220)]. For a more detailed discussion, refer to Subsection 4.4.2.

compensation time of $\left.\frac{\partial\phi(r,\omega)}{\partial\omega}\right|_{\omega=\omega_{c}}$ , light at frequency $\omega$ obtains an additional phase compensation of

$$
\Delta \phi (r, \omega) = \phi (r, \omega) - \phi (r, \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}) = \frac {\partial \phi (r , \omega)}{\partial \omega} \bigg | _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}} (\omega - \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}) \tag {212}
$$

relative to light at the center frequency $\omega_{c}$ , thereby achieving simultaneity of all frequencies of light at the focus. When the above three conditions are all satisfied, ensuring that light of all frequencies at any position on the metalens arrives at the focus simultaneously, the correction of chromatic aberration, along with the spherical aberration, is successfully achieved.

To further elucidate the meaning of the coefficients in Eq. (211), let's express $C(\omega)$ in a form with clearer physical significance:

$$
C (\omega) = \frac {\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r _ {\mathrm{a}} ^ {2}} - f}{c} \omega + \phi_ {\mathrm{a}}, \tag {213}
$$

where $r_{a}$ and $\phi_{a}$ are parameters that can be freely adjusted. Substituting Eq. (213) into the target phase distribution $\phi(r,\omega)$ gives

$$
\phi (r, \omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - f\right) + C (\omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - \sqrt {f ^ {2} + r _ {\mathrm{a}} ^ {2}}\right) + \phi_ {\mathrm{a}}. \tag {214}
$$

When $r = r_{a}$ , we have $\phi(r_{\mathrm{a}}, \omega) = \phi_{\mathrm{a}}$ , meaning that $r_{a}$ represents the position where the target phase of light at all frequencies coincides, and $\phi_{a}$ determines the target phase value at the coincidence point. At $r = r_{a}$ , the group delay is zero. For $r < r_{a}$ and $r > r_{a}$ , the group delay is positive and negative, respectively. As an intuitive illustration, when

Figure 32  
![](images/e30526aa96c4471ffc2ce19f592b8dcbdb0bc260764766c932d29e89db75479b.jpg)

Schematic of the physical meaning of $r_{a}$ and $\phi_{a}$ in Eq. (213). (a) Target phase profiles of the achromatic metalens when $r_{a}=0$ and $\phi_{a}=0$ . (b) Destination phase profiles of the achromatic metalens when $r_{a}=f$ and $\phi_{a}=\pi$ .

$r_{a}=0$ and $\phi_{a}=0$ [i.e., $C(\omega)=0$ ], the target phase distribution diagram is shown in Fig. 32(a); when $r_{a}=f$ and $\phi_{a}=\pi$ , the target phase distribution diagram is shown in Fig. 32(b).

# 4.4.2. Fundamental Limitations of Achromatic Metalenses

Given that the maximum and minimum group delays achievable by meta-atoms are $GD_{max}$ and $GD_{min}$ , respectively, the span of the group delay is $\Delta GD = GD_{max} - GD_{min}$ (assuming the meta-atom database samples this range adequately). For the meta-atom based on the geometric phase modulation mechanism, the group delay is always 0, making it impossible to achieve achromatic imaging. For the meta-atom based on the resonant or propagation phase modulation mechanisms, from Eq. (6) (taking $\lambda_{0} = \frac{2\pi cn_{back}}{\omega}$ ), the group delay is given by

$$
\frac {\mathrm{d} \phi}{\mathrm{d} \omega} = \frac {n _ {\mathrm{eff}} (\omega) h}{n _ {\mathrm{back}} c} + \frac {\omega h}{n _ {\mathrm{back}} c} \frac {\mathrm{d} n _ {\mathrm{eff}} (\omega)}{\mathrm{d} \omega}, \tag {215}
$$

where the first and second terms on the right side represent the contributions of propagation phase and resonant phase modulation mechanisms, respectively. For the meta-atom based only on the propagation phase, $\frac{\mathrm{d}n_{\mathrm{eff}}(\omega)}{\mathrm{d}\omega}=0$ , thus Eq. (215) becomes

$$
\frac {\mathrm{d} \phi}{\mathrm{d} \omega} = \frac {n _ {\mathrm{eff}} (\omega) h}{n _ {\mathrm{back}} c}. \tag {216}
$$

From Eq. (4), the range of the effective refractive index $n_{\mathrm{eff}}(\omega) \in \left[n_{\mathrm{claddng}}(\omega), n_{\mathrm{meta}}(\omega)\right]$ . As a matter of fact, it's inevitable that the group delay of the meta-atom will be affected by the dispersion characteristics of the meta-atom material as well as the phase jump near the resonance frequency [502]. Ignoring these additional dispersion effects and making a rough estimate, the maximum achievable range of group delay is approximated by

$$
\frac {\mathrm{d} \phi}{\mathrm{d} \omega} \in \left[ \frac {h}{c} \frac {n _ {\text {cladding}}}{n _ {\text {back}}}, \frac {h}{c} \frac {n _ {\text {meta}}}{n _ {\text {back}}} \right], \tag {217}
$$

leading to an upper limit of the span of group delay:

$$
\Delta \mathrm{GD} _ {\max} = \frac {h}{c} \frac {\Delta n}{n _ {\mathrm{back}}}, \tag {218}
$$

where $\Delta n = n_{meta} - n_{cladding}$ . Considering the material dispersion, when $n_{meta}(\omega)$ and $n_{cladding}(\omega)$ in Eq. (218) are taken as the maximum and minimum values within the bandwidth range, respectively, the upper limit of the span of group delay is

$$
\Delta \mathrm{GD} _ {\max} = \frac {h}{c} \frac {\Delta n _ {\max}}{n _ {\text {back}}}, \tag {219}
$$

where $\Delta n_{\mathrm{max}} = \max_{\omega} n_{\mathrm{meta}}(\omega) - \min_{\omega} n_{\mathrm{cladding}}(\omega)$ . Equation (218) or Equation (219) indicates that to cover a broader range of group delays, it's necessary to increase the height of the meta-atoms or use meta-atoms made of materials with a higher refractive index.

The time-bandwidth product (TBP) of optical devices like waveguides is defined as the product of the interaction time or delay time $\Delta T$ of the signal light within the device and the signal light's bandwidth $\Delta \omega$ , with a theoretical upper limit given by

$$
\Delta T \Delta \omega \leq \kappa , \tag {220}
$$

where $\kappa$ is a dimensionless quantity that depends on the general properties of the specific device. For metasurfaces based on different phase modulation mechanisms, $\kappa$ has different values, detailed in reference [136].

From Eq. (209), for an achromatic metalens with a radius of $R$ , the range of the required group delay is

$$
- \frac {1}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + R ^ {2}} - f\right) + \left. \frac {\mathrm{d} C (\omega)}{\mathrm{d} \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}} \leq \left. \frac {\partial \phi (r , \omega)}{\partial \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}} \leq \left. \frac {\mathrm{d} C (\omega)}{\mathrm{d} \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}}. \tag {221}
$$

Thus, the span of the required group delay is

$$
\Delta \left(\left. \frac {\partial \phi (r , \omega)}{\partial \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}}\right) = \frac {1}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + R ^ {2}} - f\right). \tag {222}
$$

Generally, the group delay has the physical significance of the actual time delay experienced by the incident light when interacting with the metalens $[136]$ . However, this kind of time delay is only meaningful in relative terms—that is, the relative magnitude of the group delay at one position compared to another is meaningful, so it cannot be directly equated to $\Delta T$ , and a reference point for the time delay must be specified first. Choosing the left boundary in Eq. (221) as the zero point for time delay, the range of $\Delta T$ is

$$
0 \leq \Delta T \leq \frac {1}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + R ^ {2}} - f\right). \tag {223}
$$

Combining Eq. (223) with Eq. (220), we obtain

$$
\Delta \omega \leq \frac {\kappa c}{\sqrt {f ^ {2} + R ^ {2}} - f}. \tag {224}
$$

Clearly, although there are other choices for the zero point for time delay, Eq. (223) provides the smallest $\Delta T_{max}$ and thus the largest bandwidth range. Therefore, for achromatic metalenses, $\Delta T$ in Eq. (220) can be directly understood as the span of the group delay in Eq. (222), i.e.,

$$
\Delta T = \Delta \left(\left. \frac {\partial \phi (r , \omega)}{\partial \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}}\right) = \frac {1}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + R ^ {2}} - f\right). \tag {225}
$$

Substituting Eq. (181) into Eq. (224), the maximum achievable achromatic bandwidth is derived as

$$
\Delta \omega_ {\max} = \frac {\kappa c}{f} \frac {\sqrt {1 - \left(\mathrm{NA} / n _ {\mathrm{back}}\right) ^ {2}}}{1 - \sqrt {1 - \left(\mathrm{NA} / n _ {\mathrm{back}}\right) ^ {2}}}. \tag {226}
$$

Equation (226) reveals the intrinsic trade-off between the achromatic bandwidth and NA. The larger the lens's NA, the more limited the achievable achromatic bandwidth. This indicates that in the design of achromatic metalenses, a balance must be struck between the NA and bandwidth. For the most commonly used meta-atoms based on the propagation phase modulation mechanism, $\kappa$ can be expressed as [136]

$$
\kappa = \frac {\omega_ {\mathrm{c}} h \Delta n _ {\max}}{c _ {0}}. \tag {227}
$$

Substituting Eq. (227) into Eq. (226), the maximum achromatic bandwidth when utilizing the propagation phase modulation mechanism to achieve group delay is

$$
\Delta \omega_ {\max} = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}} \frac {h \Delta n _ {\max}}{f n _ {\mathrm{back}}} \frac {\sqrt {1 - \left(\mathrm{NA} / n _ {\mathrm{back}}\right) ^ {2}}}{1 - \sqrt {1 - \left(\mathrm{NA} / n _ {\mathrm{back}}\right) ^ {2}}}. \tag {228}
$$

The Fresnel number FN serves as a good measure of the practical workload of a lens $[503]$ , which is defined as

$$
\mathrm{FN} (r) = \frac {d (r)}{\lambda_ {\mathrm{c}} / 2}, \tag {229}
$$

where $\lambda_{c}=\frac{2\pi}{\omega_{c}}c$ is the wavelength in the environmental medium corresponding to the center angular frequency $\omega_{c}$ , and $d(r)$ is the optical path difference at a radius of r provided by a metalens conforming to the hyperbolic phase distribution, given by

$$
d (r) = \sqrt {r ^ {2} + f ^ {2}} - f. \tag {230}
$$

When assuming $\frac{r}{f} \ll 1$ , we have $d(r) \approx \frac{r^2}{2f}$ , thus $\mathrm{FN} \approx \frac{r^2}{\lambda_c f}$ , which is the traditional expression for the Fresnel number under the paraxial approximation. Considering a lens with a radius of $R$ , and defining the Fresnel number of the lens as $\mathrm{FN}(r = R) = \frac{d(R)}{\lambda_c / 2}$ [using Eq. (229)], from Eqs. (225) and (230), we obtain

$$
d (r = R) = c \Delta T. \tag {231}
$$

For an achromatic metalens based on the propagation phase modulation mechanism, by combining Eqs. (220), (227), (229), and (231), the product of the achievable bandwidth and Fresnel number satisfies [503]

$$
\Delta \omega \cdot \mathrm{FN} \leq \frac {n _ {\mathrm{back}} \Delta n _ {\mathrm{max}} h \omega_ {\mathrm{c}} ^ {2}}{\pi c}. \tag {232}
$$

Compared to Eq. (228), Eq. (232) indicates that, relative to NA, the Fresnel number has a more fundamental limiting relationship with the achromatic bandwidth. For an achromatic metalens with a specific achromatic bandwidth, the Fresnel number is subject to an upper limit as constrained by Eq. (232). In this case, from the definition of the Fresnel number, there must be a trade-off between the achievable NA and the size of the metalens: on the one hand, a high-NA achromatic metalens will inevitably have

a very limited metalens size; on the other hand, for a metalens of a certain size, the limitation of the achromatic bandwidth on the metalens's Fresnel number will translate into a restriction on its NA.

Given Eq. (209), the target group delay decreases from the center to the edge of the metalens. By adjusting $C(\omega)$ , it is always possible to set the target group delay at the metalens center to correspond to $GD_{max}$ , i.e.,

$$
\mathrm{GD} _ {\max} = \left. \frac {\mathrm{d} C (\omega)}{\mathrm{d} \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}}. \tag {233}
$$

This alignment makes the achievable range of group delay correspond to the target range of group delay. Therefore, from Eq. (221), to meet the group delay requirement, it must hold that

$$
- \frac {1}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - f\right) + \left. \frac {\mathrm{d} C (\omega)}{\mathrm{d} \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}} \geq \mathrm{GD} _ {\min}. \tag {234}
$$

Combining Eqs. (233) and (234), we obtain

$$
\Delta \mathrm{GD} = \mathrm{GD} _ {\max} - \mathrm{GD} _ {\min} \geq \frac {1}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - f\right). \tag {235}
$$

Thus, considering only the group delay, the maximum achievable radius $r_{max}$ of the metalens is

$$
r _ {\max} = f \sqrt {\left(1 + \frac {c \Delta \mathrm{GD}}{f}\right) ^ {2} - 1}. \tag {236}
$$

Combining Eq. (181) and (235), taking $R = r_{\mathrm{max}}$ , and eliminating $f$ , another expression for $r_{\mathrm{max}}$ is obtained [144]

$$
r _ {\max} = c \Delta \mathrm{GD} \left(\sqrt {\left(n _ {\text {back}} / \mathrm{NA}\right) ^ {2} - 1} + \left(n _ {\text {back}} / \mathrm{NA}\right)\right). \tag {237}
$$

Here, we see again the fundamental trade-off between the NA and the maximum achievable size of a metalens. In practical design, given a specific focal length or NA, when the maximum span of group delay $\Delta GD_{max}$ that can be achieved by meta-atoms is determined, Eq. (236) or Eq. (237) can be used to predict the maximum radius of an achromatic metalens achievable under the group delay limitation.

For cases utilizing the propagation phase modulation mechanism to achieve group delay, substituting the upper limit of group delay span expressed by Eq. (219) into Eq. (237) yields

$$
r _ {\max} = h \frac {\Delta n _ {\max}}{n _ {\text {back}}} \left(\sqrt {\left(n _ {\text {back}} / \mathrm{NA}\right) ^ {2} - 1} + \left(n _ {\text {back}} / \mathrm{NA}\right)\right). \tag {238}
$$

We note that the aforementioned limitations apply specifically to broadband achromatic metalenses. If chromatic aberration correction is required only at multiple discrete operational wavelengths rather than across a continuous bandwidth, these constraints can be significantly relaxed. This adjustment gives rise to the concept of quasi-achromatic metalenses $[504]$ , which effectively extends the bandwidth, as illustrated in Fig. 33. Details of this approach will be further explored in Subsection 4.4.3. Additionally, this flexibility enables broadband achromatic imaging using a multi-wavelength achromatic metalens with sufficiently small spectral sampling, a method that will also be introduced in Subsection 4.4.4.

# 4.4.3. Design Methods for Broadband Achromatic Metalenses

The main methods for designing broadband achromatic metalenses are presented in Fig. 34, which will be discussed in detail below.

Classic design methods for polarization-sensitive achromatic metalenses. The hybrid phase modulation mechanism is widely utilized to achieve polarization-sensitive achromatic metalenses.

By tailoring the shape and size parameters of meta-atoms, a meta-atom database is constructed to ensure coverage of the target group delay range as indicated by Eq. (221), while maintaining the group delay dispersion close to zero. Since $\left.\frac{\mathrm{d}C(\omega)}{\mathrm{d}\omega}\right|_{\omega = \omega_{\mathrm{c}}} = C_1$ is adjustable in Eq. (209), here the "coverage" of the target group delay range essentially requires the group delay span of the meta-atoms in the database to exceed the target

group delay span in Eq. (222). In the arrangement of meta-atoms, for a unit positioned at $(x,y)$ , one should first select a meta-atom from the database that satisfies the dispersion requirements [Eqs. (209) and (210)] at that position while maintaining the highest possible transmittance and PCE. Eq. (208) determines the target phase for the central frequency $\omega_{c}$ at that position, which is achieved through introducing a compensation of phase $\Delta\phi(x,y)$ by rotating the meta-atom, i.e.,

$$
\Delta \phi (x, y) = \phi (r, \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}) - \phi_ {0} (x, y) = - \frac {\omega_ {\mathrm{c}}}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - f\right) + C (\omega_ {\mathrm{c}}) - \phi_ {0} (x, y), \tag {239}
$$

where $\phi_{0}(x,y)$ is the phase shift for $\omega_{c}$ introduced by this meta-atom when the rotation angle is $0^{\circ}$ , which can be obtained through simulation in actual design. Hence, according to Eq. (23), this meta-atom should rotate counterclockwise around the z-axis at an angle of $\theta(x,y)=\frac{\Delta\phi(x,y)}{2\sigma}$ , where $\sigma=\pm1$ corresponds to the cases when the incident light is LCP and RCP, respectively.

In 2018, Capasso's group [139] achieved an achromatic metalens with an NA of 0.2 and a focal length of $63~\mu \mathrm{m}$ , covering a bandwidth from $470~\mathrm{nm}$ to $670~\mathrm{nm}$ . To provide sufficient design freedom, the metasurface unit consists of one or more $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ nanofins with varying lateral dimensions but a uniform height of $600~\mathrm{nm}$ . The lattice constant was $400~\mathrm{nm}$ . As shown in Fig. 35(a), the actual focal lengths of the metalens at various wavelengths matched the designed focal length, demonstrating its achromatic performance. The Strehl ratios were above 0.8 across all wavelengths, but the absolute focusing efficiencies at various wavelengths were all below $20\%$ (with an effective focusing area diameter close to the diameter of the Airy disk), due to the compromise of using meta-atoms with low PCE to cover the complete range of group delay. To optimize efficiency, there is a need to encompass a broader array of meta-atom shapes to expand the design space, thereby constructing a more comprehensive database that meets both dispersion control and device efficiency requirements.

In 2017 and 2018, Tsai's group and their collaborators achieved achromatic metalenses operating in the near-infrared [100] and visible [351], respectively. Although their design algorithm differed in form from the aforementioned method, it was essentially consistent. For Eqs. (208)–(210), instead of positioning $\omega_{c}$ at the center of the bandwidth, it was chosen as the minimum angular frequency within the bandwidth. In addition, they set $r_{a}=R$ and $\phi_{a}=-\frac{\omega_{c}}{c}\left(\sqrt{f^{2}+R^{2}}-f\right)$ for $C(\omega)$ in Eq. (213). In this way, we obtain the target phase distribution for achromatic metalenses that they proposed in both works. Utilizing meta-atoms with an MIM structure (layers of Au, $SiO_{2}$ , and Au), the near-infrared metalens operated in reflection mode, with a lens radius of 27.775 $\mu$ m, a focal length of 100 $\mu$ m, and an NA of 0.268, covering a bandwidth from 1200 nm to 1680 nm. The visible metalens operated in transmission mode, using GaN meta-atoms with a height of 800 nm. The units were arranged in a hexagonal lattice with a lattice constant of 120 nm, employing both pillar and hole-type meta-atoms to meet dispersion engineering requirements. This metalens had a focal length of 235 $\mu$ m, an NA of 0.106, and operated across a bandwidth from 400 nm to 660 nm, with an average absolute focusing efficiency of about 40% across the bandwidth. The original images, as shown in the left panel of Fig. 35(b), and the metalens imaging results, as shown in the middle panel of Fig. 35(b), display minor color differences mainly due to variations in focusing efficiency across wavelengths. After the intensity compensation for the red, green, and blue color channels, the corrected images are shown in the right panel of Fig. 35(b). In 2019, Tsai's group and their collaborators [119] further assembled the aforementioned visible achromatic metalenses into a $60\times60$ metalens array, thus achieving an achromatic full-color light field camera with a resolution of 1.95 $\mu$ m under the white light illumination.

Figure 35  
![](images/11b76db87d8b41215523dbbfeef5b47400ded7e7f78615fbd4906eb91a1328f1.jpg)

Design methods for polarization-insensitive achromatic metalenses. The achromatic metalenses introduced above are polarization-sensitive. To achieve polarization-insensitive achromatic metalens designs, the most straightforward method involves solely using isotropic meta-atoms based on resonant or propagation phase modulation mechanisms to produce polarization-insensitive electromagnetic responses. In terms of design methodology, the distinction lies in that the target phase distribution at the central angular frequency can no longer be achieved by the geometric phase modulation mechanism. Thus, when constructing the meta-atom database, it is necessary to introduce an additional design dimension to cover the $2\pi$ phase shift for incident light at $\omega_{c}$ , while maintaining the coverage of the group delay range and ensuring that the group delay dispersion is near zero. Utilizing this approach (but without considering the group delay dispersion dimension), in 2018, Yu's group [144] developed a polarization-insensitive achromatic metalens with a radius of $50 \mu m$ , a focal length of $30 \mu m$ , an NA of approximately 0.88, and a bandwidth from 1200 nm to 1400 nm. To cover the complete phase and group delay modulation, the meta-atoms were made of high-refractive-index Si, with a height of 1400 nm and featuring various shapes such as crosses, squares, and square holes. The relative deviation of the focal length at each wavelength from the mean focal length was constrained within a range from 2% to 5%. The Strehl ratios were above 0.8 across all wavelengths, with an average absolute focusing efficiency (with an effective focusing area diameter approximately 3–5 times the FWHM of the focal spot) around 35%. Similarly, in 2020, Ndao et al. [510] introduced a meshed metasurface structure, achieving a polarization-insensitive achromatic metalens with dimensions of $20 \mu m \times 20 \mu m$ , an NA of 0.12, a bandwidth from 640 nm to 1200 nm, and an average absolute focusing efficiency exceeding 70%. The $TiO_{2}$ meta-atoms utilized isotropic structures, with a height of 350 nm and a lattice constant of 370 nm.

Based on Eq. (232), the most direct approach to further enhance the size, NA, or achromatic bandwidth of an achromatic metalens is to increase the height of the meta-atoms. In 2021, Xiao's group and their collaborators [138] developed a top-down etching fabrication technique that achieved a height of $1500\mathrm{nm}$ for $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ meta-atoms, with an aspect ratio of approximately 37.5. Utilizing four shapes of isotropic meta-atoms, they created a polarization-insensitive achromatic metalens with a radius of $15~{\mu\mathrm{m}}$ , an NA of 0.24, and a bandwidth from $650\mathrm{nm}$ to $1000\mathrm{nm}$ . The Strehl ratios were above 0.81 across all wavelengths within the bandwidth, with an average absolute focusing efficiency (with an effective focusing area diameter six times the FWHM of the focal spot) of approximately $77.1\%$ .

In 2019, Capasso's group [156] proposed a counterintuitive design approach that achieves polarization-insensitive metalens designs using polarization-sensitive structures. Based on Eq. (23), a meta-atom rotated by $\theta$ introduces geometric phase shifts of $-2\theta$ and $+2\theta$ for LCP and RCP incident light, respectively. The polarization sensitivity of the geometric phase is evident since these two phase shifts are usually not equal. However, by restricting the rotation angle of the meta-atoms to only $0^{\circ}$ or $90^{\circ}$ , it results in $2\theta = -2\theta$ , indicating that the introduced geometric phase is polarization-insensitive at these specific rotation angles, as discussed in detail in Subsection 2.1.3. The advantage of this approach is that the meta-atoms possess an anisotropic structure that provides a higher degree of shape tunability compared to the isotropic structures typically used in polarization-insensitive designs. In constructing the meta-atom database, it is only necessary to calculate the phase shift and dispersion properties of meta-atoms at a rotation angle of $0^{\circ}$ , as shown by the green dots in Fig. 35(c). When the meta-atom is rotated to $90^{\circ}$ , the introduced geometric phase increases by $\pi$ , while the dispersion properties remain unchanged. Hence, the electromagnetic response characteristics of the meta-atoms rotated by $90^{\circ}$ can be directly obtained, as indicated by the purple dots in Fig. 35(c). Utilizing this approach, they achieved a polarization-insensitive achromatic metalens with a radius of $13.2\mu \mathrm{m}$ , an NA of 0.2, and a bandwidth from $460\mathrm{nm}$ to $700\mathrm{nm}$ . The meta-atoms were made of $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ with a height of $600\mathrm{nm}$ and a lattice constant of $400\mathrm{nm}$ . The metalens showed deviations in the actual focal lengths from the set value of no more than $9\%$ across the wavelengths, with the Strehl ratios maintained around 0.8. The absolute focusing efficiency was about $30\%$ at the wavelength near $532\mathrm{nm}$ , with only about a $4\%$ variation under different incident polarization states.

In 2022, Ren et al. [511] employed the same method to develop a polarization-insensitive metalens with a radius of 100 $\mu$ m, an NA of 0.2, and an achromatic bandwidth ranging from 1.25 $\mu$ m to 1.65 $\mu$ m. Employing the 3D laser printing technology based on two-photon polymerization, the high degree of freedom for meta-atoms was unlocked. This adjustment allowed for a broader range of group delay modulation, extending from Eq. (218) to

$$
\Delta \mathrm{GD} _ {\max} = \frac {h _ {\max} n _ {\text {meta}} - h _ {\min} n _ {\text {cladding}}}{c n _ {\text {back}}}, \tag {240}
$$

where $h_{max}$ and $h_{min}$ represent the maximum and minimum heights of meta-atoms, respectively.

New advances for achromatic metalens design theory. Recently, there have been new developments in the design theory of achromatic metalenses, thereby breaking through the performance limits of the achromatic metalens design methods mentioned above.

Beforehand, we have consistently set $C(\omega)$ to satisfy the form of Eq. (211), thereby ensuring that the group delay dispersion expressed by Eq. (210) is 0, which requires the

phase shift introduced by the meta-atom to be linearly related to the angular frequency. However, as indicated by Eqs. (6) and (215), due to the effective refractive index of meta-atoms being frequency-dependent, the relationship between the phase shift of the meta-atom and angular frequency often exhibits nonlinearity, especially for achromatic metalens designs with a wide bandwidth, making the chromatic aberration correction less than ideal, as shown in the middle panel of Fig. 35(d). If the second derivative of $C(\omega)$ is non-zero, a nonlinear relationship between the target phase shift and angular frequency can be accommodated. In this way, $C(\omega)$ expressed in Eq. (213) becomes

$$
C (\omega) = \frac {\omega}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r _ {\mathrm{a}} ^ {2} (\omega)} - f\right) + \phi_ {\mathrm{a}}, \tag {241}
$$

where $r_{\mathrm{a}}(\omega)$ is a now function of angular frequency. Consequently, the hyperbolic phase distribution, as given by Eq. (168), can be rewritten as

$$
\phi_ {\text {hyperbolic}} (r, \omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} - \sqrt {f ^ {2} + r _ {\mathrm{a}} ^ {2} (\omega)}\right) + \phi_ {\mathrm{a}}. \tag {242}
$$

Here, $r_{\mathrm{a}}(\omega)$ represents the distance from the intersection of the metasurface and the spherical wavefront of the output light with angular frequency $\omega$ to the center of the metalens $[\phi_{\mathrm{hyperbolic}}(r_{\mathrm{a}}(\omega),\omega)=\phi_{\mathrm{a}}]$ . In this case, the target group delay is given by

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {\partial \phi (r , \omega)}{\partial \omega} \bigg | _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}} = - \frac {1}{c} \sqrt {f ^ {2} + r ^ {2}} \\ \left. + \frac {1}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r _ {\mathrm{a}} ^ {2} \left(\omega_ {\mathrm{c}}\right)} + \frac {\omega_ {\mathrm{c}} r _ {\mathrm{a}} \left(\omega_ {\mathrm{c}}\right)}{\sqrt {f ^ {2} + r _ {\mathrm{a}} ^ {2} \left(\omega_ {\mathrm{c}}\right)}} \frac {\mathrm{d} r _ {\mathrm{a}} (\omega)}{\mathrm{d} \omega} \right| _ {\omega = \omega_ {\mathrm{c}}}\right). \tag {243} \\ \end{array}
$$

For a specific central angular frequency $\omega_{c}$ , the second term in Eq. (243) becomes a constant with respect to $\omega$ , which corresponds to the scenario discussed earlier. However, by dividing the achromatic bandwidth into a series of sub-bandwidths, each with a central angular frequencies $\omega_{c_{i}}$ ( $i=1,2,\ldots,M$ , where M is the number of sub-bandwidths), different ranges of group delay can be adopted for different sub-bandwidths by selecting different values for $r_{\mathrm{a}}\left(\omega_{\mathrm{c}_{\mathrm{i}}}\right)$ . We note that the span of the target group delay for different $\omega_{c_{i}}$ remains consistent, as represented by Eq. (222). This approach is particularly advantageous for designing achromatic metalenses with very wide bandwidths, where the dispersion characteristics of meta-atoms cannot be accurately approximated by a linear relationship between phase shift and angular frequency. The main challenge in this design lies in selecting appropriate values for $r_{\mathrm{a}}\left(\omega_{\mathrm{c}_{\mathrm{i}}}\right)$ so that the target dispersion characteristic for different values of $\omega_{c_{i}}$ and r, as described by Eq. (243), can be covered as much as possible by the meta-atoms in the database. In 2023, Hu et al. [354] optimized $r_{\mathrm{a}}\left(\omega_{\mathrm{c}_{\mathrm{i}}}\right)$ using a particle swarm optimization method and achieved a polarization-insensitive achromatic metalens with a radius of 25 $\mu$ m, an NA of 0.164, and a bandwidth covering from 400 nm to 1000 nm. This type of metalens is termed “asymptotic matched metalenses.” The meta-atoms were made of $TiO_{2}$ , with a height of 1 $\mu$ m, an aspect ratio of up to 20, and a lattice constant of 500 nm, featuring isotropic structures. The absolute focusing efficiency within the bandwidth (with an effective focusing area diameter approximately three times the FWHM of the focal spot) varied from 30% to 80%, with an average value of about 50%. The focusing performance, as shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 35(d), exhibited chromatic aberration correction superior to that of the metalens designed by the previous method under the same condition.

Due to manufacturing constraints associated with high aspect ratio nanostructures and multilayer metasurfaces, the achievable group delay range of meta-atoms, and

consequently, the performance of achromatic metalenses, are constrained. In 2023, Chen et al. [504] introduced the concept of quasi-achromatic metalenses to address this problem in a novel way. Unlike achromatic metalenses, in the design of quasi-achromatic metalenses, akin to allowing phase to extend periodically with $2\pi$ , group delay is permitted to extend periodically with a period of $\frac{2\pi}{\Delta\omega}$ . In this case, chromatic aberration can be corrected only for a set of discrete extension frequencies with a certain angular frequency interval $\Delta\omega$ within the quasi-achromatic bandwidth, but this approach significantly reduces the stringent requirements on the range of group delay for wide bandwidth, high NA, or large-size metalenses, thus overcoming the fundamental performance limitations indicated by Eq. (232). To validate this approach, a quasi-achromatic metalens with a radius of 1 mm, an NA of 0.55, and a quasi-achromatic bandwidth from 400 nm to 1500 nm was simulated. Its focal lengths at different wavelengths are depicted in the top panel of Fig. 35(e), showcasing good achromatic focusing performance at the extension frequencies. As shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 35(e), at non-extension frequencies, although the focal length deviations are large, their absolute focusing efficiencies are close to 0 (meanwhile, the average absolute focusing efficiency at the extension frequencies is about 70%), thus not severely impacting the imaging quality.

Achromatic metalens imaging based on computational imaging methods. Combining computational imaging methods with metalenses represents a cutting-edge trend in the field.

(I) Integration of traditional digital image processing with metalenses. In 2018, Colburn et al. [505] achieved the achromatic metalens imaging in the visible range from 400 nm to 700 nm using computational imaging techniques. As previously mentioned, the chromatic aberration of a metalens manifests as a correlation between the actual focal length and the angular frequency. However, by employing a metalens with extended depth of focus (EDOF), it's possible to preserve spatial frequency information within an expanded focal range at the cost of reduced image SNR, thereby increasing the imaging system's tolerance to focal shifts. Superimposing a cubic phase on the hyperbolic phase distribution effectively extends the depth of focus, with the phase distribution expressed as

$$
\phi_ {\mathrm{EDOF}} (x, y, \omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} \left(\sqrt {f ^ {2} + x ^ {2} + y ^ {2}} - f\right) + \frac {\gamma}{R ^ {3}} \left(x ^ {3} + y ^ {3}\right) + C (\omega), \tag {244}
$$

where $\gamma$ is a constant that represents the contribution of the cubic phase. Setting $\gamma = 55\pi$ , and using isotropic cylindrical c-Si meta-atoms with a height of $633~\mathrm{nm}$ and a lattice constant of $443~\mathrm{nm}$ , they realized a metalens that operates at a wavelength of $550~\mathrm{nm}$ with a focal length of $200\mu \mathrm{m}$ and a radius of approximately $100\mu \mathrm{m}$ . Simulations of its focusing performance at different wavelengths are shown in Fig. 35(f). Further improvements in image quality were achieved using the Wiener filtering method, where the convolution kernel was calibrated by measuring the PSF of the metalens. Under white light illumination, the comparison of imaging results of the hyperbolic metalens, EDOF metalens, and EDOF metalens combined with deconvolution computational backend is shown in Fig. 35(g), which demonstrates that this method can achieve good chromatic aberration correction with a certain degree of sacrifice in SNR.

(II) End-to-end optimization methods. A particularly promising design approach involves jointly optimizing the meta-optics and the computational backend within an end-to-end framework, thereby ensuring optimal performance at the system level.

In 2021, Tseng et al. [506] implemented a neural network as the computational backend for a metalens, achieving wide-FOV and chromatic aberration-free high-quality imaging. The algorithm is shown in Fig. 35(h). The initial target phase distribution of

the metalens, operating at a single wavelength, was defined by Eq. (194) to support wide FOV imaging. Subsequently, the metasurface structure was co-optimized with a neural network performing image post-processing in an end-to-end manner, where the optimization loss function was defined as the discrepancy between the imaging result of the metalens processed by the neural network and the ground truth. By incorporating a surrogate model that solves for the electromagnetic response of the meta-atoms, the forward computational process from the metasurface structure to the loss function was ensured to be differentiable, allowing the entire system to be optimized based on the gradient descent algorithm. Compared to the imaging result of a hyperbolic metalens operating at a wavelength of 511 nm shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 35(i), the post-processed imaging result of the metalens shown in the top panel of Fig. 35(i) exhibits the excellent achromatic imaging effect. In 2025, a similar end-to-end framework was employed by Park et al. to achieve full-color imaging with a 70° FOV [512].

Another end-to-end optimization method, proposed by Pinilla et al. [508], will be discussed in the context of refractive-metasurface hybrid imaging systems.

Additionally, we note that end-to-end-optimized metasurfaces have also been applied to a wide range of applications, including subwavelength-resolution imaging $[513]$ , multidimensional information extraction $[513]$ , multi-channel imaging $[514]$ , compressed sensing recovery $[515]$ , and temperature imaging in the long-wavelength infrared regime $[516]$ .

(III) Through deep-learning-enabled image denoising and super-resolution reconstruction, high-quality imaging can be achieved for metalenses without requiring any hardware modifications to the metalens itself $[507,517–519]$ . For instance, in 2024, Dong et al. $[507]$ achieved chromatic aberration correction for the imaging result of a metalens operating at a wavelength of 526 nm, with the task being accomplished using solely the U-Net, a universal neural network architecture. The metalens, composed of cylindrical Si pillar meta-atoms, has a radius of 2.5 mm, a focal length of 8 mm, and a lattice constant of 180 nm. To prevent overfitting and boost generalization, the training dataset incorporates 1500 images from both real and synthetic objects. The experimental results shown in Fig. 35(j) demonstrate the neural network's capability to significantly enhance the metalens's imaging quality across a variety of environmental conditions and focusing distances.

Refractive-metasurface imaging system. Due to the limitation between the achromatic bandwidth, NA, and lens size as indicated by Eq. (232), the radii of almost all of the aforementioned achromatic metalenses are confined to the scale of tens of micrometers, failing to meet the demands of practical applications. By utilizing the flexible phase and dispersion modulation features of metasurfaces in conjunction with other optical elements such as traditional lenses, it is possible to achieve the design of large-scale and high-NA achromatic optical systems.

In 2018, Capasso's group [121] introduced the concept of the refractive-metasurface imaging system, which utilizes a metalens to correct for the spherical aberration and chromatic aberration for a refractive optical system. The advantage of this approach is twofold: from the perspective of the metalens, the span of group delay provided by the meta-atoms only needs to compensate for the material dispersion of the refractive optical system, which is significantly less than the span of group delay required by a standalone metalens as represented by Eq. (222); from the perspective of the refractive optical system, the metalens is expected to reduce the number of refractive optical elements needed to correct aberrations, thereby reducing the volume and weight of the optical system. For a standard refractive spherical lens (LA4249, Thorlabs Inc.), the imaging results under non-coherent white light illumination with and without a preceding corrective metalens are shown in Fig. 35(k). This refractive-metasurface

imaging system has an NA of 0.075, an effective focal length of 9.96 mm, and a peak focusing efficiency of approximately 35%, covering the entire visible spectrum for chromatic aberration correction.

In 2023, Pinilla et al. [508] leveraged a refractive-metasurface imaging system and computational imaging technology to develop a compact camera capable of high-quality full-color imaging with a large DOF. As shown in the top right of Fig. 35(1), the imaging system consists of a CMOS sensor, a refractive lens, and a front-mounted metalens, with an aperture and focal length of 5 mm. The original imaging results of the system are further reconstructed through a computational backend based on the neural network. In the design process, the hardware system was first assembled, where a spatial light modulator (SLM) was used in place of the metalens. Then, a hardware-in-loop end-to-end strategy was employed to co-optimize the phase distribution of the SLM and the neural network. Subsequently, the metalens was designed based on the optimized phase distribution of the SLM. The imaging results of the designed camera compared to those of a commercial traditional camera for a real-world scene are shown in Fig. 35(1), demonstrating the advantage of its large DOF.

Other design methods for achromatic metalenses. Beyond the methods mentioned above, there are other approaches to achieve broadband achromatic metalens designs. In 2020, Balli et al. [509] achieved a hybrid achromatic metalens with an entrance pupil diameter of 40 $\mu$ m, an NA of 0.32, and a bandwidth from 1000 nm to 1800 nm, utilizing the unit structure composed of a phase plate cascaded with a meta-atom, as illustrated in Fig. 35(m), along with a compatible design method and lithography process. The average absolute focusing efficiency (with an effective focusing area diameter three times the FWHM of the focal spot) was 27%, and the maximum relative deviation between the designated and actual focal lengths across the wavelengths was 8.1%. In 2021, this approach was further expanded by the same group to design a hybrid achromatic metalens with an entrance pupil diameter of 20 $\mu$ m, an NA of 0.27, and a bandwidth from 450 nm to 1700 nm [520].

# 4.4.4. Design Methods for Multi-Wavelength Achromatic Metalenses

In application fields such as digital imaging systems $[521]$ , augmented and virtual reality $[464]$ , and microscopy $[471]$ , multi-wavelength achromatic metalenses are fully capable of meeting performance requirements. In these cases, the NA, size, and achromatic bandwidth of the metalens are not constrained by Eq. (232).

While such metalenses are typically designed to operate under illumination at specific wavelengths, a straightforward approach for using them under broadband illumination is to introduce an appropriate bandpass filter in front of the metalens $[522]$ . Furthermore, recent experimental studies have demonstrated that multi-wavelength achromatic metalenses can also directly achieve achromatic full-color imaging under broadband illumination, provided that the sampled achromatic wavelengths are sufficiently dense to cover the target spectral bandwidth $[463,523,524]$ .

It should be noted that, theoretically, the maximum achievable range and the upper limit of the chromatic aberration-free wavelengths, as well as the limits on the lens radius and NA, have not yet been elucidated.

(I) Constructing a meta-atom database with various shapes and sizes to independently modulate the phase shift of light at different wavelengths is the most direct method to achieve multi-wavelength achromatic metalenses. Based on this approach, in 2015, Capasso's group $[525]$ achieved a 1D achromatic metalens operating at the wavelengths of 1300 nm, 1550 nm, and 1800 nm, with a focal length of 7.5 mm and an NA of 0.05. In 2016, Faraon's group $[526]$ realized a metalens with an NA of 0.7 and a ra-

dius of 100 $\mu$ m, which multiplexed for orthogonally polarized light at the wavelengths of 780 nm and 915 nm, respectively. In 2018, Capasso's group and their collaborators [527] developed a metalens operating in reflection mode with a focal length of 0.98 mm, an NA of 0.2, and a radius of 300 $\mu$ m, which corrected the chromatic aberration at the wavelengths of 460 nm, 540 nm, 567 nm, and 700 nm. These designs are either limited to restricted performance, polarization sensitivity, or reflection mode, largely due to the limited design freedom of meta-atoms, making it difficult to independently modulate the phase at each wavelength. To address this, Faraon's group [528] used 2 × 2 cylindrical a–Si meta-atoms with a height of 718 nm as the metasurface unit to realize a polarization-insensitive achromatic metalens operating at the wavelengths of 915 nm and 1550 nm, with an NA of 0.46 and a radius of 150 $\mu$ m, achieving absolute focusing efficiencies (with an effective focusing area diameter of 20 $\mu$ m) of 22% and 65% at 915 nm and 1550 nm, respectively. In 2022, Feng et al. [521] proposed using a bilayer metasurface to expand the design space and realized a polarization-insensitive achromatic metalens operating at the wavelengths of 488 nm, 532 nm, and 633 nm, with an NA of 0.8 and a diameter of 1 mm. The c–Si bilayer meta-atoms took the shapes of square pillars and cylinders, respectively, with a uniform height of 400 nm, and a uniform lattice constant of 300 nm. The layer spacing was set to be 800 nm to eliminate the interlayer coupling, making the bilayer metasurface satisfy Eq. (72). The similar work by Zhou et al. [187] has already been introduced in Subsection 2.2.1.

(II) To address the issue of insufficient design degrees of freedom, another approach is based on the strategy of spatial multiplexing $[186,187,529]$ . The most common method involves the use of multilayer metasurfaces. In 2017, Avayu et al. $[186]$ employed a three-layer cascaded structure as shown in Fig. 36(a), with a layer spacing of 200 nm to minimize interlayer coupling. Three layers of metasurfaces were designed to focus light of wavelengths 450 nm, 550 nm, and 650 nm to the same focal spot, respectively. The absolute focusing efficiency at these wavelengths ranged approximately from 5.8% to 8.7%. The metalens's imaging result is shown in Fig. 36(b), indicating the chromatic aberration-free imaging for the three wavelengths. In 2023, Baranikov et al. $[530]$ adopted a system structure with multiple metasurfaces arranged in parallel, as shown in Fig. 36(c), where three metalenses were designed as quadratic metalenses operating at the wavelengths of 460 nm, 530 nm, and 620 nm, respectively, thus achieving wide-FOV imaging without chromatic aberration for the three wavelengths. In 2024, Wang et al. $[531]$ drew inspiration from the design concept of the color metasurface hologram $[532]$ (which will be introduced in Subsection 5.2.2), arranging meta-atoms corresponding to different wavelengths in a single metasurface, thereby achieving chromatic aberration correction at the wavelengths of 400 nm, 590 nm, and 750 nm.  
(III) Applying the design method for broadband achromatic metalenses based on the hybrid phase modulation mechanism to the design of multi-wavelength achromatic lenses is expected to yield better device performance. In 2021, Capasso's group and their collaborators $[464]$ utilized this approach to achieve a metalens that corrected chromatic aberration at three wavelengths of 488 nm, 532 nm, and 658 nm, with an NA of 0.7 and a radius of 1 mm. The actual focal lengths at the operating wavelengths deviate from the set value by approximately 0.1%. To break through the constraints imposed by the achievable group delay range on the lens size and NA, as shown in Fig. 36(d), the target group delay distribution given by Eq. (209) is folded over the span of the achievable group delay $\Delta$ GD, forming several annular zones. Additional optimization is necessary for the issue of phase discontinuities at the edges of the annular zones. The experimental demonstration of the augmented reality based on the fabricated RGB-achromatic metalens is shown in Fig. 36(e).

| Zone #1 | \(\phi0\) | 0 |

| Zone #3 | \(\Delta\phi3\) | 0 |
</details>

(e)  
![](images/0a7584150f34f3345b13f92e944faf59caef2727ad2488feab661224d675c46b.jpg)

</details>
