Design methods for multi-wavelength achromatic metalenses. (a) Schematic of the structure of the multilayer multi-wavelength achromatic metalens. (b) Imaging result of the multilayer multi-wavelength achromatic metalens. (c) Schematic of the structure of a parallel imaging system composed of multiple metasurfaces. (d) Schematic of the design method for multi-wavelength achromatic metalenses based on group delay wrapping. (e) Experimental augmented reality imaging results based on an RGB-achromatic metalens, which show the RGB-color virtual image floating in a real-world scene. (f) Imaging result of a fiber endoscope based on a metalens capturing a real scene of a caterpillar crawling on a strawberry leaf. The scene was illuminated using a broad halogen light source, with the endoscope positioned approximately 10 mm away from the scene. (a) and (b) Adapted from $[186]$ under a Creative Commons license. (c) Reprinted with permission from Baranikov et al., Laser Photonics Rev. 18, 2300553 (2024) $[530]$ . Copyright © 2023 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (d) and (e) Adapted from $[464]$ under a Creative Commons license. (f) Adapted from $[463]$ under a Creative Commons license.

(IV) Inverse design-enabled multi-wavelength achromatic metalenses for monochromatic imaging. Utilizing inverse design algorithms for designing multi-wavelength achromatic metalenses holds the promise of breaking through the performance limitations imposed by traditional forward design methods $[3]$ . In 2020, Chung and Miller $[403]$ validated the superiority of topological optimization in achieving high-efficiency or high-NA achromatic metalenses through simulations. In 2023, Jiang et al. $[428]$ employed the shape optimization algorithm based on the surrogate model to design a polarization-insensitive metalens eliminating chromatic aberration at four wavelengths of 465 nm, 548 nm, 600 nm, and 620 nm, with an NA of 0.3 and a radius of 57.6 $\mu$ m. This algorithm has been discussed in Subsection 3.3. The GaN meta-atoms were rectangular pillars with a height of 600 nm and a lattice constant of 400 nm. Experimentally, the maximum deviation between the actual and designed focal lengths across the operating wavelengths was 5.2778%, and the absolute focusing efficiencies at the four wavelengths were 42.8%, 42.4%, 38%, and 33.3%, respectively.

(V) Inverse design-enabled multi-wavelength achromatic metalenses for broadband imaging. Utilizing inverse design methods to realize achromatic metalenses for multiple wavelengths with sufficiently small spectral separation enables direct full-color achromatic imaging under broadband illumination $[463,523,524,533]$ .

In 2018, Mohammad et al. [523] experimentally demonstrated an achromatic diffractive lens with an NA of 0.05 and a focal length of 1 mm, achieving a measured focusing efficiency exceeding 40% across the entire visible spectrum. The lens consisted of concentric rings with varying heights and fixed widths, optimized using a modified binary-search algorithm to maximize the average focusing efficiency across all target wavelengths within the bandwidth.

In 2023, Fröch et al. [463] utilized the inverse design method to achieve an achromatic metalens in the visible with a radius of 0.5 mm, an FOV of 22.5°, and an effective NA of 0.24, which could replace traditional gradient-index lenses in fiber endoscopes to effectively reduce the length of the rigid tip. In the design process, a surrogate model mapping from the structure of the meta-atom to its phase shift was first constructed utilizing the neural network. For incident light at a specific wavelength and incident angle, the surrogate model determines the phase distribution introduced by the current metalens design, and the PSF is calculated using the angular spectrum method (which will be introduced in Appendix A), further yielding the corresponding Strehl ratio. The optimization objective is to maximize the Strehl ratios for all incident angles within the FOV and all wavelengths within the bandwidth. In practice, continuous incident angles and wavelengths are sampled at certain intervals, with an incident angle sampling interval of 5° and a wavelength sampling interval of 10 nm. Due to the differentiability of the surrogate model and the implementation of subsequent computations using an automatic differentiation framework, the entire forward problem from the metalens structure to the objective function is differentiable, allowing for the derivation of gradient information for optimizing the metalens. The imaging result of a fiber endoscope system based on this metalens for a real scene is shown in Fig. 36(f).

In 2024, a similar approach was employed to realize a polarization-insensitive metalens with a radius of 0.5 cm, an NA of 0.45, and an achromatic bandwidth ranging from 8 $\mu$ m to 12 $\mu$ m [524].

(VI) Clearly, the previously mentioned method of employing the neural network as the computational backend for a metalens to achieve broadband achromatic imaging can also be applied to the design of multi-wavelength achromatic metalenses. Utilizing this approach, in 2024, Cheng et al. [534] achieved chromatic aberration correction at three wavelengths of 488 nm, 532 nm, and 660 nm, just using a geometric phase metalens designed for the operating wavelength of 532 nm. The image post-processing by the neural network could result in an improvement of up to 12 dB in the peak SNR of the imaging.

# 5. METASURFACE HOLOGRAMS

Computational holography creates holograms through numerical calculations without the need for actual optical devices and physical recording materials. Such holograms are known as computer-generated holograms (CGHs) [535]. Traditionally, CGHs are realized using spatial light modulators (SLMs), which are arrays of units capable of independently modulating the amplitude, phase, or polarization of light waves. In contrast, metasurface holography utilizes metasurfaces to achieve CGHs, with these holograms referred to as metasurface holograms or meta-holograms. Traditional holography manipulates wavefronts through phase accumulation, and the current SLM units

are of micrometer-scale dimensions, leading to limitations such as large device size, low holographic image resolution, low SNR, small FOV, and difficulties in eliminating higher-order diffractions and twin images $[536]$ . Metasurface holograms, with their advantages of being lightweight and ultrathin, subwavelength unit sizes, supporting flexible design, etc., can effectively address these issues.

The design of metasurface holograms proceeds in two steps. First, theoretical modeling and calculations are performed to generate the target hologram based on the desired holographic display objective. Then, adopt a metasurface to realize the target hologram, i.e., achieve the required modulation of amplitude, phase, or polarization of the incident light. Depending on the modulation type of holograms, metasurface holograms can be categorized into phase-only holograms, amplitude-only holograms, and complex amplitude holograms. Due to the advantages of metasurfaces in phase modulation, phase-only holograms are the most commonly used. Based on the multiplexing characteristics, metasurface holograms can be divided into various categories, including non-multiplexed holograms, polarization-multiplexed holograms, wavelength-multiplexed holograms, orbital angular momentum (OAM)-multiplexed, and so on.

The design workflow for metasurface holograms, including the CGH algorithms as well as metasurface design and analysis methods, is available in our open-source code repository $[73]$ .

# 5.1. Theory, Design, and Performance Analysis of Metasurface Holograms

When the plane wave interacts with a hologram, it forms a diffraction field of finite size, whose complex amplitude distribution is consistent with that of the hologram (the difference in the phase distributions is a constant term that can be ignored). The propagation of the field from the diffraction plane to the observation plane is based on the scalar diffraction theory of monochromatic light propagating in the isotropic, linear, and uniform background medium. The diffraction model for the hologram, detailed in Appendix A, serves as the foundation for the discussion on metasurface holograms. We strongly encourage readers to review this appendix, as the symbols and equations introduced therein will be frequently referenced in the subsequent discussion.

# 5.1.1. Computer-Generated Hologram Algorithms in Metasurface Holography

Corresponding to the design steps of metasurface holograms, this Subsection presents fundamental and widely used techniques for generating holograms in metasurface holography, and Subsection 5.1.2 will delve into the application of various metasurface designs to realize different types of holograms.

Computational holography involves solving for the hologram based on the target of the holographic display, which can be categorized into two types: 2D holographic images and 3D holographic scenes. Each category requires different CGH algorithms. It's necessary to emphasize that, whether designing metasurface holograms for 2D images or 3D scenes, the differences lie mainly in the hologram generation, while the methods for designing metasurfaces generally remain the same.

For 3D holographic scenes, the basic method for generating holograms is as follows. Firstly, break down 3D objects into multiple primitives using the point-based method, the polygon-based method, the layer-based method, etc. Then, based on scalar diffraction theory, the diffractive superposition of all primitives on the hologram plane is calculated to obtain the complex amplitude distribution of the hologram. To ensure subsequent metasurface designs only need to consider phase modulation, methods like adding random phases to the primitives to simulate diffuse scatterers $[537]$ can be em-

ployed to uniformize the amplitude distribution of the hologram. Based on this method, as early as 2013, Huang et al. [537] realized a 3D holographic display utilizing geometric phase metasurfaces. In recent years, CGH algorithms based on deep learning have surpassed traditional methods in terms of performance for 3D scene reconstruction [538]. For a detailed introduction to these algorithms, refer to reference [539].

Current research in metasurface holography focuses mainly on 2D holographic display, which will be discussed in detail below.

The Gerchberg–Saxton algorithm (GS algorithm) and its variants for metasurface holograms. The GS algorithm, proposed by Gerchberg and Saxton in 1972 $[540]$ , might be the first successful algorithm developed for phase retrieval. In 1978, Fienup $[541]$ reinterpreted the GS algorithm and introduced the Fienup algorithm, which can be considered a more general form of the GS algorithm. The GS algorithm for CGHs is currently the most commonly used CGH algorithm in metasurface holography. It is suitable for phase-only holograms, but can also be extended for amplitude-only or complex amplitude holograms. The core idea of the algorithm is to allow the freedom of the phase distribution on the output plane (the observation plane) and solve for the phase distribution on the input plane (the hologram) iteratively, while maintaining fixed amplitude distributions on both the input and output planes. It’s essential to note that this algorithm has diverged from its original form. For instance, the GS and Fienup algorithms were initially developed for real, non-negative signals in fields such as astronomy, not the complex amplitude signals we discuss here $[542]$ . For a systematic discussion on phase retrieval algorithms, see references $[542,543]$ .

The details of the GS algorithm and its variants can be found in Appendix B.

When the output plane lies in the Fraunhofer diffraction zone, the holograms are referred to as Fraunhofer holograms. Conversely, when the output plane is within the Fresnel diffraction zone but outside the Fraunhofer zone, they are classified as Fresnel holograms. It is essential to highlight the differences in characteristics between Fraunhofer and Fresnel holograms, as these distinctions significantly impact their optical properties and applications. For Fraunhofer holograms, it can be seen from Eq. (A18) that the diffraction pattern of the same hologram at different positions only varies in scale, as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 37. However, for Fresnel holograms, due to the impact of the $e^{i\frac{\pi}{\lambda d}(x^{2}+y^{2})}$ term within the Fourier transform in Eq. (A17), the diffraction pattern of the same hologram is related to the position of the observation plane, as illustrated in the right panel of Fig. 37. From one perspective, this makes position-multiplexed holographic display possible for the latter but not for the former; from another perspective, the holographic image of the former can be observed at any sufficiently far position meeting the Fraunhofer diffraction approximation condition in Eq. (A5), whereas the holographic image of the latter can only be observed at a fixed position.

Now, we consider the phase dispersion-free metasurface hologram based on the geometric phase modulation mechanism. Similarly, it can be observed from Eq. (A18) that for Fraunhofer holograms, the diffraction pattern of the same hologram at different wavelengths only varies in scale $[544]$ ; whereas for Fresnel holograms, due to the impact of the $e^{i\frac{\pi}{\lambda d}(x^{2}+y^{2})}$ term within the Fourier transform in Eq. (A17), the diffraction pattern of the same hologram is wavelength-dependent. From one perspective, this makes wavelength-multiplexed geometric phase-based metasurface holograms possible for the latter but not for the former; from another perspective, the former exhibits broadband characteristics not present in the latter.

Figure 37  
![](images/989aaa5d09b9edb3387179b266f22445507e34e1442859e089e5b7a89428dde2.jpg)

Schematic of holographic display of the holograms operating under Fraunhofer diffraction (left panel) and Fresnel diffraction (right panel), respectively.

Speckle suppression or high-quality holographic image generation. Speckle noise in traditional digital holography primarily originates from two sources. The first source is technical noise, which includes photon noise, electronic noise from the recording sensor, and quantization noise introduced by analog-to-digital conversion. The second source is coherent noise, which results from the differences between the actual modulation (primarily phase modulation) introduced by the hologram and the target modulation, due to factors such as the surface roughness of the sample during recording $[545]$ . For metasurface holography, speckle noise mainly arises from the latter source. The cause of modulation error of metasurface holograms is attributed to the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms not considered in the design and fabrication defects of the nanoscale features of the metasurface $[546]$ . Besides speckle noise, it's crucial to emphasize that insufficient sampling of the hologram for the target holographic image results in the actual holographic image being composed of individual speckles. In fact, the speckle issue in holographic images generated by numerous demonstrated metasurface holograms is mainly due to inadequate sampling. This type of speckle is referred to as the sampling speckle to distinguish it from the noise speckle due to the modulation error. We note that by clearly differentiating the origins of the noise speckle and sampling speckle, it is helpful to suppress the speckle problem for acquiring high-quality holographic images.

Focus on the sampling speckle first. As illustrated in Fig. 38, for the same pattern (the capital letter H), when sampled at a lower sampling rate (first row), the holographic image exhibits severe speckle issues. Increasing the sampling rate (rows 2 to 4) can significantly suppress the speckle and enhance the quality of the holographic image. However, it's important to note that increasing the sampling rate does not truly eliminate speckles but only reduces their feature size, as shown in the last column of Fig. 38. The feature size $s_{\mathrm{speckle}}$ of speckles can be estimated by the diameter of the Airy disk [547], i.e.,

$$
s _ {\text {speckle}} = 1. 2 2 \frac {\lambda}{L} d, \tag {245}
$$

where L is the aperture size of the hologram, and d is the distance from the hologram to the observation plane. For a hologram composed of $N \times N$ units, with each unit size being a, L = Na can be used as an estimate. From Eq. (245), the feature size of speckles is determined by the physical size of the hologram when the operating wavelength and the position of the observation plane are fixed. When the feature size of speckles is smaller than the size of detector pixels on the observation plane (as in the situation illustrated in the fourth row of Fig. 38), speckles will no longer be observed. Instead, differences in the intensity distributions of the actual holographic image and the target holographic image will be seen, appearing similar to noise.

Figure 38  
![](images/29d9d3bc6aedc81454b4ab77f1efff6c6db17ad025a221290bb175d12ba448f7.jpg)

Impact of the sampling speckle on the quality of the holographic image. For the target patterns in the second column with different sampling conditions (the first column), the GS algorithm is applied to solve the phase distributions of the holograms, as shown in the third column. The Fourier transform results of the complex amplitude distributions of the holograms are shown in the fourth column. When the unit size of the hologram is 300 nm, the operating wavelength is 633 nm, and the observation plane is located at 10 m from the hologram, the Rayleigh–Sommerfeld diffraction results of the complex amplitude distributions of the holograms on the observation plane are shown in the fifth column.

Next, we consider the noise speckle. As shown in Fig. 39, applying different degrees of random noise $\alpha_{\mathrm{n}}U\left(-\frac{1}{2}\pi,\frac{1}{2}\pi\right)$ [where $\alpha_{n}$ is the noise intensity factor, and $U\left(-\frac{1}{2}\pi,\frac{1}{2}\pi\right)$ represents the random number obtained from a uniform distribution between $-\frac{1}{2}\pi$ and $\frac{1}{2}\pi$ ] to the phase distribution of a hologram solved by the GS algorithm, it can be seen that when the phase deviation reaches a certain degree, severe noise speckles appear in the holographic image generated by the hologram.

The field of traditional digital holography has extensively researched speckle suppression methods. Techniques such as using a diffuser to reduce the coherence of a laser light source $[548]$ , employing partially coherent light sources $[549]$ , spatial filtering $[550]$ , and improving CGH algorithms $[547]$ can be directly adopted or adapted for use in metasurface holography. For a systematic review of these methods, see reference $[545]$ .

The field of metasurface holography has also noticed the speckle issue and made some preliminary efforts. In 2015, Zheng et al. [114] used $2 \times 2$ periodic metasurface holograms in place of a single metasurface hologram, where each hologram generates the

Holographic images of a metasurface hologram under the spatial coherent laser illumination with different numbers of effective spatial modes $N_{E}$ . Adapted from [546] under a Creative Commons license.

same holographic image but exhibits different speckle distributions, thereby effectively suppressing speckles through the superposition multiple holographic images. Both the sampling speckle and noise speckle are the result of the coherence of light waves on the observation plane, and reducing the coherence of the light source helps eliminate speckles. In 2021, Eliezer et al. [546] used a degenerate cavity laser to adjust the number of spatial modes of the laser light source, thereby modulating the spatial coherence of the light waves. As shown in Fig. 40, the more effective spatial modes a laser has, the poorer its coherence, leading to a more pronounced smoothing effect on the speckles of the holographic image. However, it's important to note that this comes at the cost of lower resolution of the holographic image.

Compensation algorithms for wide-FOV and color holographic display. The holographic image generated by the hologram solved by the GS algorithm based on the Fraunhofer or Fresnel diffraction formula will show significant distortion under a wide FOV, as shown in Fig. 38. Besides, from Fig. 38, it's also observed that when comparing the intensity distribution between the holographic image and the target image, the holographic image displays a problem that the brightness gradually decreases as the diffraction angle increases. To solve these problems, to address these issues, compensation algorithms for wide-FOV holograms are provided in Appendix C.

To achieve a color holographic display, holograms generally need to generate independent holographic images for the red, green, and blue wavelengths. According to Eqs. (A4) and (A6), for both Fresnel and Fraunhofer holograms, the diffraction pattern on the observation plane remains unchanged when $\lambda d$ is constant. Consequently, for incident light of different wavelengths, the diffraction pattern undergoes varying degrees of scaling on a fixed observation plane. From another perspective, this can also be considered the result of varying FOV angles of the same hologram for different wavelengths, as determined by Eq. (275). To mitigate this issue, compensation algorithms for color holograms are also provided in Appendix D.

Multi-objective CGH algorithms. Position multiplexing of Fresnel holograms is achieved through the multi-objective CGH algorithm. Extending the GS algorithm's notation to this context, here, the holographic display target comprises multiple output planes located at $z^i$ , each generating a holographic image $I^i(x,y)$ , corresponding to target amplitude distributions $A_2^i(x,y) = \sqrt{I^i(x,y)}$ , where the superscript $i$ , ranging from 1 to $M$ , denotes the channel index for position multiplexing. There are several algorithms available to realize this target [159,551,552], and one algorithm [159] is as follows. Specifying the positions of the output planes, perform multiple diffraction calculations to obtain the complex amplitude distributions $A_1^i(x,y) e^{i\phi_1^i(x,y)}$ for each output plane. By replacing $A_1^i(x,y)$ with $A_2^i(x,y)$ and then calculating the complex amplitude distributions $A_3^i(x,y) e^{i\phi_3^i(x,y)}$ on the input plane, the complex amplitude distribution of the hologram is taken as

$$
A _ {0} (x, y) e ^ {i \phi_ {3} (x, y)} = \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {M} w ^ {i} A _ {0} (x, y) e ^ {i \phi_ {3} ^ {i} (x, y)}, \tag {246}
$$

where $w^{i}$ is the weight of the $i^{th}$ multiplexing channel, which can be estimated by the root mean square error $RMSE^{i}$ between the amplitude distribution of the output plane in the previous iteration and the corresponding target amplitude distribution, for example,

$$
w ^ {i} = \frac {\mathrm{RMSE} ^ {i}}{\sum_ {i = 1} ^ {M} \mathrm{RMSE} ^ {i}}. \tag {247}
$$

The remaining steps are the same as those described for the GS algorithm.

Based on the multi-objective CGH algorithm, in 2017, Wei et al. [154] achieved a position-multiplexed Fresnel metasurface hologram with a multiplexing channel count of up to 6.

Since position multiplexing can be achieved solely through multi-objective CGH algorithms, it's easy to be integrated with other multiplexing methods implemented through metasurface design, such as OAM multiplexing and polarization multiplexing, thus obtaining a higher number of multiplexing channels [173,392]. Additionally, position multiplexing can enable Fresnel holograms with multiplexed orthogonal circular polarization states, which will be introduced in Subsection 5.1.2.

Apart from position multiplexing, multi-objective CGH algorithms can also be leveraged to achieve wavelength multiplexing for Fresnel holograms based on geometric phase metasurfaces $[552]$ . The algorithm is similar to the one previously described. It's evident that for geometric phase-based Fresnel metasurface holograms, implementing both position multiplexing and wavelength multiplexing is not contradictory. Applications of this will be introduced in Subsection 5.2.2. In addition, multi-

objective CGH algorithms have also been applied to realize nonlinear geometric phase metasurface holograms with simultaneous polarization and wavelength multiplexing [160].

Other CGH algorithms. Beyond the iterative algorithms such as the GS algorithm and its variants, the field of holography has been developing novel CGH algorithms in recent years, including the speckle-free CGH algorithm for partially coherent light sources based on camera-in-the-loop calibration technology $[549]$ , the real-time CGH algorithm for 3D scenes based on deep learning $[538]$ , the high-fidelity and high-resolution CGH algorithm also based on deep learning $[553]$ , and the CGH algorithm based on optimization methods $[552]$ . Among these, the algorithm utilizing automatic differentiation technology for gradient optimization has already been applied in the domain of metasurface holography $[552]$ . As the field of metasurface holography demands higher-quality holograms and better real-time performance, these computational methods are likely to see increased applications.

The implementations of the GS algorithm and the compensation algorithms for wide-FOV holographic displays are provided in our open-source code repository [73].

# 5.1.2. Metasurface Design

Holograms can be classified by their modulation type into phase-only holograms, amplitude-only holograms, and complex amplitude holograms. Here, we briefly outline the implementation methods of these three types of holograms using metasurfaces.

Phase-only metasurface holograms. It is straightforward to implement phase-only metasurface holograms based on resonant phase, propagation phase, or geometric phase modulation mechanisms. Here we focus on the characteristics of metasurface holograms using these phase modulation mechanisms.

(I) Based on resonant phase or propagation phase, by selecting appropriate structural parameters, it's possible to achieve metasurface holograms that multiplex orthogonal linear polarization states [170] and even wavelength-multiplexed metasurface holograms [527].  
(II) Geometric/Detour phase metasurfaces have the characteristic of being phase dispersion-free, meaning the phase shift distribution introduced to incident light at different wavelengths varies only by a constant term. As mentioned in Subsection 5.1.1, Fraunhofer holograms implemented based on this principle exhibit a certain working bandwidth. Ideally, without considering the influence of the coupling effect of adjacent meta-atoms, the holographic images generated within this working bandwidth will differ in only two aspects for light at different wavelengths. The first is the degree of scaling, as explained in Subsection 5.1.1. The second is the variation in energy utilization efficiency, which arises from differences in transmittance and PCE for geometric phase metasurfaces and diffraction efficiency for detour phase metasurfaces at different wavelengths.  
(III) For a Fraunhofer hologram based on the geometric phase metasurface, assuming it is designed to operate in the scenario of converting incident LCP light to output RCP light and located at $z = z_{0} = 0$ (for ease of discussion, let's denote x and y on this plane as $x'$ and $y'$ ), the introduced phase distribution is $\phi(x', y', 0)$ , with a constant amplitude distribution $A_{0}$ . From Eq. (A18), the holographic image $I(x, y, d)$ generated on the observation plane at z = d satisfies (at this point, the factors before the Fourier transform can be ignored)

$$
I (x, y, d) = \left| \mathcal {F} \left\{A _ {0} e ^ {i \phi (x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, 0)} \right\} \left(u = \frac {x}{\lambda d}, v = \frac {y}{\lambda d}, d\right) \right| ^ {2}. \tag {248}
$$

For the same metasurface hologram, when operating in the scenario of converting incident RCP light to output LCP light, the introduced phase distribution $\phi^{\prime}(x^{\prime},y^{\prime},0)=-\phi(x^{\prime},y^{\prime},0)$ [see Eq. (23)], and the holographic image $I^{\prime}(x,y,d)$ generated on the observation plane satisfies [554]

$$
I ^ {\prime} (x, y, d) = \left| \mathcal {F} \left\{A _ {0} e ^ {- i \phi (x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, 0)} \right\} \left(u = \frac {x}{\lambda d}, v = \frac {y}{\lambda d}, d\right) \right| ^ {2}. \tag {249}
$$

Expanding Eqs. (248) and (249) using Eq. (A11) reveals the relationship between the two holographic images:

$$
I ^ {\prime} (x, y, d) = I (- x, - y, d), \tag {250}
$$

which is a diagonal mirror image transformation. Based on this, Fraunhofer holograms based on geometric phase metasurfaces can achieve the holographic display as shown in Fig. 41(a) under the incidence of different circularly polarized light $[554]$ . By further restricting the observation area to either the left half or the right half of the observation plane, a display effect of orthogonal circular polarization state multiplexing can be realized.

(IV) For a Fresnel hologram based on the geometric phase metasurface, assuming it is designed to operate in the scenario of converting incident LCP light to output RCP light, with all conditions and symbols consistent with those in the Fraunhofer hologram case, the holographic image generated on the observation plane satisfies [from Eq. (A17)]

$$
I (x, y, d) = \left| \mathcal {F} \left\{A _ {0} e ^ {i \phi \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, 0\right)} e ^ {i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} \left(x ^ {\prime 2} + y ^ {\prime 2}\right)} \right\} \left(u = \frac {x}{\lambda d}, v = \frac {y}{\lambda d}, d\right) \right| ^ {2}. \tag {251}
$$

For the same metasurface hologram, when operating in the scenario of converting incident RCP light to output LCP light, the holographic image generated on the observation plane satisfies

$$
I ^ {\prime} (x, y, d) = \left| \mathcal {F} \left\{A _ {0} e ^ {- i \phi \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, 0\right)} e ^ {i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} \left(x ^ {\prime 2} + y ^ {\prime 2}\right)} \right\} \left(u = \frac {x}{\lambda d}, v = \frac {y}{\lambda d}, d\right) \right| ^ {2}. \tag {252}
$$

Expanding Eqs. (251) and (252) using Eq. (A11) reveals the relationship between the two holographic images:

$$
I ^ {\prime} (x, y, d) = I (- x, - y, - d), \tag {253}
$$

which implies that the holographic image generated at $z = d$ when operating in the scenario of converting incident RCP light to output LCP light is the diagonal mirror image of the holographic image generated at $z = -d$ when operating in the scenario of converting incident LCP light to output RCP light. Combining this rule with the position multiplexing of Fresnel holograms, when setting $z^1 = d, z^2 = -d$ in the multi-objective CGH algorithm for position multiplexing, and implementing the hologram with the geometric phase metasurface, one can achieve the holographic display effect of multiplexed orthogonal circular polarization states on the observation plane at $z = d$ [154,558]. Based on this, in 2017, Huang's group [154] achieved a metasurface hologram that combines position multiplexing with polarization multiplexing, reaching a multiplexing channel number of 8, with experimental results shown in Fig. 41(b).

Amplitude-only metasurface holograms. In the GS algorithm introduced in Subsection 5.1.1, the hologram's amplitude distribution is constant, with its phase distribution serving as the degree of freedom to generate the target holographic image. It's also conceivable to set the hologram's phase distribution as constant and utilize its amplitude modulation as the degree of freedom for hologram design [559].

Figure 41  
![](images/6548fc3bf001995cb8a73104dae38309eb97a859983de8b8fb93dea43d7298c0.jpg)

Metasurface design of the hologram. (a) Experimental holographic images generated by the geometric phase-based Fraunhofer metasurface hologram under the incidence of different circularly polarized light at a wavelength of 633 nm. (b) Experimental holographic images generated by the geometric phase-based Fresnel metasurface hologram at different observation positions and under different circular polarization states of the incident and output light. (c) Schematic of metasurface unit used for amplitude manipulation or complex amplitude manipulation, in which orange and yellow represent two kinds of meta-atoms with different sizes and rotation angles. (d) Schematic of metasurface unit used for complex amplitude hologram design. (e) Experimental holographic images generated by the complex amplitude Fresnel metasurface hologram with orthogonal linear polarization multiplexing. $|LP_{x}\rangle$ and $|LP_{y}\rangle$ mean the linear polarization state along the x and y directions, respectively. Top: polarization states of the incident and output light are $|LP_{x}\rangle$ and $|LP_{y}\rangle$ , respectively. Bottom: polarization states of the incident and output light are $|LP_{y}\rangle|LP_{y}$ and $|LP_{x}\rangle$ , respectively. (f) Experimental holographic images generated by a complex amplitude Fresnel metasurface hologram at the operating wavelength of 532 nm. (g) Schematic of metasurface unit for complex amplitude modulation based on geometric phase and detour phase. (h) Experimental reconstruction result of the complex amplitude metasurface hologram for a 3D object. (i) Top: complex amplitude profile of destination holographic scene. Middle and bottom: experimental holographic images under the observation angle $\beta$ of $-20^{\circ}$ and $0^{\circ}$ . (a) Adapted from [554] under a Creative Commons license. (b) Adapted with permission from Wei et al., Adv. Opt. Mater. 5, 1700434 (2017) [154]. Copyright © 2017 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (d) and (f) Adapted with permission from Lee et al., Nanoscale 10, 4237–4245 (2018) [555]. Copyright © 2018 The Royal Society of Chemistry. (e) Adapted from [556] under a Creative Commons license. (h) and (i) Adapted from [557] under a Creative Commons license.

(I) Generally, metasurfaces are better at phase modulation, making the former approach more commonly used. However, for several types of metasurfaces suitable for amplitude modulation, such as those based on photon sieves $[560]$ , the latter approach

also finds its application [561]. Due to the isotropic nature of photon sieve structures, metasurface holograms based on this principle exhibit polarization-insensitive characteristics, but the quantized range of amplitude modulation is often quite limited [559].

(II) Utilizing the advantage of metasurfaces in phase modulation can indeed enable arbitrary manipulation of the light field's amplitude. As shown in Fig. 41(c), we consider a $2 \times 2$ meta-atom array as the metasurface unit for amplitude modulation, containing two types of meta-atoms with different sizes and rotation angles. Let the modulation introduced by these two types of meta-atoms for any orthogonal polarization states be denoted as $t_1^\pm e^{i\phi_1^\pm}$ and $t_2^\pm e^{i\phi_2^\pm}$ , where $t$ represents the amplitude transmission coefficient, $\phi$ represents the introduced phase shift, superscript $\pm$ indicates the two orthogonal polarization states, and subscript 1 or 2 denotes the type of meta-atoms. Assuming the influence of the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms is neglected (the coupling may cause actual amplitude and phase modulation to deviate from the designed values because of the disruption of the LPA), due to the interference effect, the overall output field of the unit is the superposition of the output fields of each meta-atom; thus, the complex amplitude modulation introduced by the unit is given by

$$
t ^ {\pm} e ^ {i \phi^ {\pm}} = \frac {1}{2} \left(t _ {1} ^ {\pm} e ^ {i \phi_ {1} ^ {\pm}} + t _ {2} ^ {\pm} e ^ {i \phi_ {2} ^ {\pm}}\right). \tag {254}
$$

From Eq. (254), $t^{\pm}$ and $\phi^{\pm}$ satisfy

$$
\left(t ^ {\pm}\right) ^ {2} = \frac {1}{4} \left(\left(t _ {1} ^ {\pm}\right) ^ {2} + \left(t _ {2} ^ {\pm}\right) ^ {2} + 2 t _ {1} ^ {\pm} t _ {2} ^ {\pm} \cos \left(\phi_ {1} ^ {\pm} - \phi_ {2} ^ {\pm}\right)\right), \tag {255}
$$

and

$$
\phi^ {\pm} = \arctan \frac {t _ {1} ^ {\pm} \sin \phi_ {1} ^ {\pm} + t _ {2} ^ {\pm} \sin \phi_ {2} ^ {\pm}}{t _ {1} ^ {\pm} \cos \phi_ {1} ^ {\pm} + t _ {2} ^ {\pm} \cos \phi_ {2} ^ {\pm}}, \tag {256}
$$

respectively.

Based on the hybrid phase modulation mechanism introduced in Subsection 2.1.5, it's possible to independently modulate the phase for any orthogonal polarization states. Thus, $\phi_1^\pm -\phi_2^\pm$ in Eq. (255) can be freely adjusted by changing the shape, size, and rotation angle of the meta-atoms, thereby varying the amplitude modulation $t^{\pm}$ introduced to the light of orthogonal polarization states. In this way, the independent amplitude modulation for any orthogonal polarization states is achieved. Based on this, in 2020, Fan et al. [562] implemented orthogonal circular polarization state multiplexing metasurface nanoprinting. Nanoprinting is not the main focus of this text, and the related introduction can be found in reference [563].

Assuming the transmittance of the meta-atoms is maintained at a high level, we have $t_1^\pm \approx 1$ , and $t_2^\pm \approx 1$ . Then from Eq. (254), we have

$$
t ^ {\pm} e ^ {i \phi^ {\pm}} = \cos \frac {\phi_ {1} ^ {\pm} - \phi_ {2} ^ {\pm}}{2} e ^ {i \frac {\phi_ {1} ^ {\pm} + \phi_ {2} ^ {\pm}}{2}}. \tag {257}
$$

For amplitude-modulated metasurface holograms requiring a constant phase distribution, the phase term in Eq. (257) must be constant. For simplicity, it is set to 0, which means that

$$
\phi_ {2} ^ {\pm} = - \phi_ {1} ^ {\pm}. \tag {258}
$$

Figure 42  
![](images/8ffad52f3dd8dfc2fe6f059c70ce5168a0abaabcd2e2633254a41e3b54ada3ec.jpg)

<details>
<summary>line</summary>

| \(\theta / \pi\) | Transmission coefficient (Amplitude) | Phase shift \((\pi)\) (Amplitude) | Phase shift \((\pi)\) (Amplitude) | Phase shift \((\pi)\) (Amplitude) |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
| -0.5 | 0.00 | -0.5 | -1.0 | 1.0 |
| -0.25 | 1.00 | -0.5 | -0.5 | 1.0 |
| 0.0 | 0.00 | 0.5 | 0.0 | 1.0 |
| 0.25 | 1.00 | 0.5 | 0.5 | 1.0 |
| 0.5 | 0.00 | 0.5 | 1.0 | 1.0 |
</details>

Transmission coefficients for various polarization channels of a meta-atom rotated by $\theta$ . Top: for $y(x)$ -polarized light under $x(y)$ -polarized incidence. Middle: for RCP light under LCP incidence. Bottom: for LCP light under RCP incidence. The meta-atom is described by Eq. (14), where $t_{x}=t_{y}=1$ and $\left|\phi_{x}-\phi_{y}\right|=\pi$ .

Substituting Eq. (258) into Eq. (257) yields the expression for the overall amplitude transmission coefficient of the unit

$$
t ^ {\pm} = \cos \phi_ {1} ^ {\pm}. \tag {259}
$$

Using Eqs. (258) and (259), along with the hybrid phase modulation mechanism, it's possible to implement metasurface holograms with independent amplitude modulation for any orthogonal polarization states. A disadvantage of this method is that the lattice constant of the metasurface doubles.

(III) From Eq. (22), geometric phase metasurfaces enable phase manipulation while maintaining constant amplitude modulation. Correspondingly, we propose here a mechanism that continuously controls the amplitude by simply rotating the meta-atom, without altering the phase shift.

For a meta-atom governed by Eq. (14), the complex amplitude of the output light polarized in the y direction under x-polarized incidence is given by

$$
O (\theta) = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} 0 & 1 \end{array} \right] \mathbf {T} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] = \frac {1}{2} \sin 2 \theta \left(t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} - t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}}\right). \tag {260}
$$

This expression also applies to the complex amplitude of the output light in the x polarization under y-polarized incidence.

Using Eqs. (22) and (260), when $t_{x}=t_{y}=1$ and $\left|\phi_{x}-\phi_{y}\right|=\pi$ , the amplitude and phase modulations for different polarization channels of the meta-atom at a rotation angle $\theta$ are illustrated in Fig. 42.

As shown in the top panel of Fig. 42, when the rotation angle of the meta-atom shifts from 0 to $\frac{\pi}{4}$ , the amplitude of $O$ transitions from 0 to $\frac{1}{2}\left(t_x e^{i\phi_x} - t_y e^{i\phi_y}\right)$ , while the phase of $O$ remains constant. The maximum amplitude variation of $O$ reaches $\frac{1}{2}(t_x + t_y)$ when $\left|\phi_x - \phi_y\right| = \pi$ , aligning with Eq. (26).

From Eq. (260), we derive

$$
O \left(\theta\right) = O \left(\frac {\pi}{2} - \theta\right) = e ^ {i \pi} \left(O \left(- \theta\right)\right) = e ^ {i \pi} \left(O \left(\theta - \frac {\pi}{2}\right)\right), \tag {261}
$$

which indicates that the amplitude modulation remains consistent across the four rotation angles— $\theta$ , $\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta$ , $-\theta$ , and $\theta-\frac{\pi}{2}$ —even though the corresponding phase shifts between the first two and the last two differ by $\pi$ . As observed from Fig. 42, these four rotation angles correspond to distinct geometric phases. This property facilitates the independent modulation of amplitude and phase for various polarization channels, as demonstrated in recent studies [564,565].

Complex amplitude metasurface holograms. The extension from phase-only metasurface holograms to complex amplitude metasurface holograms introduces a series of new advantages. Firstly, under the same design conditions, a complex amplitude metasurface hologram provides an additional dimension of the light field's amplitude modulation, hence capable of generating higher quality holographic images $[11,555,557]$ , achieving a greater number of multiplexing channels $[555]$ , and even utilizing amplitude and phase modulation for distinct functionalities such as nanoprinting and holographic display $[557]$ . Secondly, phase-only metasurface holograms are generally reliant on iterative methods such as the GS algorithm, which can only reproduce the amplitude information of the target holographic display. Conversely, complex amplitude metasurface holograms overcome this limitation. Utilizing the inverse diffraction formula, either Eq. (B2) or Eq. (B1), the complex amplitude distribution of the hologram can be directly obtained from the target holographic display, thereby reconstructing both the amplitude and phase information $[557]$ . This aspect is particularly beneficial for the holographic display of 3D scenes. As described in Subsection 5.1.1, if based on phase-only holograms, achieving a uniform amplitude distribution of the hologram requires adding random phases to the primitives to simulate a diffuse body $[537]$ , at the cost of destroying the surface texture information of 3D objects. In contrast, complex amplitude holograms do not have this limitation. Below, we introduce the implementation methods of complex amplitude metasurface holograms and further present several experimental results to substantiate the aforementioned conclusions.

(I) From Eq. (257), for the metasurface unit structure as depicted in Fig. 41(c), based on the hybrid phase modulation mechanism, by modifying the shape, size, and rotation angles of two meta-atoms to adjust $\phi_{1}^{\pm}$ and $\phi_{2}^{\pm}$ , it becomes feasible to implement arbitrary complex amplitude modulation for any orthogonal polarization states. On this foundation, in 2021, Liu et al. [556] realized a complex amplitude Fresnel metasurface hologram multiplexing for orthogonal linearly polarized light, with the holographic display demonstrated in Fig. 41(e). The meta-atoms were rectangular $TiO_{2}$ pillars with a height of 600 nm. The unit size was $2 \times 450$ nm, and the operating wavelength was around 530 nm.

(II) Continue with the method previously discussed, but now consider relying solely on the geometric phase modulation mechanism. Taking orthogonal polarization states as LCP and RCP, respectively, according to Eq. (23), we have

$$
\phi_ {1} ^ {\sigma} = 2 \sigma \theta_ {1}, \phi_ {2} ^ {\sigma} = 2 \sigma \theta_ {2}, \tag {262}
$$

where $\sigma=\pm1$ corresponds to the scenarios of LCP light converting to RCP light and RCP light converting to LCP light, respectively, and $\theta_{1}$ and $\theta_{2}$ are the rotation angles

of the two types of meta-atoms, respectively. In this case, Eq. (257) simplifies to

$$
t ^ {\sigma} e ^ {i \phi^ {\sigma}} = \cos \left(\theta_ {1} - \theta_ {2}\right) e ^ {i \sigma \left(\theta_ {1} + \theta_ {2}\right)}. \tag {263}
$$

In Eq. (263), there are only two degrees of freedom on the right side of the equality $(\theta_{1}$ and $\theta_{2})$ , whereas there are four parameters to be adjusted on the left side ( $t^{\pm}$ and $\phi^{\pm}$ ), indicating that multiplexing for orthogonal circular polarization states cannot be achieved. The reason is that, when based solely on geometric phase, we have

$$
\phi_ {1} ^ {+} = - \phi_ {1} ^ {-}, \quad \phi_ {2} ^ {+} = - \phi_ {2} ^ {-}, \tag {264}
$$

meaning that the phase shifts introduced by the meta-atom for the orthogonal polarization states are coupled, rather than being independent as in the hybrid phase modulation mechanism, thus reverting to the situations described in Eq. (250) or Eq. (253) discussed earlier. If a certain value is set for $\sigma$ in Eq. (263), then arbitrary complex amplitude modulation can be realized under the corresponding polarization operational scenario by adjusting the rotation angles of the two meta-atoms. In this way, the hologram will inherit the broadband characteristic of geometric phase metasurfaces. Based on this, in 2018, Lee et al. [555] implemented a design of a complex amplitude Fresnel metasurface hologram. In this work, they overlapped the centers of two rectangular pillar meta-atoms with different rotation angles as the metasurface unit, as depicted in Fig. 41(d). The metasurface operated in the scenario of converting incident RCP light to output LCP light. Experimental demonstration of the position multiplexed holographic display is shown in Fig. 41(f). Compared to phase-only holograms designed and processed under the same conditions, the complex amplitude hologram exhibited superior SNR and speckle suppression effects.

(III) In 2020, Deng et al. [168] proposed to combine the detour phase and geometric phase to achieve independent and arbitrary control of the amplitude, phase, and polarization of light. As shown in Fig. 41(g), the metasurface unit consists of two meta-atoms with the same anisotropic structure, each possessing rotation angles of $\theta_{1}$ and $\theta_{2}$ , and displacements of $d_{1}$ and $d_{2}$ , respectively. According to Eq. (33), assuming the detour phase to be zero when $d_{1}=0$ and $d_{2}=0$ , the detour phases introduced by the two meta-atoms are $\sigma_{d}2\pi\frac{d_{1}}{a}$ and $\sigma_{d}2\pi\frac{d_{2}}{a}$ , respectively. By rationally designing the size of the meta-atoms so that the electromagnetic response intensity along their short axis is negligible compared to that along their long axis. Thus, from Eq. (14), the Jones matrices of the two meta-atoms can be expressed as

$$
\mathbf {T} _ {1} = \mathbf {R} \left(\theta_ {1}\right) \left[ \begin{array}{c c} e ^ {i \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}} 2 \pi \frac {d _ {1}}{a}} & 0 \\ 0 & 0 \end{array} \right] \mathbf {R} \left(- \theta_ {1}\right), \mathbf {T} _ {2} = \mathbf {R} \left(\theta_ {2}\right) \left[ \begin{array}{c c} e ^ {i \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}} 2 \pi \frac {d _ {2}}{a}} & 0 \\ 0 & 0 \end{array} \right] \mathbf {R} \left(- \theta_ {2}\right). \tag {265}
$$

When disregarding the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms, due to the interference effect, the unit's Jones matrix is given by

$$
\mathbf {T} = \mathbf {T} _ {1} + \mathbf {T} _ {2}. \tag {266}
$$

Taking $\sigma_{\mathrm{d}} = +1$ and assuming the displacements of the two meta-atoms satisfy $d_{1} - d_{2} = \frac{a}{2}$ , for incident light with a Jones vector of $\vec{E}^{\mathrm{in}} = [01]^{\top}$ , the Jones vector $\vec{E}^{\mathrm{out}}$ of the output light is given by

$$
\vec {E} ^ {\text {out}} = \mathbf {T} \vec {E} ^ {\text {in}} = i e ^ {i \pi \frac {d _ {1} + d _ {2}}{a}} \sin \left(\theta_ {1} - \theta_ {2}\right) \left[ \begin{array}{l} \cos \left(\theta_ {1} + \theta_ {2}\right) \\ \sin \left(\theta_ {1} + \theta_ {2}\right) \end{array} \right]. \tag {267}
$$

From Eq. (267), it is evident that the sum $d_{1} + d_{2}$ of the displacements of the two meta-atoms modulates the phase of the light field, the difference $\theta_{1} - \theta_{2}$ in their rotation angles modulates the amplitude of the light field, and the sum $\theta_{1} + \theta_{2}$ of their rotation angles controls the polarization state of the output light. The application of this method in color holographic display will be introduced in Subsection 5.2.2.

Additionally, the metasurface based on this unit structure was also applied by Bao et al. [566] in the same year for the generation of vector vortex beams. There was no change in principle, but instead of deriving the explicit modulation formulas mentioned above, a genetic algorithm was used to find the structural parameters corresponding to the targeted complex amplitude modulation.

To achieve higher modulation freedom, in 2021, Bao et al. [11] further expanded the aforementioned unit structure to a unit composed of $2 \times 2$ meta-atoms, thus extending the unit's Jones matrix from Eq. (266) to

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathbf {T} = \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {n} \mathbf {T} _ {j} = \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {n} \mathbf {R} \Big (\theta_ {j} \Big) \left[ \begin{array}{c c} e ^ {i \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}} 2 \pi \frac {d _ {j}}{a}} & 0 \\ 0 & 0 \end{array} \right] \mathbf {R} \Big (- \theta_ {j} \Big) \\ = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {n} e ^ {i \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}} 2 \pi \frac {d _ {j}}{a}} \cos^ {2} \theta_ {j} & \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {n} e ^ {i \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}} 2 \pi \frac {d _ {j}}{a}} \sin \theta_ {j} \cos \theta_ {j} \\ \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {n} e ^ {i \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}} 2 \pi \frac {d _ {j}}{a}} \sin \theta_ {j} \cos \theta_ {j} & \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {n} e ^ {i \sigma_ {\mathrm{d}} 2 \pi \frac {d _ {j}}{a}} \sin^ {2} \theta_ {j} \end{array} \right], \tag {268} \\ \end{array}
$$

where n is the number of meta-atoms in the unit, here taken as 4, and $\theta_{j}$ and $d_{j}$ represent the rotation angle and displacement of the $j^{th}$ meta-atom, respectively. The application of the metasurface based on this unit structure will be introduced in Subsection 5.2.1.

(IV) Metasurfaces composed of single meta-atom units. In essence, the methods mentioned above are all based on the interference of the electromagnetic responses of multiple meta-atoms within a metasurface unit to produce a more complex light field modulation. For the metasurface composed of single meta-atom units, if resonant or propagation phase is used for complex amplitude modulation, it is necessary to traverse the shape and size parameters of the meta-atoms to simultaneously satisfy the amplitude and phase modulation requirements. This is often not trivial and might require a large enough unit to provide sufficient design space, as well as the use of various shapes of meta-atoms $[567]$ .

In 2018, Song et al. [357] utilized the hybrid phase modulation mechanism to realize the complex amplitude metasurface composed of single meta-atom units. Firstly, the shape and size parameters of the meta-atoms are traversed to select multiple meta-atoms with different efficiencies (the product of transmittance and PCE) corresponding to discrete amplitude modulation at different quantization levels. From the PCE expressions in Eq. (25) or Eq. (28), the amplitude modulation is primarily realized through resonant or propagation phase [568]. Subsequently, phase modulation is introduced by rotating the meta-atoms. It should be noted that when the rotation angles of the meta-atoms with different shape and size parameters are all 0, the introduced geometric phases $\phi_{i}$ are not consistent (the subscript $i$ indicates the index of each type of meta-atom). To compensate for the initial phase inconsistency, when the $i^{\mathrm{th}}$ meta-atom is used to achieve the target phase shift $\phi_{\mathrm{d}}$ , its rotation angle should be

$$
\theta = \frac {\phi_ {\mathrm{d}} - \phi_ {i}}{2 \sigma}, \tag {269}
$$

where $\sigma = \pm 1$ corresponds to the cases when the incident light is LCP and RCP, respectively. Since meta-atoms of different shapes and sizes have different phase dispersion characteristics, the complex amplitude metasurface based on this method can only operate at a single operating wavelength. On this foundation, in 2019, Overvig et al. [557] experimentally demonstrated the superiority of complex amplitude metasurface holograms over phase-only metasurface holograms. To verify that the complex amplitude hologram can simultaneously reconstruct the amplitude and phase information of holographic scenes, as shown in the top panel of Fig. 41(i), the target holographic scene is a superposition of the amplitude distribution of the Tai Chi diagram and the

phase distribution with a specific gradient. The holographic image generated for this target scene using the complex amplitude hologram can only be observable at a certain tilted viewing angle determined by the phase gradient, as shown in the middle and bottom panels of Fig. 41(i). Besides, the 3D object generated by another complex amplitude metasurface hologram is shown in Fig. 41(h), demonstrating its fidelity in reproducing the surface texture effects such as the roughness of 3D objects.

The aforementioned method achieves different quantization levels of amplitude modulation by changing the shape and size of the meta-atoms. Similarly, in 2020, Ren et al. [392] realized the same effect by adjusting the height of the meta-atoms. However, metasurfaces composed of meta-atoms with different heights require specific fabrication techniques, such as the 3D laser nanoprinting mentioned in Subsection 3.1.

# 5.1.3. Performance Metrics and Analysis of Metasurface Holography

Unlike metalenses, the performance evaluation of metasurface holograms lacks a unified system. Moreover, the quantitative assessment of efficiency, FOV, and quality of holographic images, among other factors, is often overlooked in some studies. This poses significant challenges for comparing different works and understanding the characteristics and performance limits of metasurface holograms.

To measure the efficiency of metasurface holograms operating in transmission mode, transmittance and absolute/relative efficiencies are commonly used. The definition of transmittance $\tau$ remains the same as before, following Eq. (173). The absolute efficiency is defined as

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{AE}} = \frac {P _ {\text {hologram}}}{P _ {\text {in}}}, \tag {270}
$$

where $P_{hologram}$ is the light power in the area of the holographic image, and $P_{in}$ is the incident light power. The relative efficiency is defined as

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{RE}} = \frac {P _ {\text {hologram}}}{P _ {\text {out}}}, \tag {271}
$$

where $P_{out}$ is the transmitted light power. Relative efficiency (more strictly, $\frac{\eta_{RE}}{1-\eta_{RE}}$ ) measures the ratio of transmitted light inside and outside the holographic image area, where the latter may contribute to undesirable effects such as stray light, twin holographic images, and unwanted diffraction orders. The relationship between these three indicators is

$$
\eta_ {\mathrm{AE}} = \tau \eta_ {\mathrm{RE}}. \tag {272}
$$

Due to the coupling between adjacent meta-atoms and the fabrication defects, a zeroth-order diffraction bright spot may appear on the observation plane, which often has a high light power $P_{\mathrm{zero}}$ [569]. When the measured result of $P_{\mathrm{hologram}}$ according to its definition includes $P_{\mathrm{zero}}$ , it's necessary to separately measure the absolute efficiency $\eta_{\mathrm{zero,AE}} = \frac{P_{\mathrm{zero}}}{P_{\mathrm{in}}}$ or relative efficiency $\eta_{\mathrm{zero,RE}} = \frac{P_{\mathrm{zero}}}{P_{\mathrm{out}}}$ of the zeroth-order diffraction spot to avoid overestimating the efficiency of the metasurface hologram.

The quality of holographic images may be affected by the speckles, zeroth-order diffraction spot, uneven energy distribution, etc. Based on theories from the field of image processing, currently, the most commonly used indicators for measuring the quality of holographic images include RMSE $[167,570]$ and peak signal-to-noise ratio (PSNR) $[388]$ . The RMSE measures the difference between the generated holographic image

and the target image, which is defined as

$$
\mathrm{RMSE} = \sqrt {\frac {\sum_ {n _ {x} = 1} ^ {N _ {x}} \sum_ {n _ {y} = 1} ^ {N _ {y}} \left(I _ {\text {target}} \left(n _ {x} , n _ {y}\right) - I _ {\text {real}} \left(n _ {x} , n _ {y}\right)\right) ^ {2}}{N _ {x} N _ {y}}}, \tag {273}
$$

where $N_{x} \times N_{y}$ is the size of the target image, $I_{\mathrm{target}}\left(n_{x}, n_{y}\right)$ is the normalized target image, $I_{\mathrm{real}}\left(n_{x}, n_{y}\right)$ is the normalized $N_{x} \times N_{y}$ holographic image obtained through sampling the actual generated holographic image. The PNSR is defined as the ratio of the peak intensity of the generated holographic image to the background noise intensity. When taking RMSE as an estimate of the background noise for the generated holographic image, its formula in decibels is

$$
\mathrm{PSNR} [ d B ] = 1 0 \lg \left(\frac {\max _ {n _ {x} , n _ {y}} I _ {\text {real}} \left(n _ {x} , n _ {y}\right)}{\mathrm{RMSE}}\right) ^ {2}. \tag {274}
$$

The hologram's FOV equals twice its maximum diffraction angle. In the design of metasurface holograms, the theoretically achievable maximum diffraction angle is generally used as an estimate of the FOV. The maximum phase gradient that a metasurface hologram can introduce is $\frac{\pi}{a}$ , where $a$ is the lattice constant of the metasurface. According to the generalized law of refraction or reflection [Eq. (57) or (58)], regardless of whether the hologram operates in transmission or reflection mode, the upper limit of the achievable diffraction angle for perpendicularly incident light is expressed as

$$
\theta_ {\max} = \arcsin \frac {\lambda_ {0}}{2 a n _ {\text {back}}} = \arcsin \frac {\lambda}{2 a}. \tag {275}
$$

From Eq. (275), it is evident that the FOV is related to the relative magnitude of the operating wavelength and the lattice constant. To achieve a holographic image with an FOV of $2\theta_{max}$ , the lattice constant of the metasurface must satisfy

$$
a \leq \frac {\lambda}{2 \sin \theta_ {\max}}. \tag {276}
$$

From Eq. (276), to achieve a holographic image with a full FOV, i.e., $2\theta_{max} = 2\pi$ , the lattice constant must satisfy

$$
a \leq \frac {\lambda}{2}. \tag {277}
$$

# 5.2. Design Methods for Multiplexed Metasurface Holography

Multiplexed metasurface holography is crucial for various applications in full-color holographic display, higher-density information storage, encryption, etc. The design methods for different types of multiplexed metasurface holograms are systematically reviewed below, with a special focus on the design philosophy behind each method.

# 5.2.1. Polarization-Multiplexed Metasurfaces

As discussed in Subsection 2.1.5, for metasurfaces operating at a single wavelength, the hybrid phase modulation mechanism can be used for realizing metasurface holograms multiplexed for arbitrary orthogonal polarization states $[169]$ and multi-channel polarization-multiplexed metasurface holograms $[135,170]$ . As mentioned in Subsection 5.1.2, by exploiting the properties of geometric phase metasurfaces, holographic displays multiplexed for orthogonal circular polarization states can be achieved $[154,554,558]$ . We now continue to introduce other design methods for polarization-multiplexed metasurface holograms.

(I) A simple form of orthogonal circular polarization multiplexing. As discussed in Subsection 2.1.5, by simultaneously utilizing geometric phase and resonant or propagating phase, it is possible to design metasurfaces multiplexing orthogonal circular polarization states. Here, a simpler form of this conclusion is presented, along with the corresponding design method.

According to Eq. (22), for the output light with the circular polarization state opposite to that of the incident light, the geometric phase introduces a phase shift of $\phi_{\mathrm{geometric}}(\sigma) = 2\sigma \theta$ . The resonant or propagating phase introduces the complex amplitude modulation $t_x e^{i\phi_x} - t_y e^{i\phi_y}$ , denoted as $te^{i\phi}$ . After normalization of the amplitude, we have

$$
t e ^ {i \phi} = \frac {1}{2} \left(t _ {x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x}} - t _ {y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y}}\right). \tag {278}
$$

Assuming the transmittance of the designed meta-atoms remains at a high level, i.e., $t_{x} \approx 1$ and $t_{y} \approx 1$ , Eq. (278) becomes

$$
t e ^ {i \phi} = \sin \frac {\phi_ {x} - \phi_ {y}}{2} e ^ {i \frac {\phi_ {x} + \phi_ {y} + \pi}{2}}, \tag {279}
$$

which indicates that the resonant or propagating phase introduced by the meta-atom is

$$
\phi_ {\text {non - geometric}} = \frac {\phi_ {x} + \phi_ {y} + \pi}{2}, \tag {280}
$$

which is independent of $\sigma$ . From Eqs. (28) and (279), meta-atoms satisfying

$$
\left| \phi_ {x} - \phi_ {y} \right| = \pi \tag {281}
$$

achieve the maximum amplitude transmission coefficient of 1 and the maximum PCE of 1 simultaneously. When the design space is sufficient, it is feasible to select meta-atoms that realize a phase coverage of $2\pi$ for $\phi_{non-geometric}$ while satisfying Eq. (281). Thus, for the output light of opposite circular polarization to the incident light, the total phase shift introduced by the meta-atom is

$$
\phi_ {\text {total}} (\sigma) = \phi_ {\text {geometric}} (\sigma) + \phi_ {\text {non - geometric}}. \tag {282}
$$

To introduce independent phase modulations $\phi_{+}$ and $\phi_{-}$ for the LCP and RCP incident light, respectively, we have

$$
\phi_ {\text {total}} (\sigma = + 1) = \phi_ {+}, \quad \phi_ {\text {total}} (\sigma = - 1) = \phi_ {-}, \tag {283}
$$

Substituting Eq. (282) into Eq. (283), we obtain

$$
\phi_ {\text {non - geometric}} = \frac {\phi_ {+} + \phi_ {-}}{2}, \quad 2 \theta = \frac {\phi_ {+} - \phi_ {-}}{2}. \tag {284}
$$

which shows that by using both geometric phase and resonant or propagating phase, independent phase modulation can be introduced to LCP and RCP incident light, respectively. Based on this, in 2020 and 2024, Li et al. [564] and Gong et al. [565] realized metasurfaces that incorporate both nanoprinting and holographic display multiplexing orthogonal circular polarization states, respectively. Wherein, the nanoprinting functionality is achieved by imposing restrictions on the rotation angles of the meta-atoms, as described by Eq. (261). In 2023, Yang et al. [571] applied this method to realize metasurface holograms multiplexing both orthogonal circular polarization states and OAMs, which will be further introduced in Subsection 5.2.3.

(II) Arbitrary symmetric Jones matrix modulation with six degrees of freedom. Now we further extend the discussion concerning J in Subsection 2.1.5. For a fixed wavelength,

arbitrary amplitude and phase modulation can be described by Eq. (36). For a single-layer metasurface with the unit composed of multiple meta-atoms, J should still satisfy the constraint of Eq. (37). In this context, J can be expressed as

$$
\mathbf {J} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} J _ {x x} & J _ {x y} \\ J _ {x y} & J _ {y y} \end{array} \right] = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} t _ {x x} e ^ {i \phi_ {x x}} & t _ {x y} e ^ {i \phi_ {x y}} \\ t _ {x y} e ^ {i \phi_ {x y}} & t _ {y y} e ^ {i \phi_ {y y}} \end{array} \right], \tag {285}
$$

which comprises a total of 6 degrees of freedom. For a single-layer metasurface with the unit composed of only one meta-atom, each unit only has three adjustment degrees of freedom $(\phi_x, \phi_y, \text{and} \theta)$ (only 5 adjustment degrees of freedom even considering $t_x$ and $t_y$ , additionally), so this kind of metasurfaces cannot be used to realize the Jones matrix expressed by Eq. (285).

To achieve the modulation expressed by Eq. (285), in 2021, Bao et al. [11] adopted the unit structure composed of $2 \times 2$ meta-atoms described by Eq. (268), which has been introduced in Subsection 5.1.2. The unit structure is depicted in Fig. 43(a). Utilizing both the detour phase and the geometric phase, each meta-atom has two degrees of freedom for adjustment: displacement $d_{j}$ and rotation angle $\theta_{j}$ , giving the entire unit a total of 8 degrees of freedom. The extra degrees of freedom are provided to impose additional constraints $d_{2} - d_{1} > \frac{a_{x}}{2}$ and $d_{4} - d_{3} > \frac{a_{y}}{2}$ [see Fig. 43(a) for the definitions of $a_{x}$ and $a_{y}$ ], to prevent overlapping of meta-atoms due to their displacements. Taking the 6 target amplitude and phase parameters in Eq. (285) as the optimization objective, with displacements $d_{j}$ and rotation angles $\theta_{j}$ as optimization variables, and using Eq. (268) and the aforementioned two inequalities as constraints, the metasurface unit structure is found through a genetic algorithm. Based on this, a polarization-multiplexed metasurface incorporating both nanoprinting and holographic display functions was realized. The rectangular pillar meta-atoms were made of c–Si, with a height of 600 nm and dimensions of 170 nm by 40 nm. The unit size is $a_{x} = 900$ nm, and $a_{y} = 450$ nm. To avoid the coupling between adjacent units, the spacing between units is set to be 4 $\mu$ m. Under different linear polarization states for incident and output light, nanoprinting images modulated by $t_{xx}$ , $t_{xy}$ , and $t_{yy}$ were observed near the metasurface, as shown in the first row of Fig. 43(b), and holographic images generated by the complex amplitude distributions $t_{xx} e^{i\phi_{xx}}$ , $t_{xy} e^{i\phi_{xy}}$ , and $t_{yy} e^{i\phi_{yy}}$ were observed at 1.5 mm above the metasurface, as shown in the second row of Fig. 43(b).

Certainly, to achieve the modulation expressed by Eq. (285), the unit structure above is not the only option. For example, as shown in Fig. 43(c), the unit is composed of $2 \times 2$ meta-atoms based on geometric phase and resonant or propagating phase. Using the Jones matrix expression of a single meta-atom described by Eq. (14), the unit's Jones matrix can be written as

$$
\mathbf {T} = \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {n} \mathbf {T} _ {j} = \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {n} \mathbf {R} \left(\theta_ {j}\right) \mathbf {T} _ {0, j} \mathbf {R} \left(- \theta_ {j}\right), \tag {286}
$$

where n is the number of meta-atoms in the unit, here taken as 4. This setup still allows for the use of the aforementioned optimization method to design the unit structure.

(III) Full-parameter Jones matrix modulation with eight degrees of freedom. The discussion above indicates that a symmetric Jones matrix, as expressed by Eq. (285), can be achieved through single-layer metasurfaces with sufficient design degrees. Yet, to realize a full-parameter Jones matrix with a total of 8 degrees of freedom, we must use metasurfaces that break the out-of-plane symmetry, with multilayer metasurfaces being a common choice.

Figure 43  
![](images/b27f15e8fe2943880ec0f8bfad94d5f8acfd196a394129da88e7cf445af33463.jpg)

Multi-polarization metasurface hologram design. (a) Schematic of metasurface unit for arbitrary polarization and complex amplitude modulation based on geometric phase and detour phase. (b) Experimental nanoprinting images and holographic images generated by the metasurface hologram under different polarization states of the incident and output light. The metasurface operated at a wavelength of 671 nm. The arrows represent the directions of the linear polarizations, and “I” and “O” represent the input and output light, respectively. (c) Schematic of metasurface unit for arbitrary polarization and complex amplitude modulation based on geometric phase and resonant or propagation phase. (d) Experimental nanoprinting and holographic images generated by the bilayer metasurface. The arrows located at the lower left and lower right of each panel represent the incident and output polarization, respectively. (e) Experimental holographic images of a multi-polarization metasurface hologram at 11 linear polarization states. The metasurface operated at a wavelength of 700 nm. (continued)

(continued) The arrows represent the directions of the linear polarizations. (f) Experimental holographic images generated by the multi-polarization detour phase metasurface hologram under the incidence of orthogonal linearly polarized light at a wavelength of 532 nm. The white arrows in the center of images indicate the directions of the linear polarizations. (g) Schematic of the detour phase metasurface unit based on geometric phase in two meta-atom rotation directions. (h) False-color SEM of two units of the multi-polarization detour phase metasurface hologram. Each unit consists of two parts painted purple and green, respectively. (i) Experimental holographic images generated by the multi-polarization detour phase metasurface hologram under the incidence of different circularly polarized light at a wavelength of 1350 nm. (b) Adapted from [11] under a Creative Commons license. (d) Adapted from [172] under a Creative Commons license. (e) Adapted with permission from Xiong et al., Science 379, 294–299 (2023) [173]. Copyright © 2023 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (f) Adapted with permission from Min et al., Laser Photonics Rev. 10, 978–985 (2016) [166]. Copyright © 2016 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (h) and (i) Adapted from [161] under a Creative Commons license.

For example, consider the case of a bilayer metasurface. The Jones matrices of the bottom and top layer metasurfaces under forward illumination are denoted, respectively, as

$$
\mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} ^ {1} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} T _ {x x} ^ {1} & T _ {x y} ^ {1} \\ T _ {y x} ^ {1} & T _ {y y} ^ {1} \end{array} \right], \quad \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} ^ {2} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} T _ {x x} ^ {2} & T _ {x y} ^ {2} \\ T _ {y x} ^ {2} & T _ {y y} ^ {2} \end{array} \right], \tag {287}
$$

where $T_{yx}^{1}=T_{xy}^{1}$ and $T_{yx}^{2}=T_{xy}^{2}$ because of the out-of-plane symmetry for single-layer metasurfaces. Under backward illumination, as described by Eq. (12), the reciprocity dictates that the Jones matrices of the two layers of metasurfaces become, respectively:

$$
\mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{b}} ^ {1} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} T _ {x x} ^ {1} & - T _ {y x} ^ {1} \\ - T _ {x y} ^ {1} & T _ {y y} ^ {1} \end{array} \right], \quad \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{b}} ^ {2} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} T _ {x x} ^ {2} & - T _ {y x} ^ {2} \\ - T _ {x y} ^ {2} & T _ {y y} ^ {2} \end{array} \right]. \tag {288}
$$

Ignoring the interlayer coupling, the total Jones matrices of the bilayer metasurface under forward illumination and backward illumination are, respectively,

$$
\mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} = \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} ^ {2} \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} ^ {1}, \quad \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{b}} = \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{b}} ^ {1} \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{b}} ^ {2}. \tag {289}
$$

Substituting Eqs. (287) and (288) into Eq. (289), we obtain

$$
\mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} A & C \\ D & B \end{array} \right], \quad \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{b}} = \left[ \begin{array}{c c} A & - D \\ - C & B \end{array} \right], \tag {290}
$$

where $A = T_{xx}^{1}T_{xx}^{2} + T_{xy}^{1}T_{xy}^{2}$ , $B = T_{xy}^{1}T_{xy}^{2} + T_{yy}^{1}T_{yy}^{2}$ , $C = T_{xy}^{1}T_{xx}^{2} + T_{yy}^{1}T_{xy}^{2}$ , and $D = T_{xx}^{1}T_{xy}^{2} + T_{xy}^{1}T_{yy}^{2}$ . To break the out-of-plane symmetry, we have

$$
C \neq D, \tag {291}
$$

i.e.,

$$
T _ {x y} ^ {1} \left(T _ {x x} ^ {2} - T _ {y y} ^ {2}\right) \neq T _ {x y} ^ {2} \left(T _ {x x} ^ {1} - T _ {y y} ^ {1}\right). \tag {292}
$$

From Eq. (292), it's evident that when $T_{xy}^{1} = T_{xy}^{2} = 0$ , it's impossible to achieve a full-parameter Jones matrix. We note that the scenario of backward illumination has also been considered in the above derivation, which will be used in Subsection 5.2.4.

In 2020, Yuan et al. [171] achieved independent phase modulation of the four polarization channels in the Jones matrix using a five-layer cascade of metasurfaces operating in the microwave band. Subsequently, in 2022, Bao et al. [172] experimentally demonstrated a bilayer metasurface supporting a full-parameter Jones matrix with 8 degrees of freedom. The layer spacing of 150 $\mu$ m corresponds to scenario (I) discussed in Subsection 2.2.1. The total electromagnetic response of the bilayer metasurface was calculated using the Rayleigh–Sommerfeld diffraction formula. The Jones matrix distributions for the two layers of metasurfaces, as expressed by Eq. (285), were optimized using the gradient descent algorithm to achieve the target full-parameter Jones matrix distribution. Each layer of metasurfaces utilized the unit composed of 2 × 2 c–Si meta-atoms to obtain a Jones matrix with 6 degrees of freedom. The unit size is 800 nm, and the operating wavelength is 808 nm. Under various linear polarization states for incident and output light, experimental nanoprinting images modulated by $t_{xx}$ , $t_{xy}$ , $t_{yx}$ , and $t_{yy}$ as well as holographic images generated by the complex amplitude distributions $t_{xx}e^{i\phi_{xx}}$ , $t_{xy}e^{i\phi_{xy}}$ , $t_{yx}e^{i\phi_{yx}}$ , and $t_{yy}e^{i\phi_{yy}}$ , are shown in Fig. 43(i).

(IV) Breaking the limitation on the number of independent polarization multiplexing channels. For a single-layer metasurface whose Jones matrix is expressed by Eq. (285), considering a linearly polarized incident light with the Jones vector of $\vec{E}^{in} = [\cos\gamma \sin\gamma]^{\top}$ , where $\gamma$ is the angle of the x axis and linear polarization direction, the complex amplitude of the output light in that polarization direction is

$$
O = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} \cos \gamma & \sin \gamma \end{array} \right] \mathbf {J} \left[ \begin{array}{l} \cos \gamma \\ \sin \gamma \end{array} \right] = \left[ \begin{array}{l l l} \cos^ {2} \gamma & \sin 2 \gamma & \sin^ {2} \gamma \end{array} \right] \left[ \begin{array}{l} J _ {x x} \\ J _ {x y} \\ J _ {y y} \end{array} \right]. \tag {293}
$$

Further considering a set of incident lights with polarization angles $\gamma_{1}, \gamma_{2}, \ldots, \gamma_{M}$ , after interacting with the metasurface, the matrix $O = [O_{1}, O_{2}, \ldots, O_{M}]^{\top}$ of complex amplitudes of the output light in the corresponding polarization direction is

$$
\mathbf {O} = \mathbf {A} \tilde {\mathbf {J}} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l l} \cos^ {2} \gamma_ {1} & \sin 2 \gamma_ {1} & \sin^ {2} \gamma_ {1} \\ \cos^ {2} \gamma_ {2} & \sin 2 \gamma_ {2} & \sin^ {2} \gamma_ {2} \\ \dots \\ \cos^ {2} \gamma_ {M} & \sin 2 \gamma_ {M} & \sin^ {2} \gamma_ {M} \end{array} \right] \left[ \begin{array}{l} J _ {x x} \\ J _ {x y} \\ J _ {y y} \end{array} \right], \tag {294}
$$

where $\tilde{J}$ can be seen as another form of the symmetric Jones matrix J. From Eq. (294), for M specific polarization angles and the corresponding complex amplitudes, the solution for the Jones matrix of the metasurface is

$$
\tilde {\mathbf {J}} = \mathbf {A} ^ {- 1} \mathbf {O}. \tag {295}
$$

From Eq. (295), it is clear that when $M \leq 3$ , an exact solution for $\tilde{\mathbf{J}}$ can be obtained, meaning that $M$ polarization channels are entirely independent, whereas when $M > 3$ , the components within the actual $\mathbf{O}$ will be correlated, leading to crosstalk between polarization channels. To disrupt the linear correlation between polarization channels when the number of channels exceeds 3, in 2023, Xiong et al. [173] introduced engineered noise into the metasurface, thereby breaking the physical limit on the number of independent polarization multiplexing channels. A novel solution for Eq. (294) was proposed as

$$
\tilde {\mathbf {J}} ^ {\prime} = \left(\mathbf {A} ^ {\top} \mathbf {A}\right) ^ {- 1} \mathbf {A} ^ {\top} \mathbf {O} + \delta \mathbf {J}, \tag {296}
$$

where the first term on the right side represents the minimum square error approximation solution for Eq. (294), and $\delta \mathbf{J}$ represents the noncorrelated noise matrix where each component is randomly sampled from a 2D complex normal distribution. The introduction of noise breaks the correlation between the components of the new output matrix $\mathbf{O}' = \mathbf{A}\tilde{\mathbf{J}}'$ , meaning that the crosstalk between different polarization channels

will be eliminated by the noise. The metasurface utilized the unit structure expressed by Eq. (286), as shown in Fig. 43(c), to achieve the modulation represented by $\tilde{\mathbf{J}}'$ . As demonstrated in Fig. 43(d), a metasurface hologram multiplexing up to 11 independent linear polarization states was realized.

The concept described above can be extended to a more generalized framework, enabling access to a larger number of polarization multiplexing channels. Consider a series of polarization states, each represented by a Jones vector $\vec{E}^{i}$ ( $i=1,2,\ldots,M$ , where M denotes the number of polarization states). According to Eq. (55), for a single-layer metasurface characterized by the Jones matrix J, the complex amplitude of the output light in the polarization state $\vec{E}^{j}$ for incident light in the polarization state $\vec{E}^{i}$ is given by

$$
O ^ {i j} = \left(\vec {E} ^ {j}\right) ^ {\dagger} \mathbf {J} \vec {E} ^ {i}. \tag {297}
$$

Due to the symmetry of J, it follows that $O^{ij} = O^{ji}$ . Consequently, the number of independent elements for $O^{ij}$ is $\left(M^{2} + M\right)/2$ . We note that the previously introduced design is a specific case of this more general form, where only symmetric polarization channels $(O^{ii})$ are utilized, and only linear polarization states are considered. The primary challenge in this approach lies in achieving holographic image generation across all these polarization channels with minimal crosstalk through the elaborate design of the metasurface's Jones matrix distribution J $(x, y)$ . In 2024, Wang et al. [572] developed a neural network to compute the diffraction patterns for various polarization channels associated with J $(x, y)$ . The neural network's support for automatic differentiation allowed for the application of the gradient descent algorithm to optimize J $(x, y)$ , minimizing the MSE between the reconstructed and target holographic images across all polarization channels. By employing a metasurface with a hybrid phase modulation mechanism [where J $(x, y)$ assumes the form given by Eq. (54)], holographic display multiplexing was achieved for up to 55 linear polarization channels $(M = 10)$ .

(V) Leveraging the polarization sensitivity of the diffraction characteristics of detour phase metasurface units, the design for polarization-multiplexed detour phase metasurface holograms can be realized.

Sub-wavelength slits on metal films exhibit strong polarization sensitivity.
