For a horizontal slit, only the incident light polarized along the vertical direction can excite surface plasmons to pass through the slit, while the incident light polarized along the horizontal direction is almost entirely reflected. Based on this, in 2016, Yuan's group $[166]$ achieved the metasurface that generated optical vortices and Airy beams, respectively, for orthogonal linearly polarized incident light, as shown in Fig. 43(e). The unit structure was composed of two slits oriented in orthogonal directions, where the slits along each direction introduced an independent detour phase for corresponding linearly polarized incident light.

Similarly, in 2018, Deng et al. [167] used the polarization-sensitive rectangular pillar as the unit for a detour phase metasurface, achieving the holographic display multiplexed for orthogonal linear polarization states.

Considering the design of the detour phase metasurface unit based on geometric phase, as shown in the first row of Fig. 43(f), by rotating the meta-atoms, a specific phase gradient is formed and the incident light will be diffracted to the corresponding diffraction angle. From Eq. (23), for LCP and RCP incident light, the introduced phase gradients are opposite to each other; thus, the diffraction angles of the output light are also opposites of each other when light is incident perpendicularly. A detour phase metasurface hologram implemented with such units will generate the same holographic image for the perpendicularly incident light with different circular polarization states at the cor-

responding opposite diffraction angles. If the meta-atoms are rotated in the opposite direction, as shown in the second row of Fig. 43(f), for LCP or RCP incident light, the introduced phase gradient will be the opposite of the original, and the corresponding diffraction angle will also be reversed. In 2016, Capasso's group $[161]$ combined two groups of geometric phase meta-atoms arranged in opposite rotation directions as the unit for a detour phase metasurface, as shown in Fig. 43(g), with the meta-atom groups with two rotation directions represented in purple and green, respectively. The meta-atom groups with different rotation directions introduce independent displacements, thus detour phase distributions for generating different holographic images. When observation is made only at one diffraction angle, with the incidence of different circularly polarized light, two distinct holographic images will be observed, as shown in Fig. 43(h).

(VI) Despite the design methods for polarization-multiplexed metasurface holograms mentioned above, the classic strategy of spatial multiplexing still finds its applications. In 2022, Rho's group $[573]$ spatially interleaved metasurface units corresponding to different vector polarization states in the design of vector metasurface holograms. In 2020, Deng et al. $[168]$ alternately arranged the metasurface units corresponding to different polarization channels in a direction perpendicular to the meta-atoms' displacement direction, achieving polarization multiplexing for the complex amplitude metasurface hologram based on detour phase and geometric phase. Essentially, these approaches are all based on the strategy of spatial multiplexing, which will not be further elaborated here.

# 5.2.2. Wavelength-Multiplexed Metasurfaces

The design of wavelength-multiplexed metasurface holograms is of significant importance for achieving the long-sought goal of full-color holographic display in the field of metasurface holography. A target color image can be decomposed into three grayscale images for the red, green, and blue primary color components. To realize a full-color holographic display, the metasurface hologram is designed to generate holographic images of the corresponding color components for the incident light at red, green, and blue wavelengths, respectively, which are finally combined into a color holographic image. In the step of solving for the holograms, the compensation algorithm for color holographic displays introduced in Subsection 5.1.1 might be required. In Subsection 2.1.5, we have introduced a design method that utilizes polarization multiplexing channels to decouple the phase modulation of meta-atoms for each wavelength, thereby achieving wavelength-multiplexed holographic display $[135]$ . Below, we continue to introduce other design methods for wavelength-multiplexed metasurface holograms.

(I) Constructing a meta-atom database to independently modulate the phase shift at different wavelengths. Directly requiring a single meta-atom to meet the independent phase modulation for multiple wavelengths is not easy to achieve. Specifically, target phase distributions for different wavelengths are generally independently solved through multiple uses of the CGH algorithm, and the target phase shifts introduced at the same position on the metasurface for different wavelengths are somewhat randomly related. Therefore, for meta-atoms with certain phase dispersion characteristics, as analyzed in Subsection 4.4.2, it is often difficult to simultaneously meet the phase modulation requirements for all wavelengths at every position on the metasurface $[429]$ . To achieve this, a large lattice constant and complex meta-atom structures are needed to provide sufficient design space $[574]$ , and it is necessary to overcome the unacceptably high time cost for the forward design method when exploring a large design space, as explained in Subsection 3.4. Leveraging inverse design methods holds promise for efficiently exploring the large design space of complex meta-atoms, with the relevant work $[449]$ already introduced at the end of Subsection 3.4.2.

Figure 44  
![](images/4c7fee77f6bc502522bad405ec9c9049206067faf5ce02d27df82d4c6d2f3838.jpg)

Multi-wavelength metasurface hologram design. (a) Schematic of the principle of the metasurface hologram integrated with the color filter microarray. (b) Experimental color holographic image generated by the multi-wavelength metasurface hologram based on space division multiplexing. (c) Schematic of the metasurface unit based on space division multiplexing. (d) Simulated spectra of the product of transmittance and PCE of three kinds of meta-atoms in (c). (e) Schematic of the principle of metasurface with both nanoprinting and color holographic display functions. (f) Experimental nanoprinting image (left) and color holographic image (right) generated by the metasurface with both nanoprinting and holography functions. (g) Schematic of the principle of color holographic display based on off-axis illumination method. (h) Experimental color holographic image generated by the metasurface hologram based on the off-axis illumination. (i) Top: target image. Bottom: experimental color holographic image generated by the metasurface hologram based on off-axis illumination. (j) Top: target image. Bottom: experimental color holographic image generated by the complex amplitude metasurface hologram based on detour phase and geometric phase. (k) Simulated (top) and experimental (bottom) color holographic images generated by the Fresnel metasurface hologram. (l) Experimental color holographic images generated by the Fresnel metasurface hologram at different positions. (a) Reprinted from [576] under a Creative Commons license. (b)–(d) Adapted with permission from Wang et al., Nano Lett. 16, 5235–5240 (2016) [356]. Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society. (e) and (f) Adapted from [532] under a Creative Commons license. (g) and (h) Adapted from [577] under a Creative Commons license. (i) Adapted with permission from Wan et al., ACS Nano 10, 10671–10680 (2016) [578]. Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society. (j) Adapted with permission from (continued)

(continued) Deng et al., Adv. Funct. Mater. 30, 1910610 (2020) [168]. Copyright © 2020 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (k) Adapted from [551] under a Creative Commons license. (l) Adapted with permission from So et al., Adv. Mater. 35, 2208520 (2023) [552]. Copyright © 2023 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim.

(II) Using the spatial multiplexing design strategy, it is possible to achieve wavelength-multiplexed metasurfaces when a single meta-atom cannot simultaneously meet the independent phase modulation for multiple wavelengths $[353,575]$ . In this case, the metasurface unit is composed of multiple meta-atoms, with each one (or possibly multiple ones) corresponding to a wavelength component, fulfilling the independent phase modulation requirements for that wavelength. Additionally, to prevent crosstalk between holographic images of different wavelengths, the transmittance (reflectance) of the meta-atom of the transmissive (reflective) metasurface corresponding to a certain wavelength should be as close to zero as possible at other wavelengths, which can be achieved through elaborate design of the meta-atom structure $[356,532]$ , or indirectly by additionally integrating a front-mounted monolithic color filter microarray based on Fabry–Pérot cavity $[576]$ , as illustrated in Fig. 44(a). In 2016, Wang et al. $[356]$ utilized this method to achieve the full-color holographic display, as shown in Fig. 44(b). The metasurface unit, as shown in Fig. 44(c), is composed of $2 \times 2$ meta-atoms corresponding to different wavelengths, with independent geometric phase modulation introduced by rotating the meta-atoms corresponding to those wavelengths. The meta-atoms are made of Si, with a height of 320 nm. The spectral characteristics of the efficiency (the product of transmittance and PCE) of the meta-atoms are shown in Fig. 44(d), indicating that crosstalk between different wavelength components is effectively eliminated. Due to the low efficiency of the meta-atom corresponding to blue light, two meta-atoms corresponding to blue light are arranged within the unit to balance the intensity of holographic images generated at each wavelength. The metasurface is composed of $452 \times 452$ units, with each unit sized at 420 nm. The experimentally measured efficiencies of the metasurface hologram at operating wavelengths of 473 nm, 532 nm, and 633 nm were 3.6%, 5.2%, and 18%, respectively. A drawback of this spatial multiplexing method is that, aside from the loss in device efficiency, the unit size is expanded to twice its original, which, according to Eq. (275), limits the FOV of the hologram.

As a variant of the spatial multiplexing strategy, the meta-atoms responsible for different wavelengths don't necessarily have to be interlaced within the same unit but can be arranged in certain patterns, as shown in Fig. 44(e). For a given wavelength component, the corresponding meta-atoms have almost zero efficiency at other wavelengths, and likewise, other meta-atoms have almost zero efficiency at that wavelength. Therefore, through the arrangement of these meta-atoms, specific amplitude modulation can be formed, thus achieving a nanoprinting function. When solving for the hologram for that wavelength through the GS algorithm, the amplitude distribution on the input plane is determined according to the arrangement of the corresponding meta-atoms. Using this method, in 2019, Wei et al. [532] achieved a geometric phase metasurface hologram multiplexed for wavelengths of 532 nm and 650 nm. The meta-atoms are made of Si, with a height of 300 nm. The metasurface consists of 666 × 666 units, with each unit sized at 300 nm. The nanoprinting image of the metasurface is shown in the left panel of Fig. 44(f), and the reconstructed color holographic image is shown in the right panel of Fig. 44(f). Similarly, in 2020, Wen et al. [579] achieved a detour phase metasurface hologram with nanoprinting functionality multiplexed for wavelengths of 450 nm, 532 nm, and 635 nm.

(III) Achieving a color holographic display based on the off-axis illumination method. Indeed, to achieve color holographic displays, it's not strictly necessary for the metasurface units to realize independent phase modulation for each wavelength. As illustrated in Fig. 44(g), the metasurface generates the same holographic image for the incident light of every wavelength, where the holographic image is an assembly of the spatially separated holographic images for all color components. This can be easily accomplished through a geometric phase metasurface. Then, by varying the incident angles of light at different wavelengths, the positions of the holographic images generated at different wavelengths are adjusted, thus retaining only the portion of the required holographic image corresponding to each wavelength within the effective FOV. Based on this, in 2016, Luo's group and their collaborators [577] and Yang's group and their collaborators [578] separately achieved full-color holographic display, with the experimental holographic images shown in Figs. 44(h) and 44(i), respectively. The advantage of this method is that the metasurface is based solely on geometric phase, making it easy to design and fabricate. While the disadvantages include reduced device efficiency, limited holographic display FOV, and the issue of unwanted holographic images outside the effective FOV. These unwanted holographic images can be eliminated through expanding the effective FOV to $\pm90^{\circ}$ [580]. By doing so, any unwanted holographic images become evanescent waves when viewing one holographic image under its specific incident angle. This method will be further detailed in Subsection 5.2.4.

Just like geometric phase, detour phase also exhibits phase dispersion-free characteristics, meaning the aforementioned method can also be implemented with detour phase metasurfaces. An additional benefit of this approach is that, as indicated by Eq. (29), for a detour phase metasurface working at a fixed diffraction order $\sigma_{\mathrm{d}}$ , incident light at different wavelengths will have different diffraction angles. Therefore, by reasonably controlling the spacing between the holographic images corresponding to different wavelengths during assembly, it's possible to directly observe the target color holographic image within the effective FOV without the need for off-axis illumination. This characteristic is still retained for the complex amplitude modulation metasurfaces based on both detour phase and geometric phase introduced in Subsection 5.1.2. Based on this, in 2020, Deng et al. [168] realized a complex amplitude metasurface hologram that generated a full-color holographic image within the effective FOV, as shown in Fig. 44(j).

(IV) Wavelength-multiplexed Fresnel metasurface holograms. The above-discussed metasurface holograms are all Fraunhofer holograms. For Fresnel holograms implemented based on geometric phase metasurfaces, holographic displays that multiplex both wavelength and position can be achieved solely through multi-objective CGH algorithms, as introduced in Subsection 5.1.1. Utilizing an iterative multi-objective CGH algorithm, in 2019, Qiu's group and their collaborators $[551]$ realized a geometric phase-based metasurface hologram multiplexed for wavelengths of 488 nm, 532 nm, and 633 nm, with the full-color holographic image shown in Fig. 44(k). In 2023, Rho's group $[552]$ solved the multi-objective CGH problem through gradient optimization based on automatic differentiation, achieving holographic display with a total of 9 multiplexing channels for wavelengths of 450 nm, 532 nm, and 635 nm and three observation plane positions. As shown in Fig. 44(l), a full-color holographic image is reconstructed at each of the three observation planes. The PSNR of the three holographic images are 12.24 dB, 12.08 dB, and 15.56 dB, respectively. The geometric phase metasurface consists of $900 \times 900$ rectangular pillar meta-atoms made of titanium dioxide nanoparticle-embedded resin, with a height of 910 nm and a lattice constant of 450 nm. Experimental results show the meta-atoms have an average

PCE of up to $63\%$ at the three wavelengths. It's important to note that the color holographic image of Fresnel holograms can only be observed at specific positions, which fundamentally differs from Fraunhofer holograms.

# 5.2.3. Orbital Angular Momentum-Multiplexed Metasurfaces

Orbital angular momentum (OAM), characterized by a light's helical wavefront, represents a novel physical property and a new degree of freedom for light field manipulation arising from the spatial variation of the light field's phase [581]. If a light field possesses a complex amplitude distribution of $e^{il\varphi}$ (the integer $l$ defined as the topological charge), where the phase continuously changes from 0 to $2l\pi$ along the azimuthal direction and returns to the starting point, then a phase singularity, where the phase is undefined, emerges at the center of this continuous loop of phase change, thereby forming an optical vortex. Microscopically, each photon in such a state carries an OAM of $l\hbar$ . A vortex beam, possessing a Hilbert factor $e^{il\varphi}$ , is a paraxial light beam that exhibits an optical vortex along its propagation axis. The design workflow for vortex-beam-generating metasurfaces is available in our open-source code repository [73].

The OAM provides a physically infinite set of orthogonal helical modes (l can be any integer); hence, OAM-multiplexed metasurface holography holds the promise of achieving an unprecedented number of multiplexing channels.

(I) Achieving OAM-multiplexed metasurface holograms using multi-objective CGH algorithms. A vortex beam inherently depends on a topological charge l for its phase distribution

$$
\phi_ {\text {vortex}} (l) = l \varphi . \tag {298}
$$

When l=0, the vortex beam transitions to the plane wave scenario, which has been consistently considered in previous designs. After interacting with a metasurface hologram with a phase distribution of $\phi_{meta}$ , the phase distribution of the output light is

$$
\phi_ {\text {output}} (l) = \phi_ {\text {vortex}} (l) + \phi_ {\text {meta}}, \tag {299}
$$

which depends on the topological charge $l$ . This means that vortex beams with different topological charges $l_{1}, l_{2}, \ldots, l_{M}$ interacting with the same metasurface will result in output light with distinct phase distributions, thereby potentially achieving holographic display multiplexing with a number of channels equivalent to $M$ . The phase distribution of the metasurface is fixed, so it can be solved using the multi-objective CGH algorithms mentioned in Subsection 5.1.1. Based on this, in 2019, Qiu's group and their collaborators [551] achieved a geometric phase metasurface hologram multiplexed for three different OAMs with topological charges of 0, +3, and +6, with simulation and experimental holographic images shown in Fig. 45(a). Operating at a wavelength of 532 nm, the metasurface is composed of $300 \times 300$ rectangular $\mathrm{TiO_2}$ pillar meta-atoms with a height of 600 nm and a lattice constant of 325 nm.

Now, let's consider a non-multiplexed metasurface hologram that selectively responds to a vortex beam with a specific topological charge of $l_0$ . The phase distribution of the metasurface hologram is defined as

$$
\phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {0}} = \phi_ {\text {plane}} - \phi_ {\text {vortex}} \left(l _ {0}\right), \tag {300}
$$

where $\phi_{plane}$ is the phase distribution of the hologram generating the target holographic image under plane wave incidence. When a vortex beam with a topological charge of l is incident, the phase distribution of the output light is [Eq. (298) is used in the last

Figure 45  
![](images/755aaf8663de91bad96048252133ff51429eb7f831b07963a8362cb1904bdd50.jpg)

Orbital angular momentum-multiplexed metasurface hologram design. (a) Holographic images generated by the OAM-multiplexed metasurface hologram based on the multi-objective computer-generated hologram algorithm under the incidence of vortex beams with different topological charges. Top: simulated results. Bottom: experimental results. (b) Experimental holographic image generated by metasurface hologram conserving the OAM property of the incident vortex beam. The topology charge of the vortex beam is +2. Pseudo colors are used to visualize different OAM modes [the same as (c) and (d)]. (c) Experimental holographic images generated by the OAM-multiplexed metasurface hologram with 4 multiplexing channels under the incidence of vortex beams with different topological charges. (d) Experimental holographic images generated by the OAM-multiplexed metasurface hologram with 10 multiplexing channels under the incidence of vortex beams with different topological charges. (e) Experimental holographic video generated by the OAM-multiplexed complex amplitude metasurface hologram. (a) Adapted from Jin et al., Nat. Commun. 10, 4789 (2019) [551] under a Creative Commons license. (b) and (c) Adapted from [582] under a Creative Commons license. (d) Reprinted with permission (continued)

(continued) from Fang et al., Nat. Photonics 14, 102–108 (2020) [583]. Copyright © 2020 Nature Springer. (e) Reprinted with permission from Ren et al., Nat. Nanotechnol. 15, 948–955 (2020) [392]. Copyright © 2020 Nature Springer.

step]

$$
\phi_ {\text {output}} (l) = \phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {0}} + \phi_ {\text {vortex}} (l) = \phi_ {\text {plane}} + (l - l _ {0}) \varphi . \tag {301}
$$

From Eq. (301), it is clear that only when the incident vortex beam has a topological charge of $l_0$ can it cancel out the $-\phi_{\mathrm{vortex}}(l_0)$ term in the metasurface phase distribution expressed by Eq. (300), allowing the output light to acquire $\phi_{\mathrm{plane}}$ , the phase distribution that can generate the target holographic image. If the topological charge of the incident vortex beam is not $l_0$ , the phase distribution of the output light is effectively $\phi_{\mathrm{plane}}$ with a disruptive phase of $(l - l_0)\varphi$ , failing to generate the target holographic image. This means that the metasurface hologram with the phase distribution satisfying Eq. (300) will exhibit OAM selectivity. In 2020, Zhou et al. [584] demonstrated the application of this feature in the field of encryption. By combining this with the 3-channel polarization-multiplexed metasurface hologram design [170] introduced in Subsection 2.1.5, they achieved a metasurface hologram that reconstructs the holographic image only under the incidence of the vortex beam with a specific polarization state and topological charge.

Now we re-examine the previously introduced OAM-multiplexed metasurface hologram [551]. From Eq. (299), in this context, the phase distribution of the metasurface is

$$
\phi_ {\text {meta}} = \phi_ {\text {output}} (l) - \phi_ {\text {vortex}} (l), \tag {302}
$$

where $\phi_{\mathrm{output}}(l)$ is the phase distribution that generates a holographic image under plane wave incidence. Equation (302) shows a corresponding relationship to the phase distribution of the OAM selective metasurface hologram expressed by Eq. (300). The difference lies in that the phase distribution of the hologram obtained through the multi-objective CGH algorithm can generate corresponding holographic images for vortex beams with a set of various topological charges $l_{1}, l_{2}, \ldots, l_{M}$ , that is, it has selectivity for multiple OAMs. From this perspective, OAM selectivity forms the basis for implementing OAM multiplexing.

(II) Achieving OAM-multiplexed metasurface holograms through the superposition of phase distributions corresponding to different topological charges. In the previous demonstrations, after interacting with the metasurface, the vortex beam loses its OAM property. When a vortex beam is perpendicularly incident on a Fraunhofer hologram, the generated holographic image can be seen as the convolution of the Fourier transform of the vortex beam's helical wavefront (which acts as a convolution kernel) with the original holographic image generated by the hologram under plane wave incidence. To avoid spatial overlap of the convolution kernel to preserve the OAM property at every point on the holographic image, the original holographic image needs to be sampled using a 2D Dirac comb function to serve as the new target holographic image, with the subsequent design process remaining unchanged. The sampling period is determined by the vortex beam, since vortex beams with different OAMs exhibit different spatial frequency characteristics. Based on this, in 2019, Ren et al. [582] achieved metasurface holograms that preserve the incident light's OAM property. The holographic image generated by a hologram designed for the vortex beam with a topological charge of +2 is shown in Fig. 45(b).

Leveraging the previous discussion concerning OAM selectivity, it's possible to further realize a metasurface hologram that exhibits OAM selectivity for the vortex beam with a specific topological charge $l_0$ . In this case, the phase distribution of the metasurface hologram is

$$
\phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {0}} = \phi_ {\text {cons}} ^ {l _ {0}} - \phi_ {\text {vortex}} \left(l _ {0}\right), \tag {303}
$$

where $\phi_{\mathrm{cons}}^{l_0}$ is the phase distribution of a hologram preserving the property of OAM with a topological charge of $l_0$ . It is important to note that for an incident vortex beam with a topological charge of $l_0$ , the phase distribution of the output light is $\phi_{\mathrm{cons}}^{l_0}$ , rather than $\phi_{\mathrm{cons}}^{l_0} + \phi_{\mathrm{vortex}}(l_0)$ as would be the case if the OAM property were preserved. Therefore, the generated holographic image will no longer preserve the OAM property.

Taking a step further, let the phase distribution of the metasurface hologram be the superposition of the OAM-selective phase distributions for vortex beams with topological charges $l_{1}, l_{2}, \ldots, l_{M}$ , i.e.,

$$
\phi_ {\text {multiplex}} = \phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {1}} + \phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {2}} + \dots + \phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {M}}. \tag {304}
$$

When an incident vortex beam has a topological charge that matches one of $l_{1}, l_{2}, \ldots, l_{M}$ , for instance, $l_{m}$ , the phase distribution of the output light is

$$
\phi_ {\text {output}} = \phi_ {\text {cons}} ^ {l _ {m}} + \phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {1}} + \dots + \phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {m - 1}} + \phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {m + 1}} + \dots + \phi_ {\text {select}} ^ {l _ {M}}, \tag {305}
$$

meaning that the metasurface will generate the corresponding holographic image associated with $\phi_{cons}^{l_{m}}$ , despite being subject to crosstalk from the diffractive patterns generated by the phase distributions corresponding to other channels [the terms except for the first term on the right side of Eq. (305)]. In this way, OAM multiplexing for a total of M channels corresponding to $l_{1}, l_{2}, \ldots, l_{M}$ is achieved. Considering another incident vortex beam with a topological charge that matches one of $l_{1}, l_{2}, \ldots, l_{M}$ , for instance, $l_{m}^{\prime}$ , the closer $l_{m}$ and $l_{m}^{\prime}$ are, the more similar the phase distributions of their respective output light, thus the stronger the crosstalk between them might be. Therefore, it's often preferred to maintain a certain separation between the topological charges of different multiplexing OAMs to achieve a higher number of multiplexing channels [583].

Building on this, in 2019, Ren et al. [582] achieved a propagation phase metasurface hologram multiplexed for vortex beams with topological charges of -2, -1, +1, and +2, with the experimental holographic images shown in Fig. 45(c). Operating at a wavelength of 632 nm, the metasurface is made of cylindrical GaN meta-atoms with a height of 1 $\mu$ m. Following the same design method, in 2020, Fang et al. [583] demonstrated a metasurface hologram with up to 10 OAM multiplexing channels, with experimental holographic images shown in Fig. 45(d). In the same year, Ren et al. [392] further achieved an OAM-multiplexed complex amplitude metasurface hologram. The meta-atoms are rectangular pillars made of polymer material, with a cross-sectional dimension of 780 nm by 390 nm and heights ranging from 3.4 $\mu$ m to 4.45 $\mu$ m. Different levels of amplitude modulation are introduced by adjusting the heights of the meta-atoms, and phase modulation is applied by rotating the meta-atoms, as introduced at the end of Subsection 5.1.2. Operating at a wavelength of 633 nm, the metasurface, composed of 2000 × 2000 meta-atoms, has a large size of 2.5 mm × 2.5 mm. The metasurface hologram features up to 60 OAM multiplexing channels and was used to realize two segments of video, each with 30 frames, with some frames from one segment shown in Fig. 45(e).

From a theoretical perspective, the phase distribution of the OAM-multiplexed meta-surface hologram, as expressed by Eq. (304), remains feasible whether $\phi_{select}^{l_{m}}$ is de-

fined by Eq. (300) or Eq. (303), because the preservation of OAM properties is not critical in this context. However, the complexity of the target holographic image after sampling with a comb function will be significantly reduced compared to the original image, thus decreasing the design pressure.

The native phase distribution introduced by the incident vortex beam is fundamentally what makes OAM multiplexing possible. Generally, this does not conflict with polarization multiplexing, wavelength multiplexing, or other types of multiplexing based on the response characteristics of metasurface units, and vortex beams can also have polarization, wavelength, or other properties at the same time, allowing for the coexistence of various multiplexing types. In 2023, Yang et al. [571] combined OAM multiplexing with orthogonal circular polarization state multiplexing to achieve a metasurface hologram with a total of 8 multiplexing channels for two circular polarization states and four OAMs. In this case, the orthogonal circular polarization state multiplexing was achieved based on Eq. (284), and OAM multiplexing was implemented using the aforementioned method. In 2024, He et al. [585] realized a complex amplitude metasurface hologram capable of generating independent nanoprinting or holographic images at two different wavelengths, $473~\mathrm{nm}$ and $633~\mathrm{nm}$ , and five distinct OAMs with topological charges of $0, \pm 3$ , and $\pm 6$ . The complex amplitude modulation was implemented by leveraging the interference of electromagnetic responses from multiple meta-atoms within a single unit. Wavelength multiplexing was accomplished using a spatial multiplexing design strategy [method (II) in Subsection 5.2.2], while OAM multiplexing was realized using the same method.

# 5.2.4. Other Types of Multiplexed Metasurfaces

Here, we primarily focused on incident angle-multiplexed metasurface holograms and Janus metasurface holograms. Other types of multiplexed metasurface holograms, such as metasurface holograms multiplexing transmitted and reflected light $[568,586]$ , metasurface holograms multiplexing free space light and guided waves $[587]$ , etc., will not be covered.

Incident angle-multiplexed metasurfaces. Incident angle multiplexing is significant for the development of off-axis optical components and further expanding the number of multiplexing channels of metasurfaces.

(I) Constructing a meta-atom database to independently modulate the phase shift at different incident angles. The electromagnetic response of meta-atoms is inherently dependent on the angle of incidence. Yet, some shapes of meta-atoms exhibit significant incident-angle-dependent electromagnetic response characteristics, while others do not. The former can be used to introduce independent modulation for the light incident at multiple angles, and the latter are more suitable for applications requiring phase modulation to remain essentially unchanged within a certain range of the incident angle $[488]$ .

In 2017, Faraon's group [588] achieved an incident angle-multiplexed metasurface hologram operating in reflection mode, utilizing meta-atoms with a U-shaped cross-section as shown in Fig. 46(a). The meta-atom database covered the complete phase

Figure 46  
![](images/673961fa8d536bef6d9756fc7d2481435c16008fdc6a69d5e810340fd6f7e2b9.jpg)

Multi-angle metasurface hologram design. (a) Schematic of meta-atom in U-shaped cross section. (b) Experimental holographic images generated by the multi-angle metasurface hologram under the illumination at different incident angles. (c) Schematic of the multi-angle metasurface hologram with an extended effective FOV. Left: diffraction pattern in the momentum space under perpendicular incidence. Right: diffraction pattern in the momentum space under specific oblique incidence. The metasurface with a lattice constant $a$ supports a spatial angular frequency range expressed by Eq. (306). The dotted circle represents the boundary of the effective FOV, constrained by the maximum spatial angular frequency $\frac{2\pi}{\lambda}$ . Oblique incident light carries an in-plane wavevector of $1.2\frac{2\pi}{\lambda}$ and is oriented at an azimuthal angle $\beta$ . The purple arrow depicts the direction and magnitude of this in-plane wavevector. Each letter pattern represents a holographic image corresponding to a specific azimuthal angle. (d) Experimental holographic images generated by the multi-angle metasurface hologram under various illumination directions and polarization channels. (a) and (b) Adapted from [588] under a Creative Commons license. (c) and (d) Adapted from [580] under a Creative Commons license.

modulation for light of TE polarization state and 915 nm wavelength incident at specific angles of $0^{\circ}$ and $30^{\circ}$ . The simulation and experimental holographic images are shown in Fig. 46(b). Using a similar method, in 2021, Wan et al. [567] covered complex amplitude modulation for two angles of incidence by traversing different shapes and sizes of meta-atoms, thus realizing a metasurface hologram working in reflection mode with both nanoprinting and holographic display functions at each incident angle.

(II) Leveraging the effective FOV of the metasurface hologram. For a metasurface with a lattice constant a, the maximum phase gradient that it can introduce is $\frac{\pi}{a}$ . Therefore, the spatial angular frequency range supported by the metasurface can be expressed as

$$
k _ {x} \in \left[ - \frac {\pi}{a}, \frac {\pi}{a} \right], \quad k _ {y} \in \left[ - \frac {\pi}{a}, \frac {\pi}{a} \right]. \tag {306}
$$

Referring to Eq. (65), the allowable spatial angular frequency range for propagation waves is

$$
k _ {x} ^ {2} + k _ {y} ^ {2} \leq \left(\frac {2 \pi}{\lambda}\right) ^ {2}. \tag {307}
$$

When $a \leq \frac{\lambda}{2}$ , the spatial angular frequency range specified in Eq. (306) exceeds that in Eq. (307), implying that any diffraction pattern of the metasurface hologram outside the range in Eq. (307) becomes invisible due to transitioning into evanescent waves, as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 46(c). However, when the incident light is oblique and carries a suitable in-plane wavevector, it is possible to shift the diffraction pattern outside the range in Eq. (307) to the center of the FOV. Drawing on the method depicted in Fig. 44(g), by carefully designing combinations of holographic images for different azimuthal angles, the specific oblique incidence can render the holographic image corresponding to the particular azimuthal angle visible while concealing the others, as demonstrated in the right panel of Fig. 46(c).

Utilizing this approach, in 2024, Liu et al. [580] developed a multi-angle geometric metasurface hologram operating at a wavelength of 637 nm and featuring a lattice constant of 177 nm. For RCP light under oblique incident LCP light at various azimuthal angles, three independent holographic images are generated in the center of FOV, as illustrated in the top panel of Fig. 46(d). Employing the technique in Eq. (250), another set of three holographic images becomes observable for LCP light under oblique incident RCP light, as depicted in the bottom panel of Fig. 46(d).

(III) Achieving incident angle-multiplexing through detour phase metasurface holograms. As introduced in Subsection 2.1.4, based on Eq. (35), it's possible to introduce independent detour phase modulation for output light at diffraction angles $\theta_{x}$ and $\theta_{y}$ in the x and y directions, respectively. Considering the light incident at angles $-\theta_{x}$ and $-\theta_{y}$ , the original output light at the diffraction angles of $\theta_{x}$ and $\theta_{y}$ will now exit the metasurface perpendicularly, thus achieving the multiplexing for incident angles of $-\theta_{x}$ and $-\theta_{y}$ . Based on this, in 2021, Jang et al. [589] achieved an incident angle-multiplexed metasurface hologram and utilized the spatial multiplexing strategy to further increase the number of angle multiplexing channels.

(IV) The incident angle-multiplexed metasurface can also be achieved through the spatial multiplexing strategy. In this design, the metasurface unit consists of multiple meta-atoms, each tasked with phase modulation for a specific angle of incidence. To reduce crosstalk among different incident angles, it is crucial to meticulously design the meta-atom structure such that the efficiency of each meta-atom is negligible at all angles of incidence other than its corresponding angle. Utilizing this approach, in 2024, Latif et al. [590] realized a metasurface hologram that multiplexes at incident angles of $\pm35^{\circ}$ for LCP light.

Janus metasurfaces. Janus metasurfaces, as illustrated in Fig. 47(a), exhibit distinct electromagnetic responses and corresponding device functions for forward and backward incident light [475]. As indicated by Eq. (290), utilizing a multilayer metasurface that supports a Jones matrix with unequal non-diagonal elements, this asymmetric electromagnetic response can be realized for the specific polarization channel.

(I) Achieving Janus metasurface holograms through the asymmetric electromagnetic response of multilayer metasurfaces. In 2019, Zentgraf's group and their collaborators [476] achieved a metasurface hologram that displays a holographic image only under a specific polarization state and illumination direction, utilizing a bilayer metasurface unit structure, as shown in Fig. 47(b). The meta-atoms are made of Au with a height of 50 nm, and the two layers are separated by a 50 nm thick dielectric layer of $SiO_{2}$ . Under

Figure 47  
![](images/c3fe42af22e214ac54b5272fc8e15ec021078e1f4b71463d40c98b21da1631e4.jpg)

Janus metasurface hologram design. (a) Schematic of the Janus metasurface hologram. (b) Schematic of bilayer structure of the unit of the Janus metasurface. (c) Experimental holographic images generated by the Janus metasurface hologram under different illumination directions and polarization states. (b) and (c) Adapted with permission from Frese et al., Nano Lett. 19, 3976–3980 (2019) [476]. Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society.

forward illumination, the L-shaped cross-section meta-atoms in the bottom layer can be represented by the Jones matrix

$$
\mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} ^ {1} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} T _ {x x} ^ {1} & T _ {x y} ^ {1} \\ T _ {y x} ^ {1} & T _ {y y} ^ {1} \end{array} \right], \tag {308}
$$

where $T_{yx}^{1}=T_{xy}^{1}\neq0$ . To introduce non-reciprocity, a top layer of meta-atoms should be added, which has polarization selectivity, maintaining high transmittance (assumed to be 1) only for light polarized along the x direction. The meta-atoms under forward illumination can be represented by the Jones matrix

$$
\mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} ^ {2} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} T _ {x x} ^ {2} & T _ {x y} ^ {2} \\ T _ {y x} ^ {2} & T _ {y y} ^ {2} \end{array} \right] = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 \end{array} \right]. \tag {309}
$$

In this case, Eq. (292) is satisfied. Ignoring the interlayer coupling in the bilayer structure, the total Jones matrices $T_{f}$ and $T_{b}$ follow Eq. (290). For incident light with a Jones vector of $[0\ 1]^{\top}$ , the complex amplitudes of the output light linearly polarized along the x direction under forward and backward illumination are, respectively,

$$
O ^ {\mathrm{f}} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} 1 & 0 \end{array} \right] \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 0 \\ 1 \end{array} \right] = T _ {x y} ^ {1}, \quad O ^ {\mathrm{b}} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} 1 & 0 \end{array} \right] \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{b}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 0 \\ 1 \end{array} \right] = 0, \tag {310}
$$

which indicates that under forward illumination, the output light linearly polarized along the x direction obtains a complex amplitude distribution determined by $T_{xy}^{1}$ . Under backward illumination, however, its amplitude is 0. By changing the size of the bottom layer meta-atoms, $T_{xy}^{1}$ can be adjusted. Based on this, a Janus metasurface hologram with dimensions of $200\mu m \times 200\mu m$ and an operating wavelength of 1150 nm was realized, with experimental holographic images corresponding to Eq. (310) shown in the first row of Fig. 47(c). Now further consider another scenario. For incident light with a Jones vector of $[10]^{\top}$ , the complex amplitudes of the output light linearly polarized along the y direction under forward and backward illumination are, respectively,

$$
O ^ {\mathrm{f}} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} 0 & 1 \end{array} \right] \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{f}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] = 0, \quad O ^ {\mathrm{b}} = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} 0 & 1 \end{array} \right] \mathbf {T} _ {\mathrm{b}} \left[ \begin{array}{l} 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] = - T _ {x y} ^ {1}, \tag {311}
$$

which explains the experimental holographic images shown in the second row of Fig. 47(c).

(II) Achieving Janus metasurface holograms using nonreciprocal metasurfaces. Nonreciprocal metasurfaces can break the reciprocity derived from the time-reversal symmetry of Maxwell's equations using magnetic materials [591], nonlinearity [592], or spatiotemporal modulation [593]. Notably, Janus metasurfaces can also be implemented using nonreciprocal metasurfaces [594].

It is important to note that while multilayer metasurfaces can achieve an asymmetric electromagnetic response, this is limited to specific polarization channels and does not violate the time-reversal symmetry inherent in Maxwell's equations. Therefore, they do not qualify as nonreciprocal metasurfaces.

# 6. METASURFACES FOR QUANTUM PHOTONICS

In recent years, the rapid development of metasurfaces in areas such as polarization optics, high-Q-factor resonances, and multifunctional multiplexing has introduced new opportunities for quantum photonics, particularly in the generation, manipulation, and characterization of quantum light. This progress offers a promising technological pathway toward miniaturized, highly integrated, high-performance, and multifunctional quantum photonic devices $[595–599]$ . Unlike previous reviews $[595,598,599]$ , we categorize metasurfaces for quantum photonics into two primary groups: direct and indirect applications. Here, “indirect applications” refer to cases where metasurfaces are first designed as independent optical components, such as lenses or gratings, before being utilized in specific quantum photonic applications. We believe that this classification provides a clearer understanding of the role of metasurfaces in different quantum photonic systems.

# 6.1. Direct Applications of Metasurfaces in Quantum Photonics

# 6.1.1. Single-Photon Source

The most common method for generating single photons is through the spontaneous emission of a two-level system, where the simultaneous emission of more than one photon is fundamentally prohibited. Typical solid-state systems used as single-photon sources include colloidal and epitaxial quantum dots, molecules, color centers in diamond, defects in solids, and two-dimensional materials $[600]$ . High-performance single-photon emitters require exceptional brightness (i.e., a high photon emission rate), remarkable indistinguishability (i.e., uniform quantum characteristics), and superior purity (i.e., ensuring that only one photon is emitted at a time) $[601]$ . Among these, brightness is directly correlated with the photon emission rate of the single-photon source.

Enhancing the brightness of single-photon sources through metasurfaces. The excited-state electrons in these typical single-photon sources generally have relatively long lifetimes, on the order of 10 ns [598], which significantly limits the photon emission rate of these single-photon emitters. According to Fermi's golden rule, the excited-state lifetime is not an intrinsic property of the system but is strongly dependent on the photonic environment through the local density of optical states. When a single-photon emitter is placed in a photonic environment with an increased local

Applications of metasurfaces in enhancing the brightness of single-photon sources. (a) Schematic of the single-photon source utilizing a single metallic nanocavity to enhance the photon emission rate from the quantum dot. (b) xOz cross-section of an SPP-based single-photon source system. (c) Schematic of a single-photon source integrated with a bullseye grating. (d) SEM of a hexagonal boron nitride flake positioned atop an Ag nanoparticle array on silica. (a) Adapted from $[603]$ under a Creative Commons license. (b) Adapted from $[604]$ under a Creative Commons license. (c) Adapted with permission from Kumar et al., J. Chem. Phys. 154, 044303 (2021) $[605]$ . Copyright © 2021 American Institute of Physics. (d) Adapted with permission from Tran et al., Nano Lett. 17, 2634–2639 (2017) $[606]$ . Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society.

density of optical states, its photon emission rate can be significantly enhanced. Leveraging the Purcell effect $[602]$ , this enhancement can be achieved by integrating the emitter into an optical cavity with a high Q-factor or a small mode volume V, where the excited-state lifetime of the quantum system is modified by a Purcell factor proportional to Q/V. Therefore, by engineering the Purcell factor of the cavity integrated with the emitter, the photon emission rate can be effectively improved.

(I) A classic approach involves utilizing a single plasmonic metal nanocavity, as illustrated in Fig. 48(a). The extremely small mode volume of the nanocavity significantly reduces the emitter's lifetime, enhancing the average emission intensity of the single-photon source by up to 1900 times compared to its original value $[603]$ . At the same time, due to the relatively low Q-factor, the nanocavity supports a broadband mode, which alleviates the stringent spectral matching requirement between the emission spectrum of the photon emitter and the resonance spectrum of the cavity—a common challenge in nanofabrication. However, a major drawback of this approach is the loss induced by metal absorption. This issue can be mitigated by adopting MIM structures or improving the metallic materials used $[607]$ .  
(II) By leveraging the resonance effects of metasurfaces, as detailed in Section 2, the integration of metasurfaces with single-photon emitters can significantly enhance the photon emission rate.

(i) Enhancing the photon emission rate of single-photon sources through SPPs along the metal/air interface. When a single-photon source is placed near a metal surface, the emitted photons excite SPPs that propagate along the metal/air interface. Since these surface waves do not radiate into the far field, additional structures, such as gratings or metasurfaces, must be introduced on the metal surface, as illustrated in Fig. 48(b), to convert the nonradiative surface waves into radiative waves for efficient photon collection $[604,605,608–614]$ . In 2021, as shown in Fig. 48(c), Bozhevolnyi's group $[605]$ integrated single germanium-vacancy (GeV) centers in nanodiamonds with a hydrogen silsesquioxane (HSQ) bullseye grating. By optimizing the grating parameters, they enhanced the radiative decay rate by approximately 5.5 times compared to the case without the grating. Moreover, the emission properties of the photons can be tailored through the careful design of the grating structure, which will be discussed in detail later.

(ii) Enhancing the photon emission rate of single-photon sources via plasmonic resonances in the meta-atom array. In 2017, Tran et al. [606] coupled photon emitters in hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) flakes to an Ag nanoparticle array, as illustrated in Fig. 48(d). By tuning the structural parameters, they matched the resonance spectrum of the Ag nanoparticle array with the emission spectrum of the quantum emitter (560–700 nm). This coupling reduced the emitter's lifetime from 8.5 ns to 0.27 ns and enhanced the emission intensity by approximately a factor of two.

Modulating the polarization, direction, and angular momentum of single-photon sources via metasurfaces. Metasurfaces offer unparalleled advantages in optical field manipulation. By integrating metasurfaces with single-photon sources, it is possible to efficiently and flexibly control various properties of emitted photons, including polarization, propagation direction, and OAM. In this field, Bozhevolnyi's group has conducted a significant body of pioneering work $[604,608–614]$ , while contributions have also been made through the collaboration between Chen's and Li's groups $[615,616]$ . We now systematically present the underlying principles of the aforementioned works, from simpler to more complex mechanisms.

(I) System prototype of variants discussed later. The system we consider, in which a single-photon source is integrated with a metasurface, is illustrated in Fig. 48(b). The centrally positioned single-photon source (e.g., a quantum dot) can be approximated as an electric dipole oriented perpendicular to the surface. Its spontaneous emission excites cylindrically diverging SPPs. When these SPPs interact with a bullseye grating, as shown in Fig. 48(c), the surface waves are converted into radiative propagation waves. The emission angle $\theta$ relative to the surface normal is determined from the grating equation

$$
k _ {0} \sin \theta = k _ {\mathrm{SPP}} + M \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda}, \tag {312}
$$

where $k_{0}$ is the free space wavevector, $k_{SPP} = 2\pi / \lambda_{SPP}$ is the wavevector of the SPPs, $\lambda_{SPP}$ is the effective wavelength of the SPPs, M is the diffraction order, and $\Lambda$ is the grating period. When the grating period satisfies the Bragg resonance condition

$$
\Lambda = N \frac {\lambda_ {\mathrm{SPP}}}{2}, \quad N = 2, 3, 4, \dots , \tag {313}
$$

the resonance enhances the Purcell factor, leading to a higher photon emission rate. Additionally, the enhanced local field facilitates the collection of scattered photons, thereby improving the overall efficiency of the single-photon source. In this case, the emitted photons exhibit radial polarization, as illustrated in Fig. 49(a), with the experimentally observed far-field intensity distribution shown in the inset of Fig. 49(a) [609].

Figure 49  
![](images/7b7061f2eebe13978a48da90f79f7304f69b3473466f80c1d84174f6c930d7a8.jpg)

Applications of metasurfaces in modulating single-photon sources in polarization, direction, and angular momentum. (a) Schematic of a bullseye grating for generating radially polarized single photons. Inset: experimental far-field intensity distribution of emitted single-photon superposition beams. (b) Left: SEM of a grating for producing RCP single photons. ND, nanodiamond. Right: experimental far-field intensity and polarization distributions of emitted single-photon beams, where height represents intensity and color indicates polarization states. (c) Left: SEM of a directional single-photon-generating grating. Right: experimental far-field intensity distribution of emitted single-photon beams. (d) Left: SEM of a grating for generating single photons with defined spin and orbital angular momentum. Right: experimental intensity distributions of LCP and RCP components in emitted single-photon beams. (e) Schematic of the metasurface for generating single photons with specific circular polarization states. (f) Metasurface design for generating single photons carrying defined OAM and linear polarization states. (g) Left: schematic and SEM of the metasurface enabling directional emission of polarization-controlled single photons. Right: experimental far-field intensity distributions of x- and y-polarized components in emitted single-photon beams. (a) Adapted with permission from Komisar et al., ACS Photonics 8, 2190–2196 (2021) [609]. Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society. (b) Adapted from [604] under a Creative Commons license. (c) Adapted with permission from Kan et al., ACS Photonics 7, 1111–1116 (2020) [608]. Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society. (d) Adapted from [610] under a Creative Commons license. (e) Reprinted with permission from Xue et al., Laser Photonics Rev. 16, 2200179 (2022) [615]. Copyright © 2022 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (f) Reprinted [614] under a Creative Commons license. (g) Adapted from [613] under a Creative Commons license.

(II) Building upon the previous design, as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 49(b), the width of each concentric ring in the bullseye grating is modified from a fixed value to a gradual increase from 110 nm to 407 nm as a function of the azimuthal angle. This gradual variation induces a continuous phase shift in the scattered optical field, ranging from 0 to $2\pi$ . At any two points on the ring separated by an azimuthal angle of $\pi/2$ , the scattered electric fields not only have orthogonal polarization directions but also exhibit a phase difference of $\pi/2$ . As a result, their superposition in the far field leads

to the generation of circularly polarized photons. The experimentally observed far-field intensity and polarization distributions are shown in the right panel of Fig. 49(b) [604].

(III) Still based on the design in (I), as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 49(c), the grating constants on the x < 0 and x > 0 sides of the bullseye grating are set to $\Lambda_{-}$ and $\Lambda_{+}$ , respectively, satisfying

$$
\frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda_ {-}} = k _ {\mathrm{SPP}} + k _ {0} \sin \tilde {\theta}, \quad \frac {2 \pi}{\Lambda_ {+}} = k _ {\mathrm{SPP}} - k _ {0} \sin \tilde {\theta}. \tag {314}
$$

Substituting these expressions into the grating equation (noting that $k_{SPP}$ should be taken as negative in the x < 0 region), we find that the first-order diffracted light on the x < 0 side and the negative first-order diffracted light on the x > 0 side (both being the only diffracted orders satisfying $k_{0} \sin \theta < k_{0}$ that can radiate outward) share the same diffraction angle $\tilde{\theta}$ . This design enables directional control of single-photon emission, with the observed effect shown in the right panel of Fig. 49(c) [608].

(IV) Still based on the design in (I), the bullseye grating is modified into a superposition of m helical gratings, as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 49(d) (where m = -3, with the negative sign indicating a counterclockwise helicity). In this configuration, the far-field polarization state of the emitted single photon can be expressed as an entangled state of LCP and RCP photons with different orbital angular momenta. Specifically, the topological charges of the LCP and RCP photons are m - 1 and $m + 1$ , respectively. The observed effect is shown in the right panel of Fig. 49(d) [610].

(V) Still based on the design in (I), as illustrated in Fig. 49(e), the concentric ring structure is replaced with an array of meta-atoms arranged in a circular pattern. These meta-atoms are deliberately designed to exhibit chiral responses, enabling the conversion of SPPs into freely propagating photons with a specific circular polarization state. This design achieves a polarization control similar to that of (II). However, a key advantage is its greater tolerance to lateral displacement of the single-photon source relative to the center, making it more robust for practical implementations $[615]$ .

(VI) By combining the designs of (III) and (V), meta-atoms with specific chiral responses are arranged along the trajectory of the grating structure in (III). This configuration enables the generation of photons with specific OAM in a designated direction [616].

(VII) By integrating the concepts of (IV) and (V), meta-atoms with specific chiral responses are arranged in a helical pattern, facilitating the generation of photons with tailored spin and orbital angular momentum $[612]$ . Furthermore, as illustrated in Fig. 49(f), when the meta-atoms are designed to convert SPPs within a specific azimuthal range into freely propagating photons with a well-defined linear polarization state, it is also possible to generate linearly polarized photons with specific OAM $[614]$ .

(VIII) Utilizing the scattering holography method developed by Bozhevolnyi's group [611,613], more sophisticated metasurfaces can be designed to generate single photons with specific polarization characteristics propagating in predefined directions. For example, a metasurface designed using this approach [illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 49(g)] can spatially separate single photons with $x$ - and $y$ -linear polarizations in two predefined directions in the far field, as demonstrated in the right panel of Fig. 49(g) [613].

For single-photon sources that do not rely on SPPs, metasurfaces can still be directly used to manipulate various properties of emitted photons. In 2019, Huang et al. [617] employed a propagation-phase-based metalens as an immersion lens to collect and collimate photons emitted by a single-photon source, achieving an NA exceeding 1. In

2020, Bao et al. [618] utilized a geometric-phase metasurface to deflect photons with different circular polarization states into distinct directions. More recently, in 2023, Li et al. [619] designed meta-atoms based on propagation phase to cover complete phase modulation for orthogonal linearly polarized light. This enabled polarization-multiplexed metasurfaces capable of deflecting single photons with different linear polarization states into different directions while simultaneously imparting distinct OAM. It is important to note that in certain cases, single-photon sources must be approximated as point dipole sources. Therefore, metasurface designs need to account for additional considerations beyond those required for plane-wave illumination [618].

# 6.1.2. Two-Photon Source Based on Nonlinear Metasurfaces

Spontaneous parametric down-conversion (SPDC) is one of the most widely used nonlinear optical techniques for generating entangled and correlated photon-pairs. When an intense pump laser photon of frequency $\omega_{p}$ interacts with the nonlinear crystal such as KDP or lithium niobate ( $LiNbO_{3}$ ), the high-energy pump photon is converted into a pair of low-energy photons with frequencies $\omega_{s}$ (signal photon) and $\omega_{i}$ (idler photon). This process must satisfy energy conservation

$$
\omega_ {\mathrm{p}} = \omega_ {\mathrm{s}} + \omega_ {\mathrm{i}}, \tag {315}
$$

and momentum conservation (phase-matching condition)

$$
\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{p}} = \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{s}} + \vec {k} _ {\mathrm{i}}, \tag {316}
$$

where $\vec{k}_{p}$ , $\vec{k}_{s}$ , and $\vec{k}_{i}$ represent the wavevectors of the pump photon, signal photon, and idler photon, respectively. We note that it is essential to satisfy the phase-matching condition to ensure both high conversion efficiency and the desired properties of the generated photons, such as polarization. When $\omega_{s} = \omega_{i}$ , the SPDC is referred to as degenerate.

Conventional SPDC implementations typically employ bulky centimeter-scale crystals, which present two main limitations: (i) relatively low photon-pair rates and (ii) considerable difficulty in aligning multiple optical components following the SPDC crystal. The advent of nonlinear metasurfaces $[254–257]$ has created novel opportunities for realizing miniaturized, easily integrated, and high-performance two-photon sources. Benefiting from subwavelength thickness, metasurfaces relax the longitudinal phase-matching condition in SPDC processes $[620]$ , enabling diverse emission configurations including simultaneous forward emission, simultaneous backward emission, or a combination of both directions $[621,622]$ —a feature unattainable in conventional SPDC systems. According to the generalized laws of refraction and reflection [Eqs. (59) and (60)], the arrangement of meta-atoms also permits the relaxation of the transverse momentum conservation condition. Furthermore, by utilizing the high-Q-factor resonant modes, metasurfaces can enhance the nonlinear effects by several orders of magnitude at the resonance wavelength $[256]$ , supporting higher photon-pair rates than precedents of enhanced SPDC using individual nanoantennas $[623]$ . Additionally, the light field manipulation capabilities of metasurfaces enable flexible and efficient control over various dimensions of the generated two photons, such as direction and polarization.

In 2021, Santiago-Cruz et al. [624] modulate the frequency of the Mie resonance in a homogeneous lithium niobate metasurface to half the pump light frequency, which enhanced the degenerate SPDC significantly. Experimentally, the metasurface composed of truncated pyramid meta-atoms with a height of $680\mathrm{nm}$ achieved a two-order-of-magnitude increase in the photon-pair rate compared to an unpatterned thin film of the same material and thickness. Following this, researchers further investigated the

application of various nonlinear metasurfaces in two-photon sources through theoretical analysis and numerical simulations $[625–627]$ . Notably, simulations demonstrated that resonant modes with ultra-high Q-factors, such as quasi-BICs, could potentially enhance the photon-pair rate by up to five orders of magnitude relative to unpatterned thin films under identical conditions $[625]$ .

In 2022, utilizing a guided-mode resonance metasurface, the Sukhorukov's group $[628]$ significantly increased the photon-pair rate by 450 times compared to an unpatterned thin film under the same conditions. As shown in Fig. 50(a), the two-photon source consists of a 304 nm thick lithium niobate film on a silica substrate, with a silica grating layer on top. The relationship between eigenfrequencies of grating resonant modes and the transverse wavevector $k_{y}$ at $k_{z}=0$ is shown in Fig. 50(b). The guided-mode resonance (Mode 2) was utilized, where half of the pump photon frequency falls within the resonance frequency range of this mode. In this case, the signal and idler photons generated symmetrically naturally satisfy energy and transverse momentum conservation. As shown in Fig. 50(b), the $k_{y}$ values of the signal and idler photons can be adjusted by fine-tuning the pump photon frequency within the resonance range of the guided mode resonance, enabling a certain extent of manipulation over the emission angles of the photon-pairs $[622]$ . This feature can be utilized in the SPDC-based ghost imaging system to realize scanning imaging, which allows the use of a 1D detector to capture 2D images $[630]$ . Further simulation analysis for this system showed that the spatial entanglement of the two photons can be adjusted by the wavelength and beam size of the pump laser $[631]$ . In 2023, the research group further demonstrated experimentally that the polarization state of the emitted photon-pairs can be manipulated by changing the groove direction of the grating $[632]$ .

In 2022, almost simultaneously with the Sukhorukov's group [628], Santiago-Cruz et al. [629] utilized the quasi-BIC metasurface to enhance the photon-pair rate by three orders of magnitude compared to an unpatterned thin film under identical conditions. The metasurface structure is shown in Fig. 50(c), with meta-atoms made of GaAs, one of the traditional materials with the highest second-order susceptibilities. As shown in Fig. 50(d), the meta-atom is designed with the symmetry-breaking structure, which transforms the symmetry-protected BIC into quasi-BIC, achieving a sufficiently high Q-factor. For three metasurfaces based on the same structure but with different meta-atom sizes and lattice constants, the experimental transmission spectra and SPDC spectra are shown in Fig. 50(e). (I) For the first metasurface (left panel), the pump photon frequency is exactly twice the resonance frequency of the quasi-BIC, resulting in the degenerate SPDC, with both the signal and idler photon frequencies matching the resonance frequency of the quasi-BIC. (II) For the second metasurface (middle panel), the pump photon frequency is slightly offset from twice the resonance frequency of the quasi-BIC, leading to the signal photon frequency matching the quasi-BIC resonance frequency, while the idler photon frequency is determined by the energy conservation condition. (III) For the third metasurface (right panel), by choosing the appropriate polarization state of the pump light, two quasi-BICs within the metasurface can be excited, leading to the emission of two sets of photon-pairs, where the frequency of the signal photon in each set is the resonance frequency of a certain quasi-BIC, and the frequency of the idler photon is given by the energy conservation condition. In 2023, Son et al. [621] reproduced the above scenario (II) based on a GaP metasurface shown in Fig. 50(f), increasing the photon-pair rate by a factor of 67 compared to an unpatterned thin film under the same conditions.

# 6.1.3. Quantum Interference

Optical quantum interference, particularly two-photon interference, is a quantum phenomenon without a classical analogue, which plays an irreplaceable role in fields such

Figure 50  
![](images/c5412c58f555cf2a38da86e03666230919491649bf0dcfff46e2ec6bc13bfc49.jpg)

Nonlinear metasurface-based two-photon sources. (a) Schematic of a silica-grating-patterned lithium niobate thin film for generating spatially entangled signal and idler photons under continuous-wave laser pumping. (b) Relationship between resonance frequencies and transverse wavenumber $k_{y}$ when $k_{z}=0$ for the grating in (a). Shaded regions indicate resonant bandwidths. (c) SEM of a BIC metasurface. (d) Structural schematic of the symmetry-broken meta-atom for the metasurface in (c). (e) Experimental transmission spectra (top) and SPDC spectra (bottom) for three metasurfaces with identical geometries but varying meta-atom dimensions and lattice constants. Black dashed lines mark the position corresponding to twice the pump wavelength for the degenerate SPDC. Colored vertical lines indicate quasi-BIC resonance wavelengths. (f) SEM of an alternative BIC metasurface architecture. (a) and (b) Adapted from [628] under a Creative Commons license. (c), (d), and (e) Adapted with permission from Santiago-Cruz et al., Science 377, 991–995 (2022) [629]. Copyright © 2022 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (f) Adapted from [621] under a Creative Commons license.

as quantum information and quantum imaging [633]. Traditionally, the beam splitter is the core element for achieving two-photon interference. When two photons, completely indistinguishable to the detector, simultaneously enter the two input ports of a 50:50 beam splitter, quantum interference results in the photon-pairs emerging only from the same output port, leading to a significant reduction in coincidence counts, known as the Hong–Ou–Mandel (HOM) effect. Essentially, such a bunching effect arises from the bosonic nature of photons.

For a traditional 50:50 beam splitter, the phase difference $\phi_{\mathrm{rt}}$ between the reflected and transmitted light is inherently $\pi /2$ . By allowing the adjustment of $\phi_{\mathrm{rt}}$ , the result of two-photon interference can overcome the limitations of the bunching effect. In 2021, Zhang's group [634] proposed replacing the traditional beam splitter with a metasurface, as shown in Fig. 51(a), to enable free adjustment of $\phi_{\mathrm{rt}}$ . The metasurface was deliberately designed with a vertically symmetric structure to satisfy the requirement of reciprocity. Through structural parameter optimization, it was achieved that when the metasurface is rotated around the $z$ -axis by an angle $\theta$ of 0 or $\pi /2$ , the phase difference $\phi_{\mathrm{rt}}$ for specific linearly polarized incident light also becomes 0 or $\pi /2$ , while maintaining equal amplitude values for both the transmission and reflection coefficients. As shown in Fig. 51(b), when $\theta = 0$ , two-photon interference exhibits

Figure 51  
![](images/22b57abc7d146d04788662c364449c7492329475a14e709d6deab8e07e277d78.jpg)

Metasurface applications in quantum interference. (a) Schematic of continuous control over two-photon interference from bunching to anti-bunching via metasurface rotation. (b) Experimentally measured second-order correlation function as a function of metasurface rotation angle. Inset: schematics of two-photon interference phenomena at rotation angles of 0, $\pi/4$ , and $\pi/2$ (left to right). (c) Structural schematics of two phase-change-material-based metasurfaces for two-photon interference manipulation. (a) and (b) Adapted with permission from Li et al., Nat. Photonics 15, 267–271 (2021) [634]. Copyright © 2021 Springer Nature. (c) Reprinted with permission from Estakhri et al., Opt. Express 29, 14245–14259 (2021) [635]. Copyright © 2021 Optica Publishing Group.

an anti-bunching effect (fermion-like); when $\theta = \pi/2$ , the interference shows a bunching effect similar to the traditional beam splitter (boson-like); and when $0 < \theta < \pi/2$ , the interference exhibits a middle state (anyon-like). Such metasurface beam splitters with the phase modulation function can also find applications in quantum computing [636]. Following similar ideas, in 2021, Estakhri and Norris [635] proposed two types of active metasurface designs based on the phase-change material GeTe [as shown in Fig. 51(c)] to control the switching between bunching and anti-bunching in two-photon interference, supported by simulation verification.

# 6.1.4. Quantum Entanglement

A landmark application of metasurfaces in quantum entanglement was made by Stav et al. in 2018 [637]. The generation of vortex beams (for example, with a topological charge of $\Delta l$ ) using geometric phase metasurfaces is a well-established topic in the metasurface field. For classical light with a linear polarization state, the outgoing light can be decomposed into orthogonal circular polarization components, each with a topological charge of $\pm \Delta l$ . However, the situation becomes much more interesting when considering non-classical light. A single photon with an OAM of 0 and horizontal polarization can be represented as a superposition of the $|L\rangle$ and $|R\rangle$ spins, i.e.,

$$
| H \rangle | l = 0 \rangle = \frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left(| L \rangle + | R \rangle\right) | l = 0 \rangle . \tag {317}
$$

Figure 52  
![](images/d78e743aaa4687897972c48c2702bfeb3e67218d31d01a9b67b0d76923cba8d5.jpg)

Metasurface applications in quantum entanglement. (a) Schematic of a geometric phase metasurface establishing spin-orbit entanglement for a single photon. (b) Design concept of a geometric phase metagrating enabling controlled entanglement generation and disentanglement of two-photon states. (a) Adapted with permission from Stav et al., Science 361, 1101–1104 (2018) [637]. Copyright © 2018 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (b) Reprinted from [638] under a Creative Commons license.

As shown in Fig. 52(a), after interaction with the metasurface, the photon state becomes

$$
\frac {1}{\sqrt {2}} \left(| L \rangle | l = \Delta l > + | R \rangle | l = - \Delta l \rangle\right). \tag {318}
$$

It is noteworthy that the single photon is now in a Bell state, with its spin entangled with the OAM. By changing the incident light to vertical polarization $|V\rangle$ and flipping the metasurface, three other Bell states can be generated. Further experiments demonstrated that the metasurface does not destroy the coherence of the wave function and allows for the generation of spin-OAM entanglement with high fidelity.

In 2019, Georgi et al. [638] used the interaction of single-photon pairs with orthogonal linear polarization states and a geometric phase metagrating to generate path-entangled two-photon N00N states. As shown in the left part of Fig. 52(b), when the single-photon pairs with orthogonal linear polarization states interact with the grating, they are decomposed into circularly polarized components propagating along different paths. This occurs because the geometric phase causes phase gradients of opposite sign for LCP and RCP light. Since the quantum state of the incident photon-pair is represented as a two-photon N00N state in the circular basis, the two photons always select the same output channel, leading to the spatial entanglement. The further experiment achieved a HOM interference visibility of $86 \pm 4\%$ . As shown in the right part of Fig. 52(b), if this process is reversed, the spatially entangled N00N state will return to its initial orthogonal polarization state after undergoing quantum interference on

the metasurface, thereby achieving spatial disentanglement of the photon-pair. Introducing a phase delay between the two incident circular polarization channels allows for further control over the degree of entanglement in the output state, a feature with promising applications in quantum sensing.

Other applications of metasurfaces in quantum entanglement will be discussed in Subsection 6.2.

# 6.2. Indirect Applications of Metasurfaces in Quantum Photonics

Multi-photon source based on the metalens array. Traditional SPDC-based multiphoton sources require multiple nonlinear crystals and long-term stable, complex optical setups. In 2020, Li et al. [639] realized a compact, stable, and controllable 100-channel SPDC photon-pair source by integrating a 0.5 mm thick type-II $\beta$ -barium borate (BBO) crystal at the focal length of a $10 \times 10$ metalens array. All the metalenses were identical and operated at a pump wavelength of 404 nm. As shown in Fig. 53(a), when the pump light is incident on the metalens array, a $10 \times 10$ focal array is formed inside the BBO crystal. Each focal point can trigger the generation of a photon pair through SPDC in the crystal with a certain probability, ultimately creating a 100-dimensional path-encoded quantum entangled state.

Cold atom generation based on the metasurface hologram. As one of the techniques for atomic cooling and trapping, the standard magneto-optical trap (MOT) typically uses three pairs of orthogonal, counter-propagating laser beams with appropriate circular polarization. When implemented with traditional optics, a large number of optical components are required to achieve functions such as laser beam transmission and polarization conversion, which detracts from the system's compactness and stability. In 2020, Zhu et al. [640] significantly simplified the system by using a metasurface hologram. As shown in Fig. 53(b), the geometric phase metasurface hologram diffracts a single LCP light beam into five RCP light beams. After being reflected by mirrors, these beams overlap in the center with the polarization states required by the MOT. The atoms are trapped near the zero point of the quadrupole magnetic field in the region where the beams overlap. Experimentally, they achieved the capture of approximately $10^{7}$ atoms at a temperature of about $35\mu \mathrm{K}$ .

Quantum computing. In 2024, Hu et al. [649] used orthogonal linear polarization states as the ground states $|0\rangle$ and $|1\rangle$ and employed the polarization-multiplexed metasurface to implement four quantum logic gates (Pauli-X, Pauli-Y, Pauli-Z, and Hadamard gates) for the superposition state $\alpha |0\rangle +\beta |1\rangle$ , where $\alpha ,\beta \in \mathbb{R}$ . The target phase distributions required for the metasurface to realize these operations were optimized with deep learning. In the same year, Tanuwijaya et al. [650] used the complex amplitude modulation metasurface described in Subsection 5.1.2 [Method (II)] for a quantum computing system, successfully implementing Grover's search algorithm and quantum Fourier transform.

Quantum entanglement. In 2018, Jha et al. [651] demonstrated through simulations that the optical deflection effect of metasurfaces could induce quantum entanglement between atomic qubits of two atoms separated by a macroscopic distance. In 2022, Zhang et al. [641] combined a nonlinear metasurface with a photoisomerizable azo layer to achieve dynamic control of the transmission and phase of orthogonal linearly polarized light by optically switching the azo molecules between their binary isomeric states. This was applied to the distillation of quantum entanglement, as shown in Fig. 53(c). In the same year, Gao et al. [642] employed a propagating phase meta-grating to achieve multi-channel distribution and entanglement state transformation of photon-pairs, as shown in Fig. 53(d). Also in 2022, Li et al. [652] encoded path

Figure 53  
![](images/c035c5837efc614de1c2483676666d5af6853aa27b12420bc4d1e2f6b43be875.jpg)

Indirect applications of metasurfaces in quantum photonics. (a) Schematic of a metal-ens array-based multiphoton source. (b) Design concept of a metasurface hologram-integrated cold atom generation system. (c) Schematic illustrating dynamic control of entangled quantum states via a nonlinear metasurface. (d) Metagrating configuration for quantum entanglement distribution and transformation. (e) Principle schematic of edge detection mode switching in polarization-entangled photon source-based imaging systems. (f) Quantum weak measurement protocol for phase reconstruction of pure-phase objects. (g) Polarization-multiplexed metasurface enabling multiphoton quantum state reconstruction. Top-right: SEM of the fabricated Si metasurface. (h) Schematic of metasurface-enabled field manipulation for polarization Bell state measurement. (i) Schematic of the metasurface for implementing positive operator-valued measures. $|\pm\rangle = (|H\rangle \pm |V\rangle) / \sqrt{2}$ (j) Top: SEMs of fabricated metasurface in top-view and tilted-view configurations. Bottom: two-input/three-output metagrating design. $\theta = \arcsin \lambda / a_x$ (the key to achieving the two-input/three-output functionality), where $\lambda$ denotes the operational wavelength and $a_x$ represents the metasurface period along the $x$ -direction.
