(b) Reprinted from [640] under a Creative Commons license. (c) Reprinted from [641] under a Creative Commons license. (d) Reprinted with permission from Gao et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 129, 023601 (2022) [642]. Copyright © 2022 American Physical Society. (e) Reprinted from [643] under a Creative Commons license. (f) Reprinted with the permission from Liu et al., Phys. Rev. Lett. 132, 043601 (2024) [644]. Copyright © 2024 American Physical Society. (g) Reprinted with permission from Wang et al., Science 361, 1104–1108 (2018) [645]. Copyright © 2018 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (h) Reprinted from [646] under a Creative Commons license. (i) Reprinted from [647] under a Creative Commons license. (j) Adapted with permission from Zhang et al., Optica 11, 753–758 (2024) [648]. Copyright © 2024 Optica Publishing Group.

(direction of deflection) and OAM information onto one of the polarization-entangled photon-pairs (simultaneously LCP or RCP) by a geometric phase metasurface, which realized the phase distribution of the superposition of vortex light generation and beam deflection.

Quantum imaging. In 2020, Zhou et al. [643] demonstrated the dynamic switching between normal imaging and edge detection modes in an imaging system using a polarization-entangled photon source. As shown in Fig. 53(e), the quantum state of the entangled photon source is $\frac{1}{\sqrt{2}}\left(|H\rangle|H\rangle+|V\rangle|V\rangle\right)$ , and the edge detection system is effective only for vertically polarized light [653]. Therefore, by selecting the appropriate polarization state in the heralding arm, either normal imaging ( $|H\rangle$ ) or edge detection ( $|V\rangle$ ) can be performed. Using a similar approach, in 2024, Liu et al. [644] introduced a nonlocal weak measurement method, as shown in Fig. 53(f), into microscopic imaging, achieving higher image contrast at low quantum levels than standard methods.

Quantum state characterization. Traditional quantum state characterization devices suffer from several drawbacks, including large size, difficulty in integration, complex alignment procedures, limited functionality, and slow measurement speeds (multiple measurements are required to complete the characterization for some complex quantum states). The application of metasurfaces holds promise in overcoming these limitations. In 2018, Sukhorukov's group $[645]$ employed a metasurface to achieve stable and efficient reconstruction of the amplitude, phase, correlations, and entanglement of the polarization-encoded quantum state of the multiphoton, as shown in Fig. 53(g). By applying the hybrid phase modulation mechanism introduced in Subsection 2.1.5 $[169]$ , they designed a metagrating capable of deflecting any orthogonal polarization state in different directions. Using spatial multiplexing, they arranged M gratings corresponding to different orthogonal elliptical polarization states on the same metasurface. By directly measuring all possible N-photon correlations from the 2M output beams, the density matrix of the N-photon incident on the metasurface could be reconstructed when $2M \geq N + 3$ . Based on a similar approach, in 2023, Gao et al. $[646]$ replaced the polarization beam splitter in the Innsbruck Bell states measurement scheme with a polarization-multiplexed metasurface to achieve the polarization and phase control of the output light, as shown in Fig. 53(h). The further numerical simulations demonstrated that this system could perform the complete polarization Bell state measurement with only two measurements. In 2023, Wang et al. $[654]$ utilized a metasurface with orthogonal linear polarization state multiplexing to reconstruct the density matrix of the OAM quantum states of single-photons. In 2024, An et al. $[647]$ significantly reduced the complexity of positive operator-valued measures (POVMs) by using three polarization-multiplexed metagratings arranged side by side, as shown in Fig. 53(i). In 2024, Zhang et al. $[648]$ used topological optimization to design a metagrating [shown in Fig. 53(j)] as a multi-channel interferometer with two inputs and three outputs. This design enables the characterization of the indistinguishability of two photons across multiple degrees of freedom, including time, spectrum, spatial mode, and polarization, all with a single measurement.

# 7. METASURFACES FOR OPTICAL COMPUTING

Compared to electronics for computing, optical computing technology is expected to demonstrate significant advantages in computational speed and energy efficiency $[655]$ . Optical computing leverages the physical properties of light to encode, transmit, and process data, with a long-standing research history. Compared to traditional optics, metasurface-based optical computing offers many important advantages, including low loss, light weight, miniaturization, ease of integration, multifunctionality, and the ability to perform parallel operations $[656–662]$ . Currently, the application of metasurfaces in optical computing mainly focuses on mathematical operations on input optical fields, constructing mathematical equation solvers, and building optical neural networks, all of which we will introduce one by one in the following.

# 7.1. Mathematical Operations

# 7.1.1. Realizing the Transformation Function with Nonlocal Metasurfaces

In 2014, Engheta's group introduced the concept of metamaterial analog computing in their pioneering paper [663], demonstrating the feasibility of using metamaterials to perform mathematical operations such as spatial differentiation, integration, and convolution. The paper proposed two architectures to achieve this, one of which employs nonlocal metamaterials to realize the transfer function $\tilde{\mathbf{H}}\left(\vec{k}_T\right)$ (i.e., the Green function kernel), thereby directly manipulating the spatial frequency spectrum of the input wave. The concept of the transfer function has been introduced in Subsection 2.2.2. Different mathematical operations in real space correspond to different transfer functions. For instance, the transfer function of the first-order differentiation operation is given by

$$
\tilde {\mathbf {H}} \left(\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}}\right) = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} i C _ {x x} k _ {x} & i C _ {x y} k _ {y} \\ i C _ {y x} k _ {x} & i C _ {y y} k _ {y} \end{array} \right], \tag {319}
$$

where $C_{xx}$ , $C_{xy}$ , $C_{yx}$ , and $C_{yy}$ are constants characterizing polarization properties. The transfer function of the second-order differentiation operation is given by

$$
\tilde {\mathbf {H}} \left(\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}}\right) = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} C _ {x x} \left(k _ {x} ^ {2} + k _ {y} ^ {2}\right) & C _ {x y} \left(k _ {x} ^ {2} + k _ {y} ^ {2}\right) \\ C _ {y x} \left(k _ {x} ^ {2} + k _ {y} ^ {2}\right) & C _ {y y} \left(k _ {x} ^ {2} + k _ {y} ^ {2}\right) \end{array} \right]. \tag {320}
$$

For the integration operation, the transfer function is expressed as

$$
\tilde {\mathbf {H}} \left(\vec {k} _ {\mathrm{T}}\right) = \left[ \begin{array}{l l} C _ {x x} \frac {1}{i k _ {x}} & C _ {x y} \frac {1}{i k _ {y}} \\ C _ {y x} \frac {1}{i k _ {x}} & C _ {y y} \frac {1}{i k _ {y}} \end{array} \right]. \tag {321}
$$

To implement these transfer functions, the amplitude of the transmission or reflection coefficient of the metasurface must vary significantly with the incident angle. Consequently, it is necessary to leverage various resonance effects introduced in Section 2.

When implementing first-order differentiation or integration operations using a metasurface, it is necessary to break the mirror symmetry of the metasurface along both the x- and z-directions. Taking the 1D mathematical operation along the x-direction as an example, the transfer function $\tilde{H}(k_{x})$ differs by a negative sign (i.e., a phase difference of $\pi$ ) between $k_{x} < 0$ and $k_{x} > 0$ . This requires the transmission coefficient of the metasurface (assuming it operates in transmission mode) to be antisymmetric, i.e., $T(k_{x}) = -T(-k_{x})$ . If the metasurface only breaks mirror symmetry along the z-direction (i.e., a nonreciprocal metasurface), as shown in the top panel of Fig. 54(a), the mirror symmetry along the x-direction ensures that

$$
T _ {1 \rightarrow 4} = T _ {2 \rightarrow 3}, \tag {322}
$$

where $T_{M \to N}$ represents the transmission coefficient from direction $M$ to direction $N$ . This equation implies that $T(k_x) = T(-k_x)$ . If the metasurface only breaks mirror symmetry along the $x$ -direction, as shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 54(a), the mirror symmetry along the $z$ -direction leads to

$$
T _ {4 \rightarrow 1} = T _ {2 \rightarrow 3}. \tag {323}
$$

Additionally, due to reciprocity,

$$
T _ {1 \rightarrow 4} = T _ {4 \rightarrow 1}. \tag {324}
$$

From the above two equations, we again obtain

$$
T _ {1 \rightarrow 4} = T _ {2 \rightarrow 3}. \tag {325}
$$

Figure 54  
![](images/29e08a14f788f06009f0cbb09bf4cb47f0a9e9718bf4d98c138a57748f4eedf6.jpg)

Mathematical operations based on metasurfaces. (a) Schematic of the meta-atom with mirror symmetry broken only in the z-direction (top) or only in the x-direction (bottom). (b) Top: original image. Bottom: experimental edge detection result using a metasurface for the second-order differentiation operation in the x-direction. (c) For p-polarization, the amplitude of the transmission coefficient as a function of the incident angle $\theta$ and frequency. $f_{0}$ is the operating frequency. $f_{BIC}$ is the BIC frequency. (d) Left: original images. Right: experimental edge detection results using a metasurface for the second-order differentiation operation. (e) Experimental edge detection results (right) on original images (left) under the illumination of different wavelengths achieved using a metasurface-based 4f system. (f) A 4f system for edge detection based on the geometric phase metasurface. CCD, charge-coupled camera; L, lens; P, polarizer. (g) Top: original images. Bottom: experimental edge detection results after performing first-order differential operation in the y-direction using the system in (f). From left to right, illumination wavelengths are 430 nm, 500 nm, and 670 nm, respectively. (h) Experimental edge detection result (right) on the original image (left) through convolution with the PSF of metalens. Scale bars: 10 $\mu$ m. (i) PSFs of a spiral metalens and a normal hyperbolic metalens. (j) Experimental edge detection results (right) on original images (left) using a geometric phase spiral metalens. From top to bottom, illumination wavelengths are 497 nm, 532 nm, 580 nm, and 633 nm, respectively (k) A 2f optical system with metasurface-enabled direct Fourier spectrum modulation. (b) Adapted from [664] under a Creative Commons license. (c) and (d) Adapted with permission from Zhou et al., Nat. Photonics 14, 316–323 (2020) [198]. Copyright © 2020 Springer Nature. (e) Adapted from [665] under a Creative Commons license. (f) and (g) Adapted from [653] under a Creative Commons license. (h) Adapted from [666] under a Creative Commons licensee. (i) and (j) Adapted from [667] under a Creative Commons license. (k) Reprinted from [668] under a Creative Commons license.

Thus, the metasurface must simultaneously break mirror symmetry along both the x- and z-directions to achieve $T_{1\to4} \neq T_{2\to3}$ , which makes it possible to realize $T(k_{x}) = -T(-k_{x})$ .

When implementing second-order differentiation along the x-direction, the transfer function $\tilde{H}(k_{x})$ remains the same for both $k_{x}<0$ and $k_{x}>0$ , meaning that the transmission coefficient of the metasurface must satisfy $T(k_{x})=T(-k_{x})$ . In this case, it is only necessary for the metasurface to maintain mirror symmetry along the x-direction.

In summary, implementing first-order differentiation or integration operations is more challenging than implementing second-order differentiation [197].

In practice, a metasurface can approximate a given transfer function for a specific mathematical operation only within a limited range of the incident angle, denoted as $\alpha \leq \alpha_{max}$ . This limitation can be described using the NA:

$$
\mathrm{NA} = n _ {\text {incident}} \sin \alpha_ {\max} = \frac {k _ {\mathrm{T} , \max}}{k _ {0}}, \tag {326}
$$

where $n_{incident}$ is the refractive index of the incident medium, and $k_{T,max}$ is the maximum transverse wavenumber. For a metasurface designed to perform differentiation operations for image edge detection, a higher NA corresponds to higher edge resolution.

In 2014, Engheta's group [663] numerically demonstrated that mathematical operations such as second-order differentiation and convolution can be achieved using the multilayer metamaterial slab that is laterally uniform but vertically inhomogeneous. The dielectric permittivity, magnetic permeability, and thickness of each layer of the metamaterial slab are optimized to realize specific transfer functions. From 2018 to 2021, researchers proposed various types of metasurface designs based on numerical simulations, leveraging resonant effects such as guided mode resonance and quasi-BIC to implement first-order differentiation [197,664,669] and second-order differentiation [197,199,669–671]. In 2019, Cordaro et al. [664] experimentally demonstrated second-order differentiation along the x-direction with an NA of 0.35 by tuning the operating frequency near the guided mode resonance of a metagrating, as shown in Fig. 54(b). In 2020, Zhou et al. [198] demonstrated a homogeneous metasurface composed of cylindrical Si meta-atoms to perform second-order differentiation. As illustrated in Fig. 54(c), this metasurface exhibited symmetry-protected BIC at normal incidence and a specific frequency $f_{BIC}$ . By selecting an operating frequency near $f_{BIC}$ , the metasurface can approximate the amplitude variation of the transmission coefficient required for second-order differentiation [Eq. (320)], within an NA of 0.32, while maintaining a nearly constant phase. The resulting edge detection performance for images is shown in Fig. 54(d). In 2021, Komar et al. [212] designed a homogeneous metasurface composed of cylindrical Si meta-atoms arranged in a hexagonal lattice. At the operating wavelength, the metasurface exhibited Mie resonance effects, enabling the experimental realization of polarization-insensitive second-order differentiation (although the NA varied for different polarizations).

Implementing a specific transfer function using a metasurface essentially involves complex amplitude modulation in momentum space (wavevector space) to realize mathematical operations in real space. Conversely, complex amplitude modulation in real space can also be used to perform mathematical operations in momentum space. In 2024, Deng et al. [672] experimentally demonstrated the differentiation operations in momentum space employing the amplitude modulation method introduced in Subsection 5.1.2 [Eq. (260)].

# 7.1.2. Metasurface-Based 4f Systems

Another architecture proposed in Engheta's paper [663] for implementing mathematical operations is the metasurface-enabled 4f system. This system consists of three cascaded subblocks: a Fourier transform subblock to obtain the frequency spectrum of the input wave (object), a metasurface to modulate the spectrum, and an inverse Fourier transform subblock to reconstruct the modulated spectrum into a real-space field distribution. In this case, only a conventional local metasurface is required to realize the transfer function $\tilde{\mathbf{H}}\left(\vec{k}_{\mathrm{T}}\right)$ for a given mathematical operation. This is because,

with the assistance of the Fourier transform and inverse Fourier transform subblocks, the metasurface needs only to perform complex amplitude modulation in real space to manipulate the frequency spectrum of the input wave. For example, in the case of first-order differentiation along the x-direction, the transfer function is given by

$$
\tilde {H} \left(k _ {x}\right) = i C _ {x x} k _ {x}. \tag {327}
$$

Due to the correspondence between the spatial position on the metasurface and the frequency spectrum, from Eq. (327), the transmission coefficient profile of the metasurface (assuming it operates in transmission mode) should satisfy

$$
T _ {x x} (x) = t _ {\max} \frac {x}{L _ {x} / 2}, \tag {328}
$$

where $t_{max}$ is the maximum amplitude transmission coefficient, $L_{x}$ is the metasurface size in the x-direction, and the coordinate origin is at the center of the metasurface. Here, we omit the constant phase factor $i = e^{i\pi/2}$ . Similarly, for second-order differentiation along the x-direction, according to Eq. (320), the transmission coefficient profile should satisfy

$$
T _ {x x} (x) = t _ {\max} \frac {x ^ {2}}{(L _ {x} / 2) ^ {2}}. \tag {329}
$$

For integration along the x-direction, based on Eq. (321), the transmission coefficient profile should satisfy

$$
T _ {x x} (x) = \left\{ \begin{array}{l l} t _ {\max}, & | x | \leq d, \\ t _ {\max} \frac {d}{x}, & | x | > d, \end{array} \right. \tag {330}
$$

where $d \ll L_{x}$ is introduced to prevent divergence in the transmission coefficient as $x \to 0$ .

Based on this approach, in 2015, Pors et al. [673] experimentally realized one-dimensional first-order differentiation and integration using MIM metasurfaces. In 2016 [674] and 2017 [675], Salehi's group numerically verified the mathematical operations of 4f systems based on the MIM metasurface and dielectric metasurface, respectively. In 2023, Tanriover et al. [665] used an annular metagrating with aluminum meta-atoms to achieve, within a certain bandwidth, a transmission coefficient profile matching Eq. (329) (with $x$ replaced by $r$ ). This second-order differentiation metasurface can be applied to image edge detection, as shown in Fig. 54(e).

7.1.3. First-Order Differentiation Operation Based on 4f System Modified by Polarization Optics In 2019, Zhou et al. [653] proposed a 4f system incorporating two orthogonal linear polarizers and a geometric phase metasurface, as shown in Fig. 54(f), to perform first-order differentiation for applications such as edge detection. After passing through the first lens and linear polarizer, the frequency spectrum of the incident optical field $E_{\mathrm{in}}(x,y)$ is modulated by the metasurface. The metasurface is designed based on the geometric phase, with a phase distribution given by

$$
\phi (x, y) = \sigma \frac {2 \pi x}{\Lambda}, \tag {331}
$$

where $\sigma=\pm1$ corresponds to LCP and RCP, respectively, and $\Lambda$ is the period over which the geometric phase varies by $2\pi$ along the x-direction. According to Fourier transform properties, a linear phase shift in the frequency domain corresponds to a spatial displacement in the real space. Since the metasurface introduces opposite phase shifts for the LCP and RCP components of the linearly polarized light, the LCP and RCP components of the output light undergo opposite displacements along the x-direction, given by $\pm\Delta=\pm\lambda f/\Lambda$ , where f is the focal length of the lens. After passing

through the second orthogonal linear polarizer, only the non-overlapping edge regions remain, leading to

$$
E _ {\mathrm{out}} (x, y) = E _ {\mathrm{in}} (x + \Delta , y) - E _ {\mathrm{in}} (x - \Delta , y) \approx 2 \Delta \frac {\mathrm{d} E _ {\mathrm{in}} (x , y)}{\mathrm{d} x}. \tag {332}
$$

Thus, the output field is approximately proportional to the first-order derivative of the input field along the x-direction. By rotating the metasurface, differentiation can be performed in different directions. The system also inherits broadband characteristics from the geometric phase. Experimental results for edge detection under different illumination wavelengths are shown in Fig. 54(g).

If the phase gradient of the metasurface extends beyond the x-direction to an arbitrary radial direction, described by

$$
\phi (x, y) = \sigma \frac {2 \pi r}{\Lambda} = \sigma \frac {2 \pi \sqrt {x ^ {2} + y ^ {2}}}{\Lambda}, \tag {333}
$$

the system can further achieve two-dimensional first-order differentiation [676].

# 7.1.4. Arbitrary Convolution Enabled by Metalenses

The imaging process can be regarded as the convolution of an object with the PSF of a lens, where the PSF acts as the convolution kernel. By tailoring the PSF through the deliberate design of a metalens, various convolution functions can be realized. Similarly, in a 4f system, applying complex amplitude modulation T to the spectrum of the incident optical field (object) is equivalent to convolving the input field with $F\{T\}$ , where $F\{T\}$ serves as the PSF [677].

In 2022, Fu et al. [666] experimentally demonstrated operations such as edge detection, spatial differentiation, and salt-and-pepper noise removal using bilayer metasurfaces. Even with a single-layer metasurface, edge detection can still be achieved, as shown in Fig. 54(h), although the FOV in this case is limited. In the same year, Kim et al. [667] proposed a helical metalens by superimposing a hyperbolic phase distribution in Eq. (168) and a vortex beam phase distribution in Eq. (298) with a topological charge of 1. The resulting PSF, illustrated in Fig. 54(i), enables helical phase-contrast imaging, which functions as edge detection, as shown in Fig. 54(j). In 2023, Zhang et al. [678] combined the helical lens phase with a beam deflection phase in one direction and the hyperbolic phase with a beam deflection phase in another direction, as given in Eq. (56). The resulting metalens supports simultaneous helical phase-contrast imaging and bright-field imaging in two distinct directions.

7.1.5. 2f Systems with Metasurface-Enabled Direct Spatial Frequency Response Modulation If additional complex amplitude modulation is applied to the metasurface beyond focusing, a 2f system, as shown in Fig. 54(k), can be used to achieve the spatial frequency response of a conventional 4f system. When the complex amplitude modulation of the metasurface satisfies

$$
E _ {\mathrm{meta}} \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}\right) = e ^ {- i \frac {k}{2 f} \left(x ^ {\prime 2} + y ^ {\prime 2}\right)} E _ {\mathrm{H}} \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}\right), \tag {334}
$$

where $1/f = 1/f_{1} + 1/f_{2}$ , the output optical field $E_{1}(x_{1}, y_{1})$ and the input optical field $E_{0}(x_{1}, y_{1})$ satisfies

$$
E _ {\mathrm{H}} \left(- x ^ {\prime}, - y ^ {\prime}\right) \frac {\mathcal {F} \left\{E _ {1} \left(x _ {1} , y _ {1}\right) \right\}}{\mathcal {F} \left\{E _ {0} \left(x _ {1} , y _ {1}\right) e ^ {i \frac {k f _ {1}}{2 f _ {2} ^ {2}} \left(x _ {1} ^ {2} + y _ {1} ^ {2}\right)} \right\}}, \tag {335}
$$

indicating that the term $E_{H}$ in the complex amplitude modulation of the metasurface effectively acts as the transfer function. Based on this principle, in 2022, Wang et al. [668] experimentally demonstrated first-order differentiation and cross-correlation operations in the microwave regime using a single-layer metasurface.

# 7.2. Equation Solving

# 7.2.1. Solving Differential Equations

The Fourier transform method is a commonly used approach for solving linear ordinary differential equations (ODEs), particularly those involving infinite domains or periodic boundary conditions. Interestingly, this method can be implemented optically to solve ODEs. Consider the following second-order ODE as an example:

$$
\frac {\mathrm{d} ^ {2} y}{\mathrm{d} x ^ {2}} - \zeta y (x) = f (x), \tag {336}
$$

where $f(x)$ is the input signal of the system, integrable over $(-\infty, +\infty)$ , $y(x)$ is the system's output signal as the solution of the equation, and the parameter $\zeta$ is an arbitrary constant. Applying the Fourier transform to this equation yields

$$
\tilde {y} (k) = \tilde {H} (k) \tilde {f} (k), \quad \tilde {H} (k) = \frac {1}{- k ^ {2} - \zeta}, \tag {337}
$$

where $\tilde{y}(k)=\mathcal{F}\{y(x)\}(k)$ and $\tilde{f}(k)=\mathcal{F}\{f(x)\}(k)$ . Thus, solving the original equation follows these steps:

(1) Compute the Fourier transform of the input signal $f(x)$ to obtain $\tilde{f}(k)$ .  
(2) Apply the modulation function $\tilde{H}(k)$ to $\tilde{f}(k)$ to obtain $\tilde{y}(k)$ .  
(3) Compute the inverse Fourier transform of $\tilde{y}(k)$ to obtain the solution $y(x)$ .

These steps correspond precisely to the 4f system, where $\tilde{H}(k)$ acts as the transfer function and can be implemented using a metasurface. Notably, when m=0, the transfer function corresponds to a one-dimensional double integration operator. In 2016, Zhang et al. [679] numerically verified the feasibility of this method for solving second- and fourth-order ODEs.

The above method can also be extended to certain other types of differential equations, such as the following integro-differential equation [675]:

$$
\zeta \frac {\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{d} x} + \eta y (x) + \kappa \int y (x) \mathrm{d} x = f (x), \tag {338}
$$

where $f(x)$ is the system's input signal, $y(x)$ is the system's output signal as the solution of the equation, and $\zeta, \eta,$ and $\kappa$ are arbitrary constants. Applying the Fourier transform to this equation, the transfer function is obtained as

$$
\tilde {H} (k) = \frac {i k}{\kappa + i \eta k - \zeta k ^ {2}}. \tag {339}
$$

# 7.2.2. Solving Integral Equations

The Fredholm integral equation of the second kind is an important class of integral equations in physics, engineering, and mathematics, expressed as

$$
g (u) = I _ {\mathrm{in}} (u) + \int_ {a} ^ {b} K (u, v) g (v) \mathrm{d} v, \tag {340}
$$

where $g(u)$ is the solution of the integral equation, given an arbitrary input function $I_{\mathrm{in}}(u)$ and a known kernel operator $K(u, v)$ .

Figure 55  
![](images/60e088464564071a22bf2850f8706bb5c6f42cffaf45c0ebf188e71229552278.jpg)

![](images/7d9b46f82c1ed1188808297183e4708eb70ced09d48a953c59df40750edd64ce.jpg)

Mathematical equation solving based on metasurfaces. (a) Left and middle: schematics of two metamaterial systems for solving the Fredholm integral equation of the second kind with waves. Right: theoretical, simulation, and experimental results of the equation solutions of the integral equation corresponding to the two input signals (insets). (b) Schematic of the principle of a metagrating-based system for solving the Fredholm integral equation of the second kind with waves. (c) Schematic of the structure of the topology-optimized metagrating. (d) Left: schematic of the metasurface-based complex matrix equation solver. Right: comparison between the exact solution and the experimental solution of a complex matrix equation. (a) Adapted with permission from Estakhri et al., Science 363, 1333–1338 (2019) [680]. Copyright © 2019 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (b) and (c) Adapted with permission from Cordaro et al., Nat. Nanotechnol. 18, 365–372 (2023) [681]. Copyright © 2023 Springer Nature. (d) Adapted with permission from Yang et al., Adv. Funct. Mater. 34, 2310234 (2024) [682]. Copyright © 2023 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim.

A common technique for solving this equation is the Neumann successive approximation method, which starts with an initial solution $g_{0}(u) = I_{\mathrm{in}}(u)$ and iteratively updates it using

$$
g _ {i + 1} (u) = I _ {\mathrm{in}} (u) + \int_ {a} ^ {b} K (u, v) g _ {i} (v) \mathrm{d} v. \tag {341}
$$

As $n \to \infty$ , the sequence $g_{n}(u)$ converges to the solution $g(u)$ . In 2019 and 2023, Mohammadi et al. [680] and Andrea et al. [681] each proposed a metasurface-based system for the physical implementation of this iterative solving process.

(I) The system proposed by Mohammadi et al. in 2019 has two slightly different configurations, as illustrated in the left and middle panels of Fig. 55(a). The system samples both the metasurface's input $g(u)$ and its output $\int_{a}^{b} K(u, v) g(v) \, \mathrm{d}v$ at $N$ discrete points over the interval $[a, b]$ , and the scattering matrix corresponding to the discretized kernel operator $\mathbf{K}$ is realized by the topology-optimized metasurface. Through the waveguide channels (left panel) or reflection (middle panel), a recursive wave propagation feedback mechanism is established within the system to implement the iterative equation in Eq. (341). The discretized input function $I_{\mathrm{in}}(u)$ is coupled into the waveguides via the couplers embedded in the feedback loop, and a single optical wave propagation cycle within the loop corresponds to one iteration of Eq. (341). At steady state, the field distribution inside the system directly represents the solution $g(u)$ of the integral equation, which is then extracted through the couplers. Experimentally, the system shown in the middle panel of Fig. 55(a) was implemented in the microwave regime. The kernel operator used was

$$
K (u, v) = 0. 0 6 \left[ (4 - 4 i) J _ {0} (u v) + 3 e ^ {i \frac {2 \pi}{5} (u + v)} - (1 - 2 i) \right], \tag {342}
$$

where $J_0$ is the zeroth-order Bessel function of the first kind. Five samples are taken for the integration range $u \in [-2, 2]$ . The results of solving the equations for different inputs are shown in the right panel of Fig. 55(a), demonstrating strong agreement between experimental results and theoretical predictions.

(II) The system proposed by Andrea et al. in 2023 is shown in Fig. 55(b). The metagrating possesses N diffraction orders, corresponding to N discrete samples. The discretized kernel operator K is realized through the scattering matrix of a topology-optimized metasurface [specifically, $K = S_{R} M_{R'}$ , where the meanings of $S_{R}$ and $M_{R'}$ are illustrated in Fig. 55(b)]. Initially, incident light waves from different directions enter the system via a semi-transparent mirror, representing the input function $I_{\mathrm{in}}(u)$ . Upon the first reflection, the waves interact with the scattering matrix of the metagrating. The reflected light is then redirected by the semi-transparent mirror, completing one iteration. This process repeats as the reflected light re-enters the metagrating for subsequent iterations. In the steady state, the transmitted light in different directions corresponds to the discretized $g(u)$ , which represents the solution of the equation. The optimized metagrating structure is shown in Fig. 55(c), where a 15 nm Al film coated on the $SiO_{2}$ cladding layer functions as the semi-transparent mirror. Based on this configuration, the experimentally measured equation solutions exhibit good agreement with the ideal solutions.

# 7.2.3. Solving Complex Matrix Equations

In 2024, Yang et al. [682] proposed a complex matrix equation solver, as shown in the left panel of Fig. 55(d). The solver consists of a metasurface, N four-port couplers, and a 2N-port transmission network composed of N transmitting antennas and N receiving antennas. The complex matrix equation has the general form:

$$
\mathbf {K} \cdot \vec {x} + \vec {c} = \vec {0}, \tag {343}
$$

where $K \in C^{N \times N}$ , $\vec{c} \in C^{N \times 1}$ , and $\vec{x} \in C^{N \times 1}$ are the complex coefficient matrix, the constant vector, and the unknown vector, respectively. When the metasurface is designed such that the scattering matrix of the transmission network satisfies

$$
\mathbf {S} = \frac {1}{s _ {1 3} ^ {\mathrm{C}}} (\mathbf {K} + \mathbf {I}), \tag {344}
$$

and the input signal vector for the couplers is set as

$$
\vec {a} _ {\mathrm{in}} = [ a _ {\mathrm{in}, 1}, a _ {\mathrm{in}, 2}, \dots , a _ {\mathrm{in}, N} ] ^ {\top} = \frac {1}{s _ {1 2} ^ {\mathrm{C}}} \vec {c}, \tag {345}
$$

the solution to the equation can be obtained as

$$
\vec {x} = \frac {s _ {1 3} ^ {\mathrm{C}}}{s _ {4 3} ^ {\mathrm{C}}} (\vec {a} _ {\text {out}} - s _ {4 2} ^ {\mathrm{C}} \vec {a} _ {\text {in}}) + s _ {1 2} ^ {\mathrm{C}} \vec {a} _ {\text {in}}, \tag {346}
$$

where $\vec{a}_{out} = [a_{out,1}, a_{out,2}, \ldots, a_{out,N}]^{\top}$ represents the output signal vector of the couplers, and $s_{ij}^{C}$ denotes the $(i,j)$ element of the scattering matrix of the four-port coupler. Experimentally, this solver was implemented in the microwave regime. For N = 3, eight sets of $\vec{c}$ were randomly selected as inputs, yielding solutions for a total of 24 unknown variables. The right panel of Fig. 55(d) presents the results, where the relative error compared to the exact solutions is approximately 21%.

# 7.3. Optical Neural Networks

Recently, with the rapid advancement of deep learning, the application of metasurfaces in optical neural networks has garnered significant attention. Compared to traditional electronic neural networks, which are constrained by energy efficiency bottlenecks, metasurface-based neural networks leverage subwavelength-scale artificial nanostructures to enable wavefront modulation and optical field interconnections, facilitating ultrafast parallel computation and ultra-low-power information processing. However, current metasurface neural networks still face several challenges, including limited dynamic reconfigurability, high losses and fabrication difficulties associated with multilayer structures, and the lack of algorithm-hardware co-optimization strategies.

# 7.3.1. All-Optical Neural Networks Based on Metasurfaces

(I) All-optical diffractive neural networks. In 2018, Ozcan's group [683] proposed an all-optical diffractive neural network architecture as a novel physical platform for implementing machine learning. Since then, researchers have realized similar designs and functionalities on various types of metasurfaces [182,684–686].

The all-optical diffractive neural network proposed by Ozcan's group is shown in Fig. 56(a), consisting of an input layer, at least one diffractive layer, and an output layer. The diffractive layer, characterized by complex-valued transmission (or reflection) coefficients, modulates the complex amplitude of the incident optical field, thereby generating a tailored output field that propagates to subsequent layers. The wave propagation between layers follows the Rayleigh–Sommerfeld diffraction formula in Eq. (157). Based on this, the entire wave diffraction process from the input layer to the output layer can be mathematically modeled. Through deep learning, the diffractive neural network can be trained by iteratively optimizing the transmission (or reflection) coefficients of the diffractive layers using the backpropagation algorithm, where the gradient information is obtained via automatic differentiation techniques. Physically, the complex amplitude distribution of the trained diffractive layers can be implemented using metasurfaces, where each meta-atom corresponds to a neuron of the neural network. By constraining the amplitude distribution of the diffractive layers to a fixed value of 1 during training, a phase-only diffractive neural network design can be obtained. For example, in the handwritten digit recognition task shown in Fig. 56(a), digit patterns are fed into the diffractive neural network as amplitude distributions of the input optical field. The different regions of the output layer's intensity distribution correspond to different digit values, with the brightest region indicating the predicted digit. Experimental validation was performed in the terahertz regime using

Figure 56  
![](images/d4c357893e87679f657cc243483043ed8e73f7d2d38acb4395835126a3bfaa60.jpg)

Applications of metasurfaces in optical neural networks. (a) Left: schematic of the all-optical diffractive neural network architecture. Right: incident and output images during the handwritten digit recognition task. The red dashed boxes represent the detector regions corresponding to different digits. (b) Schematic of a programmable diffractive neural network. (c) Schematic of the structure of a diffractive neural network system operating in the visible. (d) Schematic of a 2D neural network for handwritten digit recognition using a topologically optimized metasurface. (e) Left: convolution operator based on a bilayer metasurface. A polarization-sensitive photodetector is achieved through the oriented grating on each detector. Right: numerically computed ideal convolution kernel (top) and the PSF of the fabricated bilayer metasurface (bottom). (a) Adapted with permission from Lin et al., Science 361, 1004–1008 (2018) [683]. Copyright © 2018 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (b) Reprinted with permission from Liu et al., Nat. Electron. 5, 113–122 (2022) [684]. Copyright © 2022 Springer Nature. (c) Reprinted from [685] under a Creative Commons license. (d) Adapted with permission from Khoram et al., Photonics Res. 7, 823–827 (2019) [687]. Copyright © 2019 Chinese Laser Press. (e) Adapted with permission from Zheng et al., Nat. Nanotechnol. 19, 471–478 (2024) [688]. Copyright © 2024 Springer Nature.

a 3D-printed diffractive neural network with five diffractive layers. Two classic machine learning tasks were successfully demonstrated: handwritten digit classification (91.75% simulated accuracy) and fashion product classification (86.33% simulated accuracy), with experimental results showing 88% and 90% agreement to the numerical tests, respectively.

In 2022, Cui's group [684] implemented a programmable diffractive neural network, as shown in Fig. 56(b), using digital-coding metasurfaces operating in the microwave regime. Each meta-atom was integrated with two amplifier chips, providing a dynamic modulation range from $-22$ dB to $13$ dB. The wave propagation through the multilayer metasurfaces in this system is governed by linear equations, resulting in an inherently linear neural network. Therefore, the network's ability to handle nonlinear functionalities is limited. In the same year, Luo et al. [685] employed the system shown in Fig. 56(c) to experimentally realize a diffractive neural network with a single-

layer metasurface diffractive layer in the visible. The propagation phase metasurface enabled full phase modulation for orthogonal linearly polarized light, allowing the system to simultaneously support two polarization-multiplexed neural networks to perform different classification tasks: four-category handwritten digit classification and four-category fashion product classification. Also in 2022, Zhao et al. [686] experimentally implemented a diffractive neural network for trigonometric operations using a single-layer metasurface operating in the microwave regime. In 2024, He et al. [182] experimentally demonstrated a diffractive neural network in the near-infrared regime, utilizing a bilayer metasurface.

Additionally, applications of metasurface-based diffractive neural networks in logical operations will be introduced in Subsection 7.4.

(II) All-optical scattering neural networks. In 2019, Khoram et al. [687] proposed another metasurface-based architecture, i.e., the scattering neural network, as illustrated in Fig. 56(b). Taking handwritten digit classification as an example, the input image is first converted into a vector and then encoded into the spatial intensity of the incident light on the left side. After undergoing complex scattering interactions with the metasurface, the light exits on the right side, where each region corresponds to a specific digit. The region with the maximum light intensity represents the predicted digit. In this setup, the scattering interactions between the wave and the metasurface are highly complex and require full-wave simulations for accurate modeling. The gradient information needed for backpropagation in neural network training is obtained using the adjoint method introduced in Subsection 3.3. Consequently, the training process of the neural network is equivalent to the inverse design of a topology-optimized metasurface. However, full-wave simulations are computationally expensive, making the training process highly time-consuming. As a result, only a 2D metasurface design was numerically demonstrated, as shown in Fig. 56(d).

# 7.3.2. Optical-Digital Hybrid Neural Networks Empowered by Metasurfaces

In 2022, Qu et al. [689] proposed a hybrid optical-digital framework for optical neural networks to bypass the need for cascading multiple metasurface layers. The intensity distribution of the output light from a single-layer metasurface neural network is encoded into a feature vector and then processed by a digital neural network with a fully connected layer in the computer to generate final predictions.

In 2024, Zheng et al. [688] proposed a hybrid neural network that leverages a bilayer metasurface to perform computationally expensive convolution operations on input images, as shown in the left panel of Fig. 56(e). The convolution results are subsequently processed by the digital neural network backend, forming a complete hybrid neural network. Since optical imaging inherently involves the convolution of the original image with a PSF, the key to implementing convolution operators lies in designing a bilayer metasurface that realizes the PSF corresponding to the convolution kernel. In this case, each focal spot in the PSF represents an element of the convolution kernel, with RCP and LCP focal spots denoting positive and negative element values, respectively. To realize a specific PSF, the bottom metasurface functions as a polarization-insensitive lens, while the top metasurface employs a hybrid phase modulation mechanism to generate polarization-multiplexed holographic images that encode the LCP and RCP components of the convolution kernel. The calculated and measured convolution kernels implemented by the bilayer metasurface are shown in the right panel of Fig. 56(e). Experimentally, the hybrid neural network achieved accuracies of $98.6\%$ for handwritten digit classification and $88.8\%$ for fashion product classification.

Logic operations based on metasurfaces. (a) Experimental setup of the diffractive neural network for performing basic logic operations. Bottom left: schematic of the encoding of logic operators and input logical states at the input layer. Bottom right: experimental result showing the light intensity distributions along the red dashed line on the output plane when the diffractive neural network performs different logic operations. (b) Left: schematic of a frequency and polarization multiplexed metasurface used for logic operations. Right: experimental SPP intensity distributions measured under incident light with different frequencies and polarizations. The inserted arrows indicate the circular polarization state of the incident light. The dashed rectangular region represents the nano-slit array. (a) Adapted from $[690]$ under a Creative Commons license. (b) Adapted with permission from Zhang et al., Nano Lett. 25, 1367–1372 (2025) $[691]$ . Copyright © 2025 American Chemical Society.

# 7.4. Logic Operations

Logic operations based on diffractive neural networks. Using the metasurface-based diffractive neural network introduced in Subsection 7.3, various logic operations can be realized by designing appropriate encoding strategies.

In 2020, Qian et al. [690] experimentally demonstrated three fundamental logic operations—NOT, OR, and AND—in the microwave regime using a bilayer metasurface-based diffractive neural network. The experimental setup is shown in the top panel of Fig. 57(a). Each region of the input layer was encoded to represent a specific logic operator or input logic state with two distinct transmittance states (0 or 1). In this

way, by defining a binary amplitude distribution, a complete logic operation input could be established. For example, the bottom left panel of Fig. 57(a) illustrates an OR operation performed on the inputs 0 and 1. The metasurface directed the diffracted wave toward one of two designated regions in the output layer, representing 0 and 1, respectively, as the logic operation result. As shown in the bottom right panel of Fig. 56(a), experimentally obtained logic operation results were in agreement with theoretical predictions. In 2022, Zhao et al. [692] utilized the same approach to implement a similar functionality using a single-layer metasurface-based diffractive neural network.

In 2021, Wang et al. [693] used OAM modes with topological charges of 1 and 2 to represent logic states 0 and 1, respectively. Numerical simulations verified that a bilayer metasurface-based diffractive neural network could independently implement AND, OR, and NOT gates. Furthermore, by cascading these logic gates, more complex logical operations could be realized.

Logic operations based on frequency and polarization multiplexed metasurfaces. In 2025, Zhang et al. [691] demonstrated the independent realization of all fundamental logic operations in the terahertz regime using a frequency and polarization multiplexed metasurface to selectively route SPPs. As shown in the left panel of Fig. 57(b), different combinations of circular polarization states ( $\sigma_{\pm}$ ) and frequencies ( $f_{1}/f_{2}$ ) of the incident light were encoded as distinct logic state inputs. The presence or absence of the SPP on either side of the metasurface represented logic state outputs 1 or 0, respectively. The spin-dependent SPP interference effect was induced by geometric phase modulation, while the frequency-dependent SPP interference effect was governed by propagation phase differences at different frequencies. In this way, various logic operations could be realized by designing appropriate metasurfaces. For example, in the structure shown in the left panel of Fig. 57(b), the experimentally observed SPP intensity distributions under different illumination conditions (varying frequency and spin states) are displayed in the right panel of Fig. 57(b). According to the definitions of input and output logic states, the metasurface implemented an XOR gate on the left side and an XNOR gate on the right side.

# 8. ACTIVE METASURFACES

Active metasurfaces, as an innovative advancement over traditional passive counterparts, achieve dynamic control over the amplitude, phase, polarization, and even frequency of electromagnetic waves by incorporating dynamically tunable materials or architectures $[5,315,694–699]$ . In recent years, driven by the rapid development of tunable materials (e.g., 2D materials, liquid crystals, phase-change materials, and transparent conductive oxides) and external excitation techniques (including electrical, optical, thermal, and mechanical actuation), active metasurfaces have transitioned from fundamental theories to functional applications. In terms of applications, active metasurfaces can be categorized into four categories: transmittance/reflectance and phase modulation, switchable or continuously tunable optical components (such as gratings for beam steering, lenses, holograms, and nanoprinting), SLMs consisting of multiple independently addressable metasurface elements, and temporal modulation. While research on the first two categories has achieved relative maturity, investigations into the latter two still face challenges spanning theoretical frameworks, device design, and fabrication. This section provides a systematical review of these metasurfaces based on their dynamic modulation mechanisms, discussing design principles, tuning strategies, and representative cutting-edge applications.

# 8.1. Active Metasurfaces Based on 2D Materials

2D materials are layered crystalline materials with atomic-scale thickness, whose quantum confinement effects and surface-dominated optical properties have sparked extensive research in photonics. Typical 2D material systems include graphene, transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDCs), and hexagonal boron nitride (hBN). Among them, graphene and TMDCs are the two primary 2D materials used for realizing active metasurfaces.

Graphene, a monolayer of hexagonally arranged carbon atoms, achieves optical modulation through electrical gating by tuning the carrier density via Fermi-level control, enabling broadband absorption modulation and plasmon resonance tuning. In contrast, TMDCs are a class of layered semiconductor materials with the chemical formula $MX_{2}$ , where M represents transition metals such as Mo or W, and X denotes chalcogen elements such as S or Se. Their monolayer structure consists of a transition metal atomic layer sandwiched between two chalcogen atomic layers, forming a hexagonal lattice through strong covalent bonding. The optical property of TMDCs can be dynamically controlled through exciton-trion transitions induced by doping (via photochemical or electrical gating), wherein injected carriers convert neutral excitons into charged trions, shifting excitonic resonances and modifying photoluminescence efficiency.

Metasurfaces integrated with 2D materials. (I) Metasurfaces integrated with a graphene layer. In 2013, Capasso's group $[700]$ proposed an active metasurface leveraging the gate-voltage-dependent optical properties of graphene, as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 58(a). By introducing a graphene layer beneath the Au meta-atom array, they achieved electro-optical modulation over the metasurface reflectance spectrum, as shown in the right panel of Fig. 58(a), with a modulation speed of approximately 40 MHz. In this design, graphene facilitates dynamic modulation of the metasurface. Conversely, the presence of the metasurface significantly enhances the modulation depth of monolayer graphene $[709]$ .

Notably, resonant modes played a crucial role in extending the modulation range of this metasurface design $[710,711]$ . In 2014, based on the same architecture, Emani et al. $[710]$ demonstrated electrically tunable Fano resonances through careful design of the meta-atoms. More recently, in 2024, Han et al. $[712]$ proposed introducing a dielectric barrier layer between the metallic meta-atom array and graphene to reduce electrical coupling, which helps to enhance the modulation range.

In 2014, Capasso's group extended the aforementioned architecture by incorporating a MIM metasurface design, enabling an electrically tunable perfect absorber operating in the 5 $\mu$ m–7 $\mu$ m wavelength range. By switching the metasurface in and out of the critical coupling condition via gate voltage, they achieved a modulation depth of up to 100% with a modulation speed of 20 GHz. In 2017, Sherrott et al. [713] demonstrated a similar active MIM metasurface, achieving a phase modulation range of 237° at an operating wavelength of 8.5 $\mu$ m.

In 2015, Miao et al. [701] proposed a slightly modified architecture, as illustrated in the top left panel of Fig. 58(b). The metasurface consisted of an array of Al meta-atoms and an Al film separated by an SU8 spacer layer. A monolayer of graphene was transferred onto the top of the metasurface and covered with a gel-like ion liquid. The electrically tunable full-phase modulation in the terahertz regime was realized, along with complex amplitude modulation shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 58(b). Furthermore, by the deliberate design of the metasurface structure, dynamic control over the polarization state of incident light could also be achieved, as demonstrated in the top right panel of Fig. 58(b).

Figure 58  
![](images/240a93eebf9e4554454085815b31333f0a729556c08d5835d6add0480dedbd0b.jpg)

Right: measured reflectance spectra under different gate voltages ( $V_{G}$ ) and corresponding charge carrier concentrations. $V_{CNP}$ denotes the gate voltage at the charge neutral point. (b) Top left: schematic of an active metasurface with monolayer graphene atop the meta-atom array. Top right: polarization states of reflected light for linearly polarized incident light (the polarization angle is $32^{\circ}$ relative to the x-axis) at 0.63 THz, characterized by elliptical axis ratio (S/L, red curve) and rotation angle (blue curve) under varying gate voltages. Bottom: measured reflectance and reflection phase shift as functions of gate voltage and frequency. (c) Left: schematic of graphene plasmonic ribbons coupled to a subwavelength metallic slit array. Right: measured modulation efficiency $\eta_{T}$ comparison at $\lambda^{-1}=1397~cm^{-1}$ as a function of Fermi level $E_{F}$ and corresponding gate voltage $V_{g}$ for the graphene plasmonic ribbons—extraordinary optical transmission (GPRs–EOT) device (left panel) versus bare GPRs. Here, $\eta_{T}=1-T/T_{max}$ , where T is the transmittance and $T_{max}$ corresponds to the maximum transmittance at the resonance frequency. (d) Schematic of a metasurface unit cell comprising a pair of independently controlled graphene-Au meta-atoms. (f) Schematic of a geometric phase graphene metasurface. Inset: detailed view of a graphene meta-atom. (g) Left: schematic of a $WS_{2}$ zone plate lens integrated into an electrochemical cell. Middle: focusing efficiency spectra in pristine (red, $V_{g}=0~V$ ), gated (blue, $V_{g}=3~V$ ), and restored states (gray, $V_{g}=0~V$ ). Shaded regions represent one standard deviation error bound. Right: xOz-plane scan of the focused beam at 620 nm wavelength, with normalized intensity cross-sections along the z-axis ( $x=0~\mum$ ) and x-axis ( $z=1993~\mum$ ) in arbitrary units (a.u.). (h) Top: schematic of conductivity profile $\sigma(x)$ modulation in monolayer graphene via periodic biasing, where $2\pi\gamma$ defines the (continued)

(continued) modulation periodicity. Bottom: simulated transmittance spectra at normal incidence under voltage profiles with varying modulation strengths $w_{0}$ . (i) Left: schematic of an exciton-based $MoSe_{2}$ metasurface for dynamic beam steering. Right: measured spectral Fourier plane images at the operating wavelength of 757 nm, demonstrating dynamic beam deflection at $\pm30^{\circ}$ under distinct electrode voltage configurations. (a) Adapted with permission from Yao et al., Nano Lett. 13, 1257–1264 (2013) [700]. Copyright © 2013 American Chemical Society. (b) Adapted from [701] under a Creative Commons license. (c) Adapted from [702] under a Creative Commons license. (d) Adapted with permission from Han et al., ACS Nano 14, 1166–1175 (2020) [703]. Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society. (e) Adapted with permission from Lee et al., Nano Lett. 17, 4541–4547 (2017) [704]. Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society. (f) Adapted with permission from Cheng et al., Adv. Opt. Copyright © 2020 Springer Nature. (h) Adapted with permission from Huidobro et al., ACS Nano 10, 5499–5506 (2016) [707]. Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society. (i) Adapted with permission from Li et al., Nano Lett. 23, 2771–2777 (2023) [708]. Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society.

(II) Metasurfaces with graphene plasmonic ribbons. In 2016, Kim et al. [702] demonstrated the dynamic control of extraordinary optical transmission (EOT) in a subwavelength metallic slit array using graphene plasmonic ribbons. As illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 58(c), TM-polarized incident light induces SPPs on the top metallic surface. These SPPs propagate through the metallic slits, exciting SPPs on the bottom metallic surface. The SPPs on the bottom surface are diffracted by the periodic structure and radiate into free space with enhanced intensity, forming EOT. When graphene plasmonic ribbons are introduced, and the gate voltage is adjusted such that their plasmonic resonance frequency matches the EOT frequency, the coupling between the SPPs on the top and bottom metallic surfaces is suppressed, thereby inhibiting the EOT. The resulting modulation of the metasurface transmittance is shown in the right panel of Fig. 58(c).

In 2020, Han et al. [703] proposed a metasurface unit composed of two Au meta-atoms, as illustrated in Fig. 58(d), where each meta-atom's response can be independently tuned using graphene ribbons controlled by different gate voltages. Simulations for this design verified a $2\pi$ phase modulation, perfect absorption, and other large dynamic-range modulations.

In 2023, Park et al. [714] realized an electrically tunable wave retarder operating in the terahertz regime by integrating the Au metasurface with graphene ribbons.

(III) Metasurfaces integrated with monolayer TMDCs. The monolayer graphene in the aforementioned active metasurface architectures can be replaced with monolayer TMDCs, as both exhibit electrically tunable optical properties $[704,715,716]$ . In 2017, Lee et al. $[704]$ implemented a similar architecture to the one shown in Fig. 58(a) but replaced graphene with monolayer $MoS_{2}$ , achieving the reflectance spectrum modulation illustrated in Fig. 58(e). In 2018, Zhang et al. $[715]$ realized optoelectronic and photochemical dynamic tuning of photoluminescence intensity and transmittance spectrum using a SiN metasurface (consisting of a periodic square array of air holes) covered with monolayer $MoS_{2}$ .

Metasurfaces with 2D-material-based meta-atoms. An emerging direction in metasurface development is their integration with 2D and layered 2D materials $[596]$ . Metasurfaces incorporating 2D-material-based meta-atoms not only enable ultrathin, com-

pact devices but also harness the unique optoelectronic properties of 2D materials. However, the inherently weak light–matter interactions present challenges for efficient optical manipulation, necessitating innovative strategies to enhance photonic behavior and interaction efficiency.

In 2015 [705] and 2018 [717], Tian's group and Chen's group collaboratively proposed graphene-based metasurfaces utilizing geometric phase, designed for operation in the terahertz and mid-infrared regimes, respectively, as illustrated in Fig. 58(f). Their simulations verified functionalities such as electrically tunable beam deflection with high efficiency.

In 2020, Brongersma's group [706] proposed an electrically tunable, atomically thin zone plate lens based on monolayer $\mathrm{WS}_2$ , as shown in the left panel of Fig. 58(g). The focusing performance of the lens without an applied bias voltage is depicted in the right panel of Fig. 58(g). As illustrated in the middle panel of Fig. 58(g), the focusing efficiency can be dynamically modulated via time-dependent ionic-liquid gate voltages, which suppress exciton resonance effects. The rise and fall times of the focal intensity at the operating wavelength of $625\mathrm{nm}$ were measured to be 39(3) ms and 16(1) ms, respectively.

2D materials modulated by a micron- or submicron-scale gate voltage profile. In 2016, as illustrated in the top panel of Fig. 58(h), Huidobro et al. [707] achieved periodic modulation of the electrical conductivity of monolayer graphene through a periodic bias. The modulated graphene acts as a grating that provides the necessary momentum mismatch to couple free-space radiation into the SPPs supported by the graphene layer. In the subwavelength regime, the modulated graphene layer behaves as a homogeneous 1D layer with an effective electrical conductivity for the incident radiation. As shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 58(h), simulations demonstrated that the transmittance spectrum of graphene could be tuned by varying the voltage profile of different modulation strengths.

In 2023, Li et al. [708] demonstrated near-infrared dynamic beam steering using the gate voltage profile modulation of monolayer $MoSe_{2}$ . As illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 58(i), monolayer $MoSe_{2}$ encapsulated in hBN was subjected to periodic modulation by a subwavelength-spaced Au electrode array, forming an excitonic metasurface for wavefront control of the reflected light. Experimentally, the electrodes were patterned to allow setting a periodic three level voltage gradient. The resulting beam steering result under different voltage gradients is shown in the right panel of Fig. 58(i).

# 8.2. Active Metasurfaces Based on Transparent Conducting Oxides

Transparent conductive oxides (TCOs) are a class of functional materials that combine high optical transparency with electrical conductivity. Their wide bandgap (>3.1 eV) allows for high transmittance, typically exceeding 80% in the visible spectrum. The carrier concentration of TCOs can be dynamically tuned via electric field effects, such as carrier injection or depletion, leading to modifications in their complex permittivity. Indium tin oxide (ITO) is a prototypical TCO, offering a balanced combination of low resistivity ( $\sim1 \times 10^{-4} \Omega \cdot cm$ ) and high transmittance (>85% at 550 nm), making it widely utilized in active metasurface applications.

Dynamic transmittance/reflectance and phase shift modulation via TCOs-integrated metasurfaces. (I) In 2016, Atwater's group [718] proposed an active MIM metasurface with an $\mathrm{Al_2O_3 / ITO}$ dielectric layer, as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 59(a). The metasurface consists of a quartz substrate, an Au back reflector, a thin ITO film, and a thin $\mathrm{Al_2O_3}$ film on which a Au stripe nanoantenna array is patterned. By applying a bias voltage between combinations of adjacent antenna electrodes and

Middle: measured reflectance spectrum of the metasurface under different applied voltages. Right: measured and simulated phase shift at a wavelength of 1573 nm as a function of applied bias. Nanotechnol. 16, 69–76 (2021) [723]. Copyright © 2021 Springer Nature.

the bottom Au layer, charge accumulation occurs at the $Al_{2}O_{3}/ITO$ interface, enabling efficient electrical modulation of ITO's refractive index and, consequently, the optical response of the metasurface. As shown in the middle and right panels of Fig. 59(a), applying a 2.5 V gate bias resulted in a $\sim30\%$ change in reflectance and a $180^{\circ}$ phase shift. The experimental modulation speed exceeded 10 MHz (with a potential speed of up to 11 GHz). Additionally, a two-level phase grating with a periodicity of 2.4 $\mu$ m operating at a wavelength of 1550 nm was also demonstrated. In 2017, Park et al. [724] experimentally demonstrated $180^{\circ}$ reflection phase tuning based on a similar architecture. In 2019, Zhao et al. [725] employed a similar design but replaced ITO with indium silicon oxide, achieving a maximum relative reflectance change of 57% at a wavelength of 2.73 $\mu$ m. In 2022, Kim et al. [45] demonstrated phase modulation exceeding $137^{\circ}$ in the infrared regime using a similar configuration. In 2023, Atwater's group [726] experimentally verified the thermal robustness of the ITO-integrated MIM metasurface under both continuous-wave and pulsed high-power laser illumination conditions, demonstrating its potential for high-power optical applications.

(II) In 2017, Atwater's group [719] proposed another active reflective metasurface design integrated with ITO, as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 59(b). The metasurface consists of nanostructured Ag and ITO electrodes separated by a 5 nm-thick $Al_{2}O_{3}$ layer. The transport of silver ions through the $Al_{2}O_{3}$ layer leads to bias-induced nucleation and growth of Ag meta-atoms in the ITO counterelectrode, thereby altering the optical extinction response. As shown in the right panel of Fig. 59(b), the fabricated metasurface exhibited a relative reflectance change of up to 30% in the visible spectral range under an applied bias of only 5 mV.  
(III) In 2018, Forouzmand et al. [727] proposed, through numerical simulations, the use of multigate biasing to further enhance the modulation depth of the ITO-integrated metasurfaces. In the same year, Atwater's group [720] experimentally demonstrated a dual-gated active metasurface operating at a wavelength of $1550~\mathrm{nm}$ . As illustrated in the top panel of Fig. 59(c), a voltage bias $V_{\mathrm{a}}$ is applied between the ITO layer and the meta-atoms, while another voltage bias $V_{\mathrm{b}}$ is applied between the Al back reflector and the ITO layer. Under applied bias, charge accumulation or depletion occurs at both the

top and bottom ITO/gate-dielectric interfaces, leading to a significant variation in the complex refractive index of the ITO layer through carrier density modulation at both interfaces. As shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 59(c), the metasurface demonstrated a continuous phase shift ranging from $0^{\circ}$ to $303^{\circ}$ and a relative reflectance modulation of 89% under an applied voltage bias of 6.5 V.

(IV) Active transmissive metasurfaces integrated with ITO. In 2018, Howes et al. [721] proposed an active Si metasurface integrated with ITO, as illustrated in the top panel of Fig. 59(d). As shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 59(d), the metasurface exhibited a transmittance modulation between 0.71 and 0.42 at a wavelength of $\sim$ 1470 nm. In 2019, Forouzmand et al. [728] proposed an active Si metasurface based on a structure similar to the one shown in Fig. 59(a) (with Au replaced by Si). Numerical simulations verified a phase shift tunability of 240° for the reflected TE-polarized light at a wavelength of 1696 nm and a phase shift tunability of 270° for the transmitted TM-polarized light at 1563 nm.

SLM based on TCOs-integrated metasurfaces. In 2020, Atwater's group [722] experimentally demonstrated a 1D SLM based on the active metasurface unit shown in the top left panel of Fig. 59(e). The SLM had a pitch size of $400\mathrm{nm}$ and consisted of 96 independently addressable metasurface elements. As illustrated in the top right panel of Fig. 59(e), the operating wavelength was chosen to be $1522\mathrm{nm}$ to achieve a phase shift exceeding $270^{\circ}$ with moderate reflectance variation. By electro-optically modulating the phase of the scattered light from each identical metasurface element in the SLM, dynamic beam steering [as shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 59(e)] and reconfigurable light focusing—with varying focal lengths of $1.5\mu \mathrm{m}$ , $2\mu \mathrm{m}$ , and $3\mu \mathrm{m}$ —were demonstrated. In 2024, Atwater's group further applied a similar active MIM metasurface design with two independent electrodes to demonstrate spatiotemporal control over scattered light frequency and wavefront [326], as discussed in Subsection 2.2.7.

In 2021, Park et al. [723] experimentally realized a 1D SLM based on the dual-gate active metasurface shown in Fig. 59(c), as illustrated in the top left panel of Fig. 59(f). The SLM had a pitch size of 5 $\mu$ m and comprised 50 independently addressable metasurface elements. Each metasurface element consisted of 11 Au ridges, whose phase response was controlled by tuning the top and bottom gate voltages, $V_{a}$ and $V_{b}$ , respectively. This configuration enabled a $2\pi$ phase shift modulation at an operating wavelength of 1.34 $\mu$ m, with an estimated modulation speed of 5.4 MHz. The dynamic beam steering demonstration of the fabricated SLM is presented in the top right and bottom panels of Fig. 59(f).

# 8.3. Active Metasurfaces Based on Liquid Crystals

Liquid crystals, a distinct state of matter combining fluidity with partial molecular ordering, bridge the properties of isotropic liquids and crystalline solids. Their optical characteristics are intrinsically linked to self-organized molecular arrangements, which define four primary mesophases: nematic, cholesteric, smectic, and columnar [729]. In the uniaxial nematic phase, the molecules—collectively described by a macroscopic orientation vector termed the director—align parallel to one another, inducing pronounced optical birefringence ( $\Delta n = n_{\mathrm{e}} - n_{\mathrm{o}}$ , where $n_{\mathrm{e}}$ and $n_{\mathrm{o}}$ are the extraordinary and ordinary refractive indices, respectively). For visible wavelengths, $\Delta n$ typically spans 0.2–0.5 in conventional nematic liquid crystals. The orientation of the liquid crystal director can be controlled by external electric fields, enabling dynamic tuning of the birefringence ( $\Delta n$ ) along a specific optical axis. This mechanism facilitates electro-optic modulation through phase retardation and enables arbitrary polarization state control via tailored optical anisotropy.

Electrically tunable metasurfaces through refractive index modulation of liquid crystal. (I) Metasurfaces with meta-atoms encapsulated in liquid crystal. In 2019, Zou et al. [730] experimentally demonstrated a metasurface composed of Si nanocylinders integrated into a nematic liquid crystal cell with a thickness of approximately 5 $\mu$ m. As illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 60(a), when a bias voltage is applied, the alignment of liquid crystal molecules transitions from in-plane to out-of-plane, significantly altering the refractive index for incident light polarized along the x-direction. This enables the electrical modulation of the metasurface's transmittance spectrum, achieving an on/off transmission switching at the operating wavelength of 669 nm.

In 2021, Bosch et al. [731] demonstrated a tunable-focus Si metalens based on a similar architecture. The phase shifts of meta-atoms are modulated by applying a bias voltage to the liquid crystal cell, thereby adjusting the focal length of the metalens. By constructing a meta-atom database featuring diverse geometric shapes, complete phase control across M operating voltages could be achieved. Experimentally, this enabled the realization of an electrically tunable bifocal metalens (M = 2), as shown in Fig. 60(b).

Beyond the aforementioned forward design approach, inverse design methods can further enhance the efficiency of these active metasurfaces [741].

(II) Metasurfaces consisting of paired meta-atoms covered with liquid crystal and poly(methyl methacrylate) (PMMA), respectively. In 2020, Liu's group [732] proposed an active metasurface based on liquid crystal, as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 60(c). The metasurface unit consisted of a pair of orthogonally arranged rectangular meta-atoms, each covered with a different dielectric medium [PMMA and liquid crystal]. The phase difference between adjacent meta-atoms was the sum of the geometric phase difference ( $\pi$ ) and the propagation phase difference caused by the refractive index contrast of the covering media. The propagation phase difference could be dynamically tuned by applying a voltage to adjust the refractive index of the liquid crystal. When the refractive index of the liquid crystal was tuned to match that of PMMA, the metasurface unit imposed a phase gradient exceeding $k = 2\pi/\lambda$ (where the lattice constant $a < \lambda/2$ ), converting the incident light into an evanescent wave that could not influence the far-field radiation. Furthermore, by rotating the liquid-crystal-covered meta-atoms to impose a geometric phase distribution corresponding to a specific holographic image (while maintaining orthogonality between PMMA-covered meta-atoms and their paired counterparts), a switchable holographic display was achieved. As shown in the middle panel of Fig. 60(c), independent electrode control enabled the on/off switching of seven metasurface holograms, where each metasurface hologram generated a single stripe of the digital pattern (as illustrated in the inset of Fig. 60(c), middle panel). Experimentally, a dynamic numeric indicator display was demonstrated, as shown in the right panel of Fig. 60(c).

In 2021, Liu's group [733] applied a similar architecture to achieve electrically tunable polarization conversion. As shown in Fig. 60(d), the liquid-crystal-covered meta-atoms were rotated clockwise to impose a geometric phase gradient of $\partial \phi / \partial x = k \sin \theta$ , while the adjacent PMMA-covered meta-atoms were rotated counterclockwise to apply an opposite gradient of $-\partial \phi / \partial x = -k \sin \theta$ . As a result, the LCP and RCP components of the reflected light for linearly polarized incidence recombined at $\pm \theta$ directions to restore linear polarization. By controlling the refractive index of the liquid crystal via an applied voltage, the propagation phase difference of the LCP and RCP components along $\pm \theta$ directions could be modulated, thereby enabling dynamic control over the polarization angle of the synthesized linearly polarized light.

Figure 60  
![](images/78539ab99e25baace5fa4e6f2adac8e0cc235a4a9997b92790392e11ad505d58.jpg)
