Right: holographic images at AC voltages of 0.8 V (top) and 1 V (bottom). (j) Measured reflectance spectra for red, green, and blue metasurfaces with respect to the linear polarization axis from 0° to 90°. (k) Reflectance spectra of the liquid crystal cell for target wavelengths as a function of the bias voltage. (a) Adapted with permission from Zou et al., ACS Photonics 6, 1533–1540 (2019) [730]. Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society. (b) Adapted from Bosch et al., Nano Lett. 21, 3849–3856 (2021) [731]. Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society. (c) Adapted from [732] under a Creative Commons license. (d) Adapted with permission from Yu et al., Nano Lett. 21, 6690–6695 (2021) [733]. Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society. (e) Adapted with permission from Li et al., Science 364, 1087–1090 (2019) [734]. Copyright © 2019 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (f) Adapted from [735] under a Creative Commons license. (g) Adapted with permission from Komar et al., ACS Photonics 5, 1742–1748 (2018) [736]. Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society. (h) Adapted from [737] under a Creative Commons license. (i) Adapted with permission from Kim et al., Adv. Mater. 32, 2004664 (2020) [738]. Copyright © 2020 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (j) Reprinted from [739] under a Creative Commons license. (k) Reprinted from [740] under a Creative Commons license.

(III) Phase-only 1D SLMs based on liquid-crystal-integrated metasurfaces. In 2019, Li et al. [734] experimentally realized a three-phase-level transmissive SLM using a $TiO_{2}$ metasurface coated with a liquid crystal layer, as illustrated in the inset of the top panel of Fig. 60(e). As shown in the top panel of Fig. 60(e), within the wavelength range of 660 nm–670 nm, the meta-atom designed based on the Huygens phase principle (see Subsection 2.1.2) exhibited a uniform phase modulation of $2\pi/3$ at liquid crystal director orientations of $0^{\circ}$ , $45^{\circ}$ , and $90^{\circ}$ (controlled by an applied bias voltage), while maintaining a transmittance of approximately 60–90%. This satisfied the requirements for achieving a three-phase-level phase-only SLM. On this basis, a 1D SLM with a pitch size of $1.08 \mu m$ (where each pitch contained three meta-atoms) was fabricated, consisting of 28 individually addressable electrodes. By selecting different addressing schemes, the SLM could function as a grating with a tunable deflection angle,

as demonstrated in the bottom panel of Fig. 60(e). In 2023, Moitra et al. [742] utilized this architecture to develop a 1D reflective SLM with a small pitch size ( $\sim$ 1 $\mu$ m), operating at a wavelength of 650 nm.

In 2022, Mansha et al. [735] experimentally realized a 1D reflective SLM with 96 independently addressable pixels ( $\sim$ 1 $\mu$ m pitch). As illustrated in the top panel of Fig. 60(f), the metasurface unit comprised two partially reflecting mirrors functioning as top and bottom distributed Bragg reflectors, a top ITO layer serving as a common electrode, a liquid crystal layer, and a set of Al electrodes forming the SLM pixels. The Fabry–Pérot nanocavity design supported multiple resonances across the visible spectrum. As shown in the bottom left panel of Fig. 60(f), under bias voltage control, the unit exhibited 2 $\pi$ phase modulation at different resonance wavelengths simultaneously. Based on this, the SLM enabled multispectral programmable beam steering with an FOV of approximately 18° and absolute efficiencies exceeding 40%, as illustrated in the bottom middle panel of Fig. 60(f). Additionally, a multispectral metalens with tunable focal length and efficiencies of $\sim$ 27% was demonstrated, as shown in the bottom right panel of Fig. 60(f).

Thermally reconfigurable metasurfaces via liquid crystal phase transition. When liquid crystals are heated beyond their clearing point, they undergo a phase transition from a mesophase (such as the nematic phase) to an isotropic liquid phase, resulting in the complete disappearance of optical anisotropy. In 2018, Komar et al. $[736]$ built a liquid crystal cell with a thickness of 5 $\mu$ m on a propagation phase Si metagrating. By heating the liquid crystal cell, they achieved the switching of a laser beam from a 12° to a 0° angle with an efficiency of 50%, as illustrated in Fig. 60(g). In the same year, using the same architecture, Bohn et al. $[743]$ demonstrated a thermally induced resonance shift in the transmittance spectrum of a Si nanodisk metasurface. This effect was utilized to tune the spontaneous emission spectrum of a fluorescent glass by modifying the local photonic density of states through temperature-controlled resonance tuning.

Polarization-multiplexed metasurfaces embedded with liquid crystals. Liquid crystals allow for facile and repeatable manipulation of the polarization state of light. By integrating liquid crystals with polarization-multiplexed metasurfaces, the polarization state of the incident light can be dynamically controlled, and the metasurface performs different functions depending on the incident polarization. This enables electrically tunable active metasurfaces for applications such as dynamic holography $[738,744–747]$ , tunable nanoprinting $[737,739,745,748]$ , focus-tunable lenses $[744,749,750]$ , and switchable enhancement of nonlinear effects $[751]$ . The design methods for polarization-multiplexed metasurfaces have been detailed in Subsections 2.1.5 and 5.2.1.

In 2017, Franklin et al. [737] demonstrated a single-pixel full-color display based on liquid-crystal-controlled plasmonic resonance, as shown in the top panel of Fig. 60(h). The nanostructured Al surface exhibited a surface roughness with grain diameters of approximately 30 nm, inducing polarization-dependent grating-coupled surface plasmons. Under zero bias, the device displayed two orthogonal color states determined by the polarization of incident light relative to the liquid crystal director—blue for parallel alignment and red for perpendicular orientation. By applying a low voltage, the orientation of the liquid crystal across the cell could be controlled, leading to a superposition of the surface's two orthogonal color states. At higher voltages, the bulk liquid crystal became vertically saturated, while the liquid crystal near the surface began to reorient, increasing the effective refractive index experienced by the resonant mode. This dual-phase modulation mechanism enabled full RGB color generation through voltage-dependent resonance tuning, as illustrated in Fig. 60(h).

In 2020, Rho's group [738] utilized a liquid-crystal-integrated metasurface to realize a stimuli-responsive dynamic holographic image, as shown in the left panel of Fig. 60(i). The Si metasurface, designed using the method (I) in Subsection 5.2.1, enabled holographic display with LCP/RCP multiplexing. A designer liquid crystal layer with a thickness of $10\mu \mathrm{m}$ was integrated into the metasurface hologram to control the incident polarization state (LCP or RCP) via applied bias voltage, as illustrated in the middle panel of Fig. 60(i). When an alternating current (AC) voltage of $0.8\mathrm{V}$ (for LCP) or $1\mathrm{V}$ (for RCP) was applied, the generated holographic images are shown in the right panel of Fig. 60(i). In addition, the heat- and touch-responsive dynamic holographic displays were also demonstrated based on the similar principle. Utilizing the same approach, in 2021, Rho's group [749] further demonstrated an electrically tunable bifocal metalens. In 2022, Ou et al. [750] extended this approach by utilizing the method (I) for the multi-wavelength achromatic metalens design introduced in Subsection 4.4.4, achieving an electrically tunable bifocal metalens that eliminated chromatic aberration at three wavelengths of $450\mathrm{nm}$ , $532\mathrm{nm}$ , and $635\mathrm{nm}$ . In 2024, Wang et al. [746] introduced a pixelated liquid crystal layer to create specific LC director distributions, enabling versatile and tunable vectorial holography. This approach allowed independent and arbitrary modulation of both polarization and amplitude at different spatial positions.

By leveraging the polarization-sensitive transmittance/reflectance spectra of anisotropic metasurfaces, switchable $[748]$ or continuously tunable $[739]$ structural colors can be achieved. Based on a similar architecture to the one shown in Fig. 60(i), in 2022, Badloe et al. $[739]$ utilized the polarization-sensitive characteristics of Mie resonances to realize an electrically tunable metasurface capable of displaying saturated red, green, and blue pixels. These pixels could be continuously controlled between on and off states, as illustrated in Fig. 60(j).

In addition to functioning as a polarization modulator, the liquid crystal layer can also act as a wavelength modulator, selectively exhibiting narrow-bandwidth Bragg reflection and allowing the reflection wavelength to be controlled by an applied bias voltage. In 2024, Kim et al. [740] combined a wavelength-multiplexed metasurface with a micro-thick liquid crystal layer sandwiched between two glass substrates to create a stimuli-responsive wavelength modulator for dynamic holographic displays. The voltage-controlled wavelength modulation is depicted in Fig. 60(k).

# 8.4. Active Metasurfaces Based on Phase Change Materials

Active metasurfaces based on vanadium dioxide. Vanadium dioxide ( $VO_{2}$ ) is a transition metal oxide with a unique crystalline structure that undergoes significant structural changes under different temperatures, leading to abrupt variations in its physical properties $[752,753]$ . The most notable characteristic of $VO_{2}$ is its reversible metal-insulator transition. When the temperature rises above the critical point of approximately $68^{\circ}$ , $VO_{2}$ transitions from an insulating monoclinic phase to a metallic tetragonal phase; when the temperature decreases, it reverts to the insulating phase. This phase transition can be triggered by Joule heating through an electrical tuning mechanism $[754–758]$ , which is crucial for enabling complex functionalities such as phased arrays. During this transformation, the optical properties of $VO_{2}$ change dramatically. Alternatively, the transition can also be induced by laser heating $[758]$ . For instance, in the infrared spectrum, $VO_{2}$ in its insulating phase allows infrared light to pass through, whereas in its metallic phase, it strongly reflects infrared light. Leveraging this property, depositing a $VO_{2}$ layer onto an MIM metasurface and using electrodes near the metasurface to control Joule heating enables dynamic switching.

Figure 61  
![](images/15ee30a42cae76b5880a1b242b6cf7ba07275767045c0f4c6d7feb0240be1923.jpg)

Active metasurfaces based on vanadium dioxide. (a) Schematic of an electrically tunable metasurface unit designed for beam steering. (b) Schematic of an addressable electrically tunable metasurface unit. (c) Left: schematic of an MIM metasurface unit incorporating $VO_{2}$ . Right: continuously tunable phase shift of the metasurface as a function of the applied voltage. (d) First column: schematic of an electrically driven active metasurface. The Au meta-atom array, which also serves as a heater, is positioned on top of a $VO_{2}$ thin film deposited on a $TiO_{2}$ substrate. Second column: transmittance spectra of the metasurface under different bias currents. Third column: output versus input optical power curves of the metasurface under different bias currents, demonstrating its application in optical limiting. Fourth column: transmittance spectra of the metasurface under forward and backward incidence when operating as a nonreciprocal optical isolator at a bias current of 80 mA. (a) Reprinted from [754] under a Creative Commons license. (b) Reprinted from [757] under a Creative Commons license. (c) Reprinted with permission from Kim et al., Nano Lett. 19, 3961–3968 (2019) [755]. Copyright © 2019 American Chemical Society. (d) Adapted with permission from Kang et al., Nat. Photonics 18, 74–80 (2024) [758]. Copyright © 2024 Springer Nature.

between two states: on ( $VO_{2}$ in the insulating phase, high transmission, allowing the metasurface to function) and off ( $VO_{2}$ in the metallic phase, high reflection, effectively turning the device into a mirror) [756].

In 2016, Hashemi et al. [754] experimentally demonstrated beam steering based on an Au metasurface, as illustrated in Fig. 61(a). By utilizing the temperature gradient generated by the current distribution in the Au layer, the metasurface induced a phase gradient, achieving a beam deflection tuning range of up to $\pm22^{\circ}$ at an operating frequency of 100 GHz. In 2022, Chen et al. [757] experimentally demonstrated beam steering at an operating frequency of 0.425 THz using an addressable metasurface, as shown in Fig. 61(b), achieving beam deflection over a $42.8^{\circ}$ angular range. In 2019, Kim et al. [755] realized an active MIM metasurface, as illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 61(c), where the temperature was modulated by controlling the current flowing through Au stripes. Experimentally, they demonstrated electrically tunable phase modulation of the reflected light over a bandwidth ranging from 1515 nm to 1575 nm, with the phase shift at 1550 nm shown in the right panel of Fig. 61(c). The maximum phase tuning range reached $250^{\circ}$ at 1520 nm. Additionally, at an operating wavelength of 1680 nm, the metasurface exhibited a maximum reflectance modulation range of 23.5%. In 2024, King et al. [758] demonstrated mid-infrared optical switching, limiting, and nonreciprocal isolation using an active metasurface, as illustrated in the first column of Fig. 61(d).

(1) Optical switching: As shown in the second column of Fig. 61(d), different bias currents induced varying temperatures, enabling the $VO_{2}$ layer to switch between a high-transmittance (on) state and a high-reflectance (off) state.  
(2) Optical limiting: As depicted in the third column of Fig. 61(d), incident light converted optical energy into thermal energy, raising the temperature of the $VO_{2}$ layer and thus driving the metasurface transmittance from high to low. If an additional bias current was applied, the threshold for this limiting effect became tunable, as the applied current reduced the optical intensity required to trigger the $VO_{2}$ phase transition.  
(3) Nonreciprocal isolation: As shown in the fourth column of Fig. 61(d), the metasurface exhibited asymmetric absorption for forward and backward incident light. Consequently, the optical intensity thresholds required to induce the $VO_{2}$ phase transition differed for forward and backward illumination. When the incident light intensity was between these two thresholds, the metasurface functioned as an optical isolator.

Active metasurfaces based on phase change chalcogenides. Chalcogenide phase-change materials are a class of functional materials based on chalcogen elements (S, Se, Te), known for their reversible phase transition between amorphous and crystalline states, which can be triggered by external energy sources such as electrical pulses. This rapid, energy-induced transition makes them ideal for active metasurface applications. The chalcogenide phase-change materials used in active metasurfaces include germanium antimony telluride (GST) [759–761], $Ge_{2}Sb_{2}Se_{4}Te$ (GSST) [762,763], $Sb_{2}Se_{3}$ [764], and $Sb_{2}S_{3}$ [765].

(I) Using chalcogenide phase-change materials as meta-atom materials. In 2021, Zhang et al. [762] proposed an active metasurface design, as illustrated in Fig. 62(a), consisting of a metal heater (which also serves as a reflector), two electrodes, and an array of GSST meta-atoms. When a long, low-voltage pulse is applied, Joule heating triggers the GSST meta-atoms to transition into the crystalline state. Conversely, applying a short, high-voltage pulse induces a melt-quench process that re-amorphizes the meta-atoms. Due to the significant optical property contrast between the crystalline and amorphous states of GSST, this enables bi-state reflectance tuning of the metasurface, as shown in Fig. 62(b). Similarly, as depicted in Fig. 62(c), a metagrating can be designed such that the diffraction order with the highest efficiency differs between the crystalline and amorphous states of GSST, enabling reconfigurable beam steering. Following the same principle, Wang et al. [759] designed an active metasurface based on GST, as illustrated in Fig. 62(d), which can also switch between crystalline and amorphous states under the same voltage pulse conditions, leading to the reflectance spectrum modulation shown in Fig. 62(e). In 2022, Abdollahramezani et al. [760] experimentally implemented the metasurface design shown in Fig. 62(a), achieving a reflectance ratio of 11 between the amorphous and crystalline states of the GST-based metasurface at the operating wavelength. Additionally, the switching speed has the potential to reach several kHz.

(II) Integrating metasurfaces with a chalcogenide phase-change material layer. In 2024, Fang et al. [764] introduced a 20 nm-thick $Sb_{2}Se_{3}$ layer into a quasi-BIC Si metasurface, as shown in Fig. 62(f). This enabled pure phase modulation of $0.2\pi$ , with transmittance variations of less than 10% between the crystalline and amorphous states, as illustrated in Fig. 62(g). Notably, this phase modulation was 10 times greater than that of a standalone $Sb_{2}Se_{3}$ film. Experimental results demonstrated that the metasurface maintained stable performance after over 1000 switching cycles. Based on

Figure 62

this, a preliminary demonstration of a 1D SLM composed of 17 independently electrically controlled pixels, each 1.8 $\mu$ m in size, was achieved, as shown in Fig. 62(h). In the same year, Sha et al. [765] experimentally integrated a chiral quasi-symmetry-protected BIC metasurface with a 30 nm layer of $Sb_{2}S_{3}$ , where the phase transition

of $Sb_{2}S_{3}$ between its amorphous and crystalline states enabled a stable chirality continuum ranging from CD = -0.947 to CD = 0.958. Also in 2024, Popescu et al. [763] integrated a 370 nm-thick GSST layer onto an $SiN_{x}$ metasurface, achieving electrically controlled switching of the transmittance spectrum between crystalline and amorphous states, as shown in Fig. 62(i). Experimental results confirmed that the device supported up to 1250 switching cycles.

# 8.5. Active Metasurfaces Based on Electro-Optic Effect

The electro-optic effect refers to the phenomenon in which a dielectric material undergoes polarization under an external electric field, leading to linear or nonlinear changes in its optical dielectric tensor. Based on the dependence of polarization on the electric field, the effect can be categorized into the first-order electro-optic effect (Pockels effect), which exhibits a linear response and occurs only in non-centrosymmetric crystals such as lithium niobate, and the second-order electro-optic effect (Kerr effect), which exhibits a quadratic nonlinear response and is present in all dielectric materials.

By integrating electro-optic materials into metasurfaces—such as using them as the dielectric layer in the MIM structure $[766–769]$ , as meta-atoms $[770,771]$ , or as a cladding medium $[772]$ —and leveraging high-Q-factor resonant modes within the metasurface, significant dynamic optical field modulation can be achieved near the resonance wavelength.

(I) Metasurfaces integrated with an electro-optic material layer. In 2021, Damgaard-Carstensen et al. [766] experimentally demonstrated an active MIM Fresnel lens using lithium niobate as the dielectric layer material, as shown in the left panel of Fig. 63(a). By leveraging the sensitivity of the MIM metasurface response to the dielectric layer, the refractive index of the lithium niobate layer was electrically tuned, enabling dynamic control over the lens's focusing efficiency, as shown in the right panel of Fig. 63(a). Based on the same architecture, in 2022, Weiss et al. [767] experimentally demonstrated dynamic reflectance modulation of an Al nanodisk metasurface coated with ITO. By applying an electric bias to a 480 nm-thick lithium niobate film, the resonance wavelength of the metasurface was tuned, achieving a maximum reflectance modulation depth of 42% at a driving voltage of ±25 V. In 2023, Damgaard-Carstensen et al. [768] further introduced the guided mode resonance into this architecture (with a lithium niobate film thickness of 300 nm) through meticulous metasurface design, achieving a maximum modulation depth of 42% at a driving voltage of ±10 V at a wavelength of 895 nm. In the same year, Zhang et al. [769] integrated this architecture into a fiber facet, realizing a metafiber electro-optic modulator, as shown in the top panel of Fig. 63(b). The electro-optic polymer used was a mixture of chromophore and PMMA. As shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 63(b), the metafiber enabled dual-band operation in the telecom O-band and S-band, with a maximum modulation depth of 11% at a wavelength of 1287 nm. The modulation speed reached approximately 1 GHz at a bias voltage of ±9 V.

(II) Metasurfaces with meta-atoms incorporating electro-optic materials. In 2021, Weigand et al. [770] proposed an active metasurface using lithium niobate as the meta-atom material, as shown in the left panel of Fig. 63(c), achieving MHz-level modulation speed. The modulation of the metasurface transmittance spectrum under different bias voltages is shown in the right panel of Fig. 63(c). Notably, the transmittance modulation was enhanced by a factor of 80 compared to an unstructured substrate. In 2022, Klopfer et al. [771] proposed the active metasurface shown in the top panel of Fig. 63(d). By introducing periodic structural perturbations into the metagrating, the guided mode resonance with a quality factor exceeding 30,000 was generated. Numerical simulations indicated that with an applied bias voltage within $\pm 25\mathrm{V}$ , a nearly $2\pi$ phase modulation was achieved at the resonance wavelength while

maintaining a reflectance of over 91%, as shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 63(d). Based on this, two active devices were demonstrated in simulations: a switchable beam splitter with a deflection angle of $51^{\circ}$ and a diffraction efficiency of 93%, as well as an angle-tunable beam steerer covering an angular range of $18^{\circ}$ to $31^{\circ}$ with an efficiency of up to 86%.

(III) Metasurfaces with a cladding medium made of electro-optic materials. In 2024, Zheng et al. [772] adopted the electrode design shown in Fig. 65(b) [773] and

proposed the active metasurface illustrated in the top panel of Fig. 63(e). An organic electro-optic material was filled into the narrow gaps of slot-mode metasurfaces with high Q-factors, enabling efficient tunability at low voltages. Experimentally, the modulation of the metasurface reflectance at different wavelengths is shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 63(e).

# 8.6. Active Metasurfaces Based on Chemical Reactions

Active metasurfaces based on the lithiation and delithiation. In 2022, Eaves-Rathert et al. [774] designed an MIM metasurface using $TiO_{2}$ as the dielectric layer material, as illustrated in the inset of Fig. 64(a). By applying a voltage between the metasurface and the counter electrode, lithium ions can reversibly transform $TiO_{2}$ into $Li_{0.5}TiO_{2}$ , resulting in significant changes in the optical properties of the dielectric layer, as shown in Fig. 64(a). This enables functionalities such as electrically controlled structural color switching.

In the same year, Yang et al. [775] arranged Si meta-atoms of varying heights on a glass substrate coated with an Ag layer (serving as a mirror) to display different structural colors. By applying a voltage, the meta-atom material can be reversibly converted between Si and $Li_{x}Si$ . Moreover, due to the stability of $Li_{x}Si$ , the intermediate states between these conversions can be maintained. Based on this mechanism, the electrically tunable nanoprinting was realized, as shown in Fig. 64(b).

Active metasurfaces based on hydride-loading transition metal. Under certain conditions, magnesium (Mg) can react with hydrogen gas to form magnesium hydride (MgH $_2$ ), undergoing a phase transition from metal to dielectric. When exposed to oxygen, MgH $_2$ can revert back to Mg. Leveraging this property, Liu's group has designed various active metasurfaces for dynamic control of chirality, nanoprinting, and holography [776–779,783–785].

In 2016, Liu's group [776] demonstrated a metasurface capable of dynamically switching chirality through hydrogenation and dehydrogenation, as shown in Fig. 64(c). As illustrated in the inset of Fig. 64(c), the metasurface unit cell consists of four Mg meta-atoms (160 nm × 160 nm × 75 nm) and four Au meta-atoms (100 nm × 100 nm × 80 nm) arranged on a glass substrate. The Mg particles are coated with a 5 nm titanium spacer and a 10 nm palladium layer. Here, palladium acts as a catalyst, dissociating molecular hydrogen into atomic hydrogen, while titanium serves as a spacer to prevent alloying between Mg and palladium and to mitigate mechanical stress caused by volume expansion due to hydrogen absorption.

In 2017, Liu's group [777] employed an Mg metasurface to realize a dynamic nanoprinting. By adjusting the size and lattice constant of square pillar Mg meta-atoms, various structural colors were generated for nanoprinting. These structural colors originate from the resonance of the Mg meta-atoms and disappear when Mg is converted into $\mathrm{MgH_2}$ . Consequently, the nanoprinting metasurface's colors can be erased or restored by introducing hydrogen or oxygen gas, respectively, as shown in Fig. 64(d). Using another type of meta-atom structure, the reverse process can also be achieved, where introducing oxygen and hydrogen corresponds to color erasure and restoration, respectively [783]. Furthermore, by controlling the sequential chemical reactions of different regions of the nanoprinting metasurface with hydrogen or oxygen, specific color-erasure and restoration animations can be generated [777].

In 2018, Liu's group, in collaboration with Zhang's group [778], implemented dynamic holographic displays using geometric phase metasurfaces based on Mg meta-atoms. As shown in the top panel of Fig. 64(e), the efficiency of the meta-atom drops to nearly zero when Mg is converted into $MgH_{2}$ , enabling on-off control of the holographic image. As illustrated in the middle panel of Fig. 64(e), when Mg meta-

Figure 64  
![](images/5421d37d9b2973687d48415a636d412a16d118f87d3ad8aacf4a4f7efbf0b222.jpg)

Active metasurfaces based on chemical reactions. (a) Refractive index and absorption coefficient spectra of $TiO_{2}$ and $Li_{0.5}TiO_{2}$ . Inset: schematic of an MIM metasurface with $TiO_{2}$ as the dielectric layer material. (b) Voltage-controlled dynamic display of an Si nanoprinting metasurface. (c) Time-dependent evolution of the experimentally measured circular dichroism spectra of the metasurface after hydrogen loading. Spectra were recorded continuously at 10 min intervals. Inset: SEM of the metasurface unit cell. Scale bar, 200 nm. (d) Optical micrographs of a nanoprinting metasurface during hydrogenation and dehydrogenation, demonstrating the dynamic process of color erasure and recovery. (e) Top: measured scattering spectra of Mg and $MgH_{2}$ meta-atoms. Middle: SEM of the metasurface, with Au meta-atoms highlighted in yellow. Inset: enlarged SEM of a metasurface unit cell (600 nm × 600 nm). Bottom: Holographic display of the metasurface under oxygen (top) and hydrogen (bottom) environments. The right-side Chinese character in the holographic image is generated by the Au meta-atom array, while the left-side character is generated by the $Mg/MgH_{2}$ meta-atom array. (g) Experimental demonstration of dynamic holographic display of a metasurface by electrochemically tuning the state of PANI under different applied voltages. (h) Schematic of an electrically tunable metasurface featuring conducting polymer nanodisks on an ITO electrode, controlled electrochemically via a transparent ion gel and a top ITO electrode. The diameter of the meta-atom is 145 nm, and the height is 65 nm. (i) Extinction spectra of the metasurface described in (h) under applied voltages of 0 V and 5 V. The dashed lines indicate the excellent reversibility of the metasurface upon repeated switching. (j) Top: schematic of the experimental setup for an electrically tunable metalens. An electrochemical three-electrode system is used to tune the applied voltage via cyclic voltammetry. Bottom: camera images of the focal plane of the metalens under different applied voltages. (a) Adapted with permission from Eaves-Rathert et al., Nano Lett. 22, 1626–1632 (2022) [774]. Copyright © 2022 (continued)

(continued) American Chemical Society. (b) Reprinted from [775] under a Creative Commons license. (c) Adapted with permission from Duan et al., Nano Lett. 16, 1462–1466 (2016) [776]. Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society. (d) Reprinted from [777] under a Creative Commons license. (e) Adapted from [778] under a Creative Commons license. (f) Reprinted from [779] under a Creative Commons license. (g) Adapted from [780] under a Creative Commons license. (h) and (i) Reprinted from [781] under a Creative Commons license. (j) Adapted from [782] under a Creative Commons license.

atoms are alternately arranged with chemically stable Au meta-atoms using spatial multiplexing, the two meta-atom arrays generate distinct holographic images, resulting in the dynamic display shown in the bottom panel of Fig. 64(e). More complex holographic display effects can be achieved through sophisticated designs combining Au and Mg meta-atoms [784].

In 2020, Liu's group, in collaboration with Duan's group [779], designed an Mg-based meta-atom that exhibited phase modulation in the metallic state and amplitude modulation in the hydride state, which enabled dynamic switching between nanoprinting and holographic display functionalities, as demonstrated in Fig. 64(f).

Active metasurfaces based on conducting polymers. Conductive polymers can reversibly switch between a lower-conducting reduced state and a highly conducting oxidized state through electrochemical redox reactions. This transition is usually accompanied by significant modulation of optical properties, including shifts in absorption spectra, changes in refractive index (due to free carrier density variations), and dynamic color switching. This makes them promising electrically tunable active materials for metasurfaces. Typical conductive polymer materials used in active metasurfaces include polyaniline (PANI) $[780,786,787]$ , poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) (PEDOT) $[787]$ , PEDOT:sulfate (PEDOT:Sulf) $[781,788]$ , and PEDOT:poly(styrene sulfonate) (PEDOT:PSS) $[782,789]$ .

In 2021, Kaissner et al. [780] designed a geometric phase metasurface hologram using Au nanorods locally conjugated with PANI as meta-atoms, demonstrating electrically switchable holographic displays, as shown in Fig. 64(g). This effect relies on the dynamic optical property of PANI, which exhibits minimal absorption in the visible and near-infrared wavelength regions when in its reduced state (placing the metasurface in the “on” state) but exhibits strong absorption in its oxidized state (turning the metasurface “off”). Experiments showed that the metasurface achieved a fast switching speed of approximately 35 ms and maintained its functionality over 100 switching cycles without significant degradation. Interestingly, when PANI was replaced with PEDOT, the switching behavior was reversed: the metasurface was in the “off” state when PEDOT was reduced and in the “on” state when PEDOT was oxidized. Based on this, for two metasurfaces utilizing PANI and PEDOT that are placed adjacent to each other, by applying a voltage to switch the conductive polymers between their oxidized and reduced states, only one metasurface remained in the “on” state at a time, enabling electrically tunable switching between their respective functions [787]. In 2022, Karki et al. [781] developed a metasurface using PEDOT:Sulf as the meta-atom material, achieving dynamically tunable extinction spectra, as shown in Figs. 64(h) and 64(i). Further improvements in metasurface structural design enabled more pronounced extinction spectrum variations [788]. In the same year, Karst et al. [782] employed PEDOT:PSS as the meta-atom material to create a metalens with a voltage-controlled on/off switching, as shown in Fig. 64(j). The experimental switching speed reached up to 33 Hz. In 2023, Jong et al. [789] extended this architecture to realize a

switchable metagrating. In 2024, Lu et al. [786] designed a propagation phase metagrating based on Si meta-atoms coated with PANI. When PANI was in its oxidized (reduced) state, the diffraction order with the highest efficiency was the zeroth (first) order, thereby enabling electrically tunable beam steering.

# 8.7. Nano-Mechanically Tunable Metasurfaces

The micro-electro-mechanical system (MEMS) and nano-electro-mechanical system (NEMS) enable dynamic optical field manipulation by converting electrical signals into structural reconfiguration of metasurfaces through actuation mechanisms such as electrostatic and piezoelectric control.

(I) Metasurfaces with a tunable gap thickness between meta-atoms and the substrate. In 2019, Brongersma's group $[790]$ proposed a MEMS-based active metasurface, as illustrated in the top panel of Fig. 65(a). Utilizing silicon-on-insulator technology, an array of Si meta-atoms with a height of $\sim100$ nm was suspended above a Si substrate. The electrostatic and capacitive coupling between the suspended metasurface and the substrate enabled vertical displacement of the meta-atom array by several hundred nanometers under low actuation voltage. By coupling the Mie resonances of the meta-atoms with the Fabry–Pérot resonances formed between the substrate and the meta-atom array, the structure exhibited vivid colors under white light reflection. As shown in the left panel of Fig. 65(a), metasurface pixels with distinct resonance peaks in the yellow and red spectral ranges achieved temporal color mixing by modulating the duty cycle of the input voltage bias. Additionally, tuning the gap between meta-atoms and the substrate allowed for dynamic phase modulation of reflected light, enabling electrically tunable wavefront control. Based on this, an active metagrating for dynamic beam steering and an active 1D metalens for dynamic light focusing were experimentally demonstrated, as shown in the middle right and bottom right panels of Fig. 65(a), respectively. Experimental results showed that the modulation speed exceeded 100 kHz.

(II) Metasurfaces with tunable meta-atom spacings. In 2021, Faraon's group [773] proposed a NEMS-based suspended metagrating, as illustrated in Fig. 65(b). The metagrating consisted of paired Si nanorods, each of which was connected to a Au electrode on one side. Under electrostatic actuation, the nanorods could shift in position along the direction indicated by the black arrows, enabling dynamic tuning of the spacing between the rods. The modulation speed reached up to 10 kHz. To ensure structural stability, the underlying $SiO_{2}$ layer is partially etched at 300 nm to create suspension conditions, with the anchors supported by the remaining $SiO_{2}$ layer. This configuration enabled electrically tunable reflectance spectrum modulation, as shown in Fig. 65(c). Additionally, as illustrated in Fig. 65(d), fine-tuning of the grating structure allowed for dynamic control of the metasurface's chirality [791]. Furthermore, adjusting the metagrating parameters enabled operation in a high-Q-factor regime, making its electromagnetic response highly sensitive to mechanical displacement [797]. In 2022, Faraon's group [792] further optimized the electrode design of the metagrating. They implemented a scheme where four distinct voltage biases ( $V_{1}, V_{2}, V_{3}, V_{4}$ ) were periodically applied to every four pairs of nanorods, significantly enhancing the dynamic optical field modulation capability. The diffraction patterns of the metagrating under different voltage biases are shown in Fig. 65(e). We note that, to improve the diffraction efficiency of the target $-1^{st}$ order, the metagrating adopted an asymmetric structure, as depicted in the inset of Fig. 65(e).

(III) MIM metasurfaces with a tunable dielectric (air) layer. In 2021, the Bozhevolnyi group, in collaboration with the Dirdal group $[793]$ , proposed an active metasurface system based on a piezoelectric MEMS mirror, as shown in the left panel of Fig. 65(f).

Figure 65  
![](images/c5137b95f9b933d2d56947371e36a4a01bcc7b3f512485386845e6cf9bdd1d5b.jpg)

Nano-mechanically tunable metasurfaces. (a) Top: schematic of a MEMS-enabled active Si metasurface under 0 V and 2.75 V bias. Bottom left: metasurface pixels modulated by voltage modulation with different duty cycles. Scale bar, 2 $\mu$ m. Middle right: SEM of the metagrating, where Si ridges appear white (scale bar, 1 $\mu$ m), and the measured intensity distribution of the beam steering under different biases (scale bar, 3 $\mu$ m). Bottom right: SEM of a 1D metalens (scale bar, 1 $\mu$ m) and the measured intensity distribution in the yOz cross-section under different biases (scale bar, 5 $\mu$ m). (b) 3D and 2D cross-sectional schematics of a nano-electromechanically tunable suspended metagrating. (c) Change in the reflectance spectrum of the metagrating under different biases compared to the unbiased state. (d) Circular dichroism spectra of the metagrating under different biases. Inset: SEM of the metagrating. Scale bar, 5 $\mu$ m. (e) Diffraction patterns generated by the metagrating under varying combinations of bias voltages ( $V_{1}$ , $V_{2}$ , $V_{3}$ , $V_{4}$ ). Inset: schematic of the asymmetric metagrating structure. (f) Left: schematic of an active MIM metasurface based on piezoelectric MEMS. Middle: diffraction efficiency of the active metagrating for the zeroth and $\pm$ first diffraction orders under different biases for TM and TE incident light at a wavelength of 800 nm. Inset: SEM of the metagrating. Right: focusing efficiency of the active metalens under different biases for TM and TE incident light at a wavelength of 800 nm. Inset: SEM of the metalens. (g) Reflectance R of the active metasurface under different biases for various polarization channels. The first and second subscripts of R denote the polarization states of the reflected and incident light, respectively, where L and R represent LCP and RCP, respectively. When RCP degenerates, $R_{LR} = 0$ . (h) Left: measured reflectance of the metasurface under different bias voltages for incident light with orthogonal linear polarizations at a wavelength of 830.2 nm. Inset: SEM of the metasurface. Scale bar, 1 $\mu$ m. Right: intensity distribution of reflected light from a nanoprinting metasurface under different bias voltages. (i) Left: schematic of an active metasurface based on an electrically driven movable membrane. Right: simulated and (continued)

(continued) experimental reflectance spectra corresponding to the optical idle (on) and activated (off) states under applied voltages of 0 V and 70 V, respectively. Inset: SEM of the metasurface from a tilted view. (a) Adapted with permission from Holsteen et al., Science 365, 257–260 (2019) [790]. Copyright © 2019 American Association for the Advancement of Science. (b) and (c) Adapted with permission from Kwon et al., Nano Lett. 21, 2817–2823 (2021) [773]. Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society. (d) Adapted with permission from Kwon et al., ACS Photonics 8, 2980–2986 (2021) [791]. Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society. (e) Adapted from [792] under a Creative Commons license. (f) Adapted from [793] under a Creative Commons license. (g) Reprinted from [794] under a Creative Commons license. (h) Adapted with permission from Deng et al., Optica 11, 326–332 (2024) [795]. Copyright © 2024 Optica Publishing Group. (i) Adapted from [796] under a Creative Commons license.

The MEMS mirror, air gap, and Au meta-atom array together form an MIM metasurface. The electromagnetic response of the MIM metasurface is highly sensitive to the thickness of the dielectric layer. By leveraging the piezoelectric effect, the air gap thickness can be dynamically tuned via electrically driven MEMS mirrors, enabling efficient optical field modulation at a modulation speed exceeding 2.5 kHz. Based on this principle, a polarization-independent dynamic metagrating and metalens were realized, as shown in the middle and right panels of Fig. 65(f), respectively. In 2022, Bozhevolnyi's group [798] conducted further analysis of the aforementioned active metagrating design. When the air layer thickness exceeds half of the wavelength, the metasurface operates in the Fabry–Pérot region, which relaxes assembly requirements and operational tolerances. When the air layer thickness is much smaller than the wavelength, the metasurface operates in the gap-surface plasmon region, offering a broader operational bandwidth. Additionally, experimental results indicate that increasing the air gap enhances the coupling between adjacent metasurface elements, leading to a reduction in diffraction efficiency. In the same year, this piezoelectric MEMS-based MIM metasurface was further employed by Bozhevolnyi's group [799] to realize a dynamic reflective wave plate. The polarization state of the reflected light traces a trajectory that fully encircles the Poincaré sphere as the bias voltage varies. In 2024, based on the same architecture (operating in the Fabry–Pérot region), Bozhevolnyi's group [699] designed a metasurface unit cell that exhibits RCP degeneracy [133] at a specific air layer thickness, achieving electrically tunable emergence and disappearance of the exceptional point, as shown in Fig. 65(g). In the same year, Bozhevolnyi's group [795] further explored the polarization-sensitive properties of a homogeneous MIM metasurface [as illustrated in the inset of the left panel in Fig. 65(h)] under varying air layer thicknesses. As shown in the left panel of Fig. 65(h), at a set of specific bias voltages $\tilde{V}_{m}$ , we have $R_{xx} \gg R_{yy}$ and $R_{yy} \rightarrow 0$ , a characteristic that can be utilized for dynamic nanoprinting, as demonstrated in the right panel of Fig. 65(h). In the Tai Chi pattern, the black and white regions are composed of mutually perpendicular meta-atoms. When observing only the $R_{yy}$ component, at the specific voltage $\tilde{V}_{m}$ , only the meta-atoms in the black regions satisfy $R_{yy} \rightarrow 0$ , causing the metasurface to display the Tai Chi pattern. At other voltages, the meta-atoms in the black regions satisfy $R_{yy} \gg 0$ , leading to the erasure of the pattern.

(IV) Metasurfaces with a height-tunable membrane on top. In 2023, Herle et al. [795] proposed the active metasurface shown in the left panel of Fig. 65(i). The metasurface consists of an array of a–Si pillar meta-atoms (supported by a silica pillar array) and a suspended, movable a–Si film with circular apertures, integrated with an electrostatic actuator. As illustrated in the right panel of Fig. 65(i), in the idle state, the top surface

of the film aligns with the top of the meta-atoms, forming a highly reflective surface. In the activated state, the film can move vertically by a distance of $\delta$ under bias control, leading to a significant reduction in reflectance.

# 8.8. Stretchable Metasurfaces

Using flexible and elastic materials such as polydimethylsiloxane (PDMS) as the substrate for metasurfaces enables dynamic control by stretching the substrate along a specific direction. This deformation alters the lattice constant (or equivalently, the spacing between meta-atoms) in that direction, thereby tuning the metasurface's resonant modes $[800–802]$ or phase gradients $[803–806]$ .

Modulating resonant modes using stretchable metasurfaces. In 2011, Aksu et al. [800] fabricated an Au nanorod array on a 13 $\mu$ m-thick low-density polyethylene (LDPE) thin film and demonstrated the dynamic tuning of the resonant mode in the mid-infrared transmittance spectrum through stretching, as shown in Fig. 66(a). In 2016, Gutruf et al. [802] utilized a $TiO_{2}$ metasurface with PDMS (2 mm thick) as both the substrate and cladding material to achieve polarization-sensitive dynamic modulation of the transmittance spectrum, as shown in Fig. 66(b). By stretching the substrate to modify the lattice constant and adjust the metasurface's resonance peaks, this approach can be further applied to realize dynamically tunable structural colors. In 2015, Zhu et al. [801] employed a Si metasurface with PDMS as both the substrate and cladding material to achieve strain-dependent structural colors, as illustrated in.?[Fig. 66(c).

Modulating phase gradients using stretchable metasurfaces. When the substrate is stretched such that the lattice constant of the metasurface increases by a factor of $\gamma$ , the phase gradient along the stretching direction decreases to $1/\gamma$ of its original value, assuming that the phase modulation of the meta-atoms remains unchanged due to the lattice constant variation. This results in a modification of the diffraction angle of a metagrating and the focal length of a metalens. Specifically, for a metagrating, according to Eq. (56), the sine of the diffraction angle decreases to $1/\gamma$ of its original value. For a hyperbolic metalens, using Eq. (169) and assuming the approximation NA $\ll 1$ , i.e., $r/f \ll 1$ , the phase distribution is given by

$$
\begin{array}{l} \phi_ {\mathrm{hyperbolic,NA} \ll 1} (r, \omega) = - \frac {\omega}{c} f \sqrt {1 + \left(\frac {r}{f}\right) ^ {2}} + C ^ {\prime} (\omega) \\ \approx - \frac {\omega}{c} f \Bigg (1 + \frac {1}{2} \left(\frac {r}{f}\right) ^ {2} \Bigg) + C ^ {\prime} (\omega) \\ = - \frac {\omega}{2 c} \frac {r ^ {2}}{f} + C ^ {\prime \prime} (\omega). \tag {347} \\ \end{array}
$$

When the substrate is stretched by a factor of $\gamma$ , the new phase distribution is

$$
\phi_ {\text {hyperbolic,NA} \ll 1} ^ {\prime} (r, \omega) = \phi_ {\text {hyperbolic,NA} \ll 1} \left(\frac {r}{\gamma}, \omega\right) = - \frac {\omega}{2 c} \frac {r ^ {2}}{f ^ {\prime}} + C ^ {\prime \prime} (\omega), \tag {348}
$$

where

$$
f ^ {\prime} = \gamma^ {2} f. \tag {349}
$$

Thus, the focal length of the metalens increases by a factor of $\gamma^{2}$ .

In 2016, Ee and Agarwal [803] experimentally demonstrated a diffraction-angle-tunable metagrating and a focal-length-tunable metalens operating at a wavelength of 632.8 nm, as shown in Fig. 66(d). These devices were based on a geometric phase Au metasurface fabricated on a $\sim$ 0.2 mm-thick PDMS film substrate. In the same year, Kamali

Figure 66  
![](images/d18086ce805049c4e38fdfe41fe27eb9ca5634fd207e301c1a1a208b44930b8a.jpg)

Stretchable metasurfaces. (a) Measured and calculated transmittance spectra of nanorod arrays with a periodicity of 1.5 $\mu$ m, a length of 1.2 $\mu$ m, a width of 250 nm, and a height of 70 nm, on an LDPE film before (blue curve) and after stretching (red curve). A 5% enlargement of the lattice constant was achieved after stretching. Inset: response of a nanorod array with a periodicity of 1.8 $\mu$ m and a length of 900 nm when stretched for a 16% enlargement in periodicity. (b) Transmittance spectra of the metasurface under increasing applied strain for incident x-polarized (left) and y-polarized (right) light. (c) Photographs of a nanoprinting metasurface (a flower pattern) under different deformations. $\epsilon$ represents the ratio of the change in sample length to its original length. (d) Top: intensity distributions of transmitted cross-polarized light along the x-axis (also the direction of the metagrating phase gradient) of a geometric phase metagrating under different stretch ratios. The stretch ratio is defined as the ratio of the stretched sample length to the original length. Bottom: intensity distributions of transmitted cross-polarized light along the optical axis of a geometric phase metalens under different stretch ratios.
