The gray shaded area represents possible displacements, which can be accessed by concurrently activating combinations of electrodes. (a) Reprinted with permission from Aksu et al., Adv. Mater. 23, 4422–4430 (2011) [800]. Copyright © 2011 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (b) Adapted with permission from Gutruf et al., ACS Nano 10, 133–141 (2016) [802]. (continued)

(continued) Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society. (c) Reprinted with permission from Zhu et al., Optica 2, 255–258 (2015) [801]. Copyright © 2015 Optica Publishing Group. (d) Adapted with permission from Ee et al., Nano Lett. 16, 2818–2823 (2016) [803]. Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society. (e) Reprinted with permission from Kamali et al., Laser Photonics Rev. 10, 1002–1008 (2016) [804]. Copyright © 2016 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (g) Adapted from [805] under a Creative Commons license.

et al. [804] realized a stretchable propagation phase a–Si metalens operating at a wavelength of 915 nm, using a $\sim$ 0.1 mm-thick PDMS film as the substrate. The focusing efficiencies for varying strains are depicted in Fig. 66(e). We note that the focusing performance of both stretchable metalens designs, based on geometric or propagation phase modulation mechanisms, degrades significantly as the stretching ratio increases, as shown in the bottom panel of Figs. 66(d) and 66(e). This degradation arises from the increased spacing between meta-atoms, which affects the meta-atom coupling and consequently alters their electromagnetic response. In contrast, employing detour phase modulation effectively circumvents this issue, as detour phase inherently relies on adjusting the spacing between adjacent units for phase control. In 2021, Wei et al. [806] realized a detour phase graphene oxide metalens on a PDMS film substrate. As shown in Fig. 66(f), this metalens inherits the broadband characteristic of detour phase and exhibits minimal degradation in focusing efficiency under stretching.

More sophisticated and precise stretching control can be achieved via MEMS-based electrical tuning. In 2018, She et al. $[805]$ experimentally demonstrated an electrically tunable metalens controlled by dielectric elastomer actuators, as shown in the left panel of Fig. 66(g). Five addressable electrodes were integrated to enable electrical control over the strain field of the metasurface, allowing the metalens to achieve focal length tuning exceeding 100%, along with on-the-fly astigmatism and image shift corrections. The displacement of the metalens focal point in all cardinal directions under appropriate control voltages is illustrated in the right panel of Fig. 66(g).

# 8.9. Microfluidic Metasurfaces

Microfluidic technology enables highly automated guidance, mixing, and manipulation of minute liquid volumes, offering advantages such as high precision, efficiency, low consumption, and miniaturization. Integrating microfluidic technology with metasurfaces provides an attractive solution for dynamic metasurfaces. By controlling the type and flow rate of liquids within microfluidic channels, the refractive index of the meta-atoms or the cladding medium surrounding the meta-atoms can be tuned in real time, thereby dynamically altering the light field modulation of the metasurface.

Modulating the shape of meta-atoms using microfluidics. In 2017, Song et al. [807] experimentally demonstrated a microfluidic metasurface based on water meta-atoms, as illustrated in Fig. 67(a). By controlling the water volume within PDMS microfluidic channels, the height of the water meta-atoms was adjusted, thereby modulating the absorption spectrum of the metasurface. In the same year, Wu et al. [808] employed a gallium–indium–tin alloy (Galinstan) metasurface with actively addressable units to realize a broadband, wide-angle dynamic polarization converter operating in the microwave regime, as shown in Fig. 67(b). Each L-shaped meta-atom was integrated with a single-inlet, dual-outlet PDMS microfluidic channel, where the injection and removal of the liquid metal controlled the arm length of the L-shaped meta-atom. This tunability allowed the reflected light to be converted among three different polarization states: linear, circular, and elliptical. Similarly, in 2021, Zhang et al. [811]

Figure 67  
![](images/492a84a88607338d49e3a58365cae57c6f11580971e57f0ef3e2d65bf0f0cbc4.jpg)

Microfluidic metasurfaces. (a) Schematic of a metasurface based on water resonators. (b) Photograph of a microfluidic metasurface designed for dynamic polarization conversion. (c) Photograph of a PDMS microfluidic chip integrated with a metasurface. (d) Dynamic display of a nanoprinting metasurface when different media (top right) with different refractive indices are introduced into the microfluidic chip. Scale bar, 150 $\mu$ m. (e) Schematic of a microfluidic chip integrated with a metasurface. Top inset: SEM of the metalens. Scale bar, 500 nm. Bottom inset: optical image of a fabricated Y-shaped microfluidic channel. Scale bar, 100 $\mu$ m. (f) Experimentally obtained dynamic light field modulation results based on microfluidic metasurfaces. From left to right: nanoprinting with switchable (first column) and color-tunable (second column) control, both based on reflectance spectrum modulation; a switchable metalens (third column) and a metasurface hologram (fourth column), both based on diffraction efficiency modulation. n represents the refractive index of the cladding medium controlled by the microfluidic chip. (a) Reprinted with permission from Song et al., Adv. Opt. Mater. 5, 1601103 (2017) [807]. Copyright © 2017 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (b) Reprinted with permission from Wu et al., Adv. Opt. Mater. 5, 1600938 (2017) [808]. Copyright © 2017 WILEY-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (c) and (d) Reprinted with permission from Sun et al., ACS Nano 12, 2151–2159 (2018) [809]. Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society. (e) and (f) Adapted with permission from Li et al., Nat. Nanotechnol. 17, 1097–1103 (2022) [810]. Copyright © 2022 Springer Nature.

demonstrated polarization control by modifying the structure of the meta-atoms via adjusting the volume of glycerol injected into the microfluidic system. Experimentally, they achieved polarization rotation of 0.19 THz linearly polarized light from $0^{\circ}$ to $14^{\circ}$ .

However, due to fabrication constraints, achieving sufficiently small microfluidic channels while maintaining effective liquid flow control remains challenging. As a result, the unit size of dynamic metasurfaces based on this approach remains on the millimeter or submillimeter scale, limiting their operation to the THz, GHz, or lower frequency bands. Current fabrication techniques are insufficient to further extend this method to higher-frequency applications.

Modulating the cladding medium of a metasurface using microfluidics. Shifting the target of microfluidic control from individual meta-atoms to the cladding medium of meta-atoms allows for eliminating the limitations imposed by microfluidic channel fabrication and realizing dynamic modulation—based on the metasurface's sensitivity to the variation of the cladding medium's refractive index $n_{\mathrm{cladding}}$ —for metasurfaces operating at high frequencies, such as in the visible spectrum.

In 2018, Sun et al. [809] demonstrated real-time tunable structural color by manipulating the refractive index of the fluid flowing over a dielectric metasurface using a microfluidic chip, as illustrated in Fig. 67(c). The system consisted of a $TiO_{2}$ metasurface embedded within a PDMS microfluidic channel. By injecting solutions with different refractive indices into the channel, the reflection peak of the metasurface could be precisely tuned, leading to dynamically adjustable structural colors, as shown in Fig. 67(d). Experimental results indicated that the color-switching response time of the system was only 16 ms.

In 2022, Brongersma's group [810] integrated a metasurface with a Y-shaped microfluidic channel, as illustrated in Fig. 67(e). This system enabled controlled injection of high-refractive-index oil ( $n = 1.7$ ) and low-refractive-index liquid cleaner (HFE-7500, $n = 1.29$ ) into the main channel (where the metasurface was placed) via pneumatic valves. By designing the meta-atom with the reflection spectrum sensitive to $n_{\mathrm{cladding}}$ , they experimentally demonstrated switchable and continuously tunable nanoprinting, as shown in the first two columns of Fig. 67(f). Furthermore, by designing a geometric phase metasurface with the meta-atom shape optimized for $n_{\mathrm{cladding}}$ -dependent diffraction efficiency—yielding near-zero efficiency at low $n_{\mathrm{cladding}}$ and high efficiency at high $n_{\mathrm{cladding}}$ —the switchable metalens and metasurface hologram were achieved, as illustrated in the last two columns of Fig. 67(f). Experiments indicated that the system operated at a modulation frequency of approximately $10\mathrm{Hz}$ .

# 8.10. Moiré Metasurfaces

A moiré lattice consists of two superimposed structures with the same periodicity but a relative rotational offset. In recent years, interest in moiré lattices has surged due to their rich physical phenomena, with the most well-known example being the unconventional superconductivity observed in magic-angle graphene. With the advancement of cascaded metasurfaces, moiré metasurfaces have emerged as a new research frontier $[812,813]$ . Here, we will introduce the applications of moiré metasurfaces in active optical field manipulation.

In 2022, Liu et al. [814] demonstrated dynamic beam steering through the mutual twisting of two closely stacked metasurfaces, as illustrated in Fig. 68(a). Due to the moiré effect, the superposition of two high-spatial-frequency patterns generates a low-spatial-frequency moiré pattern. When the twist angle between the two high-spatial-frequency patterns changes, the spatial frequency of the resulting moiré pattern also varies, thereby deflecting the incident light to different angles. For instance, in the bilayer metasurface shown in Fig. 68(a), when the twist angle is set to $10^{\circ}$ , the corresponding spatial frequency and its superposition are depicted in Fig. 68(b). The superposition produces two sets of spatial frequency distributions, represented in yellow and blue, respectively. The blue set contains spatial frequencies exceeding the free-space limit ( $k = 2\pi/\lambda$ ) and is therefore not observable, while only the yellow set contributes to the beam radiation. By continuously adjusting the twist angle from $0^{\circ}$ to $90^{\circ}$ , dynamic beam steering, as illustrated in Fig. 68(c), can be achieved. Experiments in the microwave regime verified the dynamic control of the deflection angle of the proposed moiré metasurface, as shown in Fig. 68(d).

Figure 68  
![](images/7a033e4b0e795bdb295862e34bba76fce2c839386505dc5e9a89550848f39b55.jpg)

Moiré metasurfaces. (a) Schematic of a moiré metasurface designed for dynamic beam steering. (b) Bilayer metasurface and the spatial frequency analysis of their superposition. (c) Theoretically predicted beam scanning trajectory as the twist angle of the Moiré metasurface varies from $0^{\circ}$ to $90^{\circ}$ . Inset: enlarged view of the central region of the scanning trajectory. (d) Cutting-plane view of the experimentally observed radiation pattern of the moiré metasurface as the twist angle changes from $0.25^{\circ}$ to $14^{\circ}$ . (e) Left: SEM of a bilayer metasurface with a square lattice at a twist angle of $45^{\circ}$ . The colored regions indicate the geometry of the base lattices. Middle: spatial frequency distribution corresponding to the first-order diffraction of the Moiré metasurface shown in the left panel. Right: laser emission perpendicular to the metasurface laser. Double-headed arrows indicate the polarization directions. (f) Same as (e), except that the metasurface laser is based on a bilayer metasurface with a hexagonal lattice at a twist angle of $30^{\circ}$ . (a)–(d) Adapted from [814] under a Creative Commons license. (e) and (f) Adapted with permission from Tan et al., ACS Nano 18, 23181–23188 (2024) [815]. Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society.

In 2023, Lyu et al. [816] leveraged symmetry breaking induced by the mutual twisting of a bilayer moiré metasurface to dynamically control the chirality of the metasurface, achieving a maximum experimentally observed circular dichroism of 0.81.

In 2024, Tan et al. [815] utilized a moiré metasurface laser to generate hybrid-electric mode beams with high rotational symmetry. The metasurface was composed of plasmonic nanoparticles arranged in a square or hexagonal lattice on a perovskite nanocrystal film. As shown in Fig. 68(e), when the twist angle of the bilayer square-lattice moiré metasurface was set to $45^{\circ}$ , the laser emission under optical pumping exhibited the $HE_{41}$ mode. Similarly, as depicted in Fig. 68(f), when the twist angle of the bilayer hexagonal-lattice moiré metasurface was set to $30^{\circ}$ , the laser emission exhibited the $HE_{61}$ mode. The high symmetry of these beam modes originates from the symmetry of the moiré pattern formed by the bilayer metasurface.

# 9. SUMMARY AND OUTLOOK

After more than a decade of development, the research on metasurfaces seems to have matured considerably. A comprehensive understanding of the physical mechanisms of light interaction with individual meta-atoms has been established. Current explorations into the phase modulation mechanisms are quite comprehensive, and even if new phase

modulation methods are proposed, it's plausible that they can be seen as supplements to the existing mechanisms [153,158]. The forward design method based on LPA has been verified in various metasurface designs, with little room for further improvement. Applications of technologies such as topology optimization, heuristic algorithms, deep learning, and automatic differentiation in the inverse design of metasurfaces have undergone preliminary but comprehensive exploration. For major metasurface devices like metalenses and metasurface holograms, a systematic design framework has gradually taken shape, encompassing basic theory, design methods, performance evaluation, and analytical approaches. Meanwhile, the applications of metasurfaces in quantum photonics, optical computing, and dynamic light field manipulation have also been under comprehensive development. In terms of achieving broadband, wide FOV, and function multiplexing in metasurface designs, current researches are gradually approaching the theoretical limits of metasurfaces [173,503,504]. Through such a comprehensive review of the metasurface field, we find that what lies before us is no longer a nascent field. Nonetheless, there remain many unsolved problems and unknown territories in metasurface design awaiting further exploration. On the road to the practical application of metasurfaces, we still face multiple scientific and engineering challenges.

More possibilities in the light–matter interaction. As showcased in Subsection 2.1, there's now a comprehensive and in-depth understanding of the physical mechanisms behind the interaction between single meta-atoms and light, which serves as the basis for the current light field manipulation methods using metasurfaces. However, the nonlocal coupling between adjacent meta-atoms is almost always neglected. How to incorporate the nonlocal coupling into the metasurface design remains a challenge $[568]$ . One approach is to introduce inverse design methods, which involve optimization algorithms $[343]$ and deep learning $[406]$ , as discussed in Section 3. Nevertheless, this approach only improves the device performance to a certain extent through utilizing an additional degree of freedom of the coupling, and it is inadequate to unveil the physical mechanisms behind the meta-atom coupling or to develop applications with novel functionalities. Another approach that builds on the forward design paradigm involves expanding the unit cell of a metasurface from a single meta-atom to a cluster of multiple meta-atoms. In this context, our primary focus is the electromagnetic coupling among the meta-atoms within each unit, which contrasts with the assumptions made in Section 5, where each meta-atom in the unit was considered to follow the electromagnetic response characteristics dictated by the LPA. For example, in 2024, Chen et al. experimentally demonstrated that even a configuration of just four meta-atoms can achieve a full $2\pi$ phase shift, enabling the efficient generation of high-purity optical vortices with varying topological charges $[817]$ .

With the proposal and advancement of active metasurfaces, along with continuous improvements in fabrication techniques, metasurfaces have evolved to incorporate time as a novel degree of freedom beyond spatial light field manipulation. The principles of causality and the second law of thermodynamics impose a unidirectional evolution of time in spatiotemporal metasurfaces, thereby enriching light–matter interactions—for example, enabling nonreciprocal effects without the need for magnetic biasing $[818]$ . We have already introduced some of the exciting and unconventional physical phenomena enabled by this in Subsection 2.2.7. Nevertheless, these findings mark only the initial stage of exploration.

The interaction between metasurfaces and novel light sources is expected to reveal new physical phenomena and functionalities. Complex-frequency light, characterized by its virtual gain or loss, has attracted increasing attention in recent years $[328]$ . An introduction to complex-frequency metasurfaces can be found in Subsection 2.2.8. Mean-

while, nonclassical optics has demonstrated remarkable potential for future applications. In Section 6, we discuss the integration of quantum photonics with metasurfaces, which opens up new opportunities for research in both fields.

The convergence of metasurfaces with related disciplines provides a powerful foundation for exploring novel light–matter interactions. For instance, as discussed in Subsection 2.2.3, metasurfaces have incorporated the concept of high-Q-factor nonlocal resonant modes from photonic crystals, topological phases from topological photonics, and exceptional points from non-Hermitian physics. In Subsection 7.1, we introduced the implementation of mathematical operations using nonlocal metasurfaces inspired by Fourier optics. In Subsection 8.10, we presented dynamic modulation mechanisms of Moiré metasurfaces inspired by Moiré optics. We believe that such interdisciplinary integration will continue to invigorate the field and create new opportunities for metasurface research.

Challenges and future developments in design methods. Nowadays, forward design methods are widely used, while inverse design approaches are gradually gaining traction but still face lots of limitations.

Inverse design methods encounter some longstanding issues that preclude their widespread adoption. The computational cost of iterative optimization is prohibitive for larger-area metasurface devices, often restricting inverse design to small-area devices $[408,453]$ or 2D structure optimizations $[3,422]$ , with heuristic algorithms facing even greater limitations than gradient optimization methods. Besides, when the number of optimization variables is large (e.g., the metasurface to be optimized contains a significant number of meta-atoms), designs obtained from inverse design methods tend to converge to local optima or saddle points, potentially resulting in device performance that is inferior to that achieved by forward design. Moreover, deep learning-based design methods also have inherent limitations, including a lack of generalization capability and excessively high training costs for complex models $[402]$ .

It is crucial for both forward and inverse design methodologies to clearly understand their respective roles within the metasurface design framework to fully exploit their inherent strengths. Forward design is predicated on an understanding of the physical mechanisms underpinning metasurface configurations $[819]$ , making it especially suited to realize novel metasurface designs from 0 to 1. In contrast, inverse design should specifically address those challenges that limit forward design, such as incorporating the coupling for enhanced device performance $[406]$ , navigating high-dimensional design spaces $[449]$ , crafting optical devices considering spatial dispersion $[338]$ , and developing multilayer metasurface designs that account for interlayer coupling $[191]$ , among others.

The advancement of electromagnetic simulation software is crucial for metasurface design. It will significantly enhance the capabilities and broaden the application scope of metasurface design methodologies. Often, the choice of simulation and design strategy for metasurfaces is dictated by the limitations of the current computational tools. The complexity of electromagnetic simulations is approximately related to the simulation area by a high-degree polynomial $[422]$ , and the accurate and efficient simulation for large-area metasurface devices remains a challenge. Recent proposals like GPU parallel computing $[820]$ and distributed computing $[400]$ represent advances, yet they are still not commonplace.

Furthermore, the impact of design methodologies on device performance has not been systematically elucidated, particularly from a theoretical perspective, despite some initial comparative discussions $[343,403,449]$ . This leads to an inadequate understanding of the fundamental limits of these design methods.

Beneficial interaction and mutual promotion between theoretical analysis and device design. On one hand, metasurface device designs provide the basis for experimental validation of theoretical analysis. On the other hand, theoretical analysis deepens our understanding of metasurfaces, pointing the way for further improvement of device design $[821]$ . The positive interaction between theoretical analysis and device design is important for the healthy development of the entire metasurface design community $[474,479]$ .

Achieving higher device performance is a ubiquitous challenge in metasurface design $[822]$ , especially when there are high demands on NA, FOV, achromatic bandwidth, and multiplexing, as pointed out in Sections 4 and 5. Although some work has theoretically indicated several limitations between various metrics, the current understanding of the mechanisms is still insufficient $[823]$ .

Realizing large-area metasurface devices has been a long-standing goal within the field $[383]$ . Theoretically, for instance, Eqs. (187) and (237), respectively, reveal the constraints imposed by the lattice constant and NA on the size of a metalens. How to maintain high performance for a large-area metasurface or even break through the aforementioned limits with inverse optimization $[426]$ , ingenious design methods $[400,504]$ , and advanced fabrication techniques $[824]$ remains an area ripe for exploration.

Improved and novel devices empowered by the development of fabrication processes. It goes without saying that advancing fabrication processes is significant for enabling metasurface designs with higher degrees of freedom and for enhancing consistency between simulated and experimental results. For example, the issue of the zeroth-order bright spot in metasurface holography is in part due to fabrication imperfections $[569]$ , and Eq. (232) reveals the impact of aspect ratio on the performance of achromatic metalenses. Challenges in this area include compatibility between current processes and the CMOS process $[341]$ , efficient and low-cost mass production $[369,385,386]$ , controlling the shape and dimensional accuracy of meta-atom structures $[825]$ , improving the surface roughness of metasurfaces $[825]$ , managing interlayer distance and alignment in cascaded metasurfaces, achieving a higher aspect ratio $[826,827]$ , etc.

In addition, we should also recognize that advanced manufacturing technologies will make more novel metasurface designs possible. For instance, multi-height meta-atoms can be used for complex amplitude modulation $[392]$ (as detailed in Subsection 5.1.2), and fabrication processes that unlock out-of-plane dimensions empower the design of chiral metasurfaces $[284,287]$ (as detailed in Subsection 2.2.5).

# APPENDIX A: DIFFRACTION MODEL FOR METASURFACE HOLOGRAMS

As shown in Fig. 69, light propagates in the positive direction of the $z$ -axis. The diffraction plane is located on the $z = z_0$ plane, whose complex amplitude distribution is denoted as $E(x,y,z_0)$ . The complex amplitude distribution on the observation plane is denoted as $E(x,y,z)$ . The refractive index of the background medium is $n_{\mathrm{back}}$ , adhering to the symbol convention consistent with Subsection 4.1. The Rayleigh-Sommerfeld diffraction formula provides an exact solution for field propagation, with the first kind of Rayleigh-Sommerfeld diffraction formula given by [535]

$$
E (x, y, z) = \iint_ {\Sigma} E \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, z _ {0}\right) \left(\frac {1}{2 \pi \bar {r}} + \frac {1}{i \lambda}\right) \frac {d}{\bar {r}} \frac {e ^ {i k \bar {r}}}{\bar {r}} \mathrm{d} x ^ {\prime} \mathrm{d} y ^ {\prime}, \tag {A1}
$$

where $\Sigma$ represents the diffracted field region, x and y on the diffraction plane are denoted as $x'$ and $y'$ for ease of subsequent discussion, $d = z - z_{0}$ , $\bar{r} =$

Figure 69  
![](images/ca0ec3ede7fef639d1ab5395d15cd489f6bf0822c8ccb23eecff69068136e5ae.jpg)

<details>
<summary>text_image</summary>

y
x
y'
O
x'
z0
E(x', y', z0)
Diffraction plane
y
x
z
E(x, y, z)
z
</details>

Observation plane

Geometry and the coordinate system used for illustrating scalar diffraction theory.

$\sqrt{(x - x')^2 + (y - y')^2 + d^2}$ is the distance between points on the diffraction plane and the observation plane. We note that Eq. (A1) can be derived from Eq. (157), which represents a more general form of this formula.

When the distance between the observation plane and the diffraction plane is sufficiently large such that the $\frac{1}{2\pi\bar{r}}$ term in Eq. (A1) can be neglected and the paraxial approximation ( $\bar{r} \approx d$ ) is applied, Eq. (A1) simplifies to

$$
E (x, y, z) = \frac {1}{i \lambda d} \iint E \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, z _ {0}\right) e ^ {i k \bar {r}} \mathrm{d} x ^ {\prime} \mathrm{d} y ^ {\prime}. \tag {A2}
$$

When the approximation

$$
d \gg \frac {1}{2} \left(\frac {\left(\left(x - x ^ {\prime}\right) ^ {2} + \left(y - y ^ {\prime}\right) ^ {2}\right) ^ {2}}{\lambda}\right) ^ {\frac {1}{3}} \tag {A3}
$$

is applied, we obtain the Fresnel diffraction formula

$$
E (x, y, z) = \frac {e ^ {i k d}}{i \lambda d} \iint_ {\Sigma} E \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, z _ {0}\right) e ^ {i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} \left(\left(x - x ^ {\prime}\right) ^ {2} + \left(y - y ^ {\prime}\right) ^ {2}\right)} \mathrm{d} x ^ {\prime} \mathrm{d} y ^ {\prime}. \tag {A4}
$$

The region constrained by Eq. (A3) where the Fresnel diffraction formula is valid, is known as the Fresnel diffraction zone. When the approximation

$$
d \gg \frac {1}{2} \frac {x ^ {\prime 2} + y ^ {\prime 2}}{\lambda} \tag {A5}
$$

is further applied, we obtain the Fraunhofer diffraction formula

$$
E (x, y, z) = \frac {e ^ {i k d}}{i \lambda d} e ^ {i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} \left(x ^ {2} + y ^ {2}\right)} \iint_ {\Sigma} E \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, z _ {0}\right) e ^ {- i \frac {2 \pi}{\lambda d} \left(x x ^ {\prime} + y y ^ {\prime}\right)} \mathrm{d} x ^ {\prime} \mathrm{d} y ^ {\prime}. \tag {A6}
$$

The region constrained by Eq. (A5) where the Fraunhofer diffraction formula is valid, is known as the Fraunhofer diffraction zone. Clearly, the range of the Fresnel diffraction zone includes that of the Fraunhofer diffraction zone. However, within the Fraunhofer diffraction zone, it is unnecessary to use the relatively more complex Fresnel formula for calculations. It is important to note that both formulas are derived based on the paraxial approximation, thus limiting the Fresnel and Fraunhofer diffraction zones to the vicinity of the optical axis.

Using the angular spectrum method, we can derive diffraction formulas based on angular spectrum propagation from the Helmholtz equation. These formulas are more suited for numerical computations compared to the integral formulas mentioned previously $[535]$ . Define u, v, and w as the spatial frequencies in the x, y, and z directions, respectively, satisfying

$$
u = \frac {k _ {x}}{2 \pi}, \quad v = \frac {k _ {y}}{2 \pi}, \quad w = \frac {k _ {z}}{2 \pi}, \tag {A7}
$$

where $k_{x}$ , $k_{y}$ , and $k_{z}$ are the components of the wavevector $\vec{k}$ in the x, y, and z directions, respectively, also known as the spatial angular frequencies. Let the direction cosines of $\vec{k}$ be $\cos\gamma_{x}$ , $\cos\gamma_{y}$ , and $\cos\gamma_{z}$ for x, y, and z directions, respectively; then we have

$$
u = \frac {\cos \gamma_ {x}}{\lambda}, \quad v = \frac {\cos \gamma_ {y}}{\lambda}, \quad w = \frac {\cos \gamma_ {z}}{\lambda}. \tag {A8}
$$

From Eqs. (A7) and (A8), it's evident that $u, v$ , and $w$ satisfy

$$
u ^ {2} + v ^ {2} + w ^ {2} = \left(\frac {k}{2 \pi}\right) ^ {2} = \left(\frac {1}{\lambda}\right) ^ {2}. \tag {A9}
$$

Thus, w can be viewed as a function of u and v, i.e.,

$$
w = w (u, v) = \sqrt {\left(\frac {1}{\lambda}\right) ^ {2} - u ^ {2} - v ^ {2}}. \tag {A10}
$$

For any field distribution $E(x,y,z)$ , its angular spectrum is defined as its 2D Fourier transform with respect to x and y, denoted as

$$
\tilde {E} (u, v, z) = \mathcal {F} \left\{E (x, y, z) \right\} (u, v, z) = \iint_ {- \infty} ^ {+ \infty} E (x, y, z) e ^ {- i 2 \pi (u x + v y)} \mathrm{d} x \mathrm{d} y, \tag {A11}
$$

where F represents the 2D Fourier transform. The spatial field distribution can be obtained from the angular spectrum by performing an inverse Fourier transform

$$
E (x, y, z) = \mathcal {F} ^ {- 1} \left\{\tilde {E} (u, v, z) \right\} (x, y, z) = \iint_ {- \infty} ^ {+ \infty} \tilde {E} (u, v, z) e ^ {i 2 \pi (u x + v y)} \mathrm{d} u \mathrm{d} v, \tag {A12}
$$

where $\mathcal{F}^{-1}$ denotes the 2D Fourier inverse transform.

The angular spectrum propagation formula corresponding to the Rayleigh–Sommerfeld diffraction formula is

$$
\tilde {E} (u, v, z) = \tilde {E} (u, v, z _ {0}) H (u, v, d), \tag {A13}
$$

where $\tilde{E}(u,v,z_{0})$ is the angular spectrum of the field distribution $E(x,y,z_{0})$ on the diffraction plane, $\tilde{E}(u,v,z)$ is the angular spectrum of the field distribution $E(x,y,z)$ on the observation plane, and $H(u,v,d)=e^{-i2\pi w(u,v)d}$ is known as the transfer function. Thus, the field distribution on the observation plane is

$$
\begin{array}{l} E \left(x, y, z\right) = \mathcal {F} ^ {- 1} \left\{\tilde {E} \left(u, v, z\right) \right\} \left(x, y, z\right) \\ = \mathcal {F} ^ {- 1} \left\{\mathcal {F} \left\{E (x, y, z _ {0}) \right\} (u, v, z _ {0}) e ^ {- i 2 \pi w (u, v) d} \right\} (x, y, z). \tag {A14} \\ \end{array}
$$

Equation (A14) is consistent with Eq. (A1), albeit expressed differently, leading to a different computational approach. Applying the paraxial approximation to the transfer function, i.e.,

$$
w (u, v) = \frac {1}{\lambda} \left(1 - \lambda^ {2} \left(u ^ {2} + v ^ {2}\right)\right) ^ {\frac {1}{2}} \approx \frac {1}{\lambda} - \frac {\lambda}{2} \left(u ^ {2} + v ^ {2}\right), \tag {A15}
$$

yields the Fresnel diffraction formula. Under this approximation, we have

$$
u = \frac {\cos \gamma_ {x}}{\lambda} \approx \frac {x}{\lambda d}, \quad v = \frac {\cos \gamma_ {y}}{\lambda} \approx \frac {y}{\lambda d}. \tag {A16}
$$

Figure 70  
![](images/566ccf9632a7cf85be09444f370edd93725572f45d8e66f1aa792cf29cb8c25f.jpg)

<details>
<summary>flowchart</summary>

```mermaid
graph LR
  InputPlane["Amplitude profile of the input plane"] --> PhasePlane["Phase profile of the input plane"]
  PhasePlane -->|Yes| Diffraction["Diffraction"]
  PhasePlane -->|No| InputPlane
  Diffraction --> InitialPhase["Initial phase profile of the hologram"]
  InitialPhase -->|Start| InputPlane
  InputPlane -->|Diffraction| OutputPlane["Real amplitude profile of the output plane"]
  OutputPlane -->|Inverse diffraction| PhasePlane
  OutputPlane -->|Inverse diffraction| OutputPlane
  OutputPlane -->|Inverse diffraction| OutputPlane
  OutputPlane -->|Inverse diffraction| OutputPlane
```
</details>

Schematic of the Gerchberg-Saxton algorithm.

In this context, by the properties of Fourier transforms, Eq. (A14) can further simplify to

$$
E (x, y, z) = \frac {e ^ {i k d}}{i \lambda d} e ^ {i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} (x ^ {2} + y ^ {2})} \mathcal {F} \left\{E (x, y, z _ {0}) e ^ {i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} (x ^ {2} + y ^ {2})} \right\} \left(u = \frac {x}{\lambda d}, v = \frac {y}{\lambda d}, z _ {0}\right), \tag {A17}
$$

which is known as the single-step Fresnel propagation formula. Introducing a further approximation that the phase factor inside the Fourier transform in Eq. (A17) satisfies $\frac{\pi}{\lambda d}\left(x^{2}+y^{2}\right)\ll2\pi$ , which aligns with Eq. (A5), we obtain the Fraunhofer diffraction formula

$$
E (x, y, z) = \frac {e ^ {i k d}}{i \lambda d} e ^ {i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} \left(x ^ {2} + y ^ {2}\right)} \mathcal {F} \left\{E (x, y, z _ {0}) \right\} \left(u = \frac {x}{\lambda d}, v = \frac {y}{\lambda d}, z _ {0}\right). \tag {A18}
$$

When only interested in the diffraction pattern or light intensity distribution on the observation plane, factors outside the Fourier transform in Eqs. (A17) and (A18) [including a constant $\frac{1}{\lambda d}$ and a spatially varying phase factor $-ie^{ikd}e^{i\frac{\pi}{\lambda d}\left(x^{2} + y^{2}\right)}]$ can be disregarded.

In practical numerical calculations, it's necessary to sample the field distribution, and the discrete Fourier transforms in Eqs. (A14), (A17), and (A18) can be efficiently computed using the fast Fourier transform (FFT) algorithm. Thus, compared to Eqs. (A1), (A4), and (A6), which rely on integrals with a higher time complexity, these methods are generally more efficient. However, it is important to note that the presence of the phase factors $-2\pi w(u,v)d$ and $\frac{\pi}{\lambda d}(x^2 + y^2)$ in Eqs. (A14) and (A17), respectively, can lead to aliasing of the transfer function when the sampling is insufficient. This aliasing effect can result in inaccurate computational outcomes. At present, there is no complete solution to this issue [828,829], which imposes certain limitations on the practical application of Eqs. (A14) and (A17).

# APPENDIX B: THE GERCHBERG-SAXTON ALGORITHM (GS ALGORITHM)

The basic processes of the GS algorithm, depicted in Fig. 70, are outlined below:

(1) From the target holographic image $I(x,y)$ , obtain the target amplitude distribution on the output plane $A_{2}(x,y) = \sqrt{I(x,y)}$ . Note that the grayscale values of the image correspond to intensity, not amplitude.  
(2) Fix the amplitude distribution on the input plane $A_0(x, y)$ , which is typically set to all ones.  
(3) Choose an initial phase distribution for the input plane, which could be a constant or random distribution.  
(4) Based on the diffraction formula, calculate the complex amplitude distribution on the output plane, denoted as $A_{1}(x,y)e^{i\phi_{1}(x,y)}$ , from the complex amplitude distribution on the input plane.  
(5) Replace $A_{1}(x,y)$ with $A_{2}(x,y)$ while retaining $\phi_{1}(x,y)$ , to get a new complex amplitude distribution $A_{2}(x,y)e^{i\phi_{1}(x,y)}$ on the output plane. Then, using the inverse diffraction formula, calculate the complex amplitude distribution on the input plane, denoted as $A_{3}(x,y)e^{i\phi_{3}(x,y)}$ .  
(6) If the root mean square error (RMSE) between $A_2(x,y)$ and $A_1(x,y)$ is below an error threshold $\delta$ , or if the current number of iterations exceeds the upper limit $N$ , exit the iteration loop and the phase distribution $\phi_3(x,y)$ is the sought phase distribution on the input plane. Otherwise, replace $A_3(x,y)$ with $A_0(x,y)$ while retaining $\phi_3(x,y)$ , to get a new complex amplitude distribution $A_0(x,y) e^{i\phi_3(x,y)}$ on the input plane. Then, repeat steps (4)-(6).

The following discussion will further explore the details of the algorithm and its subsequent evolution.

(I) In the iteration, the amplitude distribution on the input plane is fixed but not necessarily constant. This feature can be utilized in the design of wavelength-multiplexed metasurface holograms based on space division multiplexing $[356,576]$ , which will be further discussed in Subsection 5.2.2.

(II) The solution for the input plane's phase distribution is not unique, and its initial phase distribution may influence the quality of the reconstructed holographic image [830].

(III) When the output plane is in the Fraunhofer diffraction zone, Eq. (A18) is used as the diffraction formula. The corresponding inverse diffraction formula, derived from Eq. (A18), is

$$
\begin{array}{l} E (x, y, z _ {0}) = \frac {i \lambda d}{e ^ {i k d}} e ^ {- i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} \left(x ^ {2} + y ^ {2}\right)} \mathcal {F} ^ {- 1} \left\{\tilde {E} (u, v, z) \right\} (x, y, z) \tag {B1} \\ = \frac {i \lambda d}{e ^ {i k d}} e ^ {- i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} \left(x ^ {2} + y ^ {2}\right)} \mathcal {F} ^ {- 1} \left\{E \left(x = \lambda d u, y = \lambda d v, z\right) \right\} (x, y, z). \\ \end{array}
$$

Since only the diffraction pattern on the observation plane is of concern, factors outside the (inverse) Fourier transform in Eqs. (A18) and (B1) can be ignored. Thus, the diffraction formula effectively becomes the Fourier transform formula, and the corresponding inverse diffraction formula becomes the inverse Fourier transform formula, with numerical implementations utilizing the FFT and inverse fast Fourier transform (IFFT) algorithms, respectively. Holograms in this category are referred to as Fraunhofer holograms.

(IV) When the output plane is in the Fresnel diffraction zone but not in the Fraunhofer zone, Eq. (A17) is used as the diffraction formula. The corresponding inverse

Figure 71  
![](images/e7bfe9a03a92d7d5b17a2222fa8b5b12a06e9423ea9fc577fcb731d147f0fffd.jpg)

</details>

Left: schematic of holographic display with uncorrected distortion. Right: schematic of distortion-free holographic display.

diffraction formula can be derived from Eq. (A17), resulting in

$$
\begin{array}{l} E \left(x, y, z _ {0}\right) = \frac {i \lambda d}{e ^ {i k d}} e ^ {- i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} \left(x ^ {2} + y ^ {2}\right)} \mathcal {F} ^ {- 1} \left\{\tilde {E} \left(u, v, z\right) e ^ {- i \pi \lambda d \left(u ^ {2} + v ^ {2}\right)} \right\} \left(x, y, z\right) \\ = \frac {i \lambda d}{e ^ {i k d}} e ^ {- i \frac {\pi}{\lambda d} \left(x ^ {2} + y ^ {2}\right)} \mathcal {F} ^ {- 1} \left\{E (x = \lambda d u, y = \lambda d v, z) e ^ {- i \pi \lambda d \left(u ^ {2} + v ^ {2}\right)} \right\} (x, y, z). \tag {B2} \\ \end{array}
$$

Similarly, factors outside the (inverse) Fourier transform in Eqs. (A17) and (B2) can be ignored. Holograms in this category are referred to as Fresnel holograms.

# APPENDIX C: COMPENSATION ALGORITHMS FOR WIDE-FOV HOLOGRAPHIC DISPLAY

For the beauty and clarity of the equations, here we denote $u$ and $v$ as $u_x$ and $u_y$ , respectively.

(I) Distortion compensation. As illustrated in the left panel of Fig. 71, the hologram is positioned at z=0, the observation plane is located at z=d, and $\theta_{x}=\frac{\pi}{2}-\gamma_{x}$ and $\theta_{y}=\frac{\pi}{2}-\gamma_{y}$ are the diffraction angles in the x and y directions, respectively. Using Eq. (A8), we have

$$
u _ {x} = \frac {\sin \theta_ {x}}{\lambda}, \quad u _ {y} = \frac {\sin \theta_ {y}}{\lambda}. \tag {C1}
$$

On a spherical surface centered at the hologram with a radius of $d$ , the coordinate of a point can be expressed as $\left(x' = d \sin \theta_x, y' = d \sin \theta_y\right)$ , which differs from $\left(u_x, u_y\right)$ in Eq. (C1) only by a constant term, so there's no distortion on the spherical surface. However, on the observation plane, the coordinate of the corresponding point is $\left(x = d \tan \theta_x, y = d \tan \theta_y\right)$ , and distortion can be ignored only under the paraxial approximation where $x \approx u_x \lambda d$ and $y \approx u_y \lambda d$ as $\theta_x$ and $\theta_y$ tend towards 0 [from Eq. (A16)]. As the diffraction angle increases, the differences between $\left(x', y'\right)$ and $(x, y)$ , i.e., $x' - x = d$ ( $\sin \theta_x - \tan \theta_x$ ) and $y' - y = d$ ( $\sin \theta_y - \tan \theta_y$ ), become more significant, leading to more severe distortion. Hence, the holographic image generated by the hologram solved by the GS algorithm based on the Fraunhofer or Fresnel diffraction formula will show significant distortion under a wide FOV.

For a point $(x,y)$ on the observation plane, we define its radius vector as $\vec{l}=\sqrt{x^{2}+y^{2}\vec{l}_{0}}$ [where $\vec{l}_{0}$ is the unit vector along the direction from the origin to $(x,y)$ ], and its diffraction angle as $\theta(x,y)$ . This point has a corresponding point $(x^{\prime},y^{\prime})$ on the spherical surface with the same diffraction angle, whose radius vector is defined as $\vec{l}^{\prime}=\sqrt{x^{\prime2}+y^{\prime2}\vec{l}_{0}}$ . The lengths of $\vec{l}$ and $\vec{l}^{\prime}$ satisfy

$$
l = d \tan \theta (x, y), \quad l ^ {\prime} = d \sin \theta (x, y), \tag {C2}
$$

leading to a relationship between the lengths:

$$
l ^ {\prime} = l \cos \theta (x, y) = \frac {l}{\sqrt {1 + \tan^ {2} \theta (x , y)}}. \tag {C3}
$$

Since the radius vectors $\vec{l}$ and $\vec{l}^{\prime}$ are co-directional, from Eq. (C3), we have

$$
x ^ {\prime} = \frac {x}{\sqrt {1 + \tan^ {2} \theta (x , y)}}, \quad y ^ {\prime} = \frac {y}{\sqrt {1 + \tan^ {2} \theta (x , y)}}, \tag {C4}
$$

which reflects the transformation relationship between a point $(x, y)$ on the observation plane and the corresponding point $(x', y')$ on the spherical surface.

To correct distortion, as illustrated in the right panel of Fig. 71, the holographic image on the observation plane, rather than that on the spherical surface, should be distortion-free, meaning that each point $(x,y)$ on the observation plane must move to a new position $(\tilde{x},\tilde{y})$ to coincide with its original undistorted counterpart $\left(x^{\prime}=d\sin\theta_{x},y^{\prime}=d\sin\theta_{y}\right)$ , i.e.,

$$
\tilde {x} = d \tan \tilde {\theta} _ {x} = d \sin \theta_ {x}, \quad \tilde {y} = d \tan \tilde {\theta} _ {y} = d \sin \theta_ {y}, \tag {C5}
$$

where $\tilde{\theta}_{x}$ and $\tilde{\theta}_{y}$ are the diffraction angles of $(\tilde{x},\tilde{y})$ in the x and y directions, respectively.

From the definition, we have

$$
\tan \tilde {\theta} _ {x} \left(\tilde {x}\right) = \frac {\tilde {x}}{d}, \quad \tan \tilde {\theta} _ {y} \left(\tilde {y}\right) = \frac {\tilde {y}}{d}, \tag {C6}
$$

and

$$
\tan \tilde {\theta} \left(\tilde {x}, \tilde {y}\right) = \frac {\sqrt {\tilde {x} ^ {2} + \tilde {y} ^ {2}}}{d}, \tag {C7}
$$

where $\tilde{\theta}\left(\tilde{x},\tilde{y}\right)$ is the corresponding diffraction angle of $(\tilde{x},\tilde{y})$ . Substituting Eq. (C6) into Eq. (C7), we obtain

$$
\tan^ {2} \tilde {\theta} \left(\tilde {x}, \tilde {y}\right) = \tan^ {2} \tilde {\theta} _ {x} \left(\tilde {x}\right) + \tan^ {2} \tilde {\theta} _ {y} \left(\tilde {y}\right). \tag {C8}
$$

Equations (C7) and (C8) will be used later.

From Eq. (C5), if the maximum diffraction angle is $\theta_{max}$ before distortion correction, which follows Eq. (275), the maximum diffraction angle $\tilde{\theta}_{max}$ after correction is

$$
\tilde {\theta} _ {\max} = \arctan \left(\sin \theta_ {\max}\right). \tag {C9}
$$

Substituting Eq. (275) into Eq. (C9) yields

$$
\tilde {\theta} _ {\max} = \arctan \frac {\lambda}{2 a}. \tag {C10}
$$

Based on Eq. (C4), the coordinate $(\tilde{x}', \tilde{y}')$ of the point on the spherical surface corresponding to $(\tilde{x}, \tilde{y})$ satisfies

$$
\tilde {x} ^ {\prime} = \frac {\tilde {x}}{\sqrt {1 + \tan^ {2} \tilde {\theta} \left(\tilde {x} , \tilde {y}\right)}}, \quad \tilde {y} ^ {\prime} = \frac {\tilde {y}}{\sqrt {1 + \tan^ {2} \tilde {\theta} \left(\tilde {x} , \tilde {y}\right)}}. \tag {C11}
$$

Equation (C11) indicates that the holographic image on the spherical surface is inevitably distorted now. In this case, from Eq. (C1), the target image for the GS algorithm possesses the same distortion as the holographic image on the spherical surface. This means that by pre-introducing specific distortions into the original target image, it is possible to achieve a distortion-free holographic display on the observation plane. The introduced distortion follows Eq. (C11), where the coordinate $(\tilde{x},\tilde{y})$ is reinterpreted as the coordinate of a pixel on the target image (taking the center of the image as the origin, the same as below) before distortion correction, and the coordinate $(\tilde{x}^{\prime},\tilde{y}^{\prime})$ is reinterpreted as the coordinate of this pixel on the distortion-corrected target image. The current issue is to calculate the diffraction angle $\tilde{\theta}\left(\tilde{x},\tilde{y}\right)$ corresponding to the pixel $(\tilde{x},\tilde{y})$ on the target image.

Equation (C6) indicates that the tangent of the diffraction angle is directly proportional to the coordinate values, with the holographic image edge marking the maximum diffraction angle, leading to

$$
\tan \tilde {\theta} _ {x} (\tilde {x}) = \frac {\tilde {x}}{D _ {x} / 2} \tan \tilde {\theta} _ {\max}, \quad \tan \tilde {\theta} _ {y} (\tilde {y}) = \frac {\tilde {y}}{D _ {y} / 2} \tan \tilde {\theta} _ {\max}, \tag {C12}
$$

where $D_{x} \times D_{y}$ is the size of the target image. We note that Eq. (C12) indicates that the degree of distortion in the x and y directions will differ if $D_{x} \neq D_{y}$ . Therefore, it is advisable to use the target holographic image with $D_{x} = D_{y}$ to avoid this issue. Substituting Eq. (C12) into Eq. (C8), we obtain

$$
\tilde {\theta} (\tilde {x}, \tilde {y}) = \arctan \left(\sqrt {\left(\frac {\tilde {x}}{D _ {x} / 2}\right) ^ {2} + \left(\frac {\tilde {y}}{D _ {y} / 2}\right) ^ {2}} \tan \tilde {\theta} _ {\max}\right). \tag {C13}
$$

Further substituting Eq. (C10) into Eq. (C13), we obtain the final expression of the diffraction angle

$$
\tilde {\theta} (\tilde {x}, \tilde {y}) = \arctan \left(\sqrt {\left(\frac {\tilde {x}}{D _ {x} / 2}\right) ^ {2} + \left(\frac {\tilde {y}}{D _ {y} / 2}\right) ^ {2}} \frac {\lambda}{2 a}\right). \tag {C14}
$$

Incorporating Eq. (C14) into Eq. (C11), we obtain the distortion correction formula for wide-FOV holography:

$$
\tilde {x} ^ {\prime} = \kappa \tilde {x}, \tilde {y} ^ {\prime} = \kappa \tilde {y}, \kappa = \frac {1}{\sqrt {1 + \left(\left(\frac {\tilde {x}}{D _ {x} / 2}\right) ^ {2} + \left(\frac {\tilde {y}}{D _ {y} / 2}\right) ^ {2}\right) \left(\frac {\lambda}{2 a}\right) ^ {2}}}. \tag {C15}
$$

To correct for distortion, the target image of the GS algorithm should undergo distortion compensation according to Eq. (C15).

(II) Intensity compensation. Aside from the distortion issue, the holographic image also suffers from a gradual decrease in brightness as the diffraction angle increases. This issue is primarily attributed to two factors.  
(i) Firstly, the intensity distribution of the holographic image on the spherical surface decreases with the diffraction angle. Now we derive the complex amplitude distribution on a spherical observation surface centered at the diffraction plane with a radius of R. From Eq. (A1), when the distance between the observation surface and the

diffracting plane is sufficiently large to neglect the $\frac{1}{2\pi\bar{r}}$ term, we obtain

$$
E (x, y, z) = \frac {1}{i \lambda} \frac {z - z _ {0}}{R ^ {2}} \iint_ {\Sigma} E \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, z _ {0}\right) e ^ {i k \bar {r}} \mathrm{d} x ^ {\prime} \mathrm{d} y ^ {\prime}. \tag {C16}
$$

Here, we use $z - z_{0}$ instead of d to emphasize that this value may vary for different points on the spherical observation surface. In Eq. (C16), since the spherical surface is used as the observation surface, we do not use the paraxial approximation $\bar{r} \approx z - z_{0}$ , but instead $\bar{r} \approx R$ . From the definition of the diffraction angle $\theta$ , we have

$$
\cos \theta = \frac {z - z _ {0}}{R}, \tag {C17}
$$

Substituting Eq. (C17) into Eq. (C16), we further obtain

$$
E (x, y, z) = \frac {1}{i \lambda R} \cos \theta \iint_ {\Sigma} E (x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}, z _ {0}) e ^ {i k \bar {r}} \mathrm{d} x ^ {\prime} \mathrm{d} y ^ {\prime}, \tag {C18}
$$

which implies that the amplitude of the electric field intensity on the spherical surface decreases with the increase of the diffraction angle according to the law of $\cos \theta$ , and thus the intensity values of the diffraction pattern on the spherical surface decrease with the increase of the diffraction angle according to the law of $\cos^2\theta$ . Comparing Eq. (C18) with Eq. (A2), it's evident that the diffraction formula based on the paraxial approximation neglects the factor $\cos \theta$ related to amplitude change, explaining why the intensity of the holographic image on the spherical surface of the hologram solved by the GS algorithm based on the Fresnel or Fraunhofer diffraction formulas decreases with the diffraction angle.

(ii) The second factor is that, as shown in Fig. 71, the propagation of light from the spherical surface to the observation plane is essentially a projection process of the diffraction pattern, where the power per unit area $P_{\mathrm{sphere}}(x',y')$ on the spherical surface is higher than the corresponding power per unit area $P_{\mathrm{plane}}(x,y)$ on the observation plane. This relationship is described by the projection formula

$$
P _ {\text {plane}} (x, y) = P _ {\text {sphere}} \left(x ^ {\prime}, y ^ {\prime}\right) \cos \theta (x, y), \tag {C19}
$$

where $\theta(x, y)$ is the diffraction angle at the location $(x, y)$ , and the coordinates $(x, y)$ and $(x', y')$ follow Eq. (C4).

To correct this issue, it's necessary to perform intensity compensation on the target image.

(I) Assume that distortion correction will follow this intensity compensation. Considering both factors, if the grayscale distribution of the target image before correction is $I(\tilde{x},\tilde{y})$ , then the corrected target image's grayscale distribution $I'(\tilde{x},\tilde{y})$ satisfies

$$
I ^ {\prime} \left(\tilde {x}, \tilde {y}\right) = \frac {I \left(\tilde {x} , \tilde {y}\right)}{\cos^ {3} \tilde {\theta} \left(\tilde {x} , \tilde {y}\right)}, \tag {C20}
$$

where $\tilde{\theta}\left(\tilde{x},\tilde{y}\right)$ is the actual diffraction angle of the corresponding point on the holographic image of a pixel at location $(\tilde{x},\tilde{y})$ on the target image. Substituting Eq. (C14) into Eq. (C20), the intensity compensation formula for the target image is obtained:

$$
I ^ {\prime} (\tilde {x}, \tilde {y}) = \frac {I (\tilde {x} , \tilde {y})}{\cos^ {3} \left(\arctan \left(\sqrt {\left(\frac {\tilde {x}}{D _ {x} / 2}\right) ^ {2} + \left(\frac {\tilde {y}}{D _ {y} / 2}\right) ^ {2}} \frac {\lambda}{2 a}\right)\right)}. \tag {C21}
$$

To correct the amplitude distribution $A(\tilde{x},\tilde{y}) = \sqrt{I(\tilde{x},\tilde{y})}$ , simply take the square root

Figure 72  
![](images/72c5f218e7a09be4e6d89a7e9fc074e5ad54fd7ba7869055a5fd1f910e0d2d79.jpg)  
Left: origin target image (the same as the target image in the third row in Fig. 38). Middle: result of distortion and intensity compensation for the origin target image. Right: Diffraction pattern on the observation plane of the hologram after distortion and intensity correction, calculated using the Rayleigh–Sommerfeld diffraction formula.

of both sides of Eq. (C21) to obtain the corresponding correction formula.

However, tests of this algorithm have indicated that there might be other reasons for the uneven brightness in holographic images. For example, the hologram solved by the GS algorithm may not always restore the holographic image intensity variations as represented by Eq. (C21), thus often requiring overcompensation in intensity correction. By further introducing an adjustment parameter $\beta$ into Eq. (C21), the intensity compensation formula becomes

$$
I ^ {\prime} \left(\tilde {x}, \tilde {y}\right) = \frac {I \left(\tilde {x} , \tilde {y}\right)}{\cos^ {\beta} \left(\arctan \left(\sqrt {\left(\frac {\tilde {x}}{D _ {x} / 2}\right) ^ {2} + \left(\frac {\tilde {y}}{D _ {y} / 2}\right) ^ {2}} \frac {\lambda}{2 a}\right)\right)}. \tag {C22}
$$

When $\beta > 3$ , the intensity compensation is excessive compared to Eq. (C21). In practice, $\beta$ can be adjusted until the Rayleigh–Sommerfeld diffraction pattern (holographic image) of the hologram obtained by the GS algorithm matches the target image well. For the original target image shown in the left panel of Fig. 72, the target image after distortion and intensity compensation (taking $\beta = 4$ ) is shown in the middle panel of Fig. 72, and the diffraction pattern of the hologram solved by the GS algorithm is shown in the right panel of Fig. 72. Note that both distortion and the issue of the image darkening with an increased FOV have been corrected.

(II) If no further distortion correction is conducted after intensity compensation, the intensity correction formula becomes

$$
I ^ {\prime} (x, y) = \frac {I (x , y)}{\cos^ {\beta} \left(\arcsin \left(\sqrt {\left(\frac {x}{D _ {x} / 2}\right) ^ {2} + \left(\frac {y}{D _ {y} / 2}\right) ^ {2}} \sin \theta_ {\max}\right)\right)}, \tag {C23}
$$

where the coordinate $(x,y)$ is the coordinate of a pixel on the target image, $I(x,y)$ is the grayscale distribution of the target image before correction, $I'(x,y)$ is the corrected target image's grayscale distribution, and $\theta_{max}$ is the maximum diffraction angle of the hologram, given by Eq. (275).

# APPENDIX D: COMPENSATION ALGORITHMS FOR COLOR HOLOGRAPHIC DISPLAY

According to Eqs. (A4) and (A6), for both Fresnel and Fraunhofer holograms, when $\lambda d$ is a constant, the diffraction pattern on the observation plane remains unchanged. This results in varying degrees of scaling of the diffraction pattern on a fixed observation plane for incident light at different wavelengths.

As shown in Fig. 73, for light at wavelengths $\lambda_{1}$ and $\lambda_{2}$ ( $\lambda_{1}<\lambda_{2}$ ), their half FOV angles (namely, maximized diffraction angles) are denoted as $\theta_{1}$ and $\theta_{2}$ , respectively. From Eq. (275), we have

$$
\sin \theta_ {1} = \frac {\lambda_ {1}}{2 a}, \quad \sin \theta_ {2} = \frac {\lambda_ {2}}{2 a}, \tag {D1}
$$

where $a$ is the unit size of the hologram. When compensating for chromatic aberration, the compensation for wide-FOV holography mentioned above is not considered. This means that intensity and distortion corrections are applied to the grayscale images of each color component of the target color image, following chromatic aberration correction, if necessary. Thus, the holographic image on a spherical surface centered at the hologram with a radius of $d$ is examined. Taking the wavelength $\lambda_{2}$ as an example, the semi-width of the holographic image is $l_{2} = \overline{\mathrm{BC}}$ , and it's easy to prove that $\triangle \mathrm{OBC}$ is congruent to $\triangle \mathrm{ODA}$ , leading to $l_{2} = \overline{\mathrm{BC}} = \overline{\mathrm{AD}} = d\sin \theta_{2}$ . Substituting Eq. (D1) gives

$$
l _ {2} = d \sin \theta_ {2} = d \frac {\lambda_ {2}}{2 a}. \tag {D2}
$$

Similarly, the semi-width of the holographic image generated by light at wavelength $\lambda_{1}$ is

$$
l _ {1} = d \sin \theta_ {1} = d \frac {\lambda_ {1}}{2 a}. \tag {D3}
$$

To ensure the holographic image generated by light at wavelength $\lambda_{2}$ is the same size as that generated by light at wavelength $\lambda_{1}$ , the target image for wavelength $\lambda_{2}$ needs to be scaled by a factor $s = \frac{l_{1}}{l_{2}}$ , referred to as chromatic aberration compensation. Using Eqs. (D2) and (D3), the scaling ratio is

$$
s = \frac {\sin \theta_ {1}}{\sin \theta_ {2}} = \frac {\lambda_ {1}}{\lambda_ {2}}. \tag {D4}
$$

This formula is given in reference [135].

Yubin Gao received his B.E. degree in Optoelectronic Information Science and Engineering from Zhejiang University, Hangzhou, China, in 2023. He is currently pursuing a Ph.D. in Optical Engineering at the College of Optical Science and Engineering, Zhejiang University. His research interests include novel physical effects in metasurfaces and advanced metasurface design methodologies.

![](images/44f3b9bae67c5a3c517ac4ede0098a827661640fc77acba041a15c95f223c4df.jpg)

<details>
<summary>natural_image</summary>

Portrait of a man wearing glasses and a suit (no text or symbols visible)
</details>

Yaoguang Ma is a Professor and Associate Dean of the College of Optical Science and Engineering, directing the NanoOptics Group at Zhejiang University. His current research interest centers on the mechanisms and associated effects of light—matter interactions at the mesoscopic scale. This encompasses areas such as meta-optics, computational imaging, and spectroscopy as well as precise and intelligent sensing technologies. His work bridges multiple disciplines, combining scientific exploration with practical engineering

applications. He has authored over 80 journal papers in many leading scientific journals, including Science, Adv. Opt. Photon., and PRL, among others.
